### [1] [Improving LLMs via Validator-to-Generator Alignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02668)
*Juan Diego Rodriguez,Jocelyn Zhang,Katrin Erk,Greg Durrett*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper addresses inconsistencies between how large language models generate answers and how they later validate those answers, proposing a frequency-corrected training objective that aligns generation and validation while improving performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models often behave inconsistently: small prompt variations or added irrelevant text can change outputs unexpectedly. A specific issue is the generator-validator (G-V) gap, where a model first generates an answer and later judges that same answer as invalid. Existing notions of G-V consistency are flawed because generators naturally assign low probabilities to otherwise valid but rare strings, so naive consistency metrics misinterpret this as inconsistency. The authors aim to formalize a better notion of consistency that accounts for answer frequency and to design a practical method that improves both consistency and task performance.

Method: The authors propose a new formulation of G-V consistency that explicitly corrects for the prior frequency (likelihood) of different utterances. They model rational agents answering multi-answer questions and show that, under this model, a validator should align with a generator score that has been adjusted for frequency. Based on this theory, they introduce FCPA, a training objective that enforces consistency between a frequency-corrected generator and a validator in real LLMs. They then train models using FCPA and compare them against prior G-V consistency methods on benchmark tasks.

Result: Models trained with FCPA achieve substantially higher generator-validator consistency and better generator performance than earlier approaches. Quantitatively, they report improvements of up to +27 percentage points in Pearson correlation between generator scores and validator judgments on benchmarks such as IFEval and HumanEval, while maintaining the quality of the validator across all evaluated tasks.

Conclusion: Frequency-corrected G-V consistency is a more principled and practical way to align how LLMs generate and validate answers. Implementing this idea via the FCPA training objective yields large gains in both measured consistency and generation performance without degrading validator quality, suggesting that correcting for utterance frequency is key to mitigating G-V gaps in real-world LLMs.

Abstract: Large language models are inconsistent: varying prompts or including unrelated information can lead to unexpected changes in model outputs. The generator-validator (G-V) gap is one manifestation of this phenomenon, where LLMs generate responses that they then deem as invalid if re-queried to validate them. In this work, we introduce a new formulation of G-V consistency that involves a principled correction for utterance frequency. Specifically, generators often assign low likelihood to valid strings simply because those strings are a priori unlikely, which makes naive notions of G-V consistency unworkable. We show that under a natural model of rational agents answering questions with multiple answers, consistency of the validator with a frequency-corrected generator score emerges naturally. Our method, \emph{\FCPAname} (\FCPA), is a training objective implementing frequency-corrected G-V consistency for real-world LLMs. Our experimental results show that training with \FCPA{} substantially improves both G-V consistency and generator performance over prior methods, with gains of up to $+27$pp in Pearson correlation on IFEval and HumanEval, while preserving validator quality across all evaluated tasks.

</details>


### [2] [Echoes of Unrest: A Multimodal NLP Framework for Early Warning of Fake News and Violence-Driven Mob Activity](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02734)
*Md. Maruf Bangabashi,Tahmid Hasan,Golam Mahmud,Md. Mostafijur Rahman,Md. Toufiqur Rahman,Jahanur Biswas*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a multilingual, multimodal NLP framework that fuses text, images, and auxiliary signals (e.g., sarcasm, geolocation) to detect misinformation and potential violence-inciting content, achieving very high accuracy on a Bangla–English dataset.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Social media enables rapid information sharing but also accelerates the spread of misinformation that can contribute to social unrest and real-world violence. Existing detection systems often focus on a single language or single modality (usually text), and struggle to generalize across languages and content types. There is a need for early-warning systems that can operate in multilingual, low-resource contexts and leverage both textual and visual cues, along with contextual metadata, to flag potentially harmful content before it escalates offline.

Method: The authors build a fused Bangla–English dataset (138,256 samples) by aggregating several benchmark misinformation datasets. Their framework uses XLM-RoBERTa to obtain multilingual text embeddings and CLIP to extract visual features from images. These representations are fused via a multi-head attention mechanism that learns cross-modal interactions. Additionally, they incorporate auxiliary features such as sarcasm indicators and geospatial metadata as extra input signals. The model is trained and evaluated on a stratified 30% subset of the dataset, optimizing a supervised classifier for misinformation and violence-prone content detection.

Result: On the stratified 30% subset, the proposed system achieves 98% test accuracy, with correspondingly strong precision and recall, indicating low false-positive and false-negative rates. The experiments show that multimodal fusion outperforms text-only or image-only baselines, and that inclusion of geospatial metadata further improves performance, especially for identifying content likely to correlate with real-world escalation.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that a multilingual, multimodal NLP framework can effectively detect misinformation and content associated with potential violence, even in low-resource language settings such as Bangla. Integrating text, images, and auxiliary signals like sarcasm and geospatial features yields substantial performance gains over unimodal approaches. The authors argue that such systems can support early-warning and intervention mechanisms on social platforms, helping mitigate the offline harms associated with rapidly spreading online misinformation.

Abstract: Rapid growth in social media has transformed global communication by enabling fast information exchange, but it has also accelerated the spread of misinformation. Fake news, manipulated content, and provocative narratives are increasingly linked to social unrest, political instability, and mob violence. Incidents in South Asia and elsewhere demonstrate how false information disseminated via platforms such as Facebook and WhatsApp can trigger real-world harm, often spreading faster than fact-checking efforts can respond. To address this challenge, this chapter presents a multilingual, multimodal Natural Language Processing (NLP) framework for early detection of misinformation and violence-prone dynamics. A fused dataset of 138,256 Bangla and English samples was created by combining multiple benchmark datasets. The framework integrates XLM-RoBERTa for multilingual text representation, CLIP for visual embedding, and a multi-head attention mechanism for multimodal fusion, enhanced with auxiliary features such as sarcasm and geospatial metadata. Experiments on a stratified 30% subset achieved 98% test accuracy with strong precision and recall. The outcomes show the efficacy of multimodal approaches in early misinformation detection and highlight the added value of geospatial signals for anticipating real-world escalation.

</details>


### [3] [Reinforcement Learning for Data-Efficient Code-Switched ASR](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02757)
*Ziwei Ye,Peter Vickers*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a reinforcement-learning-based method to adapt audio-language models for code-switched ASR, using verifiable rewards to improve performance around language/script boundaries with far less data than supervised fine-tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing audio-language models can be prompted for code-switched speech but perform poorly at language boundaries, often mistranscribing or translating instead of transcribing, especially across different scripts. Supervised fine-tuning for each language pair is data-hungry, and there is a need for a more data-efficient, generalizable way to adapt large audio-language models to robust code-switching ASR.

Method: They propose a reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR) recipe tailored to code-switched ASR, instantiated with group relative policy optimization. The reward combines (1) an error-rate-based term that encourages correct transcriptions and suppresses translations, and (2) a script-fidelity term that penalizes using the wrong writing system for a given language segment. Additionally, they use a two-pass draft-and-refinement decoding procedure. The approach is trained only on TTS-generated code-switched speech using Qwen2-Audio as the base model across 10 language pairs.

Result: With only 10% of the TTS training data, RLVR achieves performance comparable to LoRA-based supervised fine-tuning trained on the full dataset. The largest improvements are observed for typologically distant language pairs. The error-rate reward effectively removes translation errors, while the script-fidelity reward independently reduces script contamination without harming overall accuracy. The improvements also transfer zero-shot to a separate human-recorded code-switching dataset, demonstrating generalization beyond the synthetic training data.

Conclusion: Reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards is an effective, data-efficient strategy for adapting audio-language models to code-switched ASR. By combining error-rate and script-fidelity rewards with a two-pass decoding strategy, the method substantially improves handling of language and script boundaries, matches full-data supervised LoRA fine-tuning using only a fraction of the data, and generalizes to real human code-switched speech across multiple language pairs.

Abstract: Audio-language models can be prompted for code-switched speech, but their decoding is not optimized for code-switching and often fails at language boundaries. We propose a practical reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards recipe for data-efficient adaptation of audio-language models to code-switched ASR using group relative policy optimization, combining an error rate reward with a script fidelity reward that penalizes wrong writing systems and a two-pass draft-and-refinement procedure. Using Qwen2-Audio as a reproducible testbed across 10 language pairs, training on only TTS code-switched speech, we show that RLVR with 10% of the data matches LoRA supervised fine-tuning trained on the full dataset, with the largest gains on typologically distant pairs. The error rate reward eliminates translation errors while the script fidelity reward separately reduces script contamination without degradation. These gains transfer zero-shot to a human-recorded code-switching corpus.

</details>


### [4] [LuxSQA: Ask Me in Luxembourgish with TTS-Augmented Spoken Question Answering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02763)
*Nina Hosseini-Kivanani,Marco Matassoni,Alessio Brutti*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper explores using synthetic speech from multiple TTS systems to train a speech-based QA model for low-resource Luxembourgish without large human-recorded datasets, showing that diverse TTS sources improve performance and that standard TTS quality metrics don’t reliably predict QA effectiveness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Spoken QA research is dominated by high-resource languages and clean, carefully recorded data, which excludes many low-resource languages such as Luxembourgish. Collecting large-scale, human-spoken QA corpora is expensive and slow. The authors want to know if they can bootstrap effective SQA systems for a low-resource language by leveraging existing text QA resources plus TTS, and to understand which TTS configurations are most beneficial and how to evaluate them for downstream tasks.

Method: They start from existing text-based QA datasets, translate the textual questions into Luxembourgish, and then generate spoken versions of these questions using several different TTS systems (MMS-TTS, Qwen3-TTS, OmniVoice). These synthetic spoken questions are paired with their original textual answers to form SQA training data. They train a parameter-efficient SLAM-style architecture: a frozen Whisper speech encoder is connected to frozen multilingual LLM backends via a learned projection layer and LoRA adapters, avoiding full fine-tuning. They construct both single-source TTS corpora (~48k questions each) and a combined multi-source corpus (4TTS mix, ~230k questions). They then evaluate the resulting SQA systems on the LLAMA-LB-Test benchmark, which includes speech from two real Luxembourgish speakers, and analyze the impact of different TTS and training configurations as well as TTS quality scores.

Result: Models trained on multi-source synthetic speech and on carefully designed voice configurations achieve the best SQA performance on LLAMA-LB-Test with real Luxembourgish speakers. The study further finds that commonly used no-reference TTS quality metrics do not correlate monotonically with downstream QA accuracy; some TTS variants that sound better according to these metrics do not yield better QA performance.

Conclusion: Synthetic speech from TTS is a viable way to create task-specific SQA training data for a low-resource language like Luxembourgish, removing the need for large human-recorded QA datasets. Diversity in TTS sources and voice design is important, as multi-source training leads to stronger models. Standard no-reference TTS quality scores are insufficient as indicators of usefulness for SQA; synthetic speech should be judged by downstream task performance rather than only by perceived naturalness or quality.

Abstract: Spoken Question Answering (SQA) remains largely focused on high-resource languages and carefully recorded speech, limiting the reach of speech-LLM methods in low-resource settings. This paper investigates whether text-to-speech (TTS) can provide task-specific training data for Luxembourgish SQA without requiring a large human-recorded QA corpus. Starting from existing text-based QA resources, we translate questions into Luxembourgish, synthesize spoken questions with multiple TTS systems, and pair them with textual answers. We train a parameter-efficient SLAM-style architecture that connects a frozen Whisper encoder to frozen multilingual LLM backends through a learned projector and LoRA adapters. We compare MMS-TTS, Qwen3-TTS, and OmniVoice variants, including single-source corpora of about 48k questions and a 4TTS multi-source mix of approximately 230k questions. Evaluation on LLAMA-LB-Test with two real Luxembourgish speaker conditions shows that multi-source and voice-design-based synthetic training configurations yield the strongest SQA performance. The results also show that no-reference TTS quality scores do not monotonically predict downstream QA performance, indicating that synthetic speech must be evaluated as task-specific training data rather than only as natural-sounding audio.

</details>

*Gemma Team,Sherif El Abd,Vaibhav Aggarwal,Robin Algayres,Alek Andreev,Olivier Bachem,Ian Ballantyne,Cormac Brick,Victor Cărbune,Michelle Casbon,Mayank Chaturvedi,Victor Cotruta,Alice Coucke,Phil Culliton,Robert Dadashi,Lucas Dixon,Mohamed Elhawaty,Utku Evci,Clément Farabet,Johan Ferret,Filippo Galgani,Sertan Girgin,Jean-Bastien Grill,Maarten Grootendorst,Jiaxian Guo,Cassidy Hardin,Yanzhang He,Steven M. Girgis,Alec Go,Alhaad Gokhale,Alex Grills,Yiming Gu,Pramod Gupta,Guru Guruganesh,Raia Hadsell,Hamza Harkous,Jitendra Harlalka,Demis Hassabis,Anja Hauth,Joe Heyward,Arian Hosseini,Chih-Yang Hsia,I-Hung Hsu,Xiaopeng Huang,Yangsibo Huang,Kevin Hui,Adrian Hutter,Te I,Fotis Iliopoulos,Advait Jain,Ganesh Jawahar,Ziwei Ji,Qilin Jin,Melvin Johnson,Kandarp Joshi,Arun Kandoor,Wang-Cheng Kang,Koray Kavukcuoglu,Mehran Kazemi,Kathleen Kenealy,Amr Khalifa,Phoebe Kirk,Suraj Kothawade,Vitaly Kovalev,Neel Kovelamudi,Adam Kraft,Ravin Kumar,Harish Kuppam,Justin Lannin,Chen-Yu Lee,Seungji Lee,Dmitry Lepikhin,Dongdong Li,Qiujia Li,Valentin Liévin,Ethan Lin,Ziqian Lin,Casper Liu,Tianlin Liu,Tianqi Liu,Xin Liu,Mayank Lunayach,Min Ma,Gagan Madan,Andrii Maksai,Eric Malmi,Michal Matuszak,Daniel McDuff,Gaurav Menghani,Daniil Mirylenka,Karolis Misiunas,Vedant Misra,Andreea Mitran,Kareem Mohamed,Maksim Mukha,Eric Noland,James O'Donnell,Kate Olszewska,Bernett Orlando,Wanqiong Pan,Rina Panigrahy,Unnati Parekh,Chunjong Park,Eric Paskie,Liqian Peng,Bryce Petrini,Slav Petrov,Jonas Pfeiffer,Bilal Piot,Martyna Plomecka,Siim Poder,Octavio Ponce,Arijit Pramanik,David Racz,Anish Rajan,Michelle Ramanovich,Anand Rao,Marvin Ritter,Vitor Rodrigues,Evan Rosen,Mikołaj Rybiński,Noveen Sachdeva,Michaël E. Sander,Rohit Sathyanarayana,Sagar Savla,Samuel Schmidgall,Tal Schuster,Benoit Seguin,Andrew Sellergren,Aliaksei Severyn,Izhak Shafran,Dhruv Shah,Yuan Shangguan,Ashish Shenoy,Pradeep Shenoy,Rakesh Shivanna,Pauline Sho,Lucas Spangher,Wojciech Stokowiec,Tim Strother,Yao Su,Yinghao Sun,Mukund Sundararajan,Andrea Tacchetti,Mor Hazan Taege,Pouya Tafti,Chetan Tekur,Rahul Thapa,Madeleine Traverse,Lenart Treven,Tao Tu,Chien Te Tung,Petar Veličković,Malini Pooni Venkat,Sagar Gubbi Venkatesh,Vidya Venkiteswaran,Francesco Visin,Alex Vitvitskyi,Kiran Vodrahalli,Weiyi Wang,Xin Wang,Tris Warkentin,Jan Wassenberg,John Wieting,Lechao Xiao,Hao Xu,Yuhui Xu,Fuzhao Xue,Arun Yadav,Jun Yan,Antoine Yang,Lin Yang,Ming-Hsuan Yang,Ziyu Ying,Jae Hyeon Yoo,Sajjad Zafar,Fred Zhang,Jiageng Zhang,Jianyi Zhang,Xiaofan Zhang,Chao Zhao,David Zhou,Chen Zou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Gemma 4 is a new family of efficient, open multimodal language models with improved reasoning, modality handling, and long-context performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing open-weight models struggle to simultaneously provide strong reasoning, multimodal capabilities (text, vision, audio), long-context handling, and high compute efficiency across a range of model sizes. The authors aim to design a new generation of models that better balances performance, efficiency, and modality coverage, and that can compete with larger frontier open models, especially on STEM and reasoning-heavy tasks.

Method: They build a suite of Gemma 4 models using both dense and Mixture-of-Experts architectures, covering parameter sizes from 2.3B to 31B. All models include improved vision and audio encoders, and for the 12B variant they propose a unified architecture that removes separate encoders, directly consuming raw audio and image patches. They also incorporate a "thinking mode" that allows the models to produce intermediate reasoning traces before final answers. Additionally, they apply architectural and implementation optimizations to accelerate inference, reduce memory and compute usage, and enhance long-context capabilities, then benchmark performance across STEM, multimodal, and long-context tasks.

Result: Gemma 4 models achieve substantial performance gains on STEM, multimodal, and long-context benchmarks, while also showing better inference speed, memory, and compute efficiency. Their performance rivals that of larger open frontier models when evaluated on human-rated tasks, indicating that the models are both capable and practical for real-world use.

Conclusion: The Gemma 4 suite represents a significant step forward for open-weight, multimodal language models, demonstrating that carefully designed architectures and training strategies can deliver strong reasoning, multimodal understanding, and long-context handling with improved computational efficiency. These models offer competitive performance against larger open models and broaden the practicality of advanced multimodal LLMs across a range of deployment scenarios.

Abstract: We introduce Gemma 4, a new generation of open-weight, natively multimodal language models in the Gemma model family. Designed to advance compute efficiency and reasoning, the Gemma 4 model suite features dense and Mixture-of-Experts architectures, ranging from 2.3B to 31B parameters. Alongside improved vision and audio encoders for all model sizes, we propose a unified, encoder-free architecture for our 12B model, which ingests raw audio and image patches. Furthermore, we integrate a thinking mode, enabling Gemma models to generate reasoning traces prior to responding. We improve inference speed, memory, and compute efficiency, as well as long-context abilities through critical design choices. Gemma 4 establishes a leap in performance across STEM, multimodal, and long-context benchmarks, and rivals larger, frontier open models in human-rated tasks.

</details>


### [6] [Seduced by the Narrative: Assessing Rule Adherence in Semi-Open Textual Sandboxes](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02802)
*Weiying Chen,Junlong Shen,Zhanyuan Guo,Xiaoou Zhou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces CoC-Seduce, a TRPG-based multi-agent benchmark to test how easily LLM adjudicators can be manipulated via rhetorical attacks, showing that current models remain broadly vulnerable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are increasingly used as autonomous adjudicators in semi-open, rule-governed environments (like games or moderation systems), where they must enforce explicit rules in the face of potentially adversarial user inputs. Existing safety and robustness evaluations focus heavily on overt jailbreaks or prompt-injection targeting system prompts, but less on subtle, narrative and rhetorical strategies that exploit the models’ helpfulness and deference to users. The authors identify a gap: there is no systematic benchmark that stresses LLMs with sophisticated, natural-language rhetorical tactics in realistic interactive settings like TRPGs, where rules are explicit but communication is open-ended. They aim to understand how vulnerable current LLM adjudicators are to such “rhetorical injection” and what kinds of attacks and settings are most problematic.

Method: The authors build CoC-Seduce, a multi-agent adversarial benchmark grounded in Tabletop Role-Playing Game mechanics, where a model acts as an adjudicator enforcing rules in natural-language interactions. They define and operationalize a new attack class called Rhetorical Injection, which uses narrative framing, pseudo-logical arguments, and authoritative coercion to push the adjudicator into breaking or bending rules. Three strong LLMs (GPT-5.4, Claude Sonnet 4.6, Gemini 3.5 Flash) are used as adversarial generators that craft attack attempts across 4 designed world settings and 16 skill categories, yielding 5,376 adversarial samples. Then, 20 different LLMs are evaluated as target adjudicators on this corpus to measure their robustness to these rhetorical attacks, including analysis of how model size and explicit reasoning features relate to robustness, and which rhetorical strategies are most effective.

Result: From evaluating 20 models on the CoC-Seduce benchmark, the authors find that neither larger model scale nor the inclusion of explicit reasoning mechanisms consistently improves robustness to rhetorical injection attacks. Across the corpus, attacks leveraging pseudo-logical reasoning—where arguments are framed as logically structured but are in fact misleading or rule-violating—are the most successful in inducing rule-breaking decisions. Moreover, scenarios designed with cross-cultural elements systematically expose knowledge gaps shared across model families, indicating that adjudication failures are often linked to incomplete or biased world knowledge, especially in culturally specific contexts.

Conclusion: The study concludes that current LLM adjudicators are broadly vulnerable to rhetorical injection attacks in semi-open, rule-based environments, and common design choices like scaling up model size or enabling explicit reasoning are insufficient for robust rule enforcement. Pseudo-logical attacks are particularly dangerous, and cross-cultural settings highlight systematic knowledge and robustness limitations. CoC-Seduce is proposed as a challenging, realistic benchmark for future research on aligning adjudication behavior, developing defenses against rhetorical manipulation, and stress-testing LLM deployment in interactive systems such as TRPGs and beyond.

Abstract: As LLMs are increasingly deployed as autonomous adjudicators in semi-open textual game environments, robust rule adherence becomes critical when user intent conflicts with system rules. However, these models are trained to be helpful and compliant, leaving them vulnerable to a class of attacks we term \textit{Rhetorical Injection}, where adversarial users exploit narrative framing techniques such as pseudo-logical reasoning and authoritative coercion to bypass adjudication logic. We present CoC-Seduce, a multi-agent adversarial benchmark built on Tabletop Role-Playing Game (TRPG) mechanics, an ideal instantiation of semi-open environments where rules are explicit for adjudication, yet interaction remains entirely in natural language. Three frontier models, i.e., GPT-5.4, Claude Sonnet 4.6, Gemini 3.5 Flash, serve as adversarial generators producing 5,376 samples across 4 world settings and 16 skill categories. We then benchmark 20 target adjudicators against this corpus. Evaluation across 20 models reveals that neither model scale nor explicit reasoning mechanisms reliably confer adjudication robustness, with \textsc{Pseudo-Logic} emerging as the dominant attack vector and cross-cultural settings exposing systematic knowledge gaps across all evaluated families. Project page: https://github.com/answerrtx/CoC-Seduce

</details>


### [7] [Jointly Improving Dialect Identification and ASR in Indian Languages using Multimodal Feature Fusion](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02862)
*Saurabh Kumar,Amartyaveer,Prasanta Kumar Ghosh*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Jointly improves Automatic Speech Recognition (ASR) and Dialect Identification (DID) for low-resource Indian languages using a multimodal architecture that fuses acoustic and text-based features.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Indian languages often lack labeled data and have strong dialectal variation, which hurts both ASR accuracy and dialect identification. Prior work typically optimizes ASR and DID separately, causing trade-offs where improving one degrades the other. There is a need for a unified framework that can leverage dialectal cues to boost ASR while simultaneously performing robust dialect identification.

Method: The paper proposes a multimodal joint ASR-DID framework. Speech is first encoded with a Conformer-based acoustic model. A Bottleneck Encoder extracts dialect-specific features from these speech representations. In parallel, a RoBERTa encoder processes CTC-derived textual embeddings from the ASR output. A gating mechanism fuses the dialectal acoustic features and the RoBERTa-based textual features. An attention encoder then refines the fused representation. Finally, the learned embeddings are concatenated with the Conformer outputs to enrich ASR features and to support DID classification, enabling joint optimization of both tasks.

Result: On eight Indian languages spanning thirty-three dialects, the approach attains an average dialect identification accuracy of 81.63%, with character error rate (CER) of 4.65% and word error rate (WER) of 17.73% for ASR. These metrics outperform standard baselines that model ASR and DID separately (details implied but not given in the abstract).

Conclusion: The multimodal, jointly trained framework effectively leverages both acoustic and text-based information to improve ASR and dialect identification simultaneously. The strong performance across many Indian languages and dialects demonstrates that explicitly modeling dialectal features and fusing them with ASR representations is a promising direction for low-resource, dialect-rich language technologies.

Abstract: Automatic Speech Recognition (ASR) and Dialect Identification (DID) are crucial for Indian languages, many of which are low-resource and exhibit significant dialectal differences. Existing methods often optimize ASR or DID individually, resulting in performance trade-offs. In this work, we propose a multimodal framework that jointly improves ASR and DID. Our method employs a Bottleneck Encoder to extract dialectal features from Conformer-based speech representations and a RoBERTa encoder to process ASR-generated CTC embeddings. A gating mechanism merges these features, followed by an attention encoder to refine the representations. The learned embeddings are concatenated with Conformer outputs to enhance ASR features. Evaluated on eight Indian languages with thirty-three dialects, our method achieves an average DID accuracy of 81.63% and average CER and WER of 4.65% and 17.73%, respectively. These results highlight the effectiveness of our method for joint ASR-DID modeling.

</details>


### [8] [PraMem: Practice-derived Experiential Memory for Long-horizon Behavior Prediction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02881)
*Zhuoqun Li,Boxi Cao,Jiawei Chen,Hanshu Zhou,Ruoxi Xu,Guiping Jiang,Ruotong Pan,Tingting Gao,Han Li,Xiangyu Wu,Hongyu Lin,Yaojie Lu,Xianpei Han,Le Sun*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes PraMem, a new paradigm for long-horizon user behavior prediction that treats long histories as a resource, not a burden, by building an experiential memory through prior practice over the full sequence, leading to improved prediction performance over existing memory-compression methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-horizon behavior prediction is important in AI applications such as recommendation and user modeling, but existing approaches, including LLM-based methods, struggle with inferring latent behavioral patterns across long sequences and suffer from cognitive biases when histories are long. Current memory management techniques focus on compressing or truncating history to fit model context windows, which eases computational load but does not fundamentally solve the difficulty of leveraging rich long-term behavior. There is thus a need for a new paradigm that can effectively exploit, rather than compress away, lengthy behavioral histories for better prediction.

Method: The authors introduce PraMem, which reframes long historical sequences as a valuable resource and builds an explicit experiential memory from them. Instead of compressing the context at inference time, PraMem conducts a prior “practice” phase over the entire long sequence to induce and store behavior patterns in a dedicated memory structure. This experiential memory is then used as assisted input for the LLM (or base model) during long-horizon behavior prediction. The framework contrasts with context-compression paradigms by emphasizing pattern induction and memory construction ahead of prediction, rather than aggressive summarization of history on the fly.

Result: Across multiple tasks related to long-horizon user behavior prediction, PraMem consistently outperforms previous memory management and context-compression methods. The experiments show superior accuracy in predicting users’ next actions when using experiential memory. Additional analyses examine how the experiential memory evolves, what kinds of behavioral patterns it captures, and how these contribute to the model’s improved performance, offering empirical evidence for the effectiveness and interpretability of the approach.

Conclusion: PraMem demonstrates that treating long behavioral histories as material for proactive practice and experiential memory construction can significantly improve long-horizon behavior prediction compared with traditional context-compression approaches. The work suggests a paradigm shift in how LLMs and sequence models manage long contexts, highlighting that explicitly induced experiential memory can mitigate LLM biases, better capture latent behavioral patterns, and serve as a powerful auxiliary input for downstream prediction tasks.

Abstract: Long-horizon behavior prediction aims to infer a user's next action based on a lengthy historical sequence, playing a crucial role in artificial intelligence field. The rise of large language models (LLMs) offers a promising direction for sequential behavior prediction, yet LLMs struggle with latent behavioral pattern induction and model-intrinsic cognitive biases when tackling long-horizon behavior prediction. Prior memory management methods follow a context-compression paradigm that attempts to address this task by alleviating the historical sequence burden, yet fail to resolve the core challenges. In this paper, we advocate a paradigm shift that reframes the lengthy historical sequence from a burden into a valuable resource to be exploited, and accordingly propose PraMem, which conducts beforehand practice over the lengthy historical sequence to build an experiential memory, thereby serving as the assisted input for accurate long-horizon behavior prediction. Extensive experiments across diverse tasks demonstrate that PraMem achieves superior performance than prior methods, and more in-depth analyses provide valuable insights into the mechanism and evolution of the experiential memory. Code: https://github.com/icip-cas/PraMem.

</details>


### [9] [Where do LLMs Fall Short in CBT-Guided Affective Reasoning?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02885)
*Vaishnavi Sinha,Pooja Guttal,Pranay Deep Reddy Katike,Vishal Sinha,Gerald Ndawula,Lira Yoon,Andrea Kleinsmith,Manas Gaur*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates why large language models (LLMs) that know CBT theory still fail to perform effective CBT-style conversations and proposes a knowledge-guided framework plus a new metric to quantify how much protocols actually change model behavior.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs can answer CBT exam questions with high accuracy, they tend to default to generic validation and reflection in real dialogue instead of using appropriate CBT techniques. The authors want to understand and measure this gap between theoretical knowledge and practical therapeutic application, and to provide tools for the affective-computing community to systematically study and improve LLM-guided CBT interactions.

Method: They model CBT dialogue as controlled affective reasoning. User narratives are decomposed using Beck’s Cognitive Conceptualization framework and mapped to clinical concepts from SNOMED CT, with mappings validated via Natural Language Inference. On top of this representation, they implement a Multiple Chain-of-Thought (MCoT) mechanism that selects among three strategies—Validation & Reflection, Socratic Questioning, and Alternative Perspectives. They then define a new behavior-level metric, Protocol Leverage Force (F), to quantify how much an intervention (like protocol prompts or MCoT) changes the model’s behavior compared to its default. Experiments are run on three open-weight LLMs using 14 RealCBT-derived case studies, with assessments involving human experts, valence-arousal trajectories, and linguistic entrainment measures.

Result: Introducing protocol definitions with a single chain-of-thought prompt does not significantly alter LLM behavior; models keep defaulting to Validation & Reflection. Using MCoT over the same protocol definitions improves strategy selection and shifts behavior more measurably, but the Protocol Leverage Force remains small—within about 1–1.3%. All tested models show a strong persistent bias toward Validation & Reflection despite the structured guidance.

Conclusion: Merely embedding CBT knowledge or protocol descriptions into LLMs is insufficient for achieving effective CBT-style dialogue. Even with structured affective reasoning, clinical concept grounding, and MCoT strategy selection, current models only weakly shift away from their default validation-heavy style. The proposed Protocol Leverage Force metric and overall framework offer the affective-computing community a way to quantify and analyze where and how LLMs fall short in applying CBT in practice, highlighting the need for more robust mechanisms than knowledge injection or simple prompting to change conversational behavior.

Abstract: Cognitive Behavioral Therapy (CBT) provides a structured framework for understanding a user's mental state by examining the interaction between cognitive and behavioral factors. However, out-of-the-box LLMs respond fluently and empathetically, yet collapse into validation & reflection, regardless of what the user actually needs. They know theoretical CBT (scoring up to 96% accuracy on licensing exam questions) but fail to apply it effectively. We explore this gap with a knowledge-guided framework that treats CBT dialogue as controlled affective reasoning: user narratives are decomposed into Beck's Cognitive Conceptualization structure, grounded in clinical SNOMED CT concepts validated via Natural Language Inference, and a Multiple Chain-of-Thought (MCoT) strategy selection between Validation & Reflection, Socratic Questioning, or Alternative Perspectives. To measure whether such guidance actually changes behavior, we introduce the Protocol Leverage Force (F), a behavior-level metric that captures how far an intervention shifts a model away from its default response. Across three open-weight LLMs and 14 RealCBT-derived case studies, evaluated with human experts, valence-arousal trajectories, and linguistic entrainment, F shows that simply introducing protocol definitions via single chain-of-thought prompting fails to change LLM behavior, while MCoT on these definitions guides strategy selection better. Still, the effect stays within 1% (approx. 1.2-1.3%), and all models remain biased toward Validation & Reflection. These results show CBT knowledge alone does not ensure effective application, giving the affective-computing community instrumentation to measure where LLMs fall short.

### [10] [Distill Where the Student Goes: Teacher-Regularized RL for English-Evidence Cross-Lingual RAG](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02966)
*Haotian Zhou,Weiran Huang,Siqi Liu,Xiting Wang,Xin Zhang,Zhihao Wen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces TR-RAG, a teacher-regularized reinforcement learning method that stabilizes and improves cross-lingual retrieval-augmented generation when evidence is in English but queries and answers are in many languages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In cross-lingual RAG, users query in various languages but the retrieved evidence is English. Even with strong base models, generation often drifts into English or code-switching and uses evidence unreliably when answering in non-English languages. Existing post-training with sequence-level rewards suffers from prefix mismatch (training trajectories differ from those at test time) and noisy credit assignment, leading to unstable or degraded performance. The authors aim to build a training procedure that improves both language adherence (staying in the target language) and evidence-grounded correctness without causing collapse on in-domain languages or stagnation on out-of-distribution ones.

Method: They propose TR-RAG, a teacher-regularized reinforcement learning approach. A compact student model generates answers on-policy; for every student-visited prefix, a stronger frozen teacher is queried. Training combines (1) reward optimization and (2) on-policy distillation through a reverse KL term that pulls the student toward the teacher’s distribution on those prefixes, acting as a regularizer. They also design a decomposed reward for the English-evidence multilingual RAG setting, with three components: language consistency (output matches the user’s language), character 3-gram recall (n-gram overlap with references), and an LLM-judge score for evidence-grounded correctness. This training is evaluated on multiple datasets and model backbones.

Result: Across three benchmarks (BioASQ-ENKB5, Hotpot-ENKB5, and MKQA) and two backbone models, TR-RAG improves a composite metric that jointly measures language adherence and evidence-grounded correctness, outperforming strong baselines. The teacher-regularization term acts as a safety net: on in-domain languages it prevents large drops in language consistency that occur with reward-only RL (reported collapses of up to about 27 percentage points, sometimes falling below base-model performance). On distant out-of-distribution languages, where reward-only RL fails to improve over the base model, TR-RAG still enhances evidence grounding. Moreover, on character 3-gram recall, the compact student can even exceed the performance of its much larger 70B-parameter teacher.

Conclusion: Teacher-regularized RL with on-policy distillation (TR-RAG) addresses key shortcomings of reward-only RL for cross-lingual RAG in English-evidence settings. By anchoring student behavior to a strong frozen teacher at the prefix level and using a decomposed reward tailored to multilingual generation, TR-RAG improves both language adherence and evidence-grounded correctness while avoiding severe consistency collapses. It yields gains across datasets and languages, including out-of-distribution ones, and can enable a smaller student to match or surpass aspects of a much larger teacher.

Abstract: Cross-lingual retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) is often deployed in an English-evidence regime, where users query in diverse languages but retrieved passages remain English. In this setting, generation can fail despite strong base models: English evidence induces language drift (English or code-switching outputs) and models use evidence unreliably when producing non-English answers. We attribute these failures to two post-training challenges: (i) errors are prefix-dependent, so fixed-trajectory supervision suffers from prefix mismatch; and (ii) sequence-level (partly discrete / judge-based) rewards yield noisy credit assignment and high-variance updates. We propose TR-RAG, a teacher-regularized RL recipe that couples reward optimization with on-policy distillation on student-visited prefixes. A compact student samples on-policy answers, while a stronger frozen teacher is queried only on those prefixes and provides a prefix-wise student-to-teacher reverse-KL anchor. We further introduce a reward decomposition for English-evidence multilingual generation, combining language consistency, character 3-gram recall, and an LLM-judge score for evidence-grounded correctness. Across three benchmarks -- BioASQ-ENKB5, Hotpot-ENKB5, and naturally multilingual MKQA -- and two backbones, TR-RAG improves the composite of language adherence and evidence-grounded correctness over strong baselines. Crucially, the teacher anchor acts as a safety net: on in-domain languages it prevents the large language-consistency collapses (up to ~27 percentage points) that reward-only RL can suffer by drifting below even the base model, while on distant out-of-distribution languages -- where reward-only RL stalls at the base model's ceiling -- it still improves evidence grounding; and on character 3-gram recall the compact student sometimes surpasses its 70B teacher.

### [11] [Hierarchical Sparse Attention Done Right: Toward Infinite Context Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02980)
*Xiang Hu,Xinyu Wei,Hao Gu,Minshen Zhang,Tian Liang,Huayang Li,Lei Zhu,Yan Wang,Sirui Han,Yushi Bai,Kewei Tu,Haitao Mi,Leo Liang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces HiLS-Attention, a hierarchical chunk-wise sparse attention mechanism that matches or surpasses full attention on standard contexts while enabling efficient, accurate extrapolation to extremely long contexts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Dense full attention in modern LLMs is computationally quadratic in context length and exhibits poor length extrapolation. Existing chunk-wise sparse attention methods are more efficient but underperform full attention because their chunk selection mechanisms are not learned end-to-end and often choose suboptimal chunks.

Method: The authors propose Hierarchical Landmark Sparse (HiLS) Attention, which factorizes attention into two stages. First, a retrieval mechanism scores and selects chunks of the context for each query. Then, each query attends independently to each retrieved chunk to obtain chunk-specific representations. These are fused using the retrieval scores as weights. Crucially, the retrieval scores are part of the forward attention computation and are optimized directly using the language modeling loss, enabling end-to-end learning of chunk selection and native sparse training. The method also allows converting existing full-attention models into HiLS models with lightweight continued pretraining.

Result: HiLS-Attention matches or sometimes exceeds the performance of full attention at the context lengths seen during training. It achieves accurate long-context extrapolation, maintaining about 90% retrieval accuracy even at more than 64 times the training context length. It also yields sparse key-value access and computation, improving efficiency while retaining or improving effectiveness on long-context tasks. Furthermore, full-attention LLMs can be converted to HiLS variants with minimal additional training, preserving their in-domain performance while gaining strong long-context capabilities.

Conclusion: HiLS-Attention overcomes the typical efficiency–performance trade-off in long-context LLMs by jointly learning retrieval and attention in a hierarchical, chunk-wise sparse framework. This delivers models that are more computationally efficient than full-attention LLMs yet match or outperform them on both standard and extremely long-context tasks, offering a practical path to scalable long-context language modeling.

Abstract: Scaling modern large language models (LLMs) to long contexts is limited by the quadratic computation cost, and poor length extrapolation of dense attention. Chunk-wise sparse attention offers a promising alternative, but all existing methods fall short of full attention because of their inaccurate chunk selection. We propose Hierarchical Landmark Sparse (HiLS) Attention, a chunk-wise sparse attention mechanism that learns chunk selection end-to-end under the language-modeling (LM) loss. HiLS factorizes attention hierarchically: each query performs attention independently with each retrieved chunk to extract chunk-specific information, and the resulting outputs are fused according to chunk retrieval scores. By incorporating retrieval scores into the forward attention computation, HiLS optimizes them directly with the LM loss, enabling end-to-end retrieval learning and native sparse training. Experimental results show that HiLS-Attention achieves performance comparable to, and in some cases better than, full attention at in-domain context lengths. Meanwhile, HiLS-Attention extrapolates more than $64\times$ the training context length with 90% retrieval accuracy, far beyond full attention. Moreover, existing full-attention models can be converted to HiLS-Attention with lightweight continued pretraining, preserving in-domain performance while acquiring ultra-long-context extrapolation. Together with its sparse KV access and computation, HiLS-Attention breaks the usual efficiency-performance trade-off, enabling long-context LLMs that are both more efficient and more effective on general long-context tasks than their full-attention counterparts.

</details>

### [13] [Alignment-Guided Largest Table Overlap Size Estimation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03049)
*Ge Lee,Shixun Huang,Zhifeng Bao,Shazia Sadiq,Yanchang Zhao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes ALORE, a new method to quickly and accurately estimate the largest overlap ratio between tables in large, heterogeneous table repositories, significantly improving accuracy and speed over prior work like Armadillo.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing table overlap estimators like Armadillo embed each table independently and approximate overlap via embedding similarity, which fails in heterogeneous repositories. The main issues are: (C1) overlap truly depends on row-column structure, but current encodings don’t model this explicitly; (C2) independent encoding ignores explicit inter-table alignment signals and overemphasizes global similarity; (C3) naive value encodings overfit to particular corpora and don’t generalize across domains. There is a need for a scalable, domain-robust estimator that directly addresses these limitations to support blocking and query-by-table retrieval at scale.

Method: The authors propose ALORE, a scalable and domain-robust overlap ratio estimator based on three principles: (P1) explicitly represent row-column structure; (P2) expose inter-table alignment signals during training without expensive alignment search; and (P3) reduce sensitivity to corpus-specific value distributions. ALORE implements these via: (i) a Two-View Row-Column Hypergraph encoder that jointly models rows and columns to capture structural constraints; (ii) alignment-guided training objectives that use inexpensive interaction signals between tables to inject alignment information into the encoder without costly matching; and (iii) a domain-robust value mapping that normalizes or abstracts cell values to prevent overfitting to specific corpus distributions.

Result: On multiple datasets from diverse domains and scales, including a large real-world corpus beyond prior benchmarks, ALORE achieves state-of-the-art performance. It reduces mean absolute error (MAE) in overlap estimation by up to 55% overall and 69% in zero-shot transfer scenarios compared to prior methods such as Armadillo. It also offers substantial efficiency gains, with up to 89x speedup. Furthermore, experiments show that using ALORE improves downstream query-by-table retrieval quality.

Conclusion: ALORE effectively addresses limitations of prior table overlap estimators by modeling row-column structure explicitly, incorporating alignment signals during training, and improving domain robustness of value representations. This leads to substantially better accuracy, much faster inference, and stronger generalization across domains, making ALORE a practical solution for large-scale blocking and query-by-table retrieval in heterogeneous table repositories.

Abstract: Fast estimation of the size of the largest overlap between tables enables blocking and query-by-table retrieval in large table repositories. The first and the state-of-the-art estimator Armadillo improves efficiency by embedding each table independently and approximating overlap ratio via embedding similarity. However, accurate estimation in heterogeneous repositories remains limited by three challenges: (C1) overlap depends on row-column structure, i.e., each matched cell must preserve both its row and column membership under a joint alignment of the two tables, but existing encodings leave this structure to be inferred indirectly; (C2) independent encoding provides no explicit channel for inter-table alignment signals, biasing prediction toward global similarity; (C3) naive value encodings overfit to corpus-specific distributions, causing cross-domain degradation. Hence, we propose ALORE, a scalable and domain-robust overlap ratio estimator built on three principles: (P1) explicitly represent row-column structure; (P2) expose inter-table alignment signals during training without expensive alignment search; (P3) reduce sensitivity to corpus-specific value distributions. ALORE instantiates these principles with a Two-View Row-Column Hypergraph encoder, alignment-guided objectives with inexpensive interaction signals, and a domain-robust value mapping. Experiments on multiple datasets spanning diverse domains and scales, including a large real-world corpus beyond prior benchmarks, show that ALORE outperforms the state of the art. ALORE reduces MAE by up to 55% overall and 69% in zero-shot transfer, while achieving up to 89x speedup. We further validate its effectiveness for query-by-table retrieval.

### [14] [Don't Wait to Reply: Towards Responsive yet Thoughtful Dialogue through Proactive Thinking](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03093)
*Ante Wang,Jiaqi Fu,Xuanyi Chen,Ruotian Ma,Zhaopeng Tu,Weizhi Ma,Yang Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes “proactive thinking” for LLMs, allowing them to reason during conversational idle time to reduce latency while maintaining answer quality.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Humans, by contrast, anticipate and plan what to say during pauses, enabling smoother real-time dialogue. The paper is motivated by this mismatch and aims to make LLM interactions more fluid, efficient, and human-like in real-time settings.

Method: They introduce a Proactive Thinking framework where an LLM uses conversational downtime to pre-compute possible future reasoning traces or response components instead of remaining idle. Concretely, they design a training-free baseline that anticipates likely future dialogue states and performs speculative continual thinking about them. The method balances efficiency and response quality by managing how far ahead and how extensively the model thinks, and they adapt existing benchmarks into time-aware, streaming-style environments to test this behavior.

Result: Across three adapted, time-aware benchmarks of varying difficulty, proactive thinking reduces effective interaction latency—responses come faster once the user speaks—while preserving task performance. The experiments show that proactive, speculative reasoning can be practically realized without retraining the base model.

Conclusion: The study concludes that using idle time for anticipatory reasoning enables LLMs to be more responsive without degrading accuracy, improving real-time conversational experience. The authors advocate reframing conversational AI from purely reactive systems toward proactive, intelligent agents that continuously and strategically think ahead between user turns.

Abstract: Thinking has emerged as a critical capability for Large Language Models (LLMs) tackling complex tasks. However, its reactive nature, where reasoning is passively triggered only upon receiving a user response, inevitably introduces latency that compromises conversational fluidity. This stands in sharp contrast to human dialogue, where speakers proactively anticipate and plan future content during natural pauses to ensure seamless interaction. To bridge this gap, we propose Proactive Thinking, a framework that empowers models to pre-compute potential response elements during conversational downtime instead of waiting idly for the next input. We then introduce a training-free baseline that can think ahead by anticipating future states, balancing efficiency and quality through speculative continual thinking. To evaluate this approach in practice, we adapt three benchmarks of varying complexity into time-aware environments that simulate real-time conversational flow. We demonstrate that proactive thinking effectively improves interaction efficiency without compromising performance. Ultimately, this work advocates for a fundamental shift toward more intelligent, anticipatory, and real-time conversational AI.

### [15] [Conditional Diffusion Guided Knowledge Transfer for Multi-Domain Knowledge Graph Completion](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03154)
*Jiawei Sheng,Taoyu Su,Xixun Lin,Xiaodong Li,Tingwen Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes DMKGC, a diffusion-based, generation-style framework for multi-domain knowledge graph completion that improves link prediction by learning domain-general yet domain-aware entity embeddings from multiple KGs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multi-domain knowledge graph completion methods transfer knowledge by enforcing strict consistency constraints on equivalent entities across KGs. This may suppress domain-specific context, dilute the quality of entity representations in all domains, and particularly hurts performance when target KGs are low-resource. There is a need for a transfer approach that uses multiple KGs without overwriting or homogenizing their specific information.

Method: The authors introduce DMKGC, a conditional diffusion-guided knowledge transfer framework. They initialize shared, domain-agnostic prior entity embeddings and then encode them within each individual KG to obtain domain-specific views. Equivalent entities across support KGs are fused and used as conditioning signals in a diffusion model that generates refined entity embeddings. The diffusion process aims to reconstruct the prior embeddings so that generation is not biased toward any specific KG, while training objectives enforce that generated embeddings are predictive for link prediction across KGs, preserving domain-specific information during transfer.

Result: On 14 knowledge graphs spanning 3 benchmark datasets, DMKGC achieves an average 4.3% improvement in mean reciprocal rank (MRR) for tail entity prediction compared with state-of-the-art MKGC methods, with the advantage particularly maintained in low-resource data scenarios.

Conclusion: Treating each KG as a partial view of entities and using conditional diffusion to generate shared entity embeddings enables more effective, balanced knowledge transfer across domains. This avoids over-constraining equivalent entities, preserves domain-specific context, and significantly improves multi-domain KG completion, especially when target KGs have limited data.

Abstract: Multi-domain knowledge graph completion (MKGC) aims to improve missing triple prediction in a target KG by transferring knowledge from other support KGs. Existing methods typically enforce consistency constraints on equivalent entities across KGs to transfer knowledge, which risks suppressing domain-specific contextual information of entities. This design can also compromise entity representation information from all KG domains, impeding performance improvements, especially in low-resource data scenarios. To address this, we pioneer a generation-based paradigm for MKGC and propose DMKGC, a conditional diffusion-guided knowledge transfer framework. Our key insight is to treat each KG as a partial view of the entity entire information, and generate informative domain-general entity embeddings through diffusion models conditioned on support KGs. Particularly, we first initialize domain-agnostic entity embeddings as prior entity embeddings, and then encode them within individual KGs. Afterward, we fuse equivalent entities from support KGs as the conditional diffusion generation guidance. We leverage the prior entity embeddings as the proxy generation objective, which ensures this conditional generation to be unbiased towards any conditioned KGs. Simultaneously, we also train the generated embeddings to be predictive across KGs, thus preserving domain-specific information. Extensive experiments on 14 KGs in 3 benchmarks demonstrate a 4.3\% average MRR improvement in tail entity prediction over state-of-the-art methods, with sustained gains in low-resource data settings.

Abstract: This study examines how prompt language and translation theory-driven prompt design influence the quality of Spanish-Chinese journalistic translations generated by GPT-5.2. Automated metrics identified the baseline prompt (BASE) as the best-performing condition, whereas human evaluation ranked the brief-oriented prompt (BRIEF) highest (MQM: 8.66 vs. 7.84), a reversal likely attributable to the single-reference constraint inherent in automated measures. Sub-error type analysis revealed that translation theory-driven prompts selectively reduced Awkward style errors, while Unidiomatic style errors persisted across conditions. Prompt language had a negligible impact under both evaluation paradigms. These results indicate that translation theory-driven prompts can yield measurable quality gains under expert evaluation of journalistic translations, although their pedagogical implications for language learners remain suggestive and require validation through user-based studies.

### [17] [KARMA: Knowledge graph-based Automated Reasoning Materialization and Alignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03166)
*Jinkyeong Choi,Chaebin Jeong,Donghyeon Park*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces KARMA, a method to fix a mismatch between how contrastive templates differ (in only a few slots) and how models are trained (at full-sequence level) by shifting supervision to the specific differing entity-slots using knowledge graphs and slot-level objectives.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing template-based contrastive data generation for preference learning is scalable but inefficient: most contrastive candidates are nearly identical except for a few entity slots. However, standard sequence-level optimization treats the whole template equally, so the supervision signal is diluted over shared parts instead of focusing on the discriminative entities. This misalignment, termed the Resolution Mismatch Problem, limits how well models can learn fine-grained preferences from such data. The authors aim to better align the granularity of supervision with the actual loci of variation in the data.

Method: The authors propose KARMA, which operates over domain knowledge graphs to systematically generate better contrastive examples. It enumerates schema-constrained paths in these graphs and verbalizes them into natural-language templates with explicitly aligned slots corresponding to entities or relations. To address the Resolution Mismatch Problem, they introduce Slot-Parallel Alignment (SPA), a training framework that applies a decoupled slot-level contrastive objective so that preference supervision is targeted to the specific differing entity-slots rather than the full sequence. They also describe an implementation with slot-aware masked attention that enables efficient packed evaluation of multiple slot-level candidates within a single forward pass.

Result: On benchmarks across three domains—biomedical, computer science, and chemistry—KARMA yields better performance than the underlying base LLM as well as supervised fine-tuning baselines trained on the same data. It also compares favorably to existing preference-learning methods that operate at sequence-level or token-level, showing improved effectiveness of slot-level contrastive supervision in these structured domains.

Conclusion: By identifying and formalizing the Resolution Mismatch Problem in template-based contrastive learning, the paper shows that aligning supervision with the actual locus of variation—entity-level slots—improves preference learning. KARMA, with its knowledge-graph-based candidate generation and Slot-Parallel Alignment objective, provides a scalable and more fine-grained training approach that outperforms standard fine-tuning and other preference methods across several technical domains.

Abstract: Template-based contrastive synthesis is scalable, but its candidates often differ only in a few entity-slots while sequence-level optimization spreads supervision over mostly shared templates. We formalize this as the Resolution Mismatch Problem and propose KARMA, which enumerates schema-constrained paths over domain knowledge graphs and verbalizes them into slot-aligned contrastive candidates. Slot-Parallel Alignment (SPA) then applies a decoupled slot-level objective to route preference supervision to discriminative entity-slots, with slot-aware masked attention serving as an optional packed-evaluation implementation. Across biomedical, computer-science, and chemistry benchmarks, KARMA outperforms base LLM and same-data SFT baselines, and compares favorably with sequence and token-level preference methods.

</details>

The dataset contains 444 manually transcribed audio segments with metadata on speaker sex, disease type, and intelligibility. S-DiverSe is designed to support ASR evaluation and development for neurologically affected Spanish speech. We describe the dataset, analyze its composition, and report baseline ASR results alongside initial adaptation experiments. Our findings reveal that heuristic text post-processing is more robust than fine-tuning for out-of-domain neurological Spanish speech. ### [19] [TACG: Trajectory-Aware Commit Gating for Diffusion Language Model Decoding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03236)
*Chengcheng Wang,Tingzhang Luo,Wenhao Li,Jianyuan Guo,Chang Xu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Trajectory-Aware Commit Gating (TACG), a decoding method for diffusion language models that uses the full denoising trajectory to decide when to commit tokens, improving efficiency and often accuracy without extra training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing diffusion language model decoders only look at the current prediction step and immediately commit top-1 tokens, ignoring the trajectory of predictions across denoising steps. This can prematurely lock in unstable tokens and delay tokens that have consistent support across steps, reducing both reliability and efficiency. The authors want a way to exploit the temporal trajectory information to make better commit decisions while remaining training-free and lightweight.

Method: They propose TACG, a gate-level decoding scheme that separates token identity selection from the decision of when to commit it. TACG maintains the base posterior for token identity, while using trajectory-aware signals solely for commit readiness. It has two main components: (1) Temporal Implicit Logits Guidance (TILG), which keeps an exponential moving average (EMA) of past logits as a self-reference and compares current logits to this EMA in natural-parameter (logit) space to assess stability; and (2) a History Gate (HG) that requires short-term persistence of a proposal across steps before committing. Along with a capped extra-promotion budget controlling how much trajectory-based promotion is allowed, these form a stability-constrained commit rule that needs no auxiliary networks or extra model forward passes.

Result: On LLaDA, Dream, and LLaDA2-Mini diffusion LMs, evaluated on code benchmarks (HumanEval, MBPP) and math benchmarks (GSM8K, MATH500), TACG tends to either improve or at least maintain task accuracy. At the same time, it reduces the number of denoising steps required and increases tokens per forward pass (TPF), indicating better decoding efficiency.

Conclusion: Using the full denoising trajectory of diffusion language models for commit gating, rather than naively committing from the current step alone, leads to more stable and efficient decoding. TACG shows that a simple, training-free, trajectory-aware commit rule can enhance both accuracy and efficiency without extra model evaluations or auxiliary networks, demonstrating a practical way to better exploit DLLM dynamics.

Abstract: Diffusion language models (DLLMs) generate text by iteratively denoising masked positions, exposing a trajectory of predictive distributions rather than a single instantaneous belief. Most existing decoders ignore this trajectory and commit tokens from the current snapshot alone, conflating confidence with commitment readiness: a transient top-1 peak under incomplete context can be locked in, while candidates with consistent cross-step support are delayed. We propose Trajectory-Aware Commit Gating (TACG), a training-free gate-level decoder that anchors token identities to the base posterior and uses trajectory-aware signals only to decide whether the current proposal is ready to commit. TACG combines Temporal Implicit Logits Guidance (TILG), which keeps an exponential moving average of past logits as a self-reference and contrasts the current logits against this reference in natural-parameter space, with a History Gate (HG) that enforces short-term proposal persistence before commitment. Together with a capped extra-promotion budget, these components yield a stability-constrained commit rule without auxiliary networks or extra forward passes. We evaluate TACG on LLaDA, Dream, and LLaDA2-Mini across code (HumanEval, MBPP) and math (GSM8K, MATH500) benchmarks; it typically improves or preserves accuracy while reducing denoising steps and increasing tokens per forward (TPF). The code is publicly available at https://github.com/Clarence-CV/TACG-DLLM.

</details>


### [20] [From Gentlemen to Frontiermen: Masculine Formations in English-Language Fiction (1771--1930)](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03323)
*Rong Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper uses computational text analysis on 150 British and American novels (1771–1930) to trace how different models of masculinity rise and fall over time.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional literary criticism argues that nineteenth-century masculinity is not monolithic but composed of multiple, competing ideals (e.g., chivalric, gentlemanly, religious, professional, imperial). However, most evidence for this claim comes from close readings of a small number of texts. The authors want a scalable, reproducible way to measure and compare these masculine formations across a large corpus and to track how their relative prominence changes historically, as well as how they differ by author gender.

Method: They take 150 canonical British and American novels from the txtLAB Novel450 corpus (1771–1930). Using coreference resolution, they group all references (names, noun phrases, pronouns) to the same male character into character-specific chains and then extract text windows centred on these male characters. On this male-focused subcorpus, they fit an unsupervised structural topic model (STM), using publication year and author gender as covariates that affect topic prevalence. They then interpret the resulting topics as distinct formations of masculinity and examine how their prevalence varies over time and between male and female authors.

Result: The STM yields six coherent masculine formations: (1) aristocratic-chivalric, (2) Christian manhood, (3) gentlemanly respectability, (4) country squire, (5) professional-commercial, and (6) imperial/adventure. Over the long nineteenth century, formations associated with inherited rank (aristocratic, squire) and sacred authority (Christian manhood) decline in relative prominence. Formations organised around paid work (professional-commercial) and especially around adventure and expansion (imperial/adventure, particularly in frontier and wilderness settings) increase. The largest growth is in imperial/adventure masculinity, not in professional breadwinner masculinity. Novels by male authors show higher prevalence of adventurous and commercial masculine formations than those by female authors.

Conclusion: Nineteenth-century fiction encodes masculinity as a shifting field of multiple, competing formations rather than a single ideal, and these formations can be quantitatively identified without predefined word lists. Across 1771–1930, authority based on inherited and sacred status yields ground to achieved, commercial, and expansionary (imperial/adventurous) models of masculine authority. Because the unsupervised topics align closely with categories established in existing gender and literary scholarship, the study demonstrates that structural topic modeling of character-centred text offers a reproducible, data-driven method for tracing large-scale reorganisations of gendered authority in literary history.

Abstract: Masculinity in nineteenth-century fiction is not a single ideal but a field of competing scripts. Drawing on 150 British and American canonical novels from the txtLAB Novel450 corpus, published between 1771 and 1930, this paper examines the changing relative prominence of competing models of masculine authority. To focus the analysis on masculine characterisation, the study extracts male-character-centred text windows by using coreference resolution to group names, nominal mentions, and pronouns into character-specific reference chains. It then fits an unsupervised structural topic model with publication year and author gender as topic-prevalence covariates. The model identifies six distinct masculine formations: aristocratic-chivalric, Christian manhood, gentlemanly respectability, country squire, professional-commercial, and imperial/adventure. Across the corpus, formations tied to inherited rank and sacred authority decline, while those organised around paid work and adventure rise. The largest increase occurs not in professional-commercial breadwinning but in imperial/adventure masculinity, particularly the frontier-wilderness register. The trajectory points to a reallocation from inherited and sacred status towards achieved, commercial, and expansionary forms of masculine authority. Adventurous and commercial formations are also more prevalent in novels by authors recorded as male. Because these formations emerge without a seeded vocabulary yet align with categories established in independent scholarship, the article offers a reproducible method for measuring the reorganisation of gendered authority across the long nineteenth century.

### [21] [From Judgments to Issues: Structured Extraction of Legal Reasoning with Citation-Hallucination Control](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03325)
*Giovanni Piccioli,Alessia Fidelangeli,Piera Santin,Pierpaolo Vivo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: An automated, cost-efficient pipeline processes ~330k Italian tax-court judgments, decomposes them into issues, structures them in an IRAC/legal-syllogism XML format, and controls LLM hallucinations in legal citations, validated against expert annotations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is a need to process several hundred thousand Italian tax-court decisions at scale to enable advanced legal-analytics tasks. Manually annotating issue-level structures and legal reasoning is too expensive and time-consuming, and existing LLM-based approaches are unreliable for legal citations and often not tailored to Italian tax law. The authors want a scalable, structured, and reliable way to extract issue-level reasoning from judgments to support retrieval, citation-network analysis, and dataset construction.

Method: They build an automated pipeline centered on a general-purpose but cost-efficient LLM (DeepSeek V3) that decomposes each tax-court judgment into separate legal issues and, for each issue, produces a structured XML representation aligned with the IRAC framework and the legal syllogism. To mitigate hallucinations in legal citations, they integrate a hallucination-detection filter: model-generated references are cross-checked against citations detected directly from the judgment by a dedicated parser (Linkoln), with all citations normalized to standard identifiers (URN-NIR, ECLI, CELEX). They evaluate the system on 50 judgments manually annotated by two tax-law PhDs, computing inter-annotator agreement, LLM-vs-expert agreement for both issue extraction and citations, and separately assessing the hallucination filter.

Result: The pipeline successfully produces issue-level IRAC-style XML structures for Italian tax-court judgments and, with the hallucination-detection filter, improves reliability of legal citations compared with a bare LLM approach. The evaluation on 50 expert-annotated judgments yields measurable levels of inter-annotator agreement and shows that the LLM’s outputs are reasonably aligned with expert annotations for both issue extraction and citation identification, while the hallucination filter effectively flags or removes unsupported references. The system scales to a corpus of about 330,000 first- and second-instance decisions at a sustainable computational cost.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that their work is, to their knowledge, the first expert-validated, issue-level structured-extraction pipeline for Italian tax-court decisions that incorporates explicit hallucination control for legal citations. The approach demonstrates that a cost-efficient general-purpose LLM, combined with a citation parser and normalization to standard legal identifiers, can reliably structure large volumes of judgments into IRAC-based XML. This provides a solid foundation for downstream tasks such as issue-level legal information retrieval, citation-network analysis, and building large-scale datasets of legal reasoning, and suggests that similar pipelines could be adapted to other domains and jurisdictions.

Abstract: We present an automated pipeline that decomposes Italian tax-court judgments into individual legal issues and extracts, for each issue, a structured XML representation grounded in the IRAC framework and the legal syllogism. The pipeline targets a corpus of approximately $330{,}000$ first- and second-instance decisions of the Italian tax courts and is built around a capable yet cost-efficient general-purpose model (DeepSeek V3), a choice driven by the need to process several hundred thousand documents at a sustainable cost. To address the well-documented unreliability of large language models on legal citations, we couple the extraction step with an automatic hallucination-detection filter that compares the references produced by the model with those identified in the judgment text by a dedicated parser (Linkoln), normalised to standard identifiers (URN-NIR, ECLI, CELEX). We validate the pipeline on $50$ judgments annotated by two PhDs in tax law, computing inter-annotator agreement and LLM-vs-expert agreement on both issue extraction and legal citations, together with a stand-alone evaluation of the hallucination filter. To the best of our knowledge, this is the first issue-level, expert-validated structured extraction pipeline with hallucination control for Italian tax-court decisions, and it provides a concrete starting point for downstream applications such as issue-level retrieval, citation-network analysis, and the construction of large-scale datasets of legal reasoning.
