### [22] [Efficient Decentralized Multi-task Dataset Valuation via Model Merging](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03346)
*Mohammadsajad Alipour,Mohammad Mohammadi Amiri*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents DMVM, a decentralized, computationally efficient framework for valuing datasets in multi-task learning by inferring their contributions through model merging instead of retraining or data sharing.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing dataset valuation methods mostly handle single-task scenarios and rely on expensive retraining or Shapley-style computations, which do not scale and are unsuitable for decentralized, privacy-sensitive marketplaces where multiple data contributors support multiple downstream tasks. There is a need for a practical valuation mechanism that aligns with multi-task performance and works without a trusted central coordinator or raw data exchange.

Method: DMVM (Decentralized Multi-task Valuation via Model Merging) uses task arithmetic in parameter space: models are first trained separately on different datasets, then systematically merged/combined to observe their joint behavior across multiple tasks. From these combinations, DMVM estimates each dataset's marginal contribution to multi-task performance without retraining from scratch or sharing raw data. The framework includes a secure aggregation protocol so that model parameters can be combined in a privacy-preserving, decentralized manner. The authors also develop theoretical error bounds for the approximation induced by this model-merging-based valuation scheme.

Result: DMVM delivers scalable and computationally efficient dataset valuation aligned with multi-task generalization. It avoids retraining and direct data sharing while still approximating marginal utilities, with theoretically bounded error. Experiments on both computer vision and NLP benchmarks demonstrate that DMVM achieves accurate and robust valuations in practice, supporting its use in realistic data marketplace settings.

Conclusion: By formulating dataset valuation through model merging and task arithmetic, DMVM enables practical, privacy-preserving valuation in decentralized multi-task settings. It offers a scalable alternative to traditional Shapley- or retraining-based approaches, provides theoretical guarantees on approximation quality, and empirically validates its effectiveness on diverse domains, positioning it as a viable foundation for fair and transparent data marketplaces.

Abstract: Accurate and efficient dataset valuation is essential for enabling fair and transparent data marketplaces, especially when multiple contributors provide data for training multi-task models. Most existing valuation methods, however, are limited to single-task settings, overlooking scenarios where a buyer aims to optimize performance across multiple downstream tasks. Moreover, traditional valuation approaches, such as Shapley-based or retraining-based methods, are computationally expensive and poorly suited for decentralized environments without a trusted central coordinator and with strict privacy constraints. We propose DMVM (Decentralized Multi-task Valuation via Model Merging), a novel framework that bypasses retraining and data sharing by leveraging task arithmetic to infer dataset contributions directly from model combinations. Instead of retraining or sharing raw data, DMVM quantifies how models trained on different datasets combine in parameter space to infer each dataset's marginal utility across multiple tasks. This formulation yields a valuation process that is scalable, computationally efficient, and explicitly aligned with multi-task generalization behavior. To support decentralized deployment, we introduce a secure aggregation protocol that enables collaborative valuation without revealing individual model parameters or private data. We also provide theoretical error bounds characterizing the approximation quality of DMVM and validate our framework through comprehensive experiments on computer vision and natural language processing tasks.

### [23] [Spectral Signatures of Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03377)
*Zhuoying Zhang,Ishan V. Prasad,Yuanzhe Hu,Zihang Liu,Hengrui Luo,Pu Ren,Yaoqing Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a compact, data-free spectral signature (based on Heavy-Tailed Self-Regularization) of LLM weight matrices to manage and compare many open-source LLMs, showing it can trace lineage, cluster similar models, and predict performance trends at scale.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is a rapidly expanding ecosystem of open-source LLMs that vary in architecture, scale, training, and licensing. Managing, organizing, and comparing these models—e.g., tracing lineage, understanding similarity, checking quality, and predicting performance—requires scalable, systematic metrics. Existing task-specific benchmarks are costly, data-dependent, and not directly comparable across heterogeneous models, motivating a model-intrinsic, efficient, and standardized way to characterize LLMs.

Method: They adopt spectral shape-based metrics grounded in Heavy-Tailed Self-Regularization theory. From each model’s weight matrices, they compute the empirical spectral density (ESD) and summarize its shape into a compact spectral signature that is scale-invariant. This signature serves as a model-level descriptor that can be computed without data, efficiently across many models. They then curate a large corpus of major open-source LLM families and systematically benchmark both spectral and non-spectral metrics against each other and across downstream tasks to evaluate how well these signatures reflect lineage, similarity, and performance.

Result: The spectral signature derived from the weight ESDs is stable under post-training modifications and captures intrinsic properties of pretrained LLMs. It enables accurate tracking of model lineage, unsupervised clustering of similar models in the curated corpus, and correlates with downstream performance, allowing quantification or ranking of models without running full benchmarks. Compared to non-spectral metrics, it performs well as an unsupervised, data-free proxy for model quality and relationships.

Conclusion: A compact, heavy-tail-based spectral signature of LLM weights serves as an effective, scalable, and data-free descriptor for model-level analysis. It provides a robust, intrinsic characterization of LLMs that supports lineage tracing, similarity clustering, and approximate performance estimation across heterogeneous open-source models. This makes it a practical tool for organizing, comparing, and managing large repositories of LLMs, offering a meaningful proxy for broad performance trends without extensive task-specific evaluation.

Abstract: The rapidly growing repository of publicly available large language models (LLMs) presents significant challenges for systematic management and quantification at scale, such as model lineage tracing, licensing, and evaluation. However, task-specific benchmarks are insufficient for this setting, as LLMs differ widely in architectures, scales, and training procedures. To address this challenge, we adopt spectral shape-based metrics for managing and quantifying LLMs based on Heavy-Tailed Self-Regularization theory. Our approach uses the shape information of the weight empirical spectral density as a compact spectral signature of each model. This signature captures intrinsic properties of pretrained models and remains robust during post-training, making it suitable for model-level analysis. In addition, this metric is data-free, computationally-efficient, and scale-invariant, enabling large-scale analysis in practice. Moreover, we curate a large and diverse model corpus consisting of major open-source LLM families, and use it to systematically benchmark spectral and non-spectral metrics across models and downstream tasks. We show that our spectral signature supports the tracking of the model lineage, the unsupervised clustering of similar models, and the quantification of the model performance. Overall, the proposed spectral signature provides a meaningful proxy for broad performance trends across LLMs, enabling efficient organization, comparison, and analysis of large model collections.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional aspect-based sentiment analysis mostly focuses on coarse, categorical labels such as positive, negative, or neutral. However, many real-world applications (e.g., nuanced customer feedback analysis, affective computing, and detailed opinion mining) require more fine-grained, continuous measures of sentiment, including both how positive/negative (valence) and how intense (arousal) the sentiment is. Furthermore, existing systems often separate the tasks of extracting sentiment elements (aspects, categories, opinion spans) from scoring their sentiment, missing the opportunity for a unified, structured prediction approach. SemEval-2026 Task 3 provides a benchmark to push toward such dimensional, aspect-based sentiment analysis; this paper aims to explore competitive methods for that benchmark and to investigate how modern transformer and LLM-based techniques can model these richer sentiment dimensions.

Method: For Subtask 1 (regression of valence and arousal given aspects), the authors build a weighted ensemble of transformer-based encoder models. These encoders output continuous sentiment scores, and the ensemble aggregates their predictions, with weights tuned to improve performance. For Russian, they augment the model inputs by prompting a large language model (LLM) to generate additional synthetic sentiment descriptions, which are concatenated or otherwise integrated with the original text to provide richer cues for the regression models. For Subtask 3 (joint extraction and scoring), they fine-tune a decoder-style LLM to perform structured prediction: the model is trained to output a structured representation that includes aspects, categories, and opinion expressions, each annotated with predicted valence and arousal scores. This effectively treats extraction and scoring as a single sequence generation task, guided by a predefined output schema.

Result: While the abstract does not provide explicit numerical performance, it indicates that the proposed methods were successfully applied to both subtasks of SemEval-2026 Task 3. The ensemble of transformer encoders produced fine-grained valence and arousal predictions, and the decoder LLM was able to simultaneously extract sentiment elements and estimate their scores. The Russian-specific augmentation with synthetic sentiment descriptions suggests improved performance for that language, though details are likely in the main paper.

Conclusion: The paper demonstrates that dimensional aspect-based sentiment analysis can be effectively addressed by combining transformer encoder ensembles for regression with decoder LLMs for structured prediction. Leveraging LLMs both as data augmenters (for generating synthetic sentiment descriptions) and as end-task models (for joint extraction and scoring) appears to be a promising strategy. The approach highlights the feasibility of moving beyond categorical sentiment to richer, continuous sentiment representations integrated directly into the extraction pipeline, and sets a foundation for further improvements and extensions in multilingual and fine-grained sentiment analysis.

Abstract: This paper presents an approach to the SemEval-2026 Task 3: Dimensional Aspect-Based Sentiment Analysis. We investigate methods for moving beyond traditional categorical sentiment (e.g., positive or negative) to predict fine-grained, real-valued scores for sentiment "valence" (positivity) and "arousal" (intensity). We participate in two subtasks: predicting these scores for given aspects (Subtask 1) and extracting full sets of sentiment details, including aspects, categories, and opinions alongside their scores (Subtask 3). Our approach for the regression task involves a weighted ensemble of transformer-based encoder models. For the Russian language, we further enhance the input by using a large language model (LLM) to generate synthetic sentiment descriptions. For the extraction task, we fine-tune a decoder LLM to perform structured prediction, allowing the system to identify sentiment elements and estimate their numerical scores simultaneously.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The study frames TNM prediction as three separate multi-label classification problems (for T, N, and M). It experiments with both classical and deep-learning pipelines. Conclusion: The study establishes an efficient, reproducible baseline for automated TNM stage prediction from pathology reports within the SMM4H-HeaRD 2026 shared task. Combining embeddings helps, and surprisingly, TF-IDF plus LightGBM outperforms more complex neural architectures in this setting. However, notable performance drops between test sets underscore limited generalizability, sensitivity to label imbalance, and difficulties handling long clinical narratives. Furthermore, the result of the Codabench for the test sets indicates a Macro-F1 score of 0.978, 0.957, and 0.879 for the T, N, and M categories respectively for test set 1; while test set 2 records a Macro-F1 score for T, N, and M is 0.807, 0.767, 1.0 respectively. ### [26] [Learning from Lost Provenance: Multiple Instance Learning for Cancer Registry Tumor Group Classification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03481)
*Leonard Ruocco,Jonathan Simkin,Lovedeep Gondora,Gregory Arbour,Raymond Ng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a way to use existing patient-level cancer registry labels to train report-level deep learning classifiers, avoiding costly manual annotation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Cancer registries want to automate tasks like coding pathology reports with deep learning, but lack large, report-level, human-annotated datasets. Meanwhile, registries already have many expert patient-level labels that are not directly tied to each report, so they are underused for model training. The authors aim to unlock these existing labels to create effective training data without extra annotation burden or large compute resources.

Method: They introduce an efficient framework based on Attention-Based Multiple Instance Learning (ABMIL). Patients are treated as bags and their pathology reports as instances, with the patient-level cancer labels as bag labels. ABMIL learns to attend to the most relevant reports for each label, effectively inferring which reports informed the patient-level coding. The attention scores are then used to select high-confidence report-level examples, distilling a large, noisy corpus into a compact, higher-quality per-report training set. A final classifier is then fine-tuned on this distilled dataset for tumor group classification in the BC Cancer Registry.

Result: The fine-tuned classifier trained on the distilled report-level dataset obtained a macro F1 score of 0.83, beating established baselines on most tumor groups. This indicates that the attention-based distillation successfully converted noisy patient-level operational labels into effective per-report supervision.

Conclusion: Operational, patient-level labels from cancer registries can be converted into high-quality report-level training data using ABMIL, without any extra manual annotation or large-scale computation. This provides a practical and accessible path for modernizing and automating cancer registry workflows, particularly tumor group classification from pathology reports.

Abstract: Modernizing cancer registries with deep learning is opening new opportunities to automate labor-intensive tasks such as the coding of pathology reports. However, progress is constrained by the scarcity of report-level human-annotated training data. Cancer registries generate substantial volumes of expert-assigned labels as a routine product of their operations, but these exist at the patient level and are not linked to the individual pathology reports that informed them, limiting their direct use for training models. We develop an efficient framework for training deep learning classifiers by leveraging these operationally-generated labels without requiring per-report human annotation, demonstrated for tumor group classification at the BC Cancer Registry. We use Attention-Based Multiple Instance Learning (ABMIL) to recover the lost link between patient-level labels and the reports that informed them, leveraging the attention the model places on each report to distil a large, noisily-labeled corpus into a compact, high-quality per-report training dataset. A classifier fine-tuned on a distilled dataset achieved a macro F1 of 0.83, outperforming established baselines across most tumor groups. By turning routine operational labels into high-quality training data without additional annotation or large-scale computing infrastructure, ABMIL offers a practical and accessible route to automating cancer registry workflows.

### [27] [Lacuna Inc. at SemEval-2026 Task 4: Structurally Gated State-Space Models for Disentangling Narrative Similarity](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03482)
*Aleksey Kudelya,Rafif Alshawi,Alexander Shirnin*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces IVD-SSM, a state-space-based model with a Structurally Gated Alignment head to disentangle invariant narrative structure from variant lexical details for narrative similarity and representation learning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Narrative similarity requires comparing abstract causal and structural patterns of stories rather than superficial overlaps in names, entities, or settings. Standard Transformer models face quadratic complexity and are easily distracted by lexical noise and keyword overlaps. There is a need for a computationally efficient model that focuses on deep narrative structure and causal chains while being robust to surface-level variation.

Method: Use a hybrid State-Space Model backbone (Jamba-1.5-Mini) to efficiently model long-range dependencies in narratives. On top of this, introduce the Structurally Gated Alignment (SGA) head, a differentiable architecture with two scales: (1) a strided Macro-path that extracts a coarse structural skeleton of the story, and (2) a full-resolution Micro-path that captures detailed content. The Macro-path acts as a gating signal to filter the Micro-path representations, suppressing semantic noise and superficial lexical overlaps. The overall architecture is termed the Invariant-Variant Disentangled State-Space Model (IVD-SSM).

Result: On SemEval-2026 Task 4, which includes Track A (pairwise narrative similarity judgments) and Track B (dense representation learning), the IVD-SSM model performs well, showing the effectiveness of disentangling structural invariants from lexical variants for narrative understanding. While exact metrics are not given in the abstract, the model is presented as competitive and robust across both tracks.

Conclusion: Explicitly separating narrative structure (invariants) from surface wording and entities (variants) via a gated, two-path architecture on top of a state-space backbone yields a strong and principled approach to narrative similarity and representation learning. This structural disentanglement helps models focus on deeper causal and plot-level patterns, improving robustness to superficial lexical noise and enabling better narrative understanding.

Abstract: In this paper, we present the Invariant-Variant Disentangled State-Space Model (IVD-SSM), our submission to SemEval-2026 Task 4 on Narrative Story Similarity and Narrative Representation Learning. Evaluating narrative similarity is a profound computational challenge that requires models to look past concrete, superficial elements such as specific names, actors, objects, or settings to isolate and compare abstract patterns of causality and plot progression. To model these extended causal chains without the quadratic bottlenecks of standard Transformers, we leverage a hybrid State-Space Model (Jamba-1.5-Mini). Building upon this backbone, we introduce the Structurally Gated Alignment (SGA) head, a novel, differentiable algorithmic architecture. The SGA head operates on two scales: a heavily strided Macro-path maps the coarse structural skeleton of a story, which then acts as a gating mechanism to filter a full-resolution Micro-path, actively suppressing semantic noise and superficial keyword overlaps. Evaluated on both pairwise comparative judgments (Track A) and dense representation learning (Track B), our approach demonstrates that explicitly disentangling structural invariants from lexical variants provides a robust, principled framework for deep narrative understanding.

### [28] [Reading Between the Dots: Decoding Hidden Computation across Filler Tokens](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03502)
*Kaley Brauer,Claudio Mayrink Verdun,Samuel Marks*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that large language models can perform multi-step reasoning entirely inside hidden states over meaningless filler tokens, yet this hidden reasoning is still systematically decodable, enabling oversight beyond visible chain-of-thought.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Behavioral oversight methods often rely on inspecting visible chain-of-thought rationales, but recent models can answer complex questions without emitting any interpretable intermediate steps, especially when reasoning is done over content-free filler tokens. This raises a safety and interpretability concern: if surface tokens carry no information about the internal reasoning, can we still monitor and understand what the model is doing? The paper is motivated by this oversight gap and aims to determine whether such "hidden" computation is in fact legible at the level of internal activations.

Method: The authors study two open frontier models (DeepSeek V3 and Kimi K2) on four task families: fact retrieval, parallel numeric composition, string manipulation, and in-context computation. They force models to reason over filler tokens (e.g., dots or counting sequences) that contain no semantic content and examine how the models still arrive at correct answers. Using interpretability tools, they: (1) analyze attention patterns to see how questions route through filler regions to answers, (2) apply logit-lens readouts across layers to detect when retrieved facts and their compositions emerge in the residual stream, and (3) perform KV-cache transplants at filler-token positions to causally swap internal computations between different prompts. They then propose an unsupervised decoding pipeline that, taking only hidden states (residual-stream activations) as input and no labels or further training, reconstructs the intermediate reasoning values and compares them with LLM-judge assessments of correctness.

Result: Across both models and all four task families, the authors find that the models compute over filler tokens in a structured and interpretable manner. Attention maps show systematic routing of information through filler positions from question to answer; logit-lens probes reveal that factual information and partial computations appear early in the layer stack and become cleanly composed in later layers. KV-cache transplant experiments demonstrate causal control: swapping caches at filler positions reliably swaps the resulting outputs between examples. Their unsupervised decoding pipeline, fed only hidden states and no ground-truth labels, recovers the models' intermediate reasoning values with approximately 80–95% accuracy (as judged by a separate LLM) for both models across all tasks, indicating that internal computations are highly legible in practice.

Conclusion: The work concludes that even when models perform multi-step reasoning entirely over semantically empty filler tokens and provide no explicit chain-of-thought in their outputs, their internal computations remain directly readable from the residual stream. This challenges the notion that hidden reasoning is fundamentally unmonitorable and suggests that effective oversight must target the full computational trace—hidden states, attention, and caches—rather than relying solely on surface tokens. Thus, monitorability appears to be an intrinsic property of the model’s internal computation, not merely its externally visible explanations.

Abstract: Frontier LLMs can perform multi-step reasoning over content-free filler tokens like dots or counting sequences, producing correct answers with no visible chain-of-thought (CoT). This is a limit case for behavioral oversight, where surface tokens carry no information about the underlying reasoning. But hidden from the output is not the same as hidden from us. On four task families (fact retrieval, parallel numeric composition, string manipulation, and in-context computation), two open-weights frontier models (DeepSeek V3, Kimi K2) compute over filler tokens in a structured, legible way: attention routes the question through the filler region to the answer, logit-lens readouts show retrieved facts emerging early and their composition crystallizing in late layers, and KV-cache transplants at filler positions causally swap outputs between examples. We introduce an unsupervised decoding pipeline that takes only hidden states as input and recovers intermediate values with 80-95% accuracy (best LLM judge) across both models and all four tasks, without ground-truth labels or training. Hidden computation that defeats behavioral CoT monitoring is, on these tasks, directly readable from the residual stream, suggesting monitorability is a property of the model's full computational trace, not just its surface tokens.

These datasets also vary in demographics, platforms, data types, annotation techniques, and methodologies. This systematic review also reveals key gaps and highlights opportunities to develop more diverse, reliable and clinically-relevant resources.

### [30] [They Infer What You Meant: Models Represent Communicative Intent More Reliably Than They Act On It](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03598)
*Alex Kwon*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that language models internally represent users’ communicative intent (e.g., wanting recognition vs evaluation) well before and more robustly than their actual outputs reflect, and that this internal signal can be decoded and causally steered to recover user-intent-aligned behavior without explicit prompting.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Language models often respond to the literal content of user messages instead of what users were trying to do by sending them (their communicative intent). For instance, they may critique code when the user wanted praise, or offer wellbeing checks for late-night messages when the user wanted help revising text. Understanding whether and how models internally represent these underlying intents, and why outputs often ignore them, is important both for interpretability and for aligning models’ behavior with user goals.

Method: The authors treat communicative intent as an object of study in interpretability. They train linear probes on hidden states from several language models (six models across four families, including base checkpoints) to decode whether a user’s message is intended for recognition vs evaluation. They test whether this decoding is surface-independent and whether it generalizes to pragmatically inferred intent and to another intent dimension (support vs help). They compare where in the network the intent is decodable vs where it actually affects outputs, and examine model-to-model differences. They then identify a ‘discriminative direction’ in activation space at a particular layer that is tightly coupled with the intent representation and use causal interventions—steering activations along this direction—to see if behavior changes to better match user intent, comparing the effect to explicit instructions and testing orthogonality to generic feedback-offering directions. They include controls and report both positive and null results.

Result: Linear probes can reliably and robustly decode user communicative intent (e.g., recognize vs evaluate) from default-pass hidden states, independent of surface form, across multiple models and families and even in base checkpoints. This representation also generalizes to pragmatically inferred intent and to a second intent dimension (support vs help). However, models’ default outputs do not consistently act on this represented intent: the layer depth at which intent becomes decodable precedes the depth at which it influences output, and only some models naturally act on this intent, with three of six showing the mismatch. A discriminative direction aligned with the intent representation at a selected layer serves as a strong causal handle: steering along this direction recovers behavior aligned with the inferred intent about as effectively as adding an explicit instruction, even without modifying the prompt. This steering direction is nearly orthogonal to a generic feedback-offering axis, indicating that it specifically routes the represented intent rather than just increasing generic feedback. At sufficient intervention strength, the routed intent can even override an explicit, conflicting request. The authors also show various control experiments and null results, arguing against simpler explanations.

Conclusion: Communicative intent is robustly encoded inside language models even when their surface behavior ignores it; the main problem is readout, not representation. Intent signals are decodable earlier in the network than they are behaviorally expressed, and whether they shape outputs by default varies idiosyncratically across models. By identifying and steering a specific activation-space direction associated with this intent representation, one can causally route internal intent signals to behavior, recovering user-intent-aligned responses on par with explicit instructions and without prompts. This direction is distinct from generic feedback-related directions, supporting the view that models contain fine-grained, latent social-pragmatic structure that can be selectively activated. The work suggests new avenues for interpretability, alignment, and control by treating user intent as a first-class feature of internal representations and explicitly bridging the gap between representation and readout.

Abstract: When a person shares something with a language model, the model often answers the surface of the message rather than what the sender was doing by sending it: share a finished project and it critiques the code; share a raw late-night line and it runs a wellness check. We treat the sender's communicative intent, the Gricean what-was-meant, as a first-class interpretability object, and show the failure is one of readout on top of a robust representation. A linear probe decodes the sender's intent, whether they want a thing recognized or evaluated, from a model's default-pass hidden states, cleanly and surface-independently, across six models and four families and in the base checkpoints. The representation generalizes further, to intent that is only pragmatically inferred, and to a second, lexically clean intent (support versus help). The behavioral half of the story, and every causal test, is established on the recognize/evaluate contrast, where what varies is whether the default output acts on the intent. The readout lags the representation in depth within a model (the intent is decodable several layers before it drives the output); across models, which ones act on it by default is model-specific, an observed stratification (three of six show the failure) that we do not read as a scaling law. Where the gap is open, a direction closely tied to the representation, the discriminative direction at a searched-for layer, is a causal handle: steering it recovers the intended behavior, as well as an explicit instruction does and with no prompt at all. This direction is near-orthogonal to the feedback-offering axis, so it routes a represented intent rather than a generic feedback knob, though at the recovery dose the routed intent can override an explicit request. We support each link with controls against obvious deflations and report the nulls as plainly as the confirmations.

### [31] [Revealing Hidden Model Behaviors with Task-Specific Self-Reports](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03640)
*Taras Kutsyk,Bartosz Zieliński*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a lightweight adapter (SAR) that makes fine-tuned language models explain their own hidden, potentially harmful behaviors, and show it works better and more reliably than prior introspection methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fine-tuned language models can develop hidden or narrow-scope misbehaviors (like lying only in specific conditions or giving harmful advice only on certain topics). Existing tools for making models “self-report” such behaviors are unreliable: they miss some behaviors and hallucinate others. Practitioners need a practical way to audit what their specific fine-tuning actually made the model learn, using just the model and its training data.

Method: They introduce Stabilized Adapter for self-Report (SAR), a lightweight LoRA-based adapter trained so that the model, when queried, describes in natural language the specific behaviors implanted via fine-tuning. They evaluate SAR across seven different implanted hidden behaviors plus a control with no hidden behavior, and compare it against Introspection Adapters (IA), the main prior baseline.

Result: SAR successfully detects and correctly verbalizes all seven implanted behaviors and correctly handles the no-behavior control. It still works even when the underlying model has generalized into broader misalignment that goes beyond what is directly apparent from the training data. Compared with IA, SAR detects more behaviors, avoids missing whole cases, and when IA fails and hallucinates incorrect behaviors, SAR keeps a positive signal and cuts hallucination rates by about half.

Conclusion: SAR provides a more accurate and reliable mechanism for model self-reporting of hidden behaviors than previous introspection adapters. This improved reliability makes it significantly easier for practitioners to audit their fine-tuned models and answer questions about what behaviors the training actually induced, thereby supporting safer deployment and oversight of language models.

Abstract: Fine-tuning can give a language model a hidden behavior--it may give false answers under a narrow condition, or give harmful advice only when a prompt touches a particular topic. We introduce the Stabilized Adapter for self-Report (SAR), a lightweight LoRA adapter that makes a fine-tuned model describe its own hidden behavior in plain language, using only the model and the dataset it was trained on. Across seven implanted behaviors (plus a no-behavior control), SAR detects the hidden behavior in every one--even when the model has generalized into broad misalignment that the training data alone does not predict. Introspection Adapters (IA), the closest existing baseline, detects some behaviors from our suite but misses others entirely--and where it misses, it hallucinates, consistently reporting wrong behaviors. SAR retains positive signal on every setting where IA fails and halves the rate of hallucinations. This makes it much easier for practitioners to audit their models and obtain reliable answers to "what did my model actually learn?" type of questions.

### [32] [Annotating Korean adnominal ending constructions in corpus data: Beyond relative-clause identification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03681)
*Jungyeul Park,Chulwoo Park*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper examines the Korean adnominal ending ETM and shows, via corpus-based annotation, that it is a shared exponent of multiple adnominal constructions rather than a dedicated relative-clause marker.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In Korean linguistics, ETM is often treated as if it directly signals relative clauses, but empirical data suggest it appears in many other noun-modifying environments. A clearer, corpus-backed typology is needed to avoid conflating distinct constructions and to improve syntactic annotation resources like treebanks.

Method: The authors develop a constructional typology for ETM-based noun-modifying constructions, differentiating them by predicate type, auxiliary configurations, argument structure compatibility, head-noun restrictions, and lexicalized patterns. They implement this typology as a construction-sensitive annotation layer on top of the KLUE dependency treebank using an ordered rule-based classification procedure, and then verify its quality through manual validation of annotated instances.

Result: Applying the typology to corpus data, they find that only 39.4% of ETM occurrences are productive relative-clause-like modifiers. The majority belong to other construction types, including adjectival, copular, bound-nominal, modal, temporal, and collocational patterns, showing substantial functional diversity for ETM.

Conclusion: ETM should not be analyzed as a simple relative-clause marker but as a general adnominal morphology that realizes multiple distinct constructions. Relative-clause-like modification in Korean cannot be reliably identified solely from ETM morphology and must be distinguished using a richer set of syntactic and lexical criteria, which their typology and annotation scheme provide.

Abstract: The Korean adnominal ending \texttt{ETM} occurs in diverse noun-modifying constructions, including relative-clause-like modifiers, adjectival and copular forms, bound-noun constructions, and lexicalized expressions. This paper argues that \texttt{ETM} is not a direct marker of relative-clause structure, but a morphological exponent shared by several adnominal constructions. We propose a corpus-based typology that distinguishes these constructions using predicate type, auxiliary structure, argument-structural compatibility, head-noun restriction, and lexicalized patterns. We operationalize the typology as a construction-sensitive annotation layer for the KLUE dependency treebank, implemented through an ordered rule-based procedure and evaluated by manual validation. Productive relative-clause-like uses account for 39.4\% of the analyzed instances; the remainder consists mainly of adjectival, copular, bound-nominal, modal, temporal, and collocational constructions. The findings show that Korean relative-clause-like modification cannot be identified from adnominal morphology alone.

### [33] [Optimizing Large Language Models for Causality Assessment in Pharmacovigilance: Developing a Performance Metric as Objective for Bayesian Hyperparameter Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03704)
*Nicole Sonne Heckmann,Arnault-Quentin Vermillet,Søren Norlin Mølgaard,Manuela Del Castillo Suero,Lars Melskens,Gerard Ompad,Maurizio Sessa*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether optimizing the temperature hyperparameter of a large language model via Gaussian Process-based Bayesian optimization can improve agreement with expert Naranjo causality assessments on pharmacovigilance case reports, and proposes new composite similarity metrics for this optimization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Individual case safety reports (ICSRs) are increasing in volume, creating a need for scalable automated tools to assess drug-event causality. Large Language Models have potential in this domain but their performance on complex clinical reasoning tasks, such as Naranjo causality assessments, remains suboptimal. Moreover, common inference-time hyperparameters like temperature are usually set heuristically, and there has been no systematic, statistically principled exploration of whether tuning temperature can meaningfully improve LLM alignment with expert judgments in pharmacovigilance.

Method: The authors collected 723 stratified FAERS cases and obtained expert Naranjo causality assessments for them. They evaluated OpenAI’s GPT-5.2 using chain-of-thought prompting on these cases. To enable Gaussian Process-based Bayesian optimization over the temperature parameter (0–2), they defined four composite, continuous agreement metrics: Weighted Cosine Similarity (WCS), Information-Weighted Agreement Score (IWAS), Entropy-Weighted Agreement and Cosine Similarity Score (EWACS), and Consensus-Weighted Cosine Similarity (CWCS). A GP surrogate model with a Probability of Improvement acquisition function was used to search temperature values that maximize these objectives, with entropy analysis to identify which Naranjo questions meaningfully contribute signal for optimization.

Result: At baseline temperature T=0, GPT-5.2 exceeded prior biomedical LLMs, achieving 74.1% agreement with experts on Naranjo question 5 and 65.4% on question 10; entropy analysis showed that only these two questions carried substantial information for optimization. At the population level, temperature had no significant linear effect on performance (beta ≈ 0, p=0.959), indicating no single best global temperature. However, using the EWACS metric as the Gaussian Process objective within Bayesian optimization substantially improved agreement in overall causality classification from 45.0% to 72.0%, with particularly large gains in the “Doubtful” causality category (+42.9 percentage points). EWACS emerged as the most effective and GP-compatible metric among those proposed.

Conclusion: The study identifies EWACS as the preferred composite objective for GP-based optimization of LLM causality assessments and demonstrates that while there is no universal optimal temperature across all cases, case-specific temperature tuning can noticeably improve alignment with expert Naranjo classifications. These findings suggest that LLM performance in pharmacovigilance is driven mainly by the underlying ICSR content rather than a single fixed temperature, but that adaptive temperature optimization remains a promising strategy to enhance LLM-assisted drug safety surveillance workflows.

Abstract: Background: Growing individual case safety report (ICSR) volumes have intensified demand for scalable automated causality assessment. Large Language Models (LLMs) show promise, yet performance on clinically demanding tasks remains suboptimal and inference-time hyperparameter optimization has not been investigated. Objective: To develop a Gaussian Process (GP)-compatible optimization objective and investigate whether temperature optimization improves LLM-expert agreement on Naranjo causality assessment of FAERS ICSRs. Methods: Expert causality assessments were performed on 723 stratified FAERS cases. OpenAI's GPT-5.2 was evaluated using chain-of-thought (CoT) prompting. Four composite metrics were developed: Weighted Cosine Similarity (WCS), Information-Weighted Agreement Score (IWAS), Entropy-Weighted Agreement and Cosine Similarity Score (EWACS), and Consensus-Weighted Cosine Similarity (CWCS) and Bayesian optimization using a GP surrogate with Probability of Improvement (PoI) acquisition was applied across temperature [0, 2]. Results: GPT-5.2 outperformed prior biomedical LLMs at baseline (T = 0), achieving 74.1% agreement on question 5 and 65.4% on question 10 of Naranjo algorithm. Entropy analysis identified these as the sole informative optimization targets. Temperature showed no systematic population-level effect (\b{eta} = 0.002, p = 0.959). EWACS-guided Bayesian optimization improved causality classification agreement from 45.0% to 72.0% (+27 pp), with the largest gain in Doubtful cases (+42.9 pp). Conclusion: EWACS was identified as the optimal GP-compatible metric. The absence of a universal temperature optimum indicates LLM performance is driven primarily by ICSR content, yet case-specific temperature selection produced meaningful improvements, supporting temperature optimization for LLM-assisted pharmacovigilance.

### [34] [GRASP: Graph-Reasoning Aided Survey Planning for High-Fidelity Related Work Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03709)

This graph-guided plan is used to generate the related work section.

Result: Using citation-analysis-based evaluation, they show that GRASP-generated related work sections closely resemble human-written ones in three aspects: the discourse roles assigned to citations (e.g., background, contrast, extension), the rhetorical intents, and how citations are grouped and organized. This indicates improved alignment with expert literature review writing compared to baselines.

Conclusion: GRASP effectively models and exploits the relational structure among cited papers to plan and generate related work sections that approximate human discourse structure and citation use. Graph-based reasoning combined with LLM planning is a promising direction for automating high-quality literature review writing.

Abstract: Writing a literature review requires a deep understanding of the relationships among cited papers: how they build on, challenge, or offer alternative perspectives to one another. We present Graph-Reasoning Aided Survey Planning (GRASP), a framework combining LLM planning for related work generation with graph algorithms to extract key relationships among cited papers. Our two-layer graph structure consists of a Graph of Thoughts and an Argument-Counterargument Planning Network, representing the cited papers at different levels of granularity, and we apply topology-aware pruning via a Steiner tree to identify the core inter-paper relationships captured in our graph. Our citation analysis-based evaluation shows that GRASP generates related work sections (RWS) that closely match human-written targets in terms of the discourse roles, intents, and grouping of citations.

Current memory systems rely on fixed, hand-tuned storage, retrieval, and summarization strategies that may not generalize across tasks or scales. Method: The authors introduce SelfMem, a framework that treats memory management as a learnable, self-optimizing process. Conclusion: Allowing an agent to self-optimize its memory strategy using provided tools and feedback is more effective than relying on fixed, manually designed memory mechanisms. SelfMem demonstrates substantial and robust performance improvements on long-context benchmarks and suggests that adaptive, self-improving memory frameworks are a promising direction for building more capable long-horizon AI agents.

Abstract: While current AI agents support increasingly long context windows, tool use, and skill execution for long-horizon tasks, they still require memory systems to effectively leverage historical experience. Existing memory frameworks typically rely on fixed storage, retrieval, and summarization mechanisms, which can be rigid across different tasks and often require manual tuning. To address this limitation, we propose SelfMem, a self-optimizing memory framework. Inspired by prior work on self-improving AI, we follow the principle of "teaching an agent to fish rather than giving it a fish." Instead of forcing the model to follow a predefined memory strategy or format, SelfMem provides an environment with memory tools and feedback signals that allow the agent to explore, evaluate, and refine its own memory strategy. Our results show that SelfMem consistently outperforms retrieval, compression, and agent-memory baselines on BEAM across conversation scales from 100K to 1M tokens. Compared with the strongest baseline, SelfMem improves the official score by 48.7%, 40.8%, and 41.9% at 100K, 500K, and 1M, respectively. Further question-type analysis shows broad robustness across diverse memory demands, and our optimization study shows that model-guided strategy refinement further improves performance.

### [36] [ProACT: Towards Breakdown-Aware Proactive Agent in Multi-User Collaboration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03730)
*Shu Yang,Difei Xu,Jiaxin Pei,Di Wang*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing conversational agents in collaborative work are reactive and passive: they wait for explicit user prompts and fail to detect when a group is struggling due to issues like disagreement, ambiguity, or looping discussions. This limits their value as true team members in multi-user collaboration. The paper aims to transform agents into active collaborators that can recognize and address collaboration breakdowns as humans would, and to provide a benchmark to systematically evaluate such proactive behavior across diverse domains.

Method: The authors design ProACT, a framework that monitors speaker-attributed conversation history in multi-party settings. At each turn, ProACT: (1) analyzes whether the current turn indicates a collaboration breakdown; (2) decides whether the agent should remain silent or intervene; and (3) if intervention is needed, routes the case to a specific collaboration skill specialized for the detected issue (e.g., clarifying goals, balancing participation, resolving disagreement). The framework is grounded in theories of common ground, collaborative planning, and coordination work. They also construct a multi-user collaboration benchmark that spans domains such as project planning, product design, research collaboration, logistics, education, and resource-constrained decision-making, with 3,244 turn-level examples. ProACT is implemented on top of five different LLM backbones and compared against direct chat baselines.

Result: On the new benchmark containing 3,244 turn-level collaborative scenarios, ProACT consistently outperforms direct chat LLM baselines across five different backbone models. The improvements are measured in terms of collaborative appropriateness (interventions being contextually useful), non-interruptiveness (avoiding disruptive or unnecessary interruptions), conciseness (brief and to-the-point contributions), and overall judged intervention quality. These gains hold across all the evaluated domains of multi-user collaboration.

Conclusion: The work demonstrates that a breakdown-aware, skill-routed framework like ProACT can turn conversational agents from passive responders into more effective, proactive collaborators in multi-user settings. By explicitly modeling when and how to intervene, and by grounding the design in collaboration theory, ProACT yields higher-quality, less intrusive interventions than standard chat-based use of LLMs. The provided multi-domain benchmark establishes an initial evaluation standard for proactive collaborative agents and highlights the importance of detecting collaboration breakdowns as a separate capability from general language generation.

Abstract: Conversational agents are increasingly embedded in human collaborative work, yet they remain fundamentally passive and reactive: they respond to explicit user requests rather than proactively recognizing moments when a team would benefit from timely intervention as human collaborators often do. This reactive design substantially limits the use of agents as active participants in multi-user collaboration, where disagreements, ambiguous goals, forgotten constraints, underspecified plans, discussion loops, and imbalanced participation can gradually undermine group progress. To move agents from passive assistants toward active participants in multi-user collaboration, we introduce ProACT, a breakdown-aware agent framework grounded in theories of common ground, collaborative planning, and coordination work. ProACT observes the speaker-attributed conversation history, determines whether the current turn contains a collaboration breakdown requiring intervention, decides whether the agent should stay silent or speak, and, when speaking is needed, routes the case to a targeted collaboration skill. We further introduce the first multi-user collaboration benchmark for evaluating proactive agents across project planning, product design, research collaboration, logistics, education, and resource-constrained decision making. Across 3,244 turn-level examples and five LLM backbones, ProACT consistently improves collaborative appropriateness, non-interruptiveness, conciseness, and judged intervention quality over direct chat.

</details>


### [37] [Beyond Static Rules: Automated Discovery of Latent Vulnerabilities in Text-to-SQL](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03833)
*Hanqing Wang,Yongdong Chi,Jian Yang,Lei Yang,Jiehui Zhao,Yun Chen,Guanhua Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces SAGE, an automated framework to systematically uncover hidden failure patterns in LLM-based Text-to-SQL systems using guided perturbations and an evolving vulnerability codex, and shows these patterns transfer across models and can support reliability improvements via fine-tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs perform well on Text-to-SQL benchmarks, they still exhibit hidden reliability issues that hinder their use in real-world database interfaces. Existing diagnostic methods rely on static, expert-crafted rules and cannot systematically or automatically explore the large space of possible failures. There is a need for an automated, data-driven way to discover latent vulnerabilities and understand structural weaknesses in LLM Text-to-SQL systems, and to use this knowledge to improve reliability.

Method: The authors propose SAGE (Systematic Automated Guided Exploration), a framework that autonomously identifies latent weaknesses in LLM-based Text-to-SQL generation. For given input samples, SAGE formulates vulnerability hypotheses and consults an evolving Vulnerability Codex—an organized collection of discovered vulnerability patterns—to design targeted perturbations of inputs. It then iteratively tests the LLM with these perturbed examples, verifies whether failures occur, and documents confirmed defects back into the codex. This loop enables systematic exploration of the model’s failure modes and continual refinement of the vulnerability patterns. The framework is evaluated on state-of-the-art open-source LLMs used for Text-to-SQL.

Result: Empirical studies show that SAGE uncovers a large number of failure cases in state-of-the-art open-source LLMs for Text-to-SQL, revealing substantial fragility that is not obvious from headline benchmark scores. The Vulnerability Codex learned by SAGE generalizes across different LLMs, demonstrating strong cross-model transferability: patterns of failures discovered on one model tend to expose weaknesses in others. Additionally, when the failure-inducing samples generated by SAGE are used for lightweight fine-tuning, the models show promising reliability gains, although the remediation results are still preliminary.

Conclusion: SAGE provides an effective and automated way to systematically discover latent failure patterns in LLM-based Text-to-SQL systems, going beyond static expert-defined diagnostics. The evolving Vulnerability Codex not only captures generalizable structural weaknesses that transfer across models but also serves as a resource for improving model robustness. Early experiments with fine-tuning on SAGE-generated failure cases indicate that such targeted remediation is feasible and beneficial, outlining a scalable path toward closing the reliability loop for real-world Text-to-SQL deployment.

Abstract: While Large Language Models (LLMs) have achieved remarkable success in Text-to-SQL tasks, their deployment in real-world environments is hindered by latent reliability issues. Identifying these latent weaknesses is critical for building trustworthy database interfaces, yet current diagnostic approaches rely heavily on static, expert-defined rules, which lack the capability for systematic and automated exploration. To bridge this gap, we propose SAGE (Systematic Automated Guided Exploration), a novel framework designed to autonomously uncover latent failure patterns in LLM-based Text-to-SQL generation. Specifically, SAGE generates vulnerability hypotheses for given samples and references a continuously evolving Vulnerability Codex to design targeted perturbations, thereby iteratively verifying and documenting potential defects. Extensive experiments on state-of-the-art open-source LLMs demonstrate that SAGE uncovers a substantial number of failure cases, highlighting the significant fragility of current models. Furthermore, our analysis reveals that the Vulnerability Codex exhibits strong cross-model transferability, indicating that the discovered patterns represent generalized structural weaknesses. Finally, we explore SAGE's potential for remediation. Although preliminary, lightweight fine-tuning on the generated samples yields promising improvements, suggesting a scalable pathway for closing the reliability loop in future work.

  <summary>Details</summary>

This paper presents \textbf{SCION (Scientific Collaborative Innovation with Agentic Organizational Nexus)}, an agentic scientific operating system that acts as an \textbf{organizational nexus}. Through a Science Agent serving as a \textbf{Meta-Harness}, SCION connects scientific tasks, tools, agents, artifacts, and memory, transforming research into an executable, auditable, and reusable operational process. At its core is the \textbf{Research Execution Plan (REP)}, which compiles high-level scientific intent into staged objectives, dependencies, verification checkpoints, tool requirements, expected artifacts, and fallback conditions. SCION further integrates hierarchical multi-agent execution, profile-driven specialization, selective context construction, governed delegation, and layered epistemic memory to support long-horizon scientific work. We formulate discovery under SCION as \textbf{Target-conditioned Inverse Search} and extend it to hidden-target settings through batch active search under finite experimental budgets. Overall, SCION shifts AI from isolated tools toward a coordinated operational layer for traceable and reusable scientific innovation.

### [39] [Consistent but Miscalibrated: Evaluating LLM Limitations for Risk Communication in Natural Language](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03882)
*Diego Cerda-Mardini,Sarath Chandar,Sreenath Madathil*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether large language models (LLMs) can reliably and consistently verbalize probabilistic information (likelihood and uncertainty) and finds they are consistent but miscalibrated, especially for uncertainty, making them unreliable as zero-shot risk communication tools.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are increasingly used to explain or communicate outputs of other AI systems, especially probabilistic predictions, it is important to know whether they can faithfully translate numerical probabilities and uncertainties into natural language descriptions. Reliable risk communication requires both consistency across identical inputs and accurate mapping from numbers to words, but this capability of LLMs is not well understood.

Method: The authors construct a two-stage prediction pipeline where an upstream model generates probabilistic predictions characterized by likelihood and uncertainty, simulated via samples from a Beta distribution parameterized by its mode and prior sample size. Nine LLMs are then prompted to select appropriate verbal descriptors for these predictions. Experiments vary domain context (six domains), temperature (ten settings), and are repeated ten times for robustness. They also test conditions where LLMs are given precomputed summary statistics (mode and prior sample size) to see whether this improves calibration or reduces contextual sensitivity.

Result: The evaluated LLMs generally produce consistent verbal descriptions for the same inputs across repeated runs, but their descriptions are systematically miscalibrated relative to the underlying numeric probabilities, with especially poor performance on communicating uncertainty as opposed to likelihood. Supplying explicit numeric summary statistics reduces the models’ susceptibility to contextual framing effects, but does not fix the core miscalibration in how probabilities and uncertainties are verbalized.

Conclusion: Current LLMs, even when consistent, do not accurately map probabilistic information into verbal risk descriptors, particularly for uncertainty, and thus should not be relied upon as zero-shot standalone tools for risk communication in probabilistic prediction settings. The main limitation lies in the verbalization step rather than in access to numerical summaries, implying that improved training or guidance is needed before LLMs can safely fulfill this explanatory role.

Abstract: LLMs are increasingly deployed as post-hoc explainers of AI-generated outputs, yet it remains unclear whether they can reliably communicate probabilistic information in natural language. For this role to be viable, models must produce identical verbal descriptions for identical inputs, and select descriptions that accurately reflect the magnitude of the underlying numerical quantities. We evaluate whether nine LLMs meet these requirements within a two-stage prediction pipeline, in which an upstream model has produced probabilistic outputs characterized by their likelihood and uncertainty, and LLMs are tasked with selecting an appropriate verbal descriptor for each. We simulate predictions from an upstream model by taking samples from a Beta distribution parameterized by its mode and prior sample size. We then prompt LLMs to explain these predictions under six domain contexts and with ten temperature settings, and repeating each experiment ten times. We find that LLMs are generally consistent but miscalibrated, with substantially weaker performance on uncertainty than on likelihood tasks. Providing models with precomputed summary statistics (mode and prior sample size) reduced sensitivity to contextual framing but did not resolve the underlying miscalibration, suggesting that the bottleneck resides in the verbalization step itself. These findings indicate that current LLMs do not yet constitute reliable zero-shot standalone risk communication tools for probabilistic predictions.

### [40] [Probe, Don't Prompt: A Hidden-State Probe for Metadata Filtering in Multi-Meta-RAG](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03929)
*Mykhailo Poliakov,Nadiya Shvai*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper replaces a GPT-3.5-based metadata extractor in Multi-Meta-RAG with a small, local probe over hidden states of an open-source language model, achieving higher accuracy and better safety for filtering news sources in multi-hop QA.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-hop question answering systems like Multi-Meta-RAG rely on metadata (e.g., news source) extracted from queries to filter a vector store. Existing implementations often use proprietary, prompt-based LLMs (like GPT-3.5) to infer this metadata, which introduces cost, non-determinism, potential safety issues (outputs outside an allow-list), and dependence on external APIs. The authors seek a cheaper, deterministic, and safer alternative that still matches or improves performance.

Method: They train a local, deterministic probe classifier on the hidden states of a small open-source language model to predict a fixed set of 49 news sources for each query. The probe uses features from a shallow transformer layer, applies mean pooling across tokens, and employs class-imbalance-aware multi-label training to handle a long tail of rarely seen sources. They compare this probe against a model-free substring baseline and GPT-3.5 prompted extraction on the MultiHop-RAG dataset, evaluating set-exact accuracy on 2556 queries and analyzing performance on null vs non-null queries. They also study the effect of model size (135M vs 1.5B parameters) on probe performance and computational cost.

Result: On all 2556 MultiHop-RAG queries, the probe achieves 90.9% set-exact accuracy, outperforming the substring baseline (88.0%) and GPT-3.5 (80.9%). The performance gain over GPT-3.5 comes entirely from null queries, where GPT-3.5 never abstains while the probe can, preventing spurious sources. On non-null queries, all three methods are within about one accuracy point of each other. The probe’s output space is constrained to a fixed 49-source vocabulary, so it cannot produce out-of-allow-list sources, unlike the prompted GPT-3.5. A 135M-parameter model with a shallow-layer probe is within ~1.5 accuracy points of a 1.5B-parameter model, indicating that accurate extraction is possible with modest compute and a cheap partial forward pass.

Conclusion: A small, local probe over hidden states of an open-source language model can effectively replace a proprietary GPT-3.5-based metadata extractor in Multi-Meta-RAG. This approach delivers higher overall accuracy, especially by correctly handling null queries, and ensures outputs remain within a fixed allow-list of sources. Design choices—using shallow layers, mean pooling, and class-imbalance-aware multi-label training—are key to its strong performance. The solution is computationally cheap and API-independent, making it a practical and safer way to implement metadata filtering for multi-hop retrieval-augmented generation systems.

Abstract: Multi-Meta-RAG improves retrieval for multi-hop question answering by filtering a vector store on metadata (the news source) that it extracts from each query by prompting gpt-3.5-turbo. We show this proprietary, free-form extractor can be replaced by a local, deterministic probe trained on the hidden states of a small open-source language model. On all 2556 MultiHop-RAG queries the probe reaches 90.9% set-exact accuracy against 88.0% for a model-free substring baseline and 80.9% for GPT-3.5, a margin that comes entirely from null queries, on which GPT-3.5 never abstains; on non-null queries all three stay within about a point. Because the probe's output space is exactly the fixed 49-source vocabulary, it cannot drift outside the allow-list as the prompted model does. Three design choices make it work: selecting a shallow layer, mean pooling, and class-imbalance-aware multi-label training over the long tail of sources. A 135M-parameter model lands within ~1.5 points of a 1.5B one, so the filter is cheap to output: a partial forward pass through the first few layers plus one linear head, with no API. The code is available at https://github.com/mxpoliakov/Multi-Meta-RAG.

### [41] [Can Dialects Be Steered Like Languages? Sparse Neurons and Distributed Directions in Arabic LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03936)
*Kareem Elozeiri,Mervat Abassy,Omar Kallas,Fahim Dalvi,Preslav Nakov,Kentaro Inui,Nadir Durrani*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Arabic NLP models are biased toward Modern Standard Arabic because there is much less training data for dialects, so models often fail to generate accurate dialectal text. The authors want to understand how dialect information is internally represented in LLMs and whether this understanding can be used to improve dialect generation without costly or data-hungry fine-tuning.

Method: They use two interpretability-based, inference-time techniques. (1) Neuron-level analysis: identify sparse sets of neurons whose activations correlate with specific Arabic dialects, then experimentally amplify or suppress these neurons during generation to steer outputs toward or away from those dialects. (2) Vector steering: because dialect information is distributed and entangled across neurons, they compute dialect-specific activation directions in the model’s representation space and inject these steering vectors at inference time to bias generation toward target dialects.

Result: They find that distinct neuron populations and activation directions correspond to particular Arabic dialects. Modifying these neurons or injecting the learned dialect vectors during inference measurably shifts the model’s output toward the desired dialect variety without retraining the model. This demonstrates successful dialect control and reveals structured geometry of dialect knowledge in the model’s internal activations.

Conclusion: Dialectal properties are systematically encoded in the internal activations of Arabic LLMs, both at the level of sparse neuron groups and distributed representation directions. These internal structures can be manipulated at inference time to steer generation toward specific dialects, enabling dialect control without dialect-specific fine-tuning and providing an interpretability-grounded view of how dialectal knowledge is organized within LLMs.

Abstract: A key challenge in Arabic NLP is the scarcity of dialectal data relative to Modern Standard Arabic (MSA), causing LLMs to overproduce MSA and struggle with dialectally accurate generation. From an interpretability perspective, this raises a fundamental question: where and how are dialectal features encoded within model internals, and can these representations be leveraged to improve dialect generation without fine-tuning? This study investigates two complementary inference-time approaches that serve simultaneously as interpretability probes and control mechanisms. First, we conduct a neuron-level analysis, identifying sparse neuron populations that encode dialect-specific features and showing that amplifying or suppressing these neurons can steer model outputs toward target dialects. Second, motivated by the entanglement of dialectal features at the single-neuron level, we apply a vector-steering approach that extracts dialect-specific activation directions and injects them during inference. Together, these methods illuminate the geometry of dialectal knowledge in Arabic LLMs and offer a principled, interpretability-grounded framework for dialect control without requiring dialect-specific fine-tuning.

### [42] [The Remarkable Effectiveness of Providing AI Agents with Natural Language Tools: A Replication Study Validating NLT Performance Across 14 Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03953)
*Alexander Somma,Isabelle Plante,Fred Premji*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper independently replicates and extends the Natural Language Tools (NLT) framework, showing it substantially improves tool-calling reliability and efficiency versus structured tool calling for many (but not all) LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Structured tool calling in LLM agents is brittle, error-prone, and token-inefficient, especially in recursive, multi-agent workflows. The original NLT work claimed that letting models describe tool calls in natural language is more reliable. However, those results needed independent validation across a broader and more modern set of models to understand where NLT actually helps and by how much.

Method: The authors re-implement the NLT framework with open-source tooling and run 8,560 tool-calling trials across 14 different LLMs, including newer frontier, reasoning, and open-weight models. They compare traditional structured tool calling against NLT on metrics such as tool-calling accuracy, critical error counts, and token usage. They also analyze how model capability (e.g., native tool calling support, reasoning specialization, scale, RL-style optimization) moderates NLT’s performance.

Result: Across all models, NLT improves tool-calling accuracy by 14.9 percentage points (62.3% vs 47.4%) and reduces critical tool-use errors by 93% (51 vs 755). The gains are largest for models without native tool calling, reasoning-specialized models, and smaller models (+24.0 to +43.1 percentage points). Highly optimized frontier models like GPT-5 and Gemini 2.5 Pro show smaller or even negative advantages from NLT, consistent with prior work on RL-optimized tool use. NLT also reduces token consumption by 25.2%. In simulated recursive, multi-agent workflows, these reliability and efficiency gains compound because each avoided failure saves retries, fallbacks, and coordination overhead.

Conclusion: NLT is validated as a robust, practical alternative to structured tool calling for many LLMs, particularly where reliability is more important than having strictly parseable tool call formats. Its benefits are moderated by model capability: less optimized or non-native tool-calling models gain the most, while state-of-the-art frontier models sometimes favor structured approaches tuned via reinforcement learning. For production LLM agents, especially those with complex, recursive tool use, NLT can significantly increase reliability and reduce costs.

Abstract: This study independently replicates and extends the Natural Language Tools (NLT) framework of Johnson et al.~(2025), which questions the use of structured tool calling in large language model (LLM) agentic systems. We evaluated NLT across 14 models and 8,560 trials, adding newer frontier, reasoning, and open-weight models to the original set. The results confirm the core findings and add detail. NLT improves tool-calling accuracy by 14.9 percentage points overall (62.3\% versus 47.4\% structured) and reduces critical errors by 93\% (51 versus 755 errors). The gains depend on model capability: models without native tool calling, reasoning models, and smaller models gain substantially (+24.0pp to +43.1pp), while heavily optimized frontier models (GPT-5, Gemini 2.5 Pro) show smaller or reversed advantages. This matches recent analyses of reinforcement-learning-optimized tool use (Martinez, 2025). NLT also cuts token usage by 25.2\%. The reliability and efficiency advantages compound in recursive agentic workflows, where agents chain many tool calls across sub-agents: a structured failure triggers retries, fallback routing, and coordination overhead, while NLT avoids most of that cost at the source. This work makes three contributions: (1) the first independent validation of NLT using open-source tooling, (2) evidence that model capability moderates NLT's advantages (Chen et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2025), and (3) a measurement of NLT's reliability benefit (93\% fewer errors), its most deployment-relevant property given the known fragility of structured tool calling. NLT is a practical alternative to structured tool calling, especially for production systems that value reliability over parseability.
