### [43] [NormWorlds-CF: Solver-Verified Counterfactual Normative Reasoning with Metamorphic-Relation GRPO](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03957)
*Xinqi Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces NormWorlds-CF, a solver-verified benchmark and environment for counterfactual normative reasoning, and shows how richer supervision and a new GRPO reward variant (MR-GRPO) can train models to capture underlying counterfactual structure rather than just final answers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Language models can output the right normative (what-should-be-done) answer but for the wrong underlying reasons, failing to capture the correct counterfactual structure of a situation. Existing evaluation and training that focus mainly on final answers (answer-only supervision or sparse rewards) cannot reliably distinguish shallow pattern-matching from genuine structural reasoning. The authors want an environment where latent reasoning structure is fully specified and verifiable, so they can diagnose and improve models' ability to track counterfactual changes and argument structure, not just outputs.

Method: They build NormWorlds-CF, an environment of executable rule-based normative scenarios equipped with a deterministic solver. This solver can generate not only final answers but also proofs, falsification certificates, argument statuses, support sets, and detailed change labels between paired worlds. They design two main experimental components: (1) SFT diagnostics comparing different supervision types—final-answer-only vs. proof-plus-falsification plus targeted replay—to see how supervision style affects multi-task performance (answer prediction, proof, falsification); and (2) a compact paired-world structured-change task with 270 root families and 1080 canonical–variant pairs, where the goal is to predict structured relation changes across worlds. For the second part, they introduce metamorphic-relation GRPO (MR-GRPO), a class-conditioned reward scheme for GRPO that gives partial credit based on relation-family correctness and solver-visible change fields, and they compare it against sparse reward and answer-only GRPO on models of similar size (1.7B and Qwen3-4B).

Result: In SFT diagnostics, answer-only supervision achieves perfect accuracy on final-answer tasks but completely fails at falsification tasks, showing that answer-only signals are an unsafe proxy for full reasoning competence. In contrast, training on proofs plus falsification signals, combined with targeted replay, yields strong performance across all evaluated tasks. On the structured-change task with 1.7B experiments, MR-GRPO improves accuracy on held-out relations, boosts correctness at the relation-family level, and reduces errors where the model predicts the wrong relation family compared with both sparse and answer-only GRPO. In Qwen3-4B validation across three random seeds, answer-only reward helps with answer-change fields but damages relation-family structure; sparse reward best preserves high-level relation labels; and MR-GRPO provides the best balanced performance across several dimensions: answer-change, support-change, status-change, and soft metamorphic-relation metrics at the root level.

Conclusion: Verified counterfactual structure, when exposed through the environment and leveraged in supervision or rewards, can significantly shape post-training behavior beyond simply improving final-answer accuracy. Rich structural signals such as proofs, falsifications, and metamorphic relation labels lead to models that better track underlying counterfactual and argumentative structure. However, the work also shows that some challenges remain unsolved, notably generating complete and exact change records, reliably recognizing invariant subtypes, and transferring these abilities to out-of-distribution settings.

Abstract: Language models can reach the right normative verdict for the wrong reason. We introduce NormWorlds-CF, a solver-verified environment for counterfactual normative reasoning in executable rule worlds. Its deterministic solver produces final answers, proof and falsification certificates, argument statuses, support sets, and paired-world change labels, enabling supervision and evaluation without LLM judges. The benchmark contains staged SFT diagnostics and a compact paired-world task with 270 root families and 1080 canonical-to-variant pairs. The SFT diagnostics show that final-answer supervision is an unsafe proxy: answer-only SFT reaches perfect accuracy on answer tasks but scores zero on falsification, while proof-plus-falsification training with targeted replay reaches strong all-task accuracy. For the structured-change task, we introduce metamorphic-relation GRPO (MR-GRPO), a class-conditioned reward for GRPO that gives partial credit for relation families and solver-visible change fields. In matched 1.7B continuation experiments, MR-GRPO improves held-out relation accuracy and relation-family correctness, and reduces wrong-family error, compared to sparse and answer-only GRPO. In Qwen3-4B three-seed validation, answer-only reward improves answer-change fields but weakens relation-family structure, sparse reward preserves coarse relation labels best, and MR-GRPO delivers the strongest balanced performance across answer-change, support-change, status-change, and soft root-level metamorphic-relation metrics. These results show that verified counterfactual structure can shape post-training beyond final answers, while exact full change-record generation, invariant subtype recognition, and out-of-distribution (OOD) transfer remain open problems.

### [44] [TRACER: Early Failure Detection for Task-Oriented Dialogue](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03974)

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing task-oriented dialogue evaluations typically detect failure only after a conversation has already gone wrong, missing opportunities to intervene earlier. The authors want a way to predict impending failures during the conversation, so systems can react before a full breakdown occurs.

Method: TRACER combines simple trajectory signals extracted from belief-state changes with text representations of the evolving dialogue. It is trained to predict, from a partial dialogue, whether the final outcome of the full conversation will be a failure. They compare it to heuristic, classical, and single-stream baselines.

Result: Across all visibility conditions and both oracle and generated belief-state settings, TRACER can detect failure signals well before the end of the conversation and consistently outperforms the competing baselines.

Conclusion: Early failure detection in task-oriented dialogue is feasible and beneficial. TRACER provides a practical warning signal that can be used to intervene or adapt system behavior before an interaction fully breaks down.

Abstract: Task-oriented dialogue systems often fail before the final breakdown is obvious, but most evaluation only measures failure after the conversation has already gone wrong. We present TRACER, a method for early failure detection in task-oriented dialogue. TRACER predicts from a partial dialogue whether the full conversation will eventually fail by combining simple trajectory signals from belief-state changes with text representations of the evolving dialogue state. We evaluate the method in both oracle and generated belief-state settings, and test how well it works when only 25%, 50%, 75%, or 100% of the dialogue is visible. Across these settings, TRACER detects useful failure signals well before the end of the conversation and outperforms heuristic, classical, and single-stream baselines. These results suggest that early failure detection can provide a practical warning signal for dialogue systems before the interaction fully breaks down.

*Yingdong Yang,Haijian Wu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes and evaluates a candidate-constrained RAG pipeline for the LongEval-RAG task, finding that a rule-based chunking system with neural sentence reranking (rule-minilm) performs best and that multi-metric evaluation is crucial.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To address the LongEval-RAG setting where retrieval and citation must be restricted to an organizer-provided candidate set, and to understand which RAG pipeline components (chunking, query expansion, reranking, citation priors, etc.) most improve answer quality under this constraint, as well as how different evaluation metrics may favor different systems.

Method: They build a modular, candidate-constrained RAG system that uses rule-based passage chunking, deterministic provenance tracking, query expansion, pseudo-relevance feedback, reciprocal rank fusion, lightweight reranking, and citation-aware evidence aggregation, with an optional MiniLM-based sentence-level reranker. They instantiate ten pipeline variants that toggle these components and evaluate them with the competition's primary (gold/nugget-based) metrics plus an auxiliary LLM-judge diagnostic protocol.

Result: Among the ten variants, the rule-minilm pipeline—rule-based chunking plus query expansion, PRF, RRF, lightweight reranking, a citation prior, and late MiniLM sentence selection—achieves the best overall performance in terms of BERTScore, retrieval precision, nugget coverage, and average grade. More sophisticated semantic or topic-shift chunking does not yield additional gains compared to this simpler, stable chunking paired with neural sentence-level selection.

Conclusion: Effective RAG in the candidate-constrained LongEval-RAG setting benefits more from reliable rule-based evidence segmentation combined with targeted neural sentence reranking than from complex chunking strategies. Additionally, reliance on a single evaluation paradigm is misleading: LLM-judge based assessments highlight different systems than gold/nugget-based metrics, underscoring the necessity of multi-metric evaluation frameworks for RAG systems.

Abstract: We present a candidate-constrained retrieval-augmented generation system for LongEval-RAG, where each query is associated with an organizer-provided candidate set and all retrieved evidence and final citations must remain within that set. The system combines deterministic provenance tracking with passage-based retrieval, deterministic query expansion, pseudo-relevance feedback (PRF), reciprocal rank fusion (RRF), lightweight evidence reranking, citation-aware evidence aggregation, and optional MiniLM sentence reranking. We evaluate ten pipeline variants using a primary organizer evaluation and a supplementary self-generated diagnostic protocol. The primary evaluation shows that the strongest balanced variant is rule-minilm: a rule-based chunking pipeline with query expansion, PRF, RRF, reranking, citation prior, and late MiniLM sentence selection. This variant obtains the highest BERTScore, retrieval precision, nugget coverage, and average grade among our submissions. The result suggests that the main gain does not come from more complex semantic or topic-shift chunking, but from pairing stable rule-based evidence units with sentence-level neural selection before generation. The supplementary LLM-judge evaluation remains useful for early diagnosis and additional analysis, but it emphasizes different systems than the primary gold-answer and nugget-based evaluation, highlighting the need for multi-metric RAG evaluation.

### [47] [Separating Representation from Reconstruction Enables Scalable Text Encoders](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04011)
*Megi Dervishi,Mathurin Videau,Yann LeCun*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: CrossBERT decouples representation learning from token reconstruction in BERT-like encoders, enabling better frozen representations, higher masking ratios, and improved efficiency and benchmark performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing BERT-style encoders have not scaled or evolved as rapidly as decoders, and their internal representations become harder to exploit with simple frozen probes even as language modeling perplexity improves. This indicates a misalignment between the training objective (token reconstruction) and the quality or usability of learned representations, motivating a new architecture that better aligns encoder representations with downstream probing and transfer tasks.

Method: The authors analyze BERT encoders using frozen backbone probing, showing that improved perplexity does not translate into more exploitable representations. They trace this to BERT’s flat architecture where representation learning is tightly coupled to the token reconstruction loss. They propose CrossBERT, a two-part architecture that explicitly separates high-quality representation learning from the token reconstruction head, and introduce a Complementary Masking Strategy that allows masking ratios of at least 50% while still collecting gradients over all tokens. This improves both training throughput and sample efficiency.

Result: CrossBERT achieves monotonic scaling behavior and outperforms standard BERT-style encoders on frozen-probe evaluations, specifically on the English v2 MTEB benchmark and frozen GLUE tasks. It also allows higher masking ratios, yielding 1.5–2x throughput gains and 2x sample efficiency improvements during pretraining, while maintaining or improving the quality of the learned representations.

Conclusion: Decoupling representation learning from token reconstruction in BERT-style encoders resolves a misalignment that makes representations hard to exploit with frozen probes. The CrossBERT architecture, combined with Complementary Masking, leads to more scalable, efficient, and transferable encoder representations, suggesting a more principled path forward for scaling encoder models compared to conventional flat BERT designs.

Abstract: While decoders have rapidly scaled, encoders have remained largely unchanged since BERT. We revisit this disparity by frozen backbone evaluation via probing. Under this lens, the representations of BERT encoders become increasingly $\textit{unexploitable}$ by frozen probes, despite improved perplexity. The misalignment originates in BERT's flat design, which couples representation learning to the token reconstruction loss. We propose $\textbf{CrossBERT}$, a two-part architecture that separates the learning of high-quality encoded representations from the rigid grounding of token reconstruction. This design further enables high masking ratios ($\ge 50\%$) and gradient collection over all tokens via a $\textit{Complementary Masking Strategy}$, respectively increasing throughput by $1.5$ to $2\times$ and sample efficiency by $2\times$. Overall, CrossBERT demonstrates monotonic scaling and superior performance on MTEB(eng, v2) and frozen GLUE benchmarks.

### [48] [CrossHallu: Do Hallucination Signals Generalize Across Languages and Domains in Large Language Model's Internals?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04029)
*Aisha Alansari,Malak Alkhorasani,Hamzah Luqman*

TL;DR: CrossHallu examines whether hallucination detection signals from LLM internal representations generalize across languages (Arabic↔English) and domains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most hallucination detection methods that use internal model states have been developed and evaluated only in English, leaving it unclear whether the same internal signals can be used reliably in other languages and across different question-answering domains.

Method: The authors extract internal representations from six LLMs on a generative QA hallucination task and train classifiers to detect hallucinations. They then run a systematic evaluation using TruthfulQA (English), its Arabic translation, and HalluScore, covering monolingual training/testing, cross-lingual transfer (train in one language, test in another), cross-domain transfer (train on one dataset, test on another), and combined cross-lingual+cross-domain transfer between Arabic and English.

Result: They find that internal-state hallucination signals generally transfer across both languages and domains for most of the examined models. Cross-lingual performance is strongly influenced by how well hallucination vs. non-hallucination classes are separated and how well Arabic and English are aligned in the feature space. Cross-domain transfer in Arabic shows more variability and depends on which datasets are used for training and testing the hallucination detector.

Conclusion: Internal representations in LLMs can support hallucination detection that generalizes beyond the language and domain on which the detector was trained, but the effectiveness of transfer depends on model-specific feature space properties (class separability and cross-lingual alignment) and on the particular training–testing dataset pairing, especially for Arabic cross-domain settings.

Abstract: Recent hallucination detection techniques in large language models (LLMs) focus on directly extracting features from a model's internal representations and training a classifier on these features to detect hallucinations, demonstrating promising results. Notwithstanding this advancement, most internal-state hallucination detection techniques have been explored predominantly in English, raising the question of whether such internal signals generalize across different languages and domains. To address this gap, we present CrossHallu, the first study to evaluate the cross-lingual and cross-domain generalization of hallucination detection using internal representations from six LLMs on the generative question-answering task. We conduct a systematic Arabic <-> English evaluation using TruthfulQA, an Arabic translated version of TruthfulQA, and HalluScore. This evaluation encompasses monolingual training and testing, cross-lingual transfer, cross-domain transfer, and combined cross-lingual and cross-domain transfer. The results reveal that internal-state hallucination signals in LLMs transfer across languages and domains for most models, with cross-lingual performance highly dependent on both class separability and language alignment in the feature space, whereas cross-domain transfer within Arabic varies depending on the training and testing datasets used for the hallucination detector. The code is publicly available at https://github.com/aishaalansari57/CrossHal.

### [49] [Telescope: Improving Zero Shot Detection of LLM Generated Content By Measuring Token Repetition Probability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04061)
*Christopher Nassif,Josh F. Cooper*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a new, efficient metric called Telescope Perplexity that exploits LLMs’ unusually strong avoidance of token repetition—learned early in training—as a reliable zero-shot signal to distinguish LLM-generated text from human writing.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: It is increasingly important yet difficult to distinguish text written by large language models from human-authored text. Existing detectors are often brittle, computationally expensive, or fail under distribution shift and adversarial perturbations. The authors hypothesize that the LLM training process leaves behind a stable behavioral artifact—an overly strong bias against repeating tokens—that could be leveraged as a more robust and efficient detection signal.

Method: They study training dynamics of LLMs and identify a pronounced early-emerging bias against token repetition, which they frame as a “vestigial heuristic.” To quantitatively capture this, they introduce Telescope Perplexity, a metric based on the model’s conditional probabilities of next tokens, focusing specifically on patterns of repetition. They then evaluate this metric as a zero-shot detector of LLM-generated text across multiple datasets, models, and perturbation settings, comparing its performance and efficiency to existing detection methods.

Result: Their experiments show that the Telescope Perplexity pattern clearly appears early in pre-training and persists, serving as a distinctive signature of LLM outputs. Using this metric alone enables highly effective zero-shot LLM detection, achieving state-of-the-art or competitive performance on several benchmarks, including new evaluation sets they propose, while being more computationally efficient than alternative approaches.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that LLMs’ early-learned, persistent aversion to token repetition constitutes a robust developmental artifact that differentiates them from human writers. Telescope Perplexity successfully operationalizes this artifact into a practical, efficient, zero-shot detection method that performs at or near state-of-the-art across diverse conditions.

Abstract: Distinguishing Large Language Model (LLM) generated text from human writing is a critical and difficult challenge. While LLMs are trained to write like humans, we hypothesize that this training leaves an indelible mark. LLMs develop a particularly strong aversion to token repetition very early in training. This bias persists as a ''Vestigial Heuristic'' (a developmental artifact) that is activated in LLM-generated text, separating LLM from human writing. To probe this phenomenon, we introduce Telescope Perplexity, a metric that evaluates the token repetition of the model, $P(s_i | s_{1:i})$ . Our empirical investigation reveals that the Telescope Perplexity signature emerges early in pre-training, and Telescope Perplexity empirically enables highly effective zero-shot LLM detection. We show state-of-the-art or competitive performance across diverse datasets (including modern evaluation sets we introduce), reference models, and perturbation schemes with greater efficiency than other methods.

However, when trained with an utterance-level cross-entropy objective, the model predicts speaker identity rather than linguistic content, thereby compromising the purity of syllabic tokens. To address this problem, we propose a speaker-disentangled syllabic tokenizer that regresses speaker-perturbed student representations toward clean teacher targets within fixed-length chunks. Experimental results demonstrate that our proposed method achieves state-of-the-art performance in syllable boundary detection and syllabic segment clustering. Moreover, a speech language model trained on our syllabic tokens achieves a 7% relative improvement in syntactic and semantic understanding over the phone-level SpiRit-LM.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Portuguese is a major world language but is barely represented in text embedding evaluations. As a result, practitioners choose embedding models using English or generic multilingual scores, with little evidence about how well these models actually work for Portuguese tasks. The authors aim to fill this gap with a systematic, task-diverse Portuguese benchmark and to understand how well current models and fine-tuning strategies serve Portuguese applications.

Method: They construct MTEB-PT by selecting 14 Portuguese datasets from MMTEB, covering STS, classification, retrieval, and reranking. Using a unified evaluation protocol, they benchmark 17 open- and closed-source embedding models, including ones with long-context capabilities. They then fine-tune three representative backbone models with Portuguese contrastive supervision, applying Matryoshka Representation Learning (MRL) to obtain embeddings that remain effective even under dimensional truncation. Performance is compared across tasks, models, and fine-tuning configurations.

Result: They find that (1) Portuguese performance varies strongly by task type; (2) model rankings on multilingual benchmarks do not reliably transfer to Portuguese across task families; (3) no single model is best on all Portuguese tasks; (4) models with strong long-context handling do particularly well on long-input tasks like retrieval and reranking; and (5) language-specific fine-tuning with Portuguese data significantly improves performance, especially on tasks closely aligned with the adaptation data. The MRL fine-tuned models see their largest gains on STS, while also improving retrieval and staying competitive when embeddings are truncated to lower dimensions.

Conclusion: MTEB-PT provides a much-needed, systematic benchmark for evaluating text embeddings in Portuguese, revealing that naive reliance on English or multilingual rankings is misleading for Portuguese use cases. Task characteristics and language-specific fine-tuning both matter greatly: long-context models shine on long-input tasks, and Portuguese contrastive training—particularly with MRL—yields substantial gains, especially on STS, without sacrificing efficiency. The authors release the benchmark, fine-tuned models, and code to support future research and more informed model selection for Portuguese NLP.

Abstract: Portuguese remains underrepresented in text embedding evaluation, despite being one of the most widely spoken languages in the world. As a result, embedding models are often selected based on English or multilingual metrics, while their effectiveness in Portuguese remains unclear. We present MTEB-PT, a Portuguese benchmark constructed from a subset of MMTEB, comprising 14 existing datasets across Semantic Textual Similarity (STS), classification, retrieval, and reranking. We use this benchmark to evaluate 17 open- and closed-source embedding models under a unified protocol. Our results show that Portuguese performance is strongly task-dependent: multilingual rankings do not reliably predict Portuguese-specific performance across task families, no single model dominates all settings, and models with stronger long-context capacity are particularly advantageous on longer-input tasks such as retrieval and reranking. The benchmark also shows that language-specific fine-tuning still improves model performance in Portuguese, especially on task types that match the adaptation data most closely. To examine this effect, we fine-tune three representative backbone models with Portuguese contrastive supervision and Matryoshka Representation Learning (MRL). These benchmark-informed baselines yield their strongest gains on STS, consistent with the predominantly symmetric supervision used during training, while also improving retrieval and remaining competitive under dimensional truncation. We release the MTEB-PT benchmark, the fine-tuned models, and the training and evaluation code.

### [52] [Semantic Integration and Lexical Expectation Shape N400 and P600 Dynamics During Naturalistic Reading](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04107)
*Kun Sun,Rong Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether a measure of how semantically relevant a word is to its recent context explains EEG activity during reading beyond standard word surprisal.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While word surprisal is known to predict neural responses during language comprehension, it is unclear if measures of local semantic fit add explanatory power for brain responses in naturalistic reading. The authors want to disentangle effects of lexical expectation from effects of semantic integration at the discourse level.

Method: They use the Dublin EEG-based Reading Experiment Corpus with 22 participants and 32 EEG channels during naturalistic reading. They compute contextual semantic relevance as an attention-aware measure of a word’s semantic connectedness to its recent discourse context, and they compute GPT-based word surprisal. Using regression-based ERP analyses and generalized additive mixed models, they test how each predictor relates to word-locked EEG activity in the N400 and P600 time windows, controlling for lexical variables and repeated measures, and compare model fits.

Result: Both surprisal and contextual semantic relevance significantly predict EEG responses but with distinct temporal and scalp distributions. Surprisal reflects expectancy-related variance, while contextual semantic relevance shows robust effects on mean voltages in both N400 and especially P600 windows. Model comparisons show that contextual semantic relevance explains additional variance beyond lexical factors and surprisal.

Conclusion: Naturalistic reading involves both predictive lexical processing and local semantic integration. Contextual semantic relevance is a useful, interpretable computational metric that captures discourse-level semantic fit and explains ERP dynamics beyond word surprisal alone, particularly in the P600 time window.

Abstract: Word surprisal is a well-established computational predictor of human neural responses during language comprehension, but it remains less clear whether local semantic fit explains neural response variation beyond lexical expectation during naturalistic reading. Using the Dublin EEG-based Reading Experiment Corpus (DERCo), this study examined whether contextual semantic relevance predicts word-locked EEG activity in the N400 and P600 windows. Contextual semantic relevance was computed as an attention-aware measure of how strongly a target word is semantically connected to its recent discourse context, and it was compared with GPT-based word surprisal. Across 22 participants and 32 EEG channels, we tested both predictors using regression-based ERP analyses and generalized additive mixed models while controlling for lexical variables and repeated observations. Both predictors were reliably associated with EEG responses, but they showed partly different temporal and scalp-level patterns. Surprisal captured expectancy-related variation, whereas contextual semantic relevance showed robust effects across N400- and P600-window mean voltages, with particularly strong explanatory support in the P600 window. Model comparisons indicated that contextual semantic relevance contributed explanatory value beyond lexical controls and surprisal. These findings suggest that naturalistic reading depends on both lexical expectation and local semantic integration, and that contextual semantic relevance offers an interpretable computational link between discourse semantic fit and ERP dynamics.

### [53] [Detecting Hallucinations in Retrieval-Augmented Generation through Grounding-Aware Sensitivity by Perturbation (GASP)](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04223)
*Mohamed Aly Bouke*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose GASP, a span-level hallucination detector for RAG that measures how much each answer sentence’s likelihood depends on retrieved documents, achieving better performance than common baselines, especially when answers are composed from context.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RAG systems still hallucinate, and existing hallucination detectors only give a single score per answer, without identifying which specific sentences are unsupported or explaining the dependency on retrieved evidence. There is a need for fine-grained, interpretable hallucination detection that works without extra training.

Method: GASP keeps the generated answer fixed and re-scores it multiple times with an instruction-tuned language model: once with full retrieved context, once with no context, and then with each context chunk removed. For each sentence, it computes grounding sensitivity features (log-likelihood drops and Jensen–Shannon divergence changes) that quantify how dependent that sentence is on the evidence. Grounded sentences show large sensitivity when their supporting chunks are removed, while hallucinated ones do not. This behavior is interpreted via a random nonlinear iterated function system view of decoding. They then use either a simple threshold on these features or a trained classifier for hallucination detection.

Result: On RAGTruth, GASP achieves about 0.73 response-level AUC and 0.67 span-level AUC, outperforming perplexity, length-based metrics, whole-context NLI, and self-consistency baselines. At the span level it is competitive only with a strong chunk-level entailment verifier that needs a separate model. A training-free threshold on GASP’s grounding features performs on par with the supervised classifier, removing the need for labeled data. The grounding signal generalizes to TofuEval but is weak for short-answer QA in RAGBench.

Conclusion: Grounding sensitivity, measured via perturbing retrieved chunks while holding the answer fixed, is an effective and interpretable signal for span-level hallucination detection in RAG, especially when answers are composed from retrieved context. GASP delivers competitive performance with minimal setup, often matching supervised methods using only simple thresholds, though its effectiveness diminishes when questions can be answered from the model’s parametric knowledge rather than the retrieved evidence.

Abstract: Retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) reduces but does not eliminate hallucination, and existing detectors return a single answer-level score that does not indicate which sentence is unsupported, or why. To close this gap, we introduce Grounding-Aware Sensitivity by Perturbation (GASP), a span-level detector that scores each answer sentence by how strongly its likelihood depends on the retrieved evidence, a quantity we term grounding sensitivity. GASP holds the answer fixed and re-scores it under the full context, under no context, and with each chunk removed, then measures the log-likelihood drops and Jensen-Shannon divergences (JSD). The likelihood of a grounded sentence collapses once its supporting passage is removed, whereas a hallucinated sentence is almost unaffected, a contrast we interpret by casting decoding as a random nonlinear iterated function system (RNIFS). We evaluate GASP on three benchmarks (RAGTruth, TofuEval, RAGBench) with three instruction-tuned scorers from two model families (Qwen2.5-0.5B, Qwen2.5-1.5B, and SmolLM2-1.7B) under a leakage-clean protocol. On RAGTruth it reaches a response-level area under the ROC curve (AUC) of about 0.73 and a span-level AUC of about 0.67, improving significantly over perplexity and by clear margins over length, whole-context natural language inference (NLI), and self-consistency baselines. The only baseline competitive at the span level is a well-configured chunk-level entailment verifier, which requires a separate model, whereas a training-free threshold on the grounding features matches the trained classifier without labeled data and serves as the default detector. Beyond RAGTruth, the signal transfers to TofuEval but not to short-answer question answering in RAGBench, showing GASP is best suited to outputs constructed from the retrieved context rather than answers recoverable from parametric knowledge.

</details>


### [54] [Spinning Straw into Gold: Relabeling LLM Agent Trajectories in Hindsight for Successful Demonstrations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04235)
*Zichao Li,Gang Wu,Zichao Wang,Ruiyi Zhang,Wanrong Zhu,Ryan A. Rossi,Vlad I Morariu,Jihyung Kil*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose Hindsight Supervised Learning (HSL), which reuses successful but unintended goals from agent trajectories as extra supervision to improve long-horizon LLM agents efficiently.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Post-training large language model agents for partially observable, long-horizon tasks is bottlenecked by expensive, scarce supervision. Existing methods mainly rely on ground-truth demonstrations or explicit human feedback and ignore the rich signal hidden in what agents incidentally accomplish during their rollouts. There is a need for a sample-efficient way to exploit this overlooked source of supervision, especially in environments with diverse goal spaces where data is hard to collect.

Method: They introduce Hindsight Supervised Learning (HSL). First, an auxiliary LLM inspects each completed trajectory (rollout) and annotates it with all natural-language goals that the agent actually achieved, including unintended but successful ones. These hindsight goals are then used to relabel the trajectory, producing new (trajectory, goal) training pairs. The model is then further fine-tuned on this augmented dataset. To reduce the effect of noisy or suboptimal relabelings, they propose (1) irrelevant-action masking, which ignores actions deemed irrelevant to the relabeled goals, and (2) sample reweighting, which downweights lower-quality or less relevant relabeled samples during training. HSL is designed to plug into standard post-training pipelines such as SFT and DPO.

Result: Across experiments, HSL consistently improves the performance of LLM agents when integrated into existing post-training approaches. It yields noticeable gains for both supervised fine-tuning (SFT) and direct preference optimization (DPO), with particularly strong improvements on long-horizon tasks featuring diverse goal spaces. On the ALFWorld benchmark, HSL achieves better performance than baselines trained on the entire ground-truth dataset while using only 25% of the human-provided demonstrations, demonstrating strong sample efficiency.

Conclusion: HSL leverages hindsight information—natural-language descriptions of goals that agents actually achieved—to create additional, cheap supervision for LLM agents. By combining trajectory relabeling with irrelevant-action masking and sample reweighting, it integrates smoothly with common post-training methods and substantially boosts performance, especially in data-scarce, long-horizon environments. This shows that repurposing unintended successes in rollouts is a powerful and efficient way to improve goal-conditioned LLM agents.

Abstract: Large language model agents operate in partially observable, long-horizon settings where obtaining supervision remains a major bottleneck. We address this by utilizing a source of supervision overlooked in existing post-training methods: unintended yet successful goals embedded within agent rollouts. Specifically, we introduce Hindsight Supervised Learning (HSL), where an auxiliary LLM reviews each completed trajectory and relabels it with all of the natural-language goals the agent actually achieved. HSL then pairs the trajectory with its relabeled goals and uses these pairs for additional fine-tuning. To mitigate suboptimality in the relabeled data, we propose two learning techniques for HSL, irrelevant-action masking and sample reweighting. Our experiments show that HSL is flexible and compatible with existing post-training pipelines. It improves both SFT and DPO, with larger gains on long-horizon tasks with more diverse goal spaces. Moreover, HSL is sample-efficient: on ALFWorld, it surpasses baselines trained on the full dataset while using only one quarter of the ground-truth demonstrations.

</details>


### [55] [Risk-Constrained Freshness-Aware Semantic Caching for Open-Web Retrieval-Augmented LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04281)
*Muhammad Mansoor,Tahir Ahmad,Yeo-Chan Yoon*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: FreshCache is a semantic cache for RAG that explicitly models time-varying freshness, trading off cache reuse vs. staleness risk to save search calls with minimal stale answers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing semantic caching methods for retrieval-augmented generation mostly treat cache reuse as a static similarity problem and ignore how web content changes over time. This can make answers stale or force conservative strategies that re-run full retrieval pipelines too often, increasing latency and cost. The paper aims to develop a caching mechanism that is both cost-efficient and freshness-aware, with explicit control over staleness risk.

Method: They design FreshCache, a three-tier semantic cache for answers, URL lists, and page content. For each cache tier, they estimate the probability that an entry is stale using a fitted exponential decay model augmented by a learned MLP. Cache reuse is framed as a risk-constrained decision: an entry is reused only if its predicted staleness probability is below a per-tier error budget (ε=0.10 for answers, 0.20 for URLs, 0.35 for page content). This allows gradual degradation of reuse over time instead of a binary hit/miss. They also build FreshCache-Bench, a benchmark of web queries with ground-truth staleness labels collected from web snapshots at multiple time intervals, and expand it via paraphrasing for evaluation.

Result: On FreshCache-Bench at a 24-hour window, FreshCache_MLP achieves 97% reduction in search API calls at only 0.1% stale error when using a strict hash-based staleness definition. An LLM-judge analysis on 396 changed content pairs finds that just 34.3% of detected changes affect answer correctness, implying a true answer-affecting stale error of ~0.034%. A simpler rule-based FreshCache gets 98% search savings with 3.3% stale error under temporal holdout calibration. It outperforms prior methods SemanticTTL (14.9% stale, 72% saved), vCache (7.2% stale, 47% saved), and SCALM (5.2% stale, 96% saved). Ablation studies show that the temporal risk gate reduces stale error by 11.6 percentage points compared with similarity-only reuse, and the MLP further cuts stale error by 3.2 points over the purely rule-based model.

Conclusion: FreshCache demonstrates that modeling freshness as a temporal risk-constrained inference problem significantly improves semantic caching for RAG, enabling very high search cost savings while keeping true answer-affecting staleness extremely low. The three-tier design with per-tier error budgets and a learned temporal model yields graceful performance degradation over time and surpasses existing caching baselines on a newly introduced benchmark. This suggests that principled temporal risk modeling is a key ingredient for practical, freshness-aware RAG systems.

Abstract: Semantic caching reduces the latency and cost of retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) by serving cached answers to semantically similar queries, but most existing methods do not model the time-varying freshness of open-web evidence. We present FreshCache, a three-tier semantic cache that treats cache reuse as a risk-constrained temporal inference problem: before approving a cache hit, FreshCache estimates the probability that the cached result is stale using a fitted exponential decay model enhanced by a learned MLP, and approves reuse only when that probability falls below a per-tier error budget across answers (epsilon = 0.10), URL lists (epsilon = 0.20), and page content (epsilon = 0.35). This allows the system to degrade gracefully as entries age rather than forcing a binary choice between a stale hit and a full pipeline execution. We introduce FreshCache-Bench, a benchmark of 8,072 base queries across five freshness classes with ground truth staleness labels drawn from real web snapshots at 1, 12, 24 hours, and 7 days after a baseline crawl, expanded to 31,201 queries via paraphrase generation. At the 24-hour evaluation window, FreshCache_MLP achieves 97% search API savings at 0.1% hash-based stale error, and an LLM-judge evaluation on 396 confirmed change pairs shows that only 34.3% of detected content changes actually affect answer correctness, placing true answer-affecting stale error at approximately 0.034%. The rule-based FreshCache achieves 98% search savings at 3.3% stale error under a temporal holdout calibration, outperforming SemanticTTL (14.9% stale, 72% saved), vCache (7.2% stale, 47% saved), and SCALM (5.2% stale, 96% saved). Ablations show the temporal risk gate accounts for an 11.6 point reduction in stale error over similarity-only reuse, and the learned MLP reduces stale error a further 3.2 points over the rule-based model.

</details>

*Zhenhao Chen,Yongqiang Chen,Chenxi Liu,Junchi Yu,Xiangchen Song,Zijian Li,Jialin Li,Philip Torr,Bo Han,Kun Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces CausalGame, an interactive benchmark to test whether LLM-based AI Scientist agents can perform genuine causal reasoning under realistic biases, and finds that current models fail substantially.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI Scientist benchmarks mainly test surface-level reasoning and problem solving but do not explicitly challenge models with real-world causal complications like selection bias, measurement error, and hidden confounders, which are central to scientific discovery. The authors aim to fill this gap by creating a benchmark that probes whether LLM agents can think causally, not just correlate patterns.

Method: The authors design CausalGame, a suite of 14 interactive game-like scenarios that simulate scientific discovery problems under selection bias, measurement error, and hidden confounding. LLM agents must propose experimental protocols, decide what data to collect, interact step-by-step with the environment, and ultimately provide a final answer plus an explanation report. They evaluate 30 different LLM agents on these games, compare their performance to analytically derived optimal strategies, and score their explanations with causal-reasoning rubrics.

Result: Across 30 tested LLM agents, none shows robust causal reasoning. The top-performing model attains only 68.0% survival rate compared to analytical optima of 78–85%, and only 5–7% of sessions are judged as meeting the causal-reasoning criteria in their explanations. This indicates substantial gaps between current LLM capabilities and the causal thinking needed for scientific discovery.

Conclusion: CausalGame serves as a scalable, controlled benchmark for systematically evaluating the causal thinking abilities of AI Scientist agents. The poor performance of current LLMs suggests that genuine causal reasoning under realistic biases remains an open challenge, highlighting the need for new methods and training strategies if AI is to effectively support scientific discovery.

Abstract: Building AI Scientist agents with Large Language Models (LLMs) has recently attracted growing attention. Since scientific discovery fundamentally relies on uncovering causal relationships from observations, the capability of causal thinking, i.e., distinguishing causation from correlation and recognizing hidden biases, is essential to LLM agents. Although a number of benchmarks exist for AI Scientists, none explicitly incorporate challenges from selection bias, measurement error, and hidden confounders that widely exist in real-world scientific discovery. To this end, we present CausalGame, a benchmark that evaluates the causal thinking capabilities of LLM agents through interactive games. CausalGame asks LLM agents to actively design experimental protocols, collect observation data, and derive a final solution with an explanation report. To emulate realistic scientific discovery challenges, we design 14 scenarios that incorporate selection bias, measurement error, and hidden confounders. Across 30 LLM agents, none demonstrates reliable causal thinking: the best model reaches only 68.0% survival against analytical optima of 78-85%, and merely 5-7% of sessions receive credits on the causal-reasoning rubrics. CausalGame provides a scalable and controlled testbed for evaluating the causal thinking of AI Scientist agents.

### [57] [Legible-by-Construction: Attention and End-to-End Transformers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04319)
*Mark Oskin*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper extends prior work that made transformer feed-forward layers interpretable using fuzzy set operations by applying similar ideas to the attention mechanism, creating a fully legible transformer whose internal units act as explicit logical and set-based feature detectors without hurting language modeling performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Transformers are highly effective but largely opaque, especially in how attention heads represent and manipulate features. Prior work showed that feed-forward layers can be expressed as fuzzy set operations with interpretable units, but attention remained a black box. The motivation is to make the entire transformer, including attention, legible by construction so that internal computations can be read as explicit logical and set-theoretic operations, while maintaining competitive performance.

Method: The authors modify attention heads so that value vectors pass through a sigmoid, turning each value channel into a bounded scalar that can be read as a detector of whether a particular feature holds at a token. This adds no new parameters and keeps the rest of the attention head standard. They also propose a Boolean variant where the value vectors are restructured to implement explicit within-token intersection and negation-capable set-difference operations. Crucially, the output projection remains unconstrained (not tied to vocabulary), enabling selective detectors by bounding what heads detect but not what they write. Training uses pressures for sparse and decisive (near-0 or near-1) activations to make detectors crisp and interpretable. They evaluate multiple specialized-attention designs in 125M-parameter models and finally couple Boolean attention with the previously proposed legible feed-forward layers to build an end-to-end legible transformer.

Result: Across five 125M-parameter models with different specialized-attention designs, 44–62% of value channels become crisp and contextually selective detectors, whose interpretability improves with layer depth instead of focusing only on superficial features like punctuation. All proposed architectures achieve language modeling performance on par with a conventional transformer baseline, demonstrating that legibility does not require sacrificing benchmark quality.

Conclusion: By minimally constraining attention values and combining them with legible feed-forward layers, the authors obtain transformers whose internal units behave as explicit, named logical and set-theoretic feature detectors, without loss in language modeling performance. The key design choice is bounding what a head detects while leaving what it writes unconstrained, which yields selective, human-readable detectors. The resulting model is legible by construction across both attention and feed-forward components, enabling one to trace how specific named operations compose to produce any generated token.

Abstract: A companion paper showed that a transformer's feed-forward layer can be rebuilt from explicit fuzzy set operations - intersection, set-difference, and a self-forgetting sequence quantifier - so its hidden units read as named logical operators at no cost to language-model quality. That left the other half of the transformer opaque. Here we carry the same idea into attention and join the two into one model. The mechanism is minimal: a head's value is passed through a sigmoid, so each value channel becomes a readable detector of whether a feature holds at a token. This adds no parameters and leaves the standard head otherwise untouched. A Boolean variant goes further, restructuring the value into an explicit within-token intersection and negation-capable set-difference. In both designs the output projection is left free, not tied to the vocabulary, which is the load-bearing decision: bounding what a head detects while leaving what it writes unconstrained yields selective detectors, whereas constraining the write does not. A bounded value is shaped into a readable detector by two selectivity pressures - one for sparse firing, one for decisive firing at the rails - and which a design wants is not universal. Across five specialized-attention designs at 125M parameters, 44 to 62 percent of value channels become crisp, contextually selective detectors, and their legibility rises with depth rather than crystallizing only on punctuation. Language-model quality is at parity with a conventional baseline. Finally, we couple the Boolean attention to the legible feed-forward layer and train an end-to-end legible-by-construction language model at benchmark parity: its feed-forward units are named set and quantifier operations throughout, and we can take a token it generates and read the named units that compose to produce it.

</details>


### [58] [WPG-MoE: Weak-Prior-Guided Dense Mixture-of-Experts for User-Level Social Media Depression Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04350)
*Xian Li,Yuanhe Tian,Yang Yang,Guoqing Wang,Yan Song*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes WPG-MoE, a weak-prior-guided mixture-of-experts framework on top of an LLM backbone for early depression detection from social media, which uses LLM-extracted structured evidence as privileged information during training to route users to specialized experts and achieves better performance and interpretability on Chinese and English datasets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing depression screening methods on social media typically rely on a single classifier that operates after an initial screening stage (e.g., risk-post selection, symptom grounding, clinically informed features). This monolithic approach ignores heterogeneity in how different users express depressive risk, especially non-self-disclosing users, leading to diluted evidence and misclassification. The authors are motivated to design a framework that can adapt to different evidence patterns across users while keeping deployment simple and scalable.

Method: The authors design WPG-MoE, a weak-prior-guided dense mixture-of-experts on a shared LLM backbone. During training, they use the LLM to extract rich structured evidence from users’ posts (e.g., symptom-like signals) and derive user-level weak semantic priors. These priors softly route users to different experts, each specializing in a certain layout of evidence. They cast this as learning using privileged information (LUPI): the structured LLM evidence is only used at training time to guide routing. At inference, the model uses only PHQ-9-style screening templates and the backbone LLM, without needing the privileged structured features, making it deployable in practice.

Result: On both Chinese and English depression-related social media datasets, WPG-MoE yields higher screening accuracy than strong baseline models that use a single classifier or simpler architectures. The routing patterns are interpretable, showing that different experts correspond to distinct evidence layouts or user expression styles, and that users are non-uniformly assigned in a way consistent with the intended design.

Conclusion: User-level heterogeneity in expressing depressive risk on social media can be better captured by a mixture-of-experts framework guided by weak semantic priors derived from LLM-extracted evidence. By formulating the problem under LUPI and discarding privileged information at inference, the proposed WPG-MoE achieves both improved performance and practical deployability, offering a more nuanced and interpretable approach to early depression screening from online posts.

Abstract: Online social media posts provide scalable signals for early depression screening, and recent studies mainly improve pre-classification evidence through risk-post selection, symptom grounding, and clinically informed feature construction. However, these screening-stage designs often leave final decisions to a single detector, overlooking how users heterogeneously express depressive risk after screening. A monolithic classifier must average across heterogeneous users, which may dilute localized evidence and cause misclassification, especially for non-self-disclosing users. To address this issue, we propose WPG-MoE, a weak-prior-guided dense mixture-of-experts framework built on a shared large language model (LLM) backbone. WPG-MoE derives user-level weak semantic priors to softly route users to experts matched to different evidence layouts. We formulate this process as learning using privileged information (LUPI): rich LLM-extracted structured evidence guides training-time routing, while inference retains only Patient Health Questionnaire-9 (PHQ-9) template screening and the deployable backbone. Experiments on Chinese and English datasets show that WPG-MoE outperforms strong baselines with interpretable routing behavior.

### [59] [Memory-Orchestrated Semantic System (MOSS): An Auditable Agentic Memory Architecture](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04391)
*Serge Lacasse,Jérémie Hatier,Alex Baker*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MOSS, a symbolic, auditable, and model-agnostic long-term memory architecture for AI agents that replaces opaque vector-based RAG with queryable relational structures, and validates it via a year-long deployment on a large personal research corpus.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current long-term memory for AI agents is dominated by RAG over vector embeddings, which is opaque, hard to audit, and limited by the properties of embedding spaces. There is a need for memory systems that are transparent, structurally unbounded, auditable, and suitable for long-lived agents that accompany individuals or organizations over years rather than short sessions.

Method: The authors design MOSS, a Memory-Orchestrated Semantic System, where an agent formulates symbolic queries over a structured relational database instead of relying on vector similarity search. MOSS automatically derives a conceptual vocabulary and ontological structure from the corpus, stores documents, segments, concepts, annotations, and metadata as relational graphs, and orchestrates retrieval and reasoning through an LLM (or other processes) only at the query-formulation and answer-synthesis stages. All steps—including indexing, retrieval queries, and answer construction—are logged for inspection. They evaluate MOSS via a longitudinal production deployment over an individual scholar’s evolving corpus for one year, tracking scale, concepts, and infrastructure generations.

Result: MOSS successfully operates in production over a year on a large, evolving personal research corpus: 44M tokens in 110k segments, 163k documents, nearly 570 induced concepts with 322k annotations, and multiple metadata graphs totaling about 5M relations, across four infrastructure generations. The system remains model-, storage-, and API-agnostic, with fully symbolic and reproducible retrieval, and demonstrates that its inductively derived conceptual vocabulary and relational structures can support long-term, agentic use at scale.

Conclusion: The study concludes that MOSS delivers an auditable, sovereign, and structurally unbounded long-term memory architecture for AI agents, overcoming key limitations of embedding-based RAG. The authors argue that such transparent, symbolic, and extensible memory is a necessary foundation for AI agents meant to persist alongside individuals or organizations over multi-year timescales, and that the architecture generalizes beyond a single-user use case to broader institutional settings.

Abstract: Long-term memory remains a structural weakness of AI agents. The dominant approach, retrieval-augmented generation (RAG), relies on embedding-based similarity search, which is opaque by construction, difficult to audit, and bounded by the theoretical limits of vector representations. We present the Memory-Orchestrated Semantic System (MOSS), an agentic memory architecture in which the agent drives retrieval over a structured relational database. MOSS is model-agnostic, storage-agnostic, and API-agnostic: it runs on any relational engine, connects to any LLM provider (or to deterministic non-LLM processes), and deploys on any infrastructure, local or cloud. Its retrieval execution is symbolic and reproducible (once a query is formulated, no LLM participates in the retrieval loop) and every step of the system, from indexing to answer formulation, is logged and inspectable, making MOSS auditable by construction. Rather than imposing an external ontology, MOSS derives its conceptual vocabulary from the corpus itself. We report on a longitudinal deployment unique in the agentic-memory literature: a year of continuous production over an individual scholar's working corpus--a conversational corpus reaching back to October 2024 (some 44 million tokens, retroactively indexed) comprising 110,183 segments, alongside 163,494 catalogued documents, 569 inductively derived concepts, 322,662 concept annotations, and eleven metadata graphs totaling approximately five million relations--across four successive infrastructure generations. While the present case is that of a single researcher, the architecture is in no way specific to one person: it serves a team, an institution, or any entity that accumulates knowledge over time. We argue that auditable, sovereign, structurally unbounded memory is a precondition for AI agents intended to accompany a person or an organization over years rather than sessions.

Method: The authors construct Uni-GUI, a curated dataset of high-quality, executable GUI interaction trajectories that span multiple platforms. Together, they enable GUI agents to better retain platform-specific skills while generalizing across platforms. The empirical results on OSWorld and MobileWorld show that the approach can balance capability retention and adaptation, representing a step forward toward practical, robust cross-platform GUI agents.

Abstract: Recent advances in multimodal foundation models and agent systems have driven GUI agents from single-platform task execution toward cross-platform interaction. On one hand, high-quality and executable cross-platform interaction trajectories are still scarce, and existing data often suffer from limited platform coverage. On the other hand, different platforms exhibit distinct interaction conventions, making joint or continual training prone to behavioral pattern mixing, platform-specific capability degradation, and catastrophic forgetting. To address these challenges, we construct Uni-GUI, a high-quality cross-platform GUI interaction dataset, and propose UI-MOPD, the first method that incorporates multi-teacher on-policy distillation into continual learning for GUI agents. UI-MOPD dynamically selects a platform-specific teacher according to the current environment and transfers platform-specific behavioral priors to a shared policy through platform-conditioned distillation, enabling adaptation to new platforms while preserving capabilities on existing ones. Experiments on OSWorld and MobileWorld show that UI-MOPD achieves task success rates of 38.2% and 12.0%, respectively, demonstrating its effectiveness in balancing cross-platform capability retention and new-platform adaptation.

### [62] [dOPSD: On-Policy Self-Distillation for Diffusion Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04428)
*Phuong Tuan Dat,Qi Li,Xinchao Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes dOPSD, a new on-policy self-distillation method tailored for diffusion LLMs that leverages the model’s own denoising trajectory as privileged information to improve reasoning without external references.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Diffusion LLMs can generate text in parallel but are hard to post-train for strong reasoning. Existing methods have drawbacks: supervised fine-tuning is off-policy and causes exposure bias; RL for reasoning uses sparse sequence-level rewards and requires tractable likelihoods; prior OPSD methods depend on external privileged information like ground-truth answers, which are unavailable at inference and lead to weak PI-free policies. There is a need for an on-policy, token-level, dense supervision method that works naturally with diffusion LLMs and does not rely on external privileged labels.

Method: The authors introduce dOPSD, an on-policy self-distillation framework for diffusion LLMs where the teacher’s privileged information comes from the model’s own denoising trajectory. Instead of using external ground-truth references, the method uses later, more decoded steps in the denoising process to supervise earlier, more masked steps at the token level. Thus, the teacher is effectively a more informed snapshot of the same model along its diffusion trajectory, providing dense on-policy guidance for learning, eliminating off-policy issues and dependencies on external privileged labels.

Result: On the Dream and LLaDA diffusion LLMs, applying dOPSD yields improvements in in-domain math reasoning and out-of-domain code generation, outperforming both supervised fine-tuning and previous on-policy baselines that rely on traditional OPSD or RL-style training. The results indicate that trajectory-based privileged information can significantly enhance reasoning performance in diffusion LLMs.

Conclusion: dOPSD demonstrates that the denoising trajectory of a diffusion LLM can itself serve as a source of privileged information for on-policy self-distillation, avoiding the need for external ground-truth references. By using later decoding steps to supervise earlier masked states, the method provides dense, token-level, on-policy supervision that improves reasoning and generalization in diffusion LLMs beyond existing supervised and on-policy techniques.

Abstract: Diffusion large language models (dLLMs) generate text by iteratively denoising a masked sequence, offering a parallel alternative to autoregressive models, but eliciting strong reasoning through post-training remains difficult: supervised fine-tuning is off-policy and suffers from exposure bias, while reinforcement learning gives only sparse, sequence-level rewards and is hard to apply without tractable sequence likelihoods. On-policy self-distillation (OPSD) offers a promising alternative, using one model as both student and teacher to provide dense, token-level, on-policy supervision, but its effectiveness hinges on giving the teacher privileged information (PI) - typically an instance-specific ground-truth reference unavailable at inference - so the student ends up distilling a weak PI-free consensus policy that yields little improvement on dLLM reasoning. We introduce dOPSD, which instead derives the teacher's privilege directly from the student's own denoising trajectory, evaluating masked positions using later, more-decoded steps of that same trajectory rather than an external label, so the teacher's advantage emerges from the model's own decoding process; on Dream and LLaDA, dOPSD improves both in-domain math reasoning and out-of-domain code generation, outperforming supervised and on-policy baselines.
