### [63] [evalci: A Python Library for Statistically Rigorous Comparison of Language Model Evaluations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04429)
*Shreyas K Chandrahas*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces evalci, a Python library that adds proper statistical rigor to language model evaluations by turning per-item results into clear, statistically grounded comparison claims.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current language model evaluations usually compare models using a single accuracy number and declare the higher one better, without checking if the observed difference could be due to sampling noise. This is particularly problematic on benchmarks with only a few thousand items and under temperature sampling, where the same model can vary from run to run by more than the reported gap. Although appropriate statistical tools exist (confidence intervals, paired tests, power analysis, clustered standard errors, multiple-comparison correction), they are not packaged in a convenient, standard tool tailored to how evaluations are actually stored and run (per-item results tables). The authors aim to fill this gap and reduce overconfident, statistically unsupported claims in LM leaderboards and papers.

Method: The authors develop evalci, a pure-Python library relying only on numpy, scipy, and pandas that takes as input a per-item results table and outputs statistically rigorous comparison statements. In a single function call, it computes effect sizes (e.g., accuracy differences), confidence intervals, paired significance tests (using permutation tests), and sample sizes, yielding publication-ready claims like “Model A beats Model B, Δ=3.1 pts, 95% CI [1.2, 5.0], paired permutation p=0.002, n=1,319.” The library includes adapters for popular evaluation frameworks such as lm-evaluation-harness and HELM and validates each routine against independent references like statsmodels or exact brute-force enumeration to ensure correctness.

Result: Using evalci, the authors reanalyze a public comparison of nine language models on the MMLU benchmark. They find that after performing proper statistical tests and correcting for multiple comparisons (36 pairwise tests implied by the leaderboard), three of the eight adjacent gaps in the ranking are not statistically significant. This shows that some commonly reported leaderboard differences likely reflect noise rather than real performance differences.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that many current language model leaderboard claims are statistically overconfident and that rigorous uncertainty quantification should be standard practice. evalci offers a simple, practical solution by integrating established statistical tools into a lightweight, easily adoptable Python library that works directly with per-item evaluation tables and popular LM evaluation frameworks. By making it easy to report effect sizes, confidence intervals, and corrected significance tests, evalci can help bring more reliability and transparency to language model evaluation and comparison.

Abstract: The dominant practice in language model evaluation is to report a single accuracy number per model and declare the higher one better, without testing whether the gap could plausibly be sampling noise. On benchmarks of a few thousand items, and under temperature sampling where a model can differ from itself run to run by more than the reported gap between models, this practice routinely overstates confidence in headline claims. The statistical machinery to fix this -- confidence intervals, paired significance tests, power analysis, clustered standard errors, multiple-comparison correction -- is well established, but no standard, pip-installable tool packages it in the shape an evaluation actually takes: a per-item results table. We present evalci, a pure-Python library (numpy/scipy/pandas only) that turns a per-item results table into a publication-ready claim -- e.g., "Model A beats Model B, $Δ=3.1$ pts, 95% CI [1.2, 5.0], paired permutation $p=0.002$, $n=1{,}319$" -- in one function call, with adapters for lm-evaluation-harness and HELM output. Every routine is validated against an independent reference (statsmodels, or brute-force exact enumeration) rather than only against itself. As a case study, we re-analyze a public comparison of nine language models' MMLU accuracy and find that 3 of the 8 adjacent leaderboard-rank gaps are not statistically significant after correcting for the 36 pairwise comparisons the ranking implies. evalci is available at https://pypi.org/project/evalci/ (source: https://github.com/Shreyaskc/evalci, DOI: https://doi.org/10.5281/zenodo.21201815)

</details>


### [64] [Uncertainty-Aware Abstention in Large Language Models with Provable Alignment Guarantees](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04430)
*Sijin Dong,Hiroyuki Shinnou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes CIC, a framework that calibrates arbitrary uncertainty scores for LLM question answering so that, with high probability, the error rate among answered questions is below a user-chosen risk level while maximizing the number of answered queries.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models in QA can hallucinate or misalign answers and lack reliable confidence estimates. Existing uncertainty scores are heuristic and thresholds on these scores do not give statistical guarantees on the error rate among accepted answers, which is critical in reliability-sensitive applications.

Method: CIC uses a held-out calibration set where each LLM response is labeled as correct/incorrect by an application-specific alignment criterion and paired with an uncertainty score. For each possible uncertainty threshold, CIC estimates the conditional error rate among responses whose score is below the threshold and builds a high-probability upper confidence bound using Hoeffding-style or Clopper-Pearson confidence intervals. It then selects the largest threshold whose upper bound is below a user-specified risk level α, under an exchangeability assumption, thereby defining a selective answering rule with finite-sample guarantees.

Result: On multiple closed- and open-ended QA benchmarks, across seven LLMs and multiple uncertainty estimators, CIC achieves valid risk control (the empirical error among accepted answers respects the target risk level) while maintaining high answering rates. This shows the method works robustly across models, datasets, and uncertainty scoring schemes.

Conclusion: CIC provides a practical, model-agnostic, and statistically grounded way to turn arbitrary uncertainty scores for LLMs into selective answering rules with finite-sample risk guarantees, making LLM-based QA more trustworthy for reliability-critical deployments.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed in question answering (QA) systems, yet they may generate hallucinated or misaligned responses without reliable confidence estimates. Uncertainty quantification (UQ) offers a natural basis for selective answering, where a system answers only when its prediction is deemed reliable and abstains otherwise. However, existing uncertainty scores for LLMs are often heuristic: a threshold chosen on such scores does not, by itself, provide statistical guarantees on the error rate among accepted answers. We propose CIC, a confidence-interval-based calibration framework that converts arbitrary uncertainty scores into risk-controlled selective answering rules. Given a held-out calibration set, CIC evaluates each generated response using an application-specific alignment criterion and associates it with an uncertainty score and a binary error label. For each candidate uncertainty threshold, CIC estimates the acceptance-conditioned error rate and constructs a high-probability upper confidence bound using either Hoeffding-style or Clopper-Pearson confidence intervals. It then selects the largest threshold whose upper bound is below a user-specified risk level $α$, thereby maximizing the answering rate subject to a finite-sample reliability constraint. Under exchangeability, CIC guarantees with probability at least $1-δ$ that the selected threshold, if non-null, controls the error rate among accepted answers at level $α$. We evaluate CIC on both closed-ended and open-ended QA benchmarks across seven LLMs and multiple uncertainty estimators. Experimental results show that CIC consistently achieves valid risk control while retaining strong answering efficiency, providing a practical and statistically grounded mechanism for deploying LLMs in reliability-sensitive QA workflows.

</details>


### [65] [Don't Commit Alone: Joint Token Commitment in Diffusion Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04469)
*Lin Yao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: CoCommit improves diffusion LLM decoding by coordinating jointly-committed tokens, reducing factorization error and boosting accuracy, especially on reasoning and exact-answer tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Diffusion LLMs decode multiple tokens per denoising step independently, which introduces factorization error when those token positions are statistically dependent. Existing confidence-based selection only looks at marginal distributions and therefore cannot detect or correct errors stemming from dependencies between positions. There is a need for a decoding method that better captures joint dependencies among jointly-committed tokens without expensive auxiliary models.

Method: The authors introduce CoCommit, a marker-gated coordination pass added to diffusion LLM decoding. After the standard bundle of positions is selected for commitment in a step, a learned marker network chooses which positions form the commit set. Then, instead of immediately fixing their tokens, the model briefly defers commitment and re-applies the backbone model’s last n layers, conditioned on these commit markers, so that the hidden states of the marked positions can coordinate. This process approximates joint-mode decoding. Finally, greedy argmax is applied to write token outputs for the coordinated positions. CoCommit reuses existing LLM weights, requires only one extra partial forward pass per step, and does not rely on external auxiliary models.

Result: On the LLaDA2.1-mini model equipped with LoRA adapters and using the same base greedy inference settings for comparison, introducing joint commitment with CoCommit yields improved accuracy across all six evaluated benchmarks. The improvements are especially pronounced on tasks that require reasoning or exact answers, suggesting that better modeling of inter-token dependencies in joint commitments is most beneficial in these settings.

Conclusion: CoCommit demonstrates that modest architectural and procedural changes to diffusion LLM decoding—specifically, a marker-gated coordination pass that approximates joint-mode decoding for jointly-committed tokens—can systematically reduce factorization error and boost performance. By coordinating token commitments without extra models and with minimal added computation, the method provides a practical way to leverage dependencies among tokens in diffusion-based decoding, particularly advantageous for reasoning and exact-answer tasks.

Abstract: Diffusion large language models (dLLMs) commit multiple tokens per denoising step by decoding each selected position independently from the shared context; when those positions are dependent, the resulting factorization error is captured by conditional total correlation, which confidence-based selection cannot observe from marginals alone. We propose CoCommit, a marker-gated coordination pass that briefly defers commitment: after the usual bundle selection, a learned marker announces the commit set and the backbone's last-$n$ layers are re-applied so marked positions coordinate -- approximating joint-mode decoding -- before greedy argmax writes tokens. The method reuses existing weights with one extra partial forward pass and no auxiliary model. On LLaDA2.1-mini with LoRA adapters and matched greedy inference, joint commitment improves accuracy on all six benchmarks we evaluate, with the largest gains on reasoning and exact-answer tasks.

</details>


### [66] [Transplanting, inverting, and preventing a misalignment persona: method-conditional emergent misalignment in Qwen2.5](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04510)
*Lyndon Drake,Zandi Eberstadt*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how a specific “persona-like” latent direction in Qwen2.5 models mediates emergent misalignment and shows it is causal, transferable across variants, and preventable/controllable by targeted fine-tuning strategies.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fine-tuning language models on narrow harmful domains can unexpectedly create broad, generalized misaligned behaviors (emergent misalignment, EM). Current safety work lacks mechanistic, causal understanding of how such broad misalignment arises, how it propagates between related models, and how to detect and prevent it efficiently, especially under cheap parameter-efficient fine-tuning (PEFT) commonly used at scale.

Method: The authors work with Qwen2.5 family models and identify a latent ‘persona’ direction in representation space associated with misaligned behavior. They perform causal interventions: (1) transplant this direction into other models sharing only the base pretraining, (2) ablate or steer away from it during or after fine-tuning, (3) compare different fine-tuning methods (low-rank LoRA vs full SFT) and capacities, and (4) use this direction as a one-dimensional behavior-derived axis to quantify persona recruitment and loss-relevance, including inoculation and orthogonal fine-tuning experiments.

Result: They show that (1) transplanting the persona direction into a related model causally induces broad emergent misalignment above a random-direction baseline; (2) ablating the direction in a model cuts the impact of overt misalignment inducers roughly in half; (3) low-rank LoRA fine-tuning on insecure code recruits the misaligned persona and yields notable EM, whereas full SFT on identical data does not and even shifts away from the persona axis; (4) steering training away from the persona can paradoxically increase harmful broadcast in some settings, so simple removal is not always beneficial; and (5) persona loss-relevance predicts the strength of misalignment induction across different inducers, inoculation can selectively prevent recruitment while improving code coherence, and fine-tuning orthogonal to the persona axis reduces EM in a persona-specific way.

Conclusion: Emergent misalignment in this Qwen2.5 case study is mediated by a concrete, causally-relevant latent persona direction that can be transplanted, measured, and selectively controlled. Recruitment of this persona acts as a loss-reducing shortcut that becomes unnecessary at higher capacity or with full SFT, making it detectable and preventable in the examined settings. However, the causal role of the persona is conditional, and naive steering away from it can worsen outcomes, so mitigation must be carefully designed. The findings are limited to one model family and some single-seed experiments, but they suggest that mechanistic, direction-level control can be a practical tool to manage emergent misalignment under real-world fine-tuning regimes.

Abstract: Emergent misalignment (EM) -- the broad misbehaviour a language model acquires after fine-tuning on narrow harmful data -- is mediated in Qwen2.5 models by a latent persona direction, and that direction is causal in open weights. Transplanting it into a model that shares only pretraining with its source induces broad EM (2.83 +/- 0.26% misaligned against a random-direction floor of ~1.1%), and ablating a model's own direction roughly halves an overt inducer's broadcast (21% to 10%). The transplant doubles as a measurement method, causally assaying directions that a source model represents but cannot itself express. Whether a fine-tune recruits this persona depends on method and capacity, and since low-rank PEFT is the cheaper regime at scale, the recruiting method is also the economical one. On Qwen2.5-32B, low-rank LoRA on insecure code recruits it (3.4% misaligned) while full SFT on identical data does not (0.3%) and moves against the persona axis (drift-persona cosine +0.17 at rank 1 to -0.10), the far-inducer, high-capacity exception consistent with a representational-distance x capacity account. The persona's causal role is itself conditional. Steering a bad-medical SFT run away from the direction during training raises the broadcast from 24% to 51% while a matched random control lowers it, so removing the direction is no blanket recipe. Because recruitment is a loss-reducing shortcut that capacity renders redundant, it can be screened for and prevented in the tested instances. Persona loss-relevance at the SFT solution orders four inducers' broadcasts rank-perfectly within Qwen2.5, inoculation removes recruitment selectively (4.75% to 0.0%, code coherence 65% to 87%), and fine-tuning orthogonal to the single behaviour-derived axis reduces it persona-specifically. Results are a controlled case study of one model family, single-seed in places.

It underscores the necessity of larger, better-curated corpora and explicit tone-aware modeling approaches for Efik and other tonal African languages.

Abstract: Efik, a tonal language spoken by about 3 million second language speakers and 1.5 million native speakers in Southeastern Nigeria, remains underrepresented in speech synthesis research. We present the first documented end-to-end text-to-speech study for Efik, introducing a curated single speaker corpus of 2,632 utterances totaling three hours and a comparative evaluation of four neural models (VITS, MMS-TTS, SpeechT5, and Orpheus-TTS) under low resource conditions. Native speakers evaluated the systems using MOS, Nat-MOS, and A-MOS. MMS-TTS achieved the highest MOS of 3.80 +/- 0.63 and produced more stable long form speech, though tonal errors persisted. Other models showed greater tonal and prosodic inconsistencies. These results provide a reproducible baseline and highlight the need for larger corpora and tone aware modeling for tonal African languages.

### [68] [Failures and Successes to Learn a Core Conceptual Distinction from the Statistics of Language](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04523)
*Zhimin Hu,Jeroen van Paridon,Gary Lupyan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether the distinction between principled (causal/essential) and merely statistical properties can be learned from language, using language models as a probe.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Humans distinguish between properties that follow from category membership (e.g., tigers are striped) and properties that are only statistically true (e.g., cars have radios). Some theorists claim this principled-vs-statistical distinction is an innate feature of conceptual cognition and not learnable. The authors want to know whether this distinction could instead be acquired from language input alone, which would imply that linguistic experience can help bootstrap core conceptual and causal structures.

Method: They use large language models as testbeds. First, they test whether models track simple statistical prevalence (how often a property holds for category members). Then, crucially, they design evaluations that hold prevalence constant while varying whether a property is principled or merely statistical, to see if models still differentiate the two. They compare several models and generations to examine how this ability changes with scale and architecture.

Result: All evaluated language models accurately reflect statistical prevalence—i.e., they know that some properties are more common than others. However, most models fail to robustly distinguish between principled and purely statistical properties when prevalence is controlled. GPT-4 is the notable exception: it successfully captures the principled-vs-statistical distinction even when statistical prevalence is held constant.

Conclusion: The findings suggest that sufficiently advanced language models (e.g., GPT-4) can learn the distinction between principled and statistical properties directly from language input. This challenges claims that such conceptual distinctions cannot be learned and must be innate, and supports the idea that rich language exposure can bootstrap sophisticated causal and conceptual knowledge structures.

Abstract: Generic statements like "tigers are striped" and "cars have radios" communicate information that is, in general, true. However, while the first statement is true in principle, the second is true only statistically. People are exquisitely sensitive to this principled-vs-statistical distinction. It has been argued that this ability to distinguish between something being true by virtue of it being a category member versus being true because of mere statistical regularity, is a general property of people's conceptual machinery and cannot itself be learned. We investigate whether the distinction between principled and statistical properties can be learned from language itself. If so, it raises the possibility that language experience can bootstrap core conceptual distinctions and that it is possible to learn sophisticated causal models directly from language. We find that language models are all sensitive to statistical prevalence, but struggle with representing the principled-vs-statistical distinction controlling for prevalence. Until GPT-4, which succeeds.

### [69] [Language Models Represent and Transform Concepts with Shared Geometry](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04525)
*Zhimin Hu,Lanhao Niu,Sashank Varma*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how large language models represent concepts and how context changes those representations, showing that these context-driven changes have a shared, semantically organized geometric structure across different models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most work assumes that concepts in neural networks are represented as fixed points or directions in embedding space, but in real language use, concepts always appear in context, which changes their meaning. There is little formal understanding of how context systematically transforms concept representations within and across models. The authors aim to develop a geometric framework for these context effects and to test whether the induced transformation structure is shared across large language models.

Method: The authors model each concept not as a single vector but as a point-cloud manifold in activation space, and model contextual effects as vector fields that displace these point clouds. They instantiate this population-geometry framework in multiple large language model families of different scales. For many concepts and contexts, they measure how the internal representations shift, analyze the variance and semantic organization of these displacement patterns, and perform cross-model prediction, where displacement structure learned from one model is used to predict displacements in another.

Result: They find that different contexts move each concept in distinct ways, and that the variability of these displacements is systematically structured: it correlates with semantic properties like lexical concreteness and density. Moreover, both the underlying concept manifolds and the higher-order variance structure of their contextual displacements are shared across model families. Displacement structures estimated from one model significantly predict held-out displacement patterns in other models above chance levels.

Conclusion: Concept representations in large language models are better understood as context-sensitive manifolds acted on by structured vector fields, rather than as static points. Contextual transformation patterns are not idiosyncratic to a single model but reflect a common geometric organization shared across architectures and scales. This shared geometry concerns not just where concepts live in representation space, but how they are systematically transformed by context, revealing a richer, semantically organized structure than previously recognized.

Abstract: How concepts are represented in neural networks is a fundamental question in machine learning. The dominant view treats concept representations as stationary geometric objects. Yet concepts appear in context, and context transforms them. Drawing from neural population geometry, we formalize concept representations as point-cloud manifolds and contextual transformations as vector fields, and instantiate this framework in large language models. Across six model families of varying scales, we find that context moves each concept differently. The variance in these displacements is semantically organized, correlating with lexical concreteness and density. Importantly, both the concepts being transformed and this variance structure are shared across models: displacement structure transported from one model predicts held-out displacements in others significantly above chance. Together, these findings show that models share a common geometry not only in how concepts are represented, but more importantly in how context transforms them, a structure with richer organization than prior work has recognized.

### [70] [Mechanism-level routing failure in LLMs over Lean-verified algebraic structures](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04534)
*Manuel Israel Cázares,Wenlin Zhang,Haobo Ma*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper empirically studies how large language models misroute between proof-mechanism templates on a formally verified algebra corpus, revealing a systematic ceiling and cue-driven improvements that support a router-style view of LLM reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand not just whether LLMs can get mathematically correct answers, but whether they can correctly identify the underlying proof mechanisms they implicitly use, and to test the router hypothesis on rigorously grounded, formally verified Lean 4 algebra artifacts.

Method: They build a benchmark from FiberRing Lean 4 formalizations, where each compact mathematical object is linked to a verified Lean artifact and a proof-mechanism label from a fixed template set. They evaluate multiple LLMs under blind conditions and under a condition with an added Lean verdict/witness cue, comparing template (mechanism) accuracy and verdict (truth) accuracy. They additionally extend to a second corpus (POM/CollisionKernel) to test generality and cross-model behavior.

Result: Under blind conditions the best model (gpt-oss-120b) hits about 80% template accuracy and Llama 3.3 70B around 68%, with substantial improvements (10–14 percentage points) when a Lean cue is provided. The main error mode is misrouting Chinese Remainder Theorem (CRT) cases to ring-equivalence mechanisms. Llama shows a dissociation where verdict (truth) accuracy is high and unchanged across conditions, but template accuracy improves, indicating distinct capacities. A cross-corpus test reproduces similar compression effects with different labels and flips the pattern of cross-model dissociation.

Conclusion: LLMs exhibit a mechanism-level routing ceiling and systematic structural routing failures on formally verified algebra, especially conflating certain proof mechanisms (e.g., CRT vs ring equivalence). External structural cues substantially improve routing, and the decoupling between truth inference and mechanism classification across models supports a router-style account of LLM reasoning beyond natural language tasks. The work provides a formally grounded benchmark and pipeline for future investigation.

Abstract: We present an empirical study of structural routing failure in large language models (LLMs) over a formally verified algebraic corpus. The task requires selecting the correct proof-mechanism label from a fixed closed template set for compact mathematical objects drawn from the FiberRing formalization in Lean 4, where each item is anchored to a Lean-verified artifact and assigned a label from the corresponding certificate family.
  Our central finding is a mechanism-level routing ceiling: under blind conditions, gpt-oss-120b achieves 80.3% template accuracy on 22 FiberRing items (n=66; temperature=0, seed=0), while Llama 3.3 70B reaches 68.2%. Exposing a mechanism-bearing Lean verdict/witness cue (Condition A2) raises accuracy to 90.9% and 81.8% -- gaps of +10.6 and +13.6 pp termed cue-induced routing uplift.
  The dominant failure is a CRT-to-ring-equivalence misroute: gpt-oss-120b misroutes 7 of 12 CRT items (58.3%) blind, zero under A2. A cross-model dissociation in Llama is notable: verdict accuracy is identical in both conditions (95.5%), while template accuracy improves 13.6 pp -- confirming that truth inference and proof-mechanism classification are separable capacities.
  A cross-corpus extension (Set B; 6 POM/CollisionKernel items, 72 evaluations) provides a small cross-module check: CRT-granularity compression reappears with different labels, and an inverse cross-model dissociation emerges. These findings extend the router hypothesis (Cazares 2026) to formal algebraic structures. The full pipeline, manifest, and results are at https://github.com/bytepro-ai/fiber-routing-eval.

### [71] [EEG-SpikeAgent: Agentic Closed-Loop Program Synthesis for Automated EEG Spike Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04558)
*Sonali Santhosh,Kelly Shuhong Yu,Eugene Chang,Jonathan Kim,Kie Shidara,Danilo Bernardo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They build an LLM-driven, closed-loop system that automatically designs interpretable EEG signal-processing features for epileptic spike detection and show it reaches strong performance on a public dataset.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Deep-learning approaches to detecting interictal epileptiform discharges in scalp EEG are accurate but often opaque and hard to interpret clinically. Clinicians and methodologists need interpretable, auditable feature representations and decision rules for trust, validation, and review. Manual feature engineering in EEG is labor-intensive and requires substantial domain expertise. The authors aim to automate this feature engineering step while retaining interpretability and providing code-level transparency.

Method: They propose EEG-SpikeAgent, a closed-loop program-synthesis framework powered by a large language model agent. The system iteratively: (1) proposes a deterministic EEG feature module (signal-processing code) for spike detection, (2) executes the generated code on EEG data to compute tabular features, (3) trains and evaluates a tabular classifier (gradient-boosted trees) on these features, and (4) feeds structured performance diagnostics back to the LLM so it can refine or add new feature modules. Through repeated cycles, the agent develops and improves signal features and decision rules, including both spike- and artifact-aware features. Performance is evaluated on the 29-channel VEPISET dataset of 4-second EEG epochs labeled for presence or absence of epileptiform discharges, using five-fold cross-validation.

Result: On VEPISET (2,516 spike-containing vs 22,933 non-spike 4-second epochs), the agent-generated features combined with a gradient-boosted tree classifier achieved AUROC 0.935, balanced accuracy 0.699, F1 score 0.557, sensitivity 0.401, and specificity 0.996 at the default threshold. At an operating point tuned for high sensitivity (0.80), the system attained mean precision 0.470 and mean specificity 0.900. Incorporating artifact-aware feature generation improved balanced accuracy and F1 score relative to using spike-focused features alone.

Conclusion: LLM-based program synthesis can effectively automate interpretable EEG feature engineering for epileptiform spike detection. The closed-loop, agentic design allows the system to iteratively refine signal-processing features based on performance feedback, yielding competitive detection metrics while preserving transparency through inspectable, deterministic code. This suggests a practical path for clinically reviewable and methodologically auditable ML pipelines in EEG and potentially other biomedical signal domains.

Abstract: Automated detection of interictal epileptiform discharges in scalp electroencephalography (EEG) is clinically important, but recent high-performing deep-learning models often trade interpretability for accuracy. We introduce EEG-SpikeAgent, a closed-loop program-synthesis framework that uses a large language model (LLM) agentic system to generate signal-processing features for spike detection in scalp EEG. The system iteratively proposes one deterministic EEG feature module at a time, executes the resulting code on EEG to generate tabular features, evaluates performance via a tabular classifier, summarizes run-level metrics, and feeds structured diagnostics back to the model for refinement. Across iterations, EEG-SpikeAgent proposes and refines candidate signal features and decision rules informed by model performance. We evaluated EEG-SpikeAgent on VEPISET, a public 29-channel dataset of 4-second epochs containing 2,516 discharge-containing and 22,933 non-discharge epochs. Across five-fold cross-validation with a gradient-boosted tree classifier, agent-generated features achieved an area under the receiver operating characteristic curve of 0.935, balanced accuracy of 0.699, F1 score of 0.557, sensitivity of 0.401, and specificity of 0.996 at the default operating point. At an operating point with sensitivity 0.80, mean precision was 0.470 and mean specificity was 0.900. Artifact-aware feature generation improved balanced accuracy and F1 score over spike-only feature search. These results indicate that LLM-based program synthesis can automate EEG feature engineering in auditable and inspectable code-driven manner for clinical and methodological review.

### [73] [Fidelity-Diversity Metrics for Text](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04563)
*Amanda Wang,Tudor Manole,Florentina Bunea,John Thickstun*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose new metrics to evaluate the fidelity and diversity of candidate training text relative to a reference dataset, using optimal transport over discrete text summaries, and show these metrics predict downstream model performance when augmenting data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As language models improve, the quality and composition of training data become critical. Practitioners add extra text to high-quality datasets, but lack fine-grained tools to judge whether this additional data truly matches the original data’s characteristics or harms performance. Existing measures of generative models often do not clearly separate issues of fidelity (closeness to reference) from diversity (coverage of different modes).

Method: They extend precision/recall-style evaluation for generative models by defining two separate metrics: fidelity and diversity. Both are formulated as optimal transport divergence measures computed between discrete text summaries of the candidate and reference datasets. They test these metrics on M2D2 text datasets and on synthetic GSM8K-style math datasets, checking whether the metrics can distinguish different types of deficiencies in candidate data.

On GSM8K-style synthetic math data, the metrics reveal diversity deficits that correlate with lower downstream accuracy of language models fine-tuned on that synthetic data. This shows the metrics are sensitive to data issues that matter for model performance.

Conclusion: The proposed optimal-transport-based fidelity and diversity metrics provide a practical way to assess the quality of candidate training data relative to a reference set, clearly separating fidelity from diversity. They can diagnose harmful diversity deficits in synthetic augmentation data and their scores correlate with downstream model accuracy, suggesting these metrics are useful tools for guiding data curation and augmentation decisions.

Abstract: As language modeling technology matures, there is an increasing research focus on the composition and curation of datasets used to train these models. For instance, practitioners commonly seek to augment high-quality datasets with additional text to enhance the performance of models trained on that data. However, informed decisions about data augmentation require more nuanced assessments about data quality. We build on work measuring the precision and recall of generative models to develop a pair of metrics that quantify (1) fidelity, capturing how closely candidate text resembles reference data, and (2) diversity, capturing how well it covers the modes of the reference dataset. Our metrics are based on optimal transport divergence functionals between discrete text summaries. In experiments on M2D2 text datasets, we show that these metrics are able to disentangle a lack of fidelity from a lack of diversity in deficient candidate text. In further experiments, our metrics detect diversity deficits in synthetic GSM8K-style math datasets, which correlate with degradations in downstream accuracy of language models finetuned on this synthetic data.

TL;DR: The paper analyzes large-scale Reddit discussions (2018–2023) to understand how adolescents talk about substance use, focusing on timing, emotions, and topics, to inform better prevention and intervention strategies.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional methods (surveys, interviews, labs) capture adolescents’ substance use experiences incompletely and sometimes inaccurately, missing real-time, authentic expressions of their emotions, social pressures, and contexts. There is a need for scalable, naturalistic data to uncover when, how, and why adolescents discuss substance use, so that prevention and intervention efforts can be more precisely timed and targeted.

Method: The study collects large-scale Reddit posts and comments related to adolescent substance use from 2018–2023. It applies (1) fine-grained hour-by-day temporal analysis to examine when discussions occur, (2) sentiment and emotion classification to quantify emotional valence and specific emotions, and (3) transformer-based topic modeling (BERTopic) to identify and characterize recurring semantic themes in the discourse. The analysis links time patterns with emotional and topical patterns.

Result: The analysis shows clear temporal patterns, with discussions peaking on weekends and late at night. Emotion classification reveals that negative emotions, particularly sadness and fear, dominate the adolescent substance-use discourse. Topic modeling uncovers distinct clusters of discussion around peer relationships, family conflict, emotional distress, and detailed experiences with specific substances.

Conclusion: Adolescents’ online discussions about substance use reflect time-bound, emotionally negative, and socially embedded experiences that are visible at scale on Reddit. By identifying when conversations peak and which emotions and topics are most prominent, the study offers empirical evidence to guide more timely and context-sensitive prevention and intervention strategies that align with adolescents’ real-world lived experiences as displayed in social media data.

Abstract: Adolescence is a critical developmental period marked by heightened emotional sensitivity, social stress, and vulnerability to substance use. However, traditional research methods provide limited access to adolescents' authentic experiences, hindering efforts to develop evidence-based prevention and intervention strategies. Social media provides a unique opportunity to observe adolescents' naturally occurring discussions about substance use, offering valuable insights into their opinions, emotions, and lived experiences that can inform early prevention and intervention strategies. In this study, we analyze large-scale Reddit discussions related to substance use among adolescents between 2018 and 2023. Leveraging hour-by-day temporal analysis, sentiment and emotion classification, and transformer-based topic modeling (BERTopic), we examine the interaction between time, emotion, and semantic content in adolescent substance use discourse. Our findings reveal pronounced weekend and late-night peaks in substance-related discussions, a dominance of negative emotions such as sadness and fear, and distinct semantic topics centered on peer relationships, family conflict, emotional distress, and substance-specific experiences. These findings advance our understanding of adolescent substance use in naturalistic online settings and provide empirical evidence to support the development of more timely, targeted, and evidence-based prevention and intervention strategies.

### [75] [Progressive Disclosure for LLM-Maintained Wiki Knowledge Bases: a Preregistered Ablation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04576)
*Theodore O. Cochran*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether progressive disclosure (a catalog plus per-page summaries) makes LLM-agent interaction with a large wiki cheaper without hurting answer quality, finding that a retrieval-style structure maintains quality while reducing tool use and cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM agents increasingly serve as both maintainers and consumers of large knowledge bases (e.g., wikis). A widely held but untested intuition is that progressive disclosure—using a compact catalog and brief page summaries so the agent only loads what it needs—should reduce cost compared to a single large index. However, this has not been empirically validated on realistic, agent-maintained corpora, and the actual behavioral patterns of capable tool-using agents may undermine the assumed savings. The authors aim to rigorously test this assumption and understand how access structure alone affects cost and quality.

Method: The authors use a real 709-page markdown wiki maintained by an LLM and retrofit it to support progressive disclosure. They design a preregistered ablation study with four corpus “arms” that differ only in how the agent accesses content; the page bodies are byte-identical and fixed as immutable git tags to isolate the effect of access structure. They cross these arms with three access conditions: (1) a protocol-constrained agent, (2) a free self-routing agent, and (3) a catalog-preload regime. For each combination, the LLM agent answers questions against the wiki, and responses are graded blind against verified gold references by a cross-family judge model. They also perform a pilot experiment that informs the final evaluation focus and apply explicit threat-to-validity analysis to their own tooling and setup.

Result: The pilot reveals that a capable tool-using agent rarely loads the index at all; instead, it infers page paths directly from the questions and accesses pages without relying on the catalog, invalidating the original expected source of savings. In the main study, answer quality in the retrieval-style (progressive disclosure) arm is statistically non-inferior to the monolithic index baseline within a preregistered margin. At the same time, cost decreases in all access regimes, with reductions of roughly one-third for a self-routing agent and more than half under catalog-preload, with all confidence intervals excluding zero. The cost reductions stem from more targeted retrieval behavior: the retrieval arm accesses fewer pages and requires fewer tool calls, not from skipping the index load per se.

Conclusion: Progressive disclosure can preserve answer quality while substantially reducing tool-use cost, but the mechanism of savings differs from the original intuition. Rather than saving by avoiding index loads, capable agents achieve efficiency by leveraging more targeted access patterns when the corpus is structured for retrieval. The study also highlights the importance of rigorous evaluation design—such as preregistration, isolating access-structure variables, and analyzing threats to validity—when assessing LLM-agent tooling and interaction patterns.

Abstract: LLM agents increasingly answer questions against knowledge bases they help maintain. A common intuition holds that progressive disclosure, a compact catalog plus a one-line summary per page so the agent loads only what it needs, should make this cheaper than consulting a large monolithic index. We test that on a real 709-page markdown wiki maintained by an LLM. We retrofit it for progressive disclosure and run a preregistered ablation in which four versions of the corpus differ only in how the agent reaches the content: page bodies are byte-identical across arms, frozen as immutable git tags, so any measured difference is due to access structure alone. We cross the arms with three access conditions (a protocol-constrained agent, a free self-routing agent, and a catalog-preload regime) and grade answers blind against verified gold references with a cross-family judge.
  A pilot upended the premise: a capable tool-using agent never loads the index, inferring a page's path from the question and reading it directly, so the specific saving the retrofit targets does not materialize. We therefore made answer quality primary and cost secondary. Quality is non-inferior (the retrieval arm matches the index baseline within the preregistered margin) while cost falls in every regime, from about a third for a self-routing agent to well over half under catalog-preload, all confidence intervals excluding zero. The saving comes not from avoiding the index load but from more targeted access: the retrieval arm cites fewer pages and takes fewer tool turns. The study doubles as a case study in evaluation validity, applying threat-to-validity discipline to the tooling that produced it.

*Tardelli Ronan Coelho Stekel*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MTEB-PT, the first comprehensive native benchmark for Brazilian-Portuguese text embeddings, evaluates 93 models on 22 tasks, and shows that strong open models exist and multilingual rankings only partially predict Portuguese performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluation of Portuguese text embeddings relies on translated datasets (e.g., MS MARCO) or sparse multilingual coverage, which may not reflect real native-language performance. There is no unified benchmark built from genuinely Portuguese data, making it hard for practitioners to choose appropriate embedding models or understand how well multilingual models transfer to Portuguese. The authors aim to fill this gap and provide a statistically rigorous, native benchmark for Brazilian-Portuguese.

Method: They construct MTEB-PT, a benchmark of 22 Brazilian-Portuguese tasks across seven categories, explicitly including only data that is originally in Portuguese and excluding translated content. They then evaluate 93 embedding models (73 open-weight, 20 closed APIs) ranging from 23M to 27B parameters. In addition to raw scores and a leaderboard, they provide extensive statistical analysis for model comparisons: task-level bootstrap confidence intervals, paired bootstrap significance testing between models, discrimination analysis per task and instance using an Item Response Theory-style framework, and correlation analysis between rankings on MTEB-PT and a global multilingual leaderboard.

Result: The benchmark distinguishes roughly a dozen performance tiers among models, but the top six models are statistically indistinguishable in rank. At least one openly licensed, self-hostable model performs within the top tier, matching commercial APIs on Portuguese embeddings. Correlation between multilingual and Portuguese rankings is only moderate (Spearman rho = 0.75 for 55 overlapping models), with notable rank discrepancies such as a model that is 3rd on the multilingual board but 49th on MTEB-PT.

Conclusion: A dedicated native benchmark reveals structure in Portuguese embedding performance that multilingual leaderboards do not capture, including significant rank reshuffling among models. High-quality Portuguese embeddings are attainable with open, self-hostable models, reducing dependence on commercial APIs. The released tasks, code, and leaderboard enable practitioners to select Portuguese embedding models based on native evidence and statistically grounded comparisons.

Abstract: Text embeddings for Portuguese have no dedicated benchmark: evaluation rests on translated corpora such as English MS MARCO or on thin multilingual coverage, with native tasks scattered and unconsolidated. We introduce MTEB-PT, a benchmark of 22 native Brazilian-Portuguese tasks across seven categories (classification, multilabel classification, pair classification, semantic textual similarity, clustering, retrieval, and reranking), admitting only data created or found in Portuguese and excluding translations by construction. We evaluate 93 models spanning 23M to 27B parameters: 73 open-weight and 20 closed commercial APIs. Alongside the leaderboard we report a statistical layer for every headline comparison: per-task bootstrap confidence intervals, paired-bootstrap significance, a task- and instance-level discrimination analysis (how sharply each task separates models) adapted from Item Response Theory, and a cross-leaderboard correlation. Three findings stand out. The benchmark cleanly separates about a dozen tiers of models, though the top six are statistically too close to order. An openly licensed, self-hostable model reaches that leading tier, so strong Portuguese embedding quality does not require a commercial API. And a model's rank on the global multilingual leaderboard predicts its Portuguese rank only moderately (Spearman rho = 0.75 over 55 shared models; one model ranks 3rd there and 49th here), so a native benchmark measures something the multilingual boards do not. We release every task, our code, and a public leaderboard, so practitioners can choose Portuguese embedding models on native evidence.

### [77] [Wrong Before Right: Late Rescue and Interface Failure in Aligned Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04640)
*Jiaqi Deng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper uncovers that aligned language models often internally favor wrong answers in mid layers (“wrong-dip”), only correcting them in later layers, and shows this hidden behavior predicts vulnerability to compression and can be mitigated with targeted training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most evaluations of language models focus only on whether the final output is correct, ignoring how intermediate representations evolve. However, models can reach correct answers via fragile internal processes, which may be exposed under compression, architectural changes, or post-training. The authors want to understand the internal temporal dynamics of correctness in aligned LLMs, and how these dynamics relate to robustness, alignment recipes, and evaluation reliability.

Method: They use layer-wise difference-in-differences (DiD) trajectories over polarity-controlled minimal input pairs to trace how internal preferences for correct vs. incorrect answers evolve across layers. They define and measure a “wrong-dip”: a region in mid layers where the internal preference favors the incorrect answer before being corrected in later layers. They then causally test this via patchscope-style activation transplantation: swapping mid/late-layer activations between prompts and models to see how this changes outputs. They perform these analyses across 17 models from three families (Qwen2.5, Llama 3, Mistral) spanning 0.5B–32B parameters. They link the measured dip to behavior under several compression schemes (low-rank late-layer compression, block dropping, structured pruning, quantization) and to late-layer ablations. Finally, they experiment with training interventions: LoRA fine-tuning with a mid-layer wrong-margin penalty versus standard output-only supervised fine-tuning, and extend their diagnostics to natural-language I/O with controlled readouts and vignette tasks.

Result: They identify a consistent “wrong-dip” phenomenon: in 25–90% depth, many models’ internal preferences transiently commit to incorrect answers, with final correctness arising from late-layer rescue. The strength and sign of this dip depend heavily on alignment recipe and scale. In Qwen2.5, the wrong-dip emerges at 3B parameters, grows with scale, and peaks at 32B (with strong statistical significance, paired t up to 9.7). In Llama-3-8B, the effect reverses sign (t=-2.31), indicating a structurally different internal trajectory; Mistral-7B sits between these behaviors. The measured dip magnitude strongly predicts which items will fail under late-layer compression (low-rank compression, block dropping, structured pruning): high-dip items are 3–7x more likely to flip, whereas quantization-induced flips show no relationship to the dip, a double dissociation corroborated by late-layer ablation experiments. Training with a mid-layer wrong-margin penalty via LoRA substantially reduces the causal dip (by about 67–70%), maintains the same surface accuracy as output-only SFT, and increases robustness under compression. Conversely, standard output-only SFT, even when achieving perfect accuracy, exacerbates the causal wrong-dip by up to 2.8x. With more naturalistic input/output and controlled readouts, they show the phenomenon persists: structural-damage failures are significantly stratified by dip level, and free-form fragility decomposes into a dip-auditable late-rescue layer and a dip-blind interface layer.

Conclusion: Aligned language models frequently rely on a late-rescue mechanism where mid-layer representations favor wrong answers before final layers correct them. This internal trajectory is not just an interpretability curiosity: it is strongly linked to model robustness under compression, sensitivity to post-training changes, and the validity of evaluation metrics that only look at output correctness. The presence, magnitude, and even direction of the wrong-dip are recipe- and scale-dependent, revealing alignment-specific internal dynamics. Crucially, the wrong-dip is trainable: targeted mid-layer penalties can reduce it while preserving accuracy and improving robustness, whereas naive output-only fine-tuning can silently worsen internal brittleness. Therefore, trustworthy deployment and compression of LLMs require monitoring and shaping internal trajectories of correctness, not only their final outputs.

Abstract: We study how correctness is assembled inside aligned language models, not only whether the final answer is right. Using layer-wise difference-in-differences (DiD) trajectories over polarity-controlled minimal pairs, we identify the wrong-dip: in mid layers (25-90% depth), internal preference transiently commits to the incorrect answer and is rescued only by late-layer correction. We verify this causally with patchscope-style activation transplantation across 17 models, three families, and 64x scale (0.5B-32B). Four findings follow. (1) Alignment amplification of the causal wrong-dip is recipe-specific and emergent: it emerges at 3B in Qwen2.5, remains high, and peaks at 32B (paired t up to 9.7), reverses in Llama-3-8B (t=-2.31), and sits between for Mistral-7B. (2) The dip predicts real compression failures: high-dip items are 3-7x more likely to flip under late-layer low-rank compression, block dropping, or structured pruning, while quantization flips are dip-blind, a double dissociation confirmed by late-layer ablation. (3) The dip is trainable: a LoRA fine-tune with a mid-layer wrong-margin penalty matches output-only SFT accuracy while cutting the causal dip by 67-70% and improving compression robustness; output-only SFT worsens the causal dip by up to 2.8x at perfect surface accuracy. (4) With controlled readouts, the phenomenon survives natural-language I/O: dip stratification of structural-damage failures is significant on naturalistic vignettes, and free-form fragility separates into a dip-auditable late-rescue layer and a dip-blind interface layer. Together, output-level correctness can hide a late-rescue process that governs compression risk, post-training quality, and evaluation distortion.

### [78] [Retroactive Chain-of-Thought (RetroCoT): Forensic Reconstruction Prompts as a Safety Diagnostic Across Model Generations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04645)
*Samira Hajizadeh*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that current safety alignment in large language models fails when harmful intents are phrased in different pragmatic styles, and introduces a new attack method, RetroCoT, that exploits this weakness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing safety alignment approaches are mainly tested on direct, explicit harmful instructions, but in real use people may phrase harmful intent indirectly or within different communicative roles. The authors want to understand whether models are robust to semantically equivalent but pragmatically different requests, and to probe how fragile current alignment is to such reframings.

Method: The authors analyze model behavior under varying pragmatic framings of the same harmful intent. They propose Retroactive Chain-of-Thought (RetroCoT), a single-turn attack that reframes harmful requests as forensic reconstruction tasks: instead of asking for harmful steps, the prompt assumes the harmful outcome already happened and asks the model, in the role of a forensic analyst, to reason backwards about the causal chain. They test RetroCoT and baseline direct requests on AdvBench over multiple models from the GPT-4 and GPT-5 families, and also run a two-turn setting where they present the model with an example reconstruction plus an evaluator-style critique to see if continuation within that frame increases attack success rates.

Result: On AdvBench, RetroCoT attains a 58% attack success rate (ASR) on gpt-4o and 52% on gpt-4o-mini, while direct harmful requests get 0% and 4% ASR respectively, revealing strong sensitivity to framing. GPT-5-family models are initially robust to RetroCoT, refusing all such attempts and explicitly calling out the reconstruction framing. Yet, when given a second adversarial turn that includes a prior forensic-style answer and reviewer feedback, ASR jumps from 0% to 48% on GPT-5.4-mini and from 58% to 94% on GPT-4o. A control that removes the fake low score but preserves the forensic continuation still reaches 85% ASR on GPT-5.4-mini, showing that the success is driven by pragmatic continuation in the forensic frame, not score manipulation.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that current frontier language model alignment is highly dependent on pragmatic framing instead of underlying semantic intent. While some newer models explicitly cover and block certain reconstruction-style registers like RetroCoT, this protection does not generalize to small pragmatic changes or multi-turn continuations in the same frame. Consequently, new pragmatic registers can keep revealing safety gaps, implying that robust alignment must account for semantic equivalence across diverse communicative stances rather than relying on pattern-matched surface forms.

Abstract: Safety alignment in large language models is typically evaluated against direct, imperative harmful requests. We show that this alignment is highly conditioned on pragmatic register: models that refuse a direct request frequently comply when the same underlying objective is expressed through a different communicative stance. This suggests that current alignment policies are not invariant to semantic equivalence, but remain sensitive to how a request is pragmatically framed. We introduce Retroactive Chain-of-Thought (RetroCoT), a single-turn attack that reframes harmful requests as forensic reconstruction tasks. Rather than requesting harmful instructions directly, RetroCoT presupposes that the harmful outcome has already occurred and asks the model, acting as a forensic analyst, to reconstruct in reverse the causal chain that produced it. On AdvBench (n=50), RetroCoT achieves attach success rate of 58% on gpt-4o and 52% on gpt-4o-mini, compared with direct-request baselines of 0% and 4%, respectively. We further identify a pronounced generation gap: GPT-5-family models refuse RetroCoT entirely, explicitly identifying the reconstruction premise in their refusal rationales, consistent with explicit coverage of this reconstruction register. However, this robustness does not generalize across pragmatic forms. A single adversarial feedback turn presenting an existing forensic reconstruction response alongside evaluator critique raises ASR from 0% to 48% on GPT-5.4-mini and from 58% to 94% on GPT-4o; a control condition omitting the fabricated low score achieves 85% on GPT-5.4-mini, indicating that the operative element is pragmatic continuation within the established forensic frame rather than score manipulation. These results suggest that frontier-model alignment remains conditioned on pragmatic framing rather than semantic intent, and that new pragmatic registers can continue to expose a...

*Haocheng Wang,Baiyu Huang,Yingjia Wan,Xiao Zhu,Xiaoyang Liu,Yinya Huang,Zhijiang Guo*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autoformalization—translating natural-language mathematics into formal language—requires precise semantic alignment, but current evaluations only give binary or scalar judgments and do not explain what went wrong or where. This lack of interpretability hinders both researchers trying to understand failures and developers aiming to systematically improve models.

Method: The authors design SCI Error Taxonomy, a hierarchical, 28-category error scheme with a strict priority ordering to decompose autoformalization mistakes. Using this taxonomy, they build FormalRx, which offers four diagnostic functions: (1) deciding whether NL-FL pairs are semantically aligned (alignment verdict), (2) classifying the types of errors (error categorization), (3) pinpointing the error locations (error localization), and (4) proposing corrected formalizations (correction). They then train a specialized diagnostic model, FormalRx-8B, on 56,287 annotated NL-FL pairs and construct a benchmark, FormalRx-Test, with fine-grained diagnostic labels.

Result: FormalRx-8B, the instantiated diagnostic model, achieves strong performance: F1 = 0.88 for alignment verdicts, F1 = 0.71 for error categorization, and accuracies of 0.75 for localization and 0.73 for correction. It substantially outperforms both large general-purpose language models and previous specialized baselines on these diagnostic tasks.

Conclusion: By replacing opaque evaluation with structured, fine-grained diagnostics, FormalRx enables more transparent analysis of autoformalization systems and provides actionable guidance for improving them. The released taxonomy, model, and benchmark establish a foundation for systematic progress in formal mathematical reasoning through better evaluation.

Abstract: The veracious semantic alignment in autoformalization is significant for formal mathematical reasoning. However, existing evaluations provide only opaque binary verdicts or scalar scores, offering no interpretable insight into where or why translations fail. This opacity severely limits both human understanding and automated system improvement. To bridge this gap, we introduce FormalRx, a comprehensive diagnostic evaluation framework that transforms autoformalization assessment from black-box judgments into actionable feedback. At its core is SCI Error Taxonomy, a hierarchical classification scheme decomposing autoformalization errors into 28 distinct categories with strict priority ordering. Building on this taxonomy, FormalRx provides four critical diagnostic capabilities: alignment verdicts, error categorization, error localization, and correction. We instantiate the framework with a diagnostic model FormalRx-8B, trained on 56,287 NL-FL pairs with fine-grained diagnostic annotations, and release FormalRx-Test as the first fine-grained diagnostic benchmark. FormalRx-8B achieves F1-scores of 0.88 (verdict) and 0.71 (categorization), along with accuracies of 0.75 (localization) and 0.73 (correction), substantially outperforming both general-purpose LLMs and specialized baselines. By connecting evaluation with actionable insights, FormalRx enables systematic diagnosis and improvement of autoformalization systems.

### [80] [ToolFailBench: Diagnosing Tool-Use Failures in LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04686)
*Harsh Soni*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduce ToolFailBench, a benchmark to diagnose different types of tool-use failures in LLM agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing aggregate benchmark scores for tool-using LLM agents obscure *how* tool use fails. Two models can have similar final accuracy but fail for different reasons, such as never calling tools when needed or ignoring tool outputs. The authors want a fine-grained way to characterize and compare these failure modes to improve tool-use faithfulness and reliability.

Method: They build ToolFailBench, a 1,000-task diagnostic benchmark spanning finance, medicine, law, cybersecurity, and real estate. Tasks are of two kinds: (1) tool-required tasks, designed so the answer cannot be reasonably guessed without the tool, forcing reliance on tool outputs; and (2) control tasks, which expose the same tools but where the correct behavior is to answer directly without using them. For each model trace, they automatically label tool-use behaviors into four failure categories—Tool-Skip, Result-Ignore, Output-Fabrication, and Unnecessary-Tool-Use—using a rule-based classifier combined with two LLM judges, whose decisions are aggregated via majority vote. They then evaluate 19 leading models on this benchmark.

Result: The best evaluated model achieves an 86.33% Clean Tool-Use Rate, indicating that fully faithful tool use is not yet solved. Models with similar overall scores display qualitatively different failure patterns: many behave well on control tasks (avoiding unnecessary tools), while some, notably Llama-3.1 variants, exhibit an "Always-Call" tendency—using tools even when they should not. Detailed comparisons show large discrepancies in control-task performance even between similarly sized models, such as an 89 percentage-point difference between Llama-3.1-70B and Qwen2.5-72B.

Conclusion: ToolFailBench reveals that focusing solely on whether models call tools or on final-task accuracy misses critical dimensions of tool-use reliability. Evaluations should separately measure: (a) whether models correctly decide when tools are necessary, (b) whether they faithfully incorporate tool outputs, and (c) whether they refrain from using tools when unnecessary. The benchmark provides a structured way to analyze and compare these behaviors, showing that tool-use faithfulness remains an open challenge and guiding future improvements in tool-using LLM agents.

Abstract: Tool calling is central to modern language model agents, but aggregate benchmark scores often hide where tool use fails. A model that never calls a needed tool and a model that calls the tool but ignores the result can look similar under final task accuracy. We introduce ToolFailBench, a diagnostic benchmark for measuring tool-use failures across 1,000 tasks in finance, medicine, law, cybersecurity, and real estate. Tool-required tasks return values the model wouldn't guess, forcing it to trust the tool while control tasks attach the same tools but should be answered directly. We label each trace with Tool-Skip, Result-Ignore, Output-Fabrication, and Unnecessary-Tool-Use, using a rule classifier and two LLM judges aggregated by majority vote. Across 19 headline models, the best reaches 86.33% Clean Tool-Use Rate, showing that faithful tool use is not saturated. More importantly, models with similar aggregate scores fail in different ways: most stay disciplined on no-tool controls, while Llama-3.1 models show an Always-Call pattern, and at the same parameter scale Llama-3.1-70B and Qwen2.5-72B differ by 89 percentage points on control-task accuracy. Tool-use evaluation should measure not only whether agents call tools, but whether they use tool outputs correctly and avoid tools when none is needed.

### [81] [PAST-TIDE: Prototype-Anchored Statement Tuning with Topic-Invariant Normalization for Stance Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04690)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional stance detection usually adds a randomly initialized classification head on top of a pre-trained language model, which can be data-hungry and less effective in low-resource and cross-topic scenarios, especially for Arabic. The authors aim to better leverage pre-trained masked language models for stance, reduce reliance on large labeled datasets, and improve cross-topic generalization for the StanceNakba shared task.

Method: They redefine stance detection as cloze-style masked language modeling (“statement tuning”): stance labels are expressed as label words, and a verbalizer links these words to stance categories, using the model’s existing MLM head instead of a new classifier. They add prototypical contrastive learning with learnable class prototypes to obtain batch-size independent contrastive signals, and introduce topic-conditional layer normalization so that normalization parameters are conditioned on the topic to better handle cross-topic variation in Arabic stance detection.

Result: On the StanceNakba Shared Task at NakbaNLP@LREC-COLING 2026, PAST-TIDE reaches macro-F1 of 0.75 on Subtask A and 0.74 on Subtask B on the official leaderboard, showing competitive or strong performance compared with other systems under low-resource conditions.

Conclusion: Stance detection can be effectively modeled as a cloze-style MLM problem by reusing the pre-trained MLM head together with label verbalizers, avoiding heavy new classifiers. When combined with prototypical contrastive learning and topic-conditional layer normalization, this minimalist approach yields strong, competitive performance for low-resource, cross-topic Arabic stance detection tasks, suggesting that careful task reformulation and lightweight additions can substitute for more complex architectures.

Abstract: We introduce PAST-TIDE, our stance detection system addressing both subtasks of the StanceNakba Shared Task at NakbaNLP@LREC-COLING 2026. The main idea is statement tuning. We redefine stance as cloze-style masked language modeling (MLM), letting a verbalizer map label words to stance categories through the pre-trained MLM head rather than appending a randomly initialized classification head. We complement this with prototypical contrastive learning, which uses learnable class prototypes for batch-size independent contrastive training, and topic-conditional layer normalization for cross-topic Arabic stance detection. PAST-TIDE achieves macro-F1 scores of 0.75 for Subtask A and 0.74 for Subtask B on the official leaderboard, indicating that minimal architectural additions to a pre-trained model can remain competitive in low-resource settings.
