### [82] [What You See Is What You Get: Observation-Aligned Supervision for Chart-to-Code Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04726)
*Tianhao Niu,Qingfu Zhu,Wanxiang Che*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper argues that standard supervised training for chart-to-code models is flawed because it forces models to predict non-identifiable raw data from images; instead, they propose an Observation-Aligned supervision scheme that rewrites targets to only include quantities actually observable from the chart, yielding better performance on chart-to-code benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current chart-to-code generation models are trained to reproduce full plotting scripts, including underlying raw data, as if these were uniquely determined by the rendered chart image. However, many chart types do not visually encode the original samples, only aggregated statistics (e.g., boxplot summaries, pie chart proportions, histogram bin masses). This mismatch between supervision and what can be inferred from the image leads to hallucinated and over-specified code. The authors are motivated to create a supervision paradigm that aligns training targets with the truly identifiable information in charts, improving both faithfulness and evaluation.

Method: They propose Observation-Aligned supervision, a target-rewriting framework for chart-to-code training data. Instead of using raw data arrays and non-identifiable variables found in conventional plotting scripts, they transform the ground-truth code so that all supervised quantities are constrained by what the chart image reveals. Concretely, for boxplots they replace raw samples with box statistics (e.g., quartiles, whiskers); for pie charts they use wedge percentages rather than arbitrary raw values; and for histograms they supervise bin weights (mass per bin) instead of individual observations. They apply this rewriting pipeline to datasets from two chart-to-code sources, generating a new set of supervision targets consistent with visual observables. They then fine-tune multiple vision-language models on these Observation-Aligned targets and evaluate them on chart benchmarks.

Result: Across several VLM architectures and two benchmarks, ChartMimic and ChartX, models trained with Observation-Aligned supervision show consistent gains in recovering observable quantities from charts. This includes improvements under a strict "both-executable" evaluation setting, where both predicted and reference code must run successfully for comparison. Metrics particularly improve on the recovery of visual statistics (boxplot stats, pie proportions, histogram bin masses), indicating reduced hallucination of non-identifiable raw data. The performance gains are robust across datasets and model backbones, suggesting the benefit comes from the supervision design rather than from model or data scale alone.

Conclusion: The study concludes that a key limitation in current chart-to-code systems is not only data volume or model architecture, but a misalignment between supervision targets and what can be inferred from images. By constraining training targets to observation-identifiable quantities, Observation-Aligned supervision yields more faithful, execution-ready chart code and better observable value recovery. The authors argue that future work in chart-to-code should explicitly respect identifiability from the visualization, and that evaluation protocols should focus on observable statistics rather than reconstructing arbitrary, unobservable raw data.

Abstract: Chart-to-code generation is commonly trained with supervised fine-tuning on reference plotting scripts, implicitly treating the gold code as a fully observable target. We argue that this assumption is often invalid: many chart programs contain latent raw variables that cannot be uniquely recovered from the rendered image. For example, a boxplot exposes summary statistics rather than original samples, a pie chart reveals proportions rather than arbitrary raw values, and a histogram shows bin-level mass rather than individual observations. Supervising models to reproduce such non-identifiable quantities encourages hallucination and over-specified code generation. We introduce Observation-Aligned supervision, a rewriting framework that replaces latent raw-data targets with quantities constrained by the visual observation: box statistics for boxplots, wedge percentages for pie charts, and bin weights for histograms. Applying this framework to chart-to-code training data from two sources, we obtain the Observation-Aligned supervision target data. Experiments across multiple VLMs on ChartMimic and ChartX demonstrate consistent improvements in observable value recovery, including under both-executable evaluation. Our results suggest that improving chart-to-code models requires not only more data or advanced learning objectives or algorithms, but also supervision targets that respect what is identifiable from the chart image.

</details>


### [83] [Turning Off-Policy Tokens On-Policy: A Plug-in Approach for Improving LLM Alignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04728)
*Yu Li,Xiuyu Li,Mingyang Yi,Jiaxing Wang,zhangliangxu,Zhaolong Xing,Zhen Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Selective Importance Sampling (SIS), a method to reduce variance in off-policy reinforcement learning post-training for large language models by selectively treating some tokens as on-policy through a rejection test.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In RL post-training for LLMs, the common rollout-then-update paradigm leads to off-policy data, requiring importance sampling for correction. However, standard token-level importance sampling over long sequences causes compounding ratios and severe variance, harming stability and performance. The authors aim to mitigate this variance while still correcting for off-policy data.

Method: The authors introduce Selective Importance Sampling (SIS), which interprets the off-policy model as a proposal distribution and applies a token-level rejection sampling test. Tokens that pass the test are treated as if they were generated on-policy and assigned an importance weight of one; tokens that fail use the standard importance sampling ratio. SIS therefore partially converts off-policy data into effective on-policy data at the token level, modifying only the importance ratios in the policy loss. They also provide theoretical analysis showing SIS reduces the discrepancy between token-level and sequence-level off-policy gradient estimators.

Result: SIS can be plugged into various RL post-training algorithms with negligible wall-clock overhead. Experiments on both dense and Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) LLMs, evaluated on math and agent benchmarks, show consistent improvement in all performance objectives and enhanced robustness when training with off-policy data compared to standard importance sampling approaches.

Conclusion: Selective Importance Sampling provides a simple, general, and computationally cheap way to stabilize and improve off-policy RL post-training for LLMs. By selectively treating some tokens as on-policy via a rejection test, SIS reduces variance and better aligns token-level updates with sequence-level objectives, leading to improved performance and robustness across models and tasks.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning (RL) post-training for large language models (LLMs) follows a efficient paradigm of "rollout then update", which inevitably results in off-policy training data. To resolve this, Importance sampling (IS) is proposed, while the token-level ratios compound over long sequences, causing severe variance exploded. A natural idea is "transferring" these off-policy token into on-policy token, so that the importance scores for correction are unnecessary. Following this idea, we propose Selective Importance Sampling (SIS), which is inspired by rejection sampling. Concretely, SIS implements by viewing off-policy model as proposal distribution, and implement a token-level rejection test: accepted tokens are viewed as on-policy, so that receive unit importance score, while rejected tokens retain the standard IS correction. Our proposed SIS is theoretically proved reducing the gap between token-level and sequence-level off-policy gradient estimators. The SIS acts as a plug-in that only modifies the importance ratio in the policy loss, adding negligible wall-clock overhead, and can be combine with a vast vary of RL post-training algorithms. Experiments on dense and MoE LLMs across math and agent benchmarks show that SIS consistently improves all objectives, while providing substantially stronger robustness under off-policy data.

</details>


### [84] [LP-SFT: Local-Preserving Supervised Fine-Tuning via Multimodal Entropy Structure](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04733)
*Yueyang Wang,Baolong Bi,Shuo Lu,Jingyuan Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper identifies that standard supervised fine-tuning of language models harms existing abilities by distorting the pretrained probability distribution, and proposes LP-SFT, an objective that preserves local distributional structure around plausible tokens while still learning from supervised labels.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard cross-entropy supervised fine-tuning improves target-domain performance but often degrades pre-existing capabilities because it only encourages the labeled token and does not control how probability mass is moved among other plausible alternatives. The authors notice that pretrained LMs have a rich, structured distribution over multiple plausible next tokens, which standard SFT can destroy, leading to capability loss and reduced diversity. They want a method that adapts models to new tasks while preserving this intrinsic structure.

Method: They first analyze pretrained language models’ next-token distributions using Shannon and Renyi entropies and find a regular multimodal entropy structure, where peaks correspond to positions with many plausible next tokens. Based on this, they introduce LP-SFT (Local-Preserving Supervised Fine-Tuning). At each training step, LP-SFT builds an adaptive support set of alternative tokens around the supervised token and adds a preservation loss that locally normalizes and tries to keep the base model’s relative probabilities among those alternatives. In parallel, standard cross-entropy is used to optimize the supervised token, so the model both learns the label and preserves the base distribution’s local structure.

Result: In experiments on both mixed-domain and single-domain fine-tuning scenarios, LP-SFT outperforms vanilla SFT and other recent SFT-enhancement baselines. It yields higher overall performance metrics, specifically achieving a better trade-off between pass@1 accuracy (top-1 correctness) and pass@k performance (quality/diversity across multiple samples).

Conclusion: The paper concludes that preserving local distributional structure during supervised fine-tuning mitigates degradation of existing capabilities and avoids collapsing the diversity accessible through sampling. LP-SFT demonstrates that it is possible to adapt models to new domains while maintaining much of the pretrained model’s rich probabilistic knowledge, suggesting a promising direction for safer, less destructive fine-tuning objectives.

Abstract: Supervised fine-tuning (SFT) is the standard approach for adapting pretrained language models to downstream domains, yet it often improves target-domain behavior at the cost of degrading pre-existing capabilities. Standard cross-entropy fine-tuning promotes only the observed label token and leaves unconstrained how probability mass is redistributed over other plausible alternatives, potentially distorting the rich local preference structure learned during pretraining. We first analyze next-token predictions using Shannon and Renyi entropies, revealing that pretrained models exhibit a regular multimodal entropy structure. These entropy peaks correspond to varying numbers of plausible alternatives, indicating that the base model intrinsically encodes rich distributional knowledge beyond the single supervised token. Motivated by this observation, we propose LP-SFT, a Local-Preserving Supervised Fine-Tuning objective designed to explicitly protect this inherent entropy structure. At each step, LP-SFT constructs an adaptive support of alternative tokens and applies a locally normalized preservation loss to maintain the base model's relative structure among them, while standard cross-entropy independently optimizes the supervised token. Across mixed-domain and single-domain fine-tuning experiments, LP-SFT improves overall performance over vanilla SFT and recent SFT-enhancement baselines, achieving the best balance between pass@1 accuracy and pass@k performance. These results suggest that local preservation helps mitigate capability degradation without collapsing sampling-accessible diversity.

</details>


### [85] [Evaluating the Effect of Linguistic Relatedness on Cross-Lingual Transfer in Large Multilingual Automatic Speech Recognition](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04814)
*Andrei Florian,Cynthia Jayne Amol,Hope Kerubo Ombaba,Xiaoyu Cui,Boniface Mwau,Biatus Maina Kamau,Lilian Diana Awuor Wanzare,Christiane Fellbaum,Happy Buzaaba*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether using linguistically related auxiliary languages helps improve large multilingual ASR for low‑resource African languages and finds it generally does not.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Low-resource African languages lack sufficient labeled speech data for training accurate ASR systems. Prior work with small models suggested that adapting models first on a linguistically related auxiliary language and then on the target language can improve performance. However, it is unknown whether this approach still works for modern large multilingual ASR models, which may behave differently in transfer and adaptation. The authors aim to rigorously test if linguistic relatedness is a useful predictor of transfer gains in this large-model setting, since this would inform practical strategies for scaling ASR to many under-resourced languages.

Method: The authors design a systematic controlled experimental setup using two Africa-centric speech corpora and four different large multilingual ASR models. They define six experimental factors that govern how models are adapted, including whether they are first adapted ("pre-adapted") on an auxiliary language that is linguistically related to the target low-resource language, and then fine-tuned on minimal target-language data. They compare transfer performance between cases with related vs. presumably less-related or no auxiliary language, measuring whether linguistic relatedness consistently leads to better ASR performance on the target languages.

Result: Across all tested models, corpora, and factor combinations, pre-adaptation on linguistically related auxiliary languages does not yield practically meaningful improvements in ASR performance when only minimal target-language data is available. Any gains from using related auxiliaries are small or inconsistent and do not constitute a reliable advantage over baselines without such pre-adaptation.

Conclusion: For large multilingual ASR models, linguistic relatedness by itself is not a reliable predictor of cross-lingual transfer gains for low-resource African languages. Pre-adapting on related auxiliary languages does not appear to be an effective general strategy for extending large ASR systems to new low-resource languages under minimal-data conditions. Future work should explore alternative factors or strategies beyond simple linguistic relatedness to improve transfer in this setting.

Abstract: Extending automatic speech recognition (ASR) to low-resource African languages is constrained by the prohibitive demands of data collection at scale. A promising direction is to leverage linguistic relatedness to enhance cross-lingual transfer from a related auxiliary language to the low-resource target by sequentially adapting on both. Although this strategy has shown meaningful improvements in small ASR models, its effectiveness in large ASR remains unclear. We extend this framework to large multilingual ASR through a systematic controlled experimental design spanning six factors, two Africa-centric corpora, and four large ASR models, isolating whether linguistic relatedness reliably predicts cross-lingual transfer gains in this setting. Across all conditions, pre-adaptation on related auxiliary languages yields no practically meaningful transfer improvements given minimal target-language data, suggesting that linguistic relatedness alone may not reliably predict cross-lingual transfer gains in large multilingual ASR, or constitute an effective strategy for extending such models to low-resource languages.

The methodology is built to be flexible and transferable to other lyric datasets.

Result: Applying this framework to the Sanremo corpus shows a gradual increase in semantic similarity among finalist songs over the 75-year period, indicating that lyrics have become more semantically uniform. This mirrors the trend previously observed in English-language popular music, suggesting that the decline in semantic variety is not limited to one language or platform but appears in this Italian cultural context as well.

Conclusion: The study concludes that Sanremo Festival lyrics have undergone a long-term shift toward greater semantic uniformity, consistent with international findings on popular music. It demonstrates that modern NLP tools—especially embeddings and LLM-based analyses—can effectively reveal long-term changes in musical language and cultural expression. The proposed methodology is positioned as a general framework that can be applied to other corpora to study similar cultural and linguistic trends.

Abstract: In recent years, studies have revealed a decline in semantic variety across popular music lyrics, particularly in English-language songs on streaming platforms like Spotify. This research examines whether a similar trend can be observed in a different linguistic and cultural context: the lyrics of all finalist songs from the 75 editions of the Sanremo Music Festival, Italy's most renowned music competition. What sets this work apart is the development of a flexible and efficient methodology for tracking changes in semantic similarity over time, which can be applied to different datasets to study similar phenomena. Drawing on a combination of full-text, segment-based, topic-based, and word-level analyses, the approach leverages both embedding techniques and large language models. When applied to the Sanremo corpus, this framework reveals a gradual move toward increasing semantic uniformity, echoing the global patterns identified in previous studies. These findings underscore the value of natural language processing tools in uncovering long-term shifts in musical language and cultural expression.

Abstract: Addressing hate and violence in society requires timely detection of hateful events from public reporting, but automated identification of hateful events remains underexplored. We introduce the task of hateful event detection and investigate the ability of AI systems, specifically large language models (LLMs), to discover and classify reports of antisemitic events with fine-grained labels. We evaluate OpenAI's GPT-4o and Meta's Llama-3.2-3B-Instruct on multiple expert-annotated datasets containing antisemitic event descriptions from news articles, civil society reports, and official records. We show that LLMs, particularly GPT-4o, have potential for this task, but substantial improvement is needed. Providing clear term definitions and in-context examples in prompts can improve performance: definitions are most helpful for rhetoric-oriented events (e.g. classical antisemitic tropes), while examples help label action-oriented events (e.g. physical assault). A case study of college newspapers demonstrates that LLMs can help surface relevant real-world events, supporting early monitoring and intervention. Overall, our findings highlight both opportunities and critical gaps in AI's ability to recognize complex harms and underscore the need for collaborative efforts among AI developers, policymakers, and civil society to design models, implement robust evaluation, and develop policy frameworks for defining and combating hate efficiently and effectively.

Applying this pipeline on large podcast collections produces the DuplexChat corpus.

Result: Using DuplexChat-Pipe, they generate a large-scale, speaker-separated dialogue corpus: 282,634 hours of English and 132,723 hours of Japanese speech. An analysis of the resulting data shows that the corpus reflects natural human turn-taking dynamics.

Conclusion: The work delivers both an open-source corpus (DuplexChat) and a reproducible pipeline (DuplexChat-Pipe) that transform monaural podcast audio into two-channel, speaker-separated dialogues. The resulting dataset is large, multilingual (at least English and Japanese), and preserves realistic conversational patterns, making it suitable for training full-duplex spoken dialogue models.

We present DuplexChat, an open-source corpus for full-duplex spoken dialogue models, and DuplexChat-Pipe, a pipeline for constructing speaker-separated full-duplex dialogue speech from public podcast feeds. DuplexChat-Pipe filters language-specific podcast feeds, retrieves and cleans episode audio, extracts diarization-guided two-speaker dialogue clips, and applies speech separation and restoration to produce one channel per speaker. Running this pipeline yields a speaker-separated spoken dialogue corpus covering 282,634 hours of English and 132,723 hours of Japanese. Analysis results on DuplexChat show that it contains turn-taking dynamics present in human dialogues.

  <summary>Details</summary>

While LLMs can be connected to external knowledge at inference time, it is unclear which forms of conceptual grounding actually help them recognize and reason about antisemitism. Method: The authors test four state-of-the-art LLMs on two expert-annotated datasets for antisemitism detection. Simply adding more conceptual material does not help much. The remaining limitations—especially around subtle, historically inflected, or justificatory antisemitism—show that current LLMs, even when conceptually grounded, are not yet reliable stand-alone tools for nuanced antisemitism detection and require careful design and human oversight.

Abstract: LLMs enable the integration of external conceptual resources at inference time, creating new opportunities for detecting ideologically and historically complex phenomena such as antisemitism.   Using two expert-annotated datasets, we compare definitional, fine-grained taxonomic, example-augmented, and large-context representations of antisemitism.
  We find that fine-grained taxonomic representations substantially improve recall, while simultaneously reducing precision. Surprisingly, supplying substantially larger conceptual resources yields no additional quantitative benefit. Post-Holocaust antisemitism poses the most persistent challenge across models and configurations. Analysis of explanations further reveals systematic limitations including overproduction of conceptual references, reliance on lexical cues, overconfidence, and difficulties with subtle or justificatory forms of antisemitism.
  Our findings highlight both the potential and the remaining limitations of conceptually grounded LLMs for antisemitism detection and reasoning.

### [92] [The syntax of wh-agreement in Yemeni Ibbi Arabic](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04986)
*Ashraf Naji,Mohammed Q. Shormani*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes wh-agreement in Yemeni Ibbi Arabic, arguing that it is a robust, morphologized instance of Agree-across-phases governed by Feature Inheritance and Matching, with implications for Universal Grammar and cross-linguistic wh-agreement.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To explain how wh-agreement operates in Yemeni Ibbi Arabic (YIA), a relatively under-studied dialect, and to situate its behavior within broader generative syntax, especially how long-distance dependencies and agreement across phases are implemented. The authors aim to show that YIA offers crucial evidence for the theory of Agree, Feature Inheritance, and Universal Grammar, and to compare its wh-agreement system to better-studied languages like Greek, Indonesian, Lubukusu, Irish, etc.

Method: The authors conduct a generative-syntactic analysis of YIA wh-questions, focusing on agreement patterns between Wh-operators and functional heads (C, T, v) as reflected in verbal complexes. They propose an Agree Across Phases (AAP) model grounded in Feature Inheritance, where Agree as MATCHING is distinct from feature valuation: Matching is implemented at C/v, while valuation occurs at T/V. They examine how specific suffixes (-eh, -uh, -nen, -um), derived via grammaticalization from Standard Arabic third person pronouns, mark wh-agreement and use these patterns to argue for their theoretical architecture.

Result: They identify a series of suffixes (-eh, -uh, -nen, -um) in YIA that function as dedicated morphological markers of wh-agreement, having grammaticalized from third person pronouns. They show that wh-agreement in YIA involves multiple heads (C, T/V, v) and can span phase boundaries, consistent with an Agree Across Phases analysis where matching and valuation are distributed across heads via Feature Inheritance. Empirically, YIA displays a more morphosyntactically transparent and robust wh-agreement system than languages like Greek, Indonesian, Palauan, and Irish.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that wh-agreement in YIA is best captured by an Agree Across Phases account with Feature Inheritance, where Agree as Matching and feature valuation are distinct operations. YIA’s rich morphological wh-agreement provides strong support for this approach and contributes to the cross-linguistic understanding of wh-agreement and long-distance dependencies. They argue that these data support the universality of Universal Grammar by illustrating how specific I-language operations in an under-studied variety instantiate general, invariant principles of human language architecture, and that YIA offers particularly strong evidence for AAP compared to other wh-agreement languages.

Abstract: This article tackles an important phenomenon in the syntax of Yemeni Ibbi Arabic (YIA), viz., wh-agreement, a phenomenon common to several languages including Greek, Indonesian, Lubukusu, Irish, etc. In YIA, wh-agreement manifests itself via agreement inflections on the Wh-Op, C, T/V, v. To account for this phenomenon, we propose an Agree across phases (AAP) approach anchored in the mechanism of Feature Inheritance (FI) in which Agree as MATCHING (AM) is a bit separated from feature valuation (FV). AM concerns Cs/vs, but FV Ts/Vs. Analyzing the agreement patterns observed between Wh-Op(erators), functional heads (precisely C, (T), v), and verbal complexes, we argue that the suffixes -eh, -uh, -nen, -um, having undergone grammaticalization process from Stannard Arabic (SA) third person pronouns, function as morphological marking of wh-agreement. Findings indicate that YIA data offer a unique empirical contribution to generative syntax, specifically concerning wh-agreement in this dialect operating via MATCHING mechanism. Our proposal straightforwardly accounts for wh-agreement cross-linguistically. This study provides further evidence that incorporating under-investigated typology provides further support for the universality of Universal Grammar (UG) by revealing how specific I-language operations reflect deeper, invariant principles of human language architecture. It concludes that the wh-agreement mechanism in YIA is more morphosyntactically robust than in languages such as Greek, Indonesian, Palauan, and Irish, providing compelling evidence for AAP as a UG approach to long-distance dependencies.

</details>

### [94] [Knowledge Knows, Verbalization Tells: Disentangling Latent Directions for Mathematical Solvability in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05013)
*Nikolaos Xiros,Maria-Eleni Zoumpoulidi,Georgios Paraskevopoulos*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how large language models internally represent two things separately: whether a math problem is actually solvable, and whether the model will *say* it is solvable. It finds that these are distinct signals in hidden states and that hallucinations come mainly from the verbalization side, which can be steered to reduce fabrications.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often hallucinate solutions or claim solvability even when problems are unsolvable. Prior work examined internal representations of solvability beliefs, but verbalization—how the model chooses to state solvability—has mainly been examined from outputs, not as an internal representation. This limits fine‑grained understanding and control. The authors want to disentangle internal knowledge about solvability from the model’s tendency to verbally claim solvability, so we can better diagnose and reduce hallucinations and improve abstention.

Method: The authors probe LLM hidden states with linear classifiers to separately decode: (1) whether the model internally ‘knows’ a math problem is solvable or not, and (2) whether it is about to *say* (verbalize) that it is solvable or provide a solution. They apply this to multiple LLMs, analyzing representations across layers and positions. They then manipulate these internal directions (activation steering) to test causality—specifically, steering along the verbalization direction and the knowledge direction to see how each affects fabrication and abstention. They also use prompting with explicit unsolvability cues to see how it shifts these internal representations and behavior.

Result: They find two key, linearly decodable directions in hidden space: one corresponding to solvability knowledge and another to verbalization about solvability, and these directions are distinct. Fabrication (hallucinated solutions or unjustified claims of solvability) is mostly tied to shifts in the verbalization representation, not to changes in underlying solvability knowledge. When prompted with unsolvability cues, the verbalization representation shifts toward more abstention / admission of unsolvability, while knowledge remains comparatively stable. Activation steering along the verbalization direction effectively reduces fabrication and increases abstention without substantially degrading underlying reasoning ability.

Conclusion: Solvability knowledge and verbalization are separable internal representations in LLMs’ hidden states. Hallucinations in mathematical reasoning arise mainly from how the model chooses to verbalize, rather than from absence or corruption of solvability knowledge. Because these representations are linearly decodable and steerable, we can mechanistically intervene—via prompting or activation steering—to reduce fabrications and improve abstention. This opens a path toward more reliable LLMs by explicitly controlling verbalization behavior rather than trying only to improve underlying reasoning.

Abstract: Although LLMs have made significant progress in mathematical reasoning, determining whether a mathematical problem is solvable remains a fundamental yet challenging capability. While recent studies have probed internal representations of model solvability beliefs, verbalization has primarily been studied behaviorally rather than as an internal representation, limiting its analysis and manipulation. We address this gap by separately probing representations of solvability knowledge and verbalization, allowing us to disentangle the two within model hidden states. Across multiple LLMs, we show that knowledge and verbalization are encoded as distinct, linearly decodable representations and that fabrication is primarily associated with changes in verbalization rather than the underlying knowledge. Prompting with unsolvability cues reduces fabrication primarily by shifting verbalization, while activation steering demonstrates that these representations can be echanistically manipulated to improve model abstention.

*Manuela Del Castillo Suero,Arnault-Quentin Vermillet,Nicole Sonne Heckmann,Darmendra Ramcharran,Maurizio Sessa*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents MOSAIC, an agentic LLM-based framework to generate and apply disease severity phenotypes from EHR data, using type 2 diabetes as a test case, and shows it aligns moderately with existing scores while capturing broader clinical dimensions and predicting outcomes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Disease severity is complex and multidimensional, and existing rule-based phenotyping methods using EHR data struggle to capture this complexity. With the emergence of agentic LLM systems capable of reasoning over structured and unstructured data, there is an opportunity to use them to better model disease severity, but they have not been evaluated for this specific task. The authors are motivated to test whether an agentic LLM framework can construct and apply richer, clinically meaningful severity phenotypes and how these compare with conventional algorithms and hard outcomes.

Method: The authors develop MOSAIC, a two-phase agentic LLM framework for severity phenotyping, demonstrated in type 2 diabetes. They apply MOSAIC to a synthetic EHR dataset (SyntheticMass) using both open-weight (locally deployable) and proprietary LLM pipelines on cohorts of size 4,886 and 200, respectively. They compare MOSAIC-derived severity tiers against three established algorithmic severity scores (DCSI, DiSSCo, Cooper) using kappa agreement statistics, and they evaluate prognostic validity by relating MOSAIC tiers to all-cause mortality and incident diabetic complications via survival analysis (log-rank tests and hazard ratios). They also compare the performance of the agentic classification to a deterministic, rule-executing version of the same rubric (MOSAIC Frozen) to test whether the agentic system adds reasoning beyond fixed rules.

Result: MOSAIC produced severity phenotypes that encompassed clinical domains missing from the comparator scores, such as glycaemic staging using biomarkers, beta-cell function, and social determinants of health. The open-weight MOSAIC pipeline showed high concordance with the proprietary pipeline (weighted kappa 0.773). Compared with existing scores, MOSAIC reached moderate agreement with Cooper (kappa 0.597) and DCSI (0.534) and fair agreement with DiSSCo (0.320). The agent-based severity tiers significantly stratified all-cause mortality (log-rank p < 0.001, with crude hazard ratios of 1.6–2.4 for non-baseline tiers) but showed non-monotonic risk separation at the highest tiers. For incident complications, the tiers showed a significant inverse gradient (log-rank p < 0.001), interpreted as depletion of susceptibles. The agentic approach diverged substantially from deterministic execution of the same rubric (kappa 0.428 vs MOSAIC Frozen), suggesting that the LLM agents perform additional reasoning beyond a fixed ruleset.

Conclusion: MOSAIC demonstrates that agentic LLM systems can be used to construct and apply nuanced, clinically meaningful severity phenotypes from structured EHR data for type 2 diabetes. These phenotypes are broader than traditional rule-based scores and show prognostic relevance for mortality and complications. The moderate agreement with existing scores and the divergence from deterministic rule execution indicate that LLM-based reasoning can contribute additional signal beyond fixed algorithms. The framework appears effective on synthetic data and supports further research into extending this agentic LLM approach to other diseases with similarly complex, multidimensional severity profiles.

Abstract: Background: Disease severity is a multidimensional construct difficult to capture with rule-based approaches in Electronic Healthcare Records (EHR). Agentic large language model (LLM) systems could synthesise clinical evidence and reason over EHRs, but remain unevaluated for this task. Methods: MOSAIC is a two-phase agentic LLM framework for severity phenotyping, using type 2 diabetes (T2D) as a proof-of-concept. MOSAIC was evaluated on a synthetic cohort (SyntheticMass; open-weight N = 4,886; closed-weight N = 200) against three algorithmic ground truths (DCSI, DiSSCo, Cooper) and against all-cause mortality and incident complications. Open-weight (locally deployable) and proprietary pipelines were also compared. Results: The generated framework spanned domains absent from the comparators, including biomarker-based glycaemic staging, beta-cell function, and social determinants of health. Open-weight MOSAIC matched the proprietary pipeline (closed- vs open-weight weighted kappa = 0.773) and reached moderate agreement with Cooper (kappa = 0.597) and DCSI (kappa = 0.534) and fair agreement with DiSSCo (kappa = 0.320). Agent-based (Type 1) tiers showed significant separation of all-cause mortality (log-rank p < 0.001; crude hazard ratios 1.6-2.4 for non-Baseline tiers), with non-monotonic separation at the upper tiers, and an inverse gradient for incident complications (log-rank p < 0.001) consistent with depletion of susceptibles. Agentic classification also diverged from deterministic execution of the same rubric (MOSAIC Frozen; kappa = 0.428), indicating reasoning beyond fixed rules. Conclusion: MOSAIC shows agentic LLM systems can generate and apply clinically meaningful severity phenotypes from structured EHR data in T2D. ### [96] [Beyond Independent Labels: Schwartz-Geometry Decoding for Human Value Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05052)
*Víctor Yeste,Paolo Rosso*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper explores making human value detection models respect the circular structure of Schwartz values by encoding that structure as geometry in the label space and using it during decoding.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing value detection models treat the 19 Schwartz values as independent labels, ignoring the theoretical circular continuum where neighboring values are compatible and opposite ones are in tension. This mismatch can yield label sets that are inconsistent with Schwartz theory, reducing interpretability and theoretical faithfulness. The authors want to see if explicitly encoding the continuum as geometry in the output space can improve coherence of predictions without hurting standard classification performance.

Method: They use a DeBERTa-v3-base multi-label classifier for value detection and inject the Schwartz continuum structure in two ways: (1) training-time geometry-aware losses that bias the model to respect distances in the continuum; and (2) a post-hoc Schwartz-aware energy-based decoder that scores and selects entire label sets jointly according to their alignment with the continuum. They also compare against random orderings and an empirical co-occurrence graph, and evaluate over five random seeds. New theory-aware coherence metrics are introduced to measure how well predicted label sets align with the continuum. Additionally, they run a bounded Qwen2.5-72B-Instruct experiment where the continuum is supplied at inference to test whether prompting alone can reproduce structured prediction benefits.

Result: Geometry-aware training yields only small improvements, and these are not specific to the true Schwartz circular ordering, as similar gains appear with random orderings. In contrast, the post-hoc Schwartz-aware decoder produces label sets that are more coherent with the theoretical continuum, according to the new coherence metrics, while keeping Macro-F1 and Micro-F1 unchanged (by design of the selection rule). The improvement is specific to the true Schwartz ordering and does not arise when using a random permutation or an empirical co-occurrence graph through the same decoder. The Qwen2.5-72B-Instruct diagnostic shows that simply providing the continuum at inference changes its behavior but still falls short of supervised structured prediction performance.

Conclusion: Explicitly encoding the Schwartz value continuum as an output-space geometry is most effective when done via a post-hoc, theory-aware decoder rather than during training. This decoder provides a lightweight, controllable way to enforce coherence with the theoretical label structure—improving theory-aligned coherence without sacrificing standard F1 metrics—and the effect depends specifically on the true Schwartz ordering. Prompting a large language model with the continuum alone cannot fully replace supervised, structure-aware decoding. Theory-aware decoding is thus a practical method to make human value detection more faithful to its underlying label space structure.

Abstract: Human value detection is commonly formulated as sentence-level multi-label classification over the 19 refined Schwartz values, typically predicted as independent labels. Schwartz theory, however, describes them as a circular motivational continuum, in which adjacent values are compatible and opposing values are in tension. We ask whether this structure can be operationalized as an explicit output-space geometry and used as a soft bias rather than a hard constraint. On a DeBERTa-v3-base classifier, we compare two ways of injecting it: training-time geometry-aware objectives and a post-hoc Schwartz-aware energy decoder that scores whole label sets jointly. Across five seeds, training-time geometry gives only limited gains-no larger for the true continuum than for a random ordering-whereas the decoder makes label sets more coherent with the continuum-on theory-aware coherence metrics we introduce-at no cost to Macro-F1 or Micro-F1 (held fixed by its selection rule). The gain is specific to the true Schwartz ordering: it does not appear for a random permutation or an empirical co-occurrence graph through the identical decoder. A bounded Qwen2.5-72B-Instruct diagnostic shows that supplying the continuum at inference shifts behavior but does not match supervised structured prediction. Theory-aware decoding thus offers a lightweight, controllable way to make value detection faithful to its label space.

### [97] [MIRAGE: Defending Long-Form RAG Against Misinformation Pollution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05069)
*Saadeldine Eletter,Ruihong Zeng,Yuxia Wang,Maxim Panov,Aleksandr Rubashevskii,Preslav Nakov*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: A defense method, MIRAGE, makes Retrieval-Augmented Generation robust to polluted evidence by filtering and gating claims using NLI-based cross-document consistency, restoring factuality even when many retrieved passages contain subtle misinformation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RAG systems depend on external documents that are assumed to be trustworthy, but real-world retrieval often brings in passages that, while topically relevant, contain misinformation or misleading framings. This "pollution" can seriously degrade the factuality of long-form answers, and existing RAG methods are not robust to it. There is a need for a simple, training-free, model-agnostic way to make RAG robust when retrieval results are partially or heavily corrupted. Additionally, there is a lack of standardized evaluation protocols that systematically test robustness under different types and degrees of retrieval pollution.

Method: The paper introduces MIRAGE, a training-free and model-agnostic defense mechanism for long-form RAG. MIRAGE constructs a cross-document claim graph using Natural Language Inference (NLI) to identify support, contradiction, and neutrality relationships between claims extracted from retrieved documents. It then applies a Defended-Claims Gate that selects only claims that are consistently supported across multiple sources, forming a defended subset of evidence. Generation is conditioned only on this defended subset; if the gate cannot find sufficiently supported claims, MIRAGE blocks the use of retrieved documents and instead falls back to parametric answering by the base LLM alone. In parallel, the authors propose a minimal-edit pollution protocol that creates controlled datasets by applying four families of perturbations (Unambiguous, Conflicting, Misleading, Fabricated) to generate clean, mixed, and fully polluted retrieval corpora for evaluation.

Result: Experiments on four long-form QA benchmarks with both commercial and open-weight LLMs show that retrieval pollution significantly harms standard RAG, causing notable drops in factuality. When MIRAGE is applied, factuality is consistently restored or substantially improved under both mixed and fully polluted evidence conditions. MIRAGE also outperforms prior robust-RAG baselines across these benchmarks. The results indicate that NLI-based cross-document consistency checking and the Defended-Claims Gate effectively filter out harmful evidence and prevent the model from being misled by polluted retrievals.

Conclusion: MIRAGE provides an effective, training-free, and model-agnostic defense for long-form RAG systems operating in noisy, partially corrupted retrieval environments. By leveraging NLI-based cross-document claim graphs and a Defended-Claims Gate, it can either safely condition on multi-source supported evidence or revert to parametric knowledge when retrieval is unreliable. The proposed minimal-edit pollution protocol further offers a principled way to evaluate robustness under varied types and levels of evidence corruption. Overall, MIRAGE significantly improves factual robustness over vanilla RAG and prior robust-RAG methods, making it a practical defense for real-world deployments where retrieval results cannot be fully trusted.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) improves factuality by grounding LLMs in external evidence, but real-world retrieval is often polluted: semantically relevant passages may contain subtle misinformation, misleading framings, or fabrications. We introduce MIRAGE, a training-free, model-agnostic defense for long-form RAG. MIRAGE builds an NLI-based cross-document claim graph and applies a Defended-Claims Gate to either condition generation on a consistent, multi-source supported subset or to block retrieval and answer parametrically. We also release a minimal-edit pollution protocol spanning four perturbation families (Unambiguous, Conflicting, Misleading, Fabricated) to construct matched clean, mixed, and fully polluted evaluation regimes. Across four long-form QA benchmarks and multiple commercial and open-weight LLMs, pollution severely degrades vanilla RAG, while MIRAGE consistently restores factuality under mixed and fully polluted evidence and outperforms prior robust-RAG methods. Our implementation and datasets are available at https://github.com/SaadElDine/MIRAGE.

</details>


### [98] [Rating the Pitch, Not the Product: User Evaluations of LLMs Reflect Expectations More Than Performance](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05113)
*Robert Morabito,Tyler McDonald,Charitra Viswanath,Angel Hsing-Chi Hwang,Susanne Gaube,Jad Kabbara,Ali Emami*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that how an LLM is marketed to users (oversold vs. accurately sold vs. undersold) strongly shapes user impressions and interaction styles, even though it does not change actual task performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand whether human evaluations of LLMs—such as those behind public leaderboards and user preference data—actually reflect model capability, or are instead heavily influenced by the framing and expectations set before users interact with the system.

Method: A controlled experiment with 162 participants using one of six LLMs from two model families on three collaborative tasks. Before interaction, participants saw a landing page that either accurately described, overstated, or understated the model’s true capabilities. The study measured task performance, user prompting behavior, and changes in user impressions (usefulness, intelligence, etc.) of the model before and after interaction, and ran regressions to relate impression changes to task performance, expectation match/mismatch, and user confidence.

Result: Pre-interaction framing (oversold vs. accurate vs. undersold) significantly changed users’ ratings of the model and their prompting style but did not affect actual task outcomes. Oversold users rated the same model higher and used more directive prompts; undersold users wrote longer, more collaborative prompts. Objective quality of user–model outputs depended only on the real capability of the underlying model. Changes in user impressions were not predicted by task performance (β≈0, non-significant) but were strongly predicted by whether expectations were met (β≈0.47–0.50, p<.001) and by self-reported confidence working with the model (β≈0.36–0.47, p<.001).

Conclusion: User evaluations of LLMs, including preference data used for rankings and fine-tuning, are strongly driven by expectation management and pre-use framing rather than by actual performance. People effectively “rate the pitch, not the product”: what they are told about the model shapes their impressions and interaction behavior, while true capability only determines task quality. This calls into question the validity of naive user-elicited evaluations and leaderboards as pure measures of model quality and highlights the importance of controlling or correcting for expectation effects in LLM evaluation and deployment.

Abstract: Imagine two users interact with the same LLM. One has been told it is the cutting-edge flagship model; the other, an older, weaker model. They walk away with markedly different ratings of its usefulness and intelligence, yet they used the same model. In a controlled study, 162 participants each used one of six LLMs from two families across three collaborative tasks, after first viewing a landing page that matched, overstated, or understated their model's true capability. This pre-interaction framing shifted user opinions and interaction behavior while task performance did not. Oversold users rated the model more favorably and used more directive prompting, while Undersold users wrote longer, more collaborative prompts. The quality of what users and the model produced together depended only on the model's true capability, not on what users were told. Participants' change in model impressions after use, measured across two impression measures, was not predicted by task performance ($β= -0.01$ and $0.11$, both n.s.), but by whether the model met users' expectations ($β= 0.47$ and $0.50$, both $p < .001$) and how confident they felt working with it ($β= 0.47$ and $0.36$, both $p < .001$). After interaction, users are still rating the pitch, not the product: user-elicited LLM evaluations, including the preference data driving public leaderboards, measure expectation management at least as much as the model itself.

### [99] [EdgeBench: Unveiling Scaling Laws of Learning from Real-World Environments](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05155)
*Deyao Zhu,Xin Zhou,Shengling Qin,Xuekai Zhu,Hangliang Ding,Shu Zhong,Zixin Wen,Zhonglin Xie,Chenhui Gou,Linxuan Ren,Yueyang Wang,Junfeng Zhong,Rui Liu,Tian Gao,Yangguang Lin,Jingyuan Zhang,Maojia Song,Xuan Qi,Jinhong Wu,Chenyang Zhang,Yinzhu Piao,Ziru Niu,Hongbin Lin,Lingxiang Meng,Peng Tang,Chengyao Tang,Shanyu Wu,Huanyu Zheng,Yu Liu,Liya Zhu,He Wang,Ming Ding,Ziyu Wan,Hao Liu,Sibo Wang,Haotian Zhu,Xintian Zhang,Nan Chai,Yipeng Liu,Panhao Lai,Sihang Yuan,Zixin Su,Ge Zhang,Wangchunshu Zhou,Yantao Du,Wenhao Huang,Guang Shi*

TL;DR: The paper presents the first evidence that real-world post-deployment agent learning follows a precise log-sigmoid scaling law and that learning speed roughly doubles every three months, based on EdgeBench, a new benchmark of 134 ultra-long-horizon real-world tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While pretraining scaling laws for models are well studied, how agents continue to learn from real-world environments after deployment is poorly understood. The authors aim to fill this gap by characterizing how performance scales with interaction time and by creating a rigorous benchmark to study real-world environment learning.

Method: They collect about 38,000 hours of interaction data from agents operating in 134 real-world, ultra-long-horizon tasks across domains such as scientific discovery, software engineering, optimization, professional knowledge work, formal mathematics, and interactive games. They then empirically fit performance curves over environment learning time, discovering a log-sigmoid scaling law with very high goodness-of-fit (R^2=0.998), and compare learning dynamics across model generations to estimate improvements in learning speed.

Result: Performance during real-world environment learning follows a highly regular log-sigmoid scaling law with R^2=0.998, suggesting predictable improvement as agents interact longer with environments. Additionally, across model generations, the agents’ environment learning speed roughly doubles every three months. The authors also build and release EdgeBench, a benchmark of 134 real-world long-horizon tasks, with 51 tasks and the full evaluation framework publicly available.

Conclusion: Real-world environment learning by agents is not chaotic but follows a precise log-sigmoid scaling law, enabling predictable extrapolation of performance with additional interaction time. Agent learning speed is improving very quickly across model generations, approximately doubling every three months. EdgeBench provides a standardized, rich, long-horizon benchmark to support future research on how agents learn from and adapt to real-world experience.

Abstract: Pretraining scaling laws reveal that model capability improves predictably with data and compute. But learning from real world environments after deployment remains far less understood. Analyzing roughly 38,000 hours of agent interaction with the environment across 134 real world tasks, we find, to the best of our knowledge, the first evidence that overall performance during environment learning follows a log-sigmoid scaling law with remarkably high precision, reaching R^2 = 0.998. Across model generations, we also find that agent learning speed roughly doubles every three months. This discovery stems from EdgeBench, a suite of 134 real world tasks with ultra-long horizons, spanning scientific discovery, software engineering, combinatorial optimization, professional knowledge work, formal mathematics, and interactive games. Each task sustains at least 12 hours of continuous agent operation under rich, multilevel feedback, and is built through substantial expert effort. We publicly release 51 tasks and our full evaluation framework to accelerate the study of how agents learn from real world experience.

### [100] [RABBiT: Rapidly adaptive BOLD foundation model via brain-tuning for accurate zero-shot and few-shot prediction of speech-elicited responses in the brain](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05171)

Method: The authors develop RABBiT, an audio-to-fMRI encoder that maps speech to voxel-level BOLD responses. Its architecture—combining region-specific attention, shared-plus-subject-specific decomposition, and a brain-tuned speech backbone—yields both strong predictive performance and interpretable representations, enabling large-scale, population-level studies of language processing in the human brain. We introduce RABBiT (Rapidly Adaptive BOLD foundation model via BraIn-Tuning), a compact audio-to-fMRI encoder designed for accurate zero- and few-shot prediction. A comprehensive evaluation on 324 participants across multiple unseen fMRI datasets shows that RABBiT enables accurate zero-shot prediction of fMRI responses to natural speech across auditory and language-selective regions, surpassing the SOTA foundation model for fMRI and predictions based on group averages. With as little as 10 minutes of participant-specific data, RABBiT further improves performance via parameter-efficient tuning, substantially outperforming per-participant linear models. RABBiT's performance is driven by two key innovations: (1) learned region-specific attention, and (2) a decomposition of brain responses into shared and subject-specific components, combined with a brain-tuned speech backbone. In addition to supporting strong predictive accuracy, the structured, region-specific representations that RABBiT learns enable interpretability. By eliminating the need for extensive per-participant data and model fitting, RABBiT enables scalable population-level analyses of language in the human brain. ### [101] [Unified Audio Intelligence Without Regressing on Text Intelligence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05196)
*Zhifeng Kong,Sang-gil Lee,Jaehyeon Kim,Boxin Wang,Zihan Liu,Sungwon Kim,Yang Chen,Arushi Goel,Rajarshi Roy,Wenliang Dai,Zhuolin Yang,Yangyi Chen,Dongfu Jiang,Sreyan Ghosh,Tuomas Rintamaki,Andrew Tao,Jonathan Raiman,Mohammad Shoeybi,Bryan Catanzaro,Wei Ping*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Audex is a 30B unified audio-text large language model that extends a strong text-only MoE LLM to handle audio understanding and generation, achieving state-of-the-art results while keeping its original reasoning and alignment strengths.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing audio and speech models are often separated from strong text LLMs or use complex multi-component architectures, making it hard to get unified audio-text capabilities and to reuse standard LLM infrastructure. The authors aim to build a single, strong audio-text LLM that can understand and generate both audio and text while preserving the reasoning and alignment strengths of a top-tier text model.

Method: They start from Nemotron-Cascade-2-30B-A3B, a strong text-only MoE LLM, and extend it into an audio-text model using a single Transformer decoder. Audio inputs are encoded and projected into the same embedding space as text tokens, and during generation, both text tokens and quantized audio tokens are handled uniformly. They curate large-scale audio-text datasets (157.4B audio tokens and 320.5B text tokens) and perform multi-stage supervised training, then apply text-only Cascade RL and multi-domain on-policy distillation.

Result: Audex achieves state-of-the-art performance across multiple audio and speech tasks: audio understanding, speech recognition and translation, text-to-speech, audio generation, and speech-to-speech generation. At the same time, it largely retains the reasoning, alignment, knowledge, long-context handling, and agentic abilities of the original text-only LLM, with minimal or no degradation.

Conclusion: A simple, unified decoder-only architecture with shared token space for text and audio can yield a powerful multimodal audio-text LLM. With careful data curation and training (including RL and distillation), such a model can reach state-of-the-art audio capabilities while preserving the strengths of its text-only backbone. The released checkpoints are intended to support further open research in unified audio-text intelligence.

Abstract: Audio intelligence involves understanding, reasoning about, and generating both audio and speech. In this work, we introduce Nemotron-Labs-Audex-30B-A3B (Audex), a unified audio-text LLM built on Nemotron-Cascade-2-30B-A3B, a strong text-only MoE LLM. Audex adopts a simple unified design with a single Transformer decoder: audio inputs are encoded and projected into the text embedding space, while text tokens and quantized audio output tokens are treated uniformly during generation. This architecture enables strong audio-text fusion, seamless multimodal generation, and compatibility with standard LLM training and inference infrastructure. For training, we meticulously curate audio-text datasets comprising 157.4B audio tokens and 320.5B text tokens. We apply multi-stage supervised training on these datasets, followed by text-only Cascade RL and multi-domain on-policy distillation. Audex delivers state-of-the-art audio understanding, speech recognition and translation, text-to-speech, audio generation, and speech-to-speech generation, while preserving very compelling reasoning, alignment, knowledge, long-context, and agentic capabilities of its text-only LLM backbone with marginal or no regression. We release the model checkpoints to facilitate open research.

</details>

*Qu Yang,Cakra Wardhana,Tim Ng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes an iterative pseudo-labeling approach to improve automatic speech recognition (ASR) for code-switching speech using large unlabeled data and achieves notable error rate reductions on a benchmark dataset.

  <summary>Details</summary>

This iterative loop progressively enhances label quality and model capability for complex CS utterances.

Result: On the SEAME code-switching corpus, their method achieves substantial reductions in Mix Error Rate (MER) compared with baselines: 6.35% MER reduction on the devman subset and 8.29% reduction on the devsge subset. These improvements indicate that the iterative pseudo-labeling and two-stage bilingual training effectively exploit unlabeled data and improve recognition in code-switching scenarios.

Conclusion: The study concludes that iterative pseudo-labeling combined with two-stage bilingual model training is an effective semi-supervised strategy for code-switching ASR. By repeatedly refining pseudo-labels and retraining, the framework successfully leverages large amounts of unlabeled speech to overcome labeled data scarcity and achieve significant performance gains on a standard CS benchmark, suggesting a promising direction for future CS-ASR research and practical systems.

Abstract: Code-switching (CS), alternating languages within the same utterance, poses significant challenges for automatic speech recognition (ASR) due to limited CS training data. This paper applies an iterative pseudo-labeling training approach to CS-ASR for the first time, demonstrating its effectiveness in leveraging unlabeled data to improve CS-ASR performance. The approach comprises three phases: pseudo-label generation, two-stage bilingual model training, and iterative improvements. It begins by generating pseudo-labels from a large unlabeled corpus, creating a semi-supervised dataset. This dataset supports a two-stage training framework where the model is pre-trained and then fine-tuned on supervised CS data. Iterative refinements further enhance the model's accuracy in handling complex CS scenarios. Our approach significantly advances CS-ASR systems, achieving notable Mix Error Rate (MER) reductions on SEAME's devman (6.35%) and devsge (8.29%) subsets.

### [103] [Streaming Neural Speech Codecs through Time-Invariant Representations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05250)
*Kélian Estève,Salima Mhdaffar,Mickael Rouvier,Richard Dufour,Yannick Estève*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Neural speech codecs are widely used as intermediate representations in modern speech generation systems, but frame-level modeling remains expensive and current factorized representations (like TiCodec’s TIRE) are not fully understood. The authors aim to clarify what information these time-invariant representations actually contain, how training choices affect their robustness, and whether such factorized codecs are suitable for low-latency, streaming speech processing.

Method: The authors perform probing tasks on different encoder layers of TiCodec’s TIRE module to measure how much speaker, environment, and linguistic information each layer contains. They test various segment selection strategies for training TIRE, including cross-file sampling, to see how they influence invariance and robustness. Building on these insights, they design Dual-TIRE, a multi-level architecture that jointly uses complementary intermediate encoder layers, and they evaluate both standard and streaming (660 ms block-wise) inference setups for reconstruction quality and speaker similarity.

Result: Intermediate encoder layers are found to encode complementary speaker- and environment-related information while containing little linguistic content. Cross-file sampling during TIRE training yields more robust time-invariant representations. Dual-TIRE, which fuses multiple encoder layers, improves speech reconstruction quality and speaker similarity compared to the original TiCodec design. In streaming inference with 660 ms blocks, TiCodec maintains reconstruction quality with no significant performance drop.

Dual-TIRE leverages complementary encoder layers to improve reconstruction and speaker similarity, and TiCodec can operate in a streaming, low-latency regime without notable degradation. Factorized neural codec representations are therefore promising building blocks for future low-latency speech generation systems.

Abstract: Neural speech codecs are increasingly used as intermediate representations in codec-based speech generation systems. TiCodec introduces a factorized representation that separates time-varying speech content from time-invariant information through a Time-Invariant Representation Extraction (TIRE) module, potentially reducing the amount of information that must be modeled at the frame-level.
  In this work, we investigate the nature of the information captured by TIRE representations and their suitability for low-latency speech processing. Using a series of probing tasks, we analyze the influence of the encoder layer and show that intermediate layers capture complementary speaker- and environment-related information while containing little linguistic content. We further study several segment selection strategies for TIRE training and demonstrate that cross-file sampling improves the robustness of invariant representations. Based on these findings, we propose Dual-TIRE, a multi-level architecture that exploits the complementarity of different encoder layers and improves speech reconstruction quality and speaker similarity.
  Finally, we evaluate TiCodec in a streaming inference setting using successive 660ms processing blocks. Results show that streaming operation can be achieved without significant degradation in reconstruction performance, highlighting the potential of factorized neural codec representations for future low-latency speech generation systems.

### [105] [How Much is Left? LLMs Linearly Encode Their Remaining Output Length](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05316)
*Mohamed Amine Merzouk,Dmitri Carpov,Mirko Bronzi,Damiano Fornasiere,Adam Oberman*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies whether large language models internally estimate how long their remaining response will be and finds evidence that such an approximate length plan is linearly decodable from hidden states.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs generate text token-by-token, their outputs exhibit stable and predictable structural patterns in length (e.g., similar lengths for step-by-step solutions or retrieval answers). This suggests they might internally track or plan how long their responses will be, but this has not been directly tested. Understanding whether and how LLMs represent planned output length informs our understanding of their internal representations and planning-like behavior, and relates to theoretical results about what transformers can and cannot compute exactly (such as exact counting).

Method: The authors train minimal-capacity linear probes on frozen hidden states of three open-weight 7–8B language models across seven completion-style datasets. They attempt to linearly decode total response length from the model’s internal representations. Specifically: (1) they test predicting total response length from just the last hidden state of the prompt, before any tokens are generated; (2) they evaluate how well probe directions trained on natural-language datasets transfer to new domains, including synthetic controlled completions, and compare with a statistical baseline; (3) they analyze curated high-loss completions where the model retracts and restarts a partial solution, examining how the probe’s per-position length estimate shifts over the course of generation.

Result: (1) Total response length is reliably and approximately linearly decodable from the final hidden state of the prompt alone, indicating that the model’s internal representation before generation already contains information about planned total length. (2) Probes trained on natural-language datasets generalize to other tasks and even unseen synthetic completions, outperforming a simple statistical baseline; however, probes trained on synthetic data do not transfer as well back to natural-language tasks. This asymmetry suggests that the natural-language-trained probes capture a more general length representation used by the model. (3) On examples where the model retracts and restarts its solution, the probe’s estimate of remaining length increases sharply at the retraction point, a pattern that cannot be captured by position-only or naive predictors and suggests dynamic adjustment of a length estimate during generation.

Conclusion: The findings indicate that large language models maintain an approximate, linearly decodable internal estimate of response length, both before generation starts and as it unfolds. This behaves like a plan-like representation of how much output remains, though the work does not claim it is causally used by the model, only that it is decodable from hidden states. This notion of approximate length estimation is compatible with theoretical results showing that transformers cannot perform exact counting, and it contributes to understanding LLMs as systems that form high-level plans for their outputs rather than purely local token-by-token decisions.

Abstract: Large language models generate one token at a time, yet their responses show remarkably consistent length structure: step-by-step solutions converge in predictable token counts, retrievals stop after a few sentences, retractions extend responses by measurable amounts. We ask whether the model carries an internal estimate of how much response remains. Training minimal-capacity linear probes on frozen hidden states of three open-weight 7-8B models across seven completion-style datasets, we find three converging pieces of evidence. First, total response length is linearly decodable from the prompt's last hidden state alone, before any output is emitted. Second, probe directions trained on natural-language datasets transfer broadly, including to controlled synthetic completions never seen in training, outperforming a statistical baseline; the converse direction generally fails, and this asymmetry is itself informative. Third, on curated high-loss completions, the probe's per-position estimate shifts upward at the moment the model retracts and restarts a partial solution, a directional behavior no position-only predictor can reproduce (qualitative, not aggregate). We frame this as approximate estimation of remaining generation length, distinct from exact-counting impossibility results for transformers, and interpret it as evidence that LLMs maintain a plan-like internal representation of output length (decodable, not necessarily used causally).

</details>


### [106] [Faithfulness to Refusal: A Causal Audit of Neuron Selectors](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05355)
*Ananth Eswar,Pratinav Seth,Utsav Avaiya,Vinay Kumar Sankarapu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether attribution methods for language models truly identify neurons that are causally important, using neuron-row zeroing as a direct causal audit.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Attribution scores are widely used to select important neurons for pruning, interpretability, and safety editing, but their causal validity is often assumed rather than tested. The authors want to directly test if high-attribution neurons are actually dispensable or sufficient to change model behavior.

Method: They design two paired audits based on one-shot neuron-row zeroing. First, they test selectors at the pure language modeling level by removing neuron rows and observing performance, comparing attribution-based selectors to baselines like activations and magnitudes across five LLMs. Second, they build a behavior-level test: using a contrastive harmful-versus-benign signal, they identify rows and zero them to induce refusal on harmful prompts while examining side effects like benign over-refusal and fluency. They also compare to layer-matched random controls and study rank stability of selectors and overlap across methods.

Result: Attribution-based selectors outperform simple activation and magnitude baselines in finding neuron rows that can be removed with minimal impact on language modeling performance. In the behavior audit, neuron rows chosen via attribution are enough to install strong refusals for hate and crime prompts while maintaining low benign over-refusal and preserving fluency, whereas random rows at the same layers fail. However, some selectors that appear highly rank-stable turn out to be among the least causally valid. Different attribution methods rely on largely disjoint neuron-row sets, implying redundancy in the underlying subspace.

Conclusion: Causal audits based on direct interventions reveal limitations in commonly used proxies like rank stability for evaluating neuron selectors. Attribution methods can find neuron subspaces that are sufficient to implement safety behaviors, but these subspaces are redundant and non-unique, and superficially reliable ranking properties may hide causal invalidity. Therefore, direct causal evaluation is necessary when using attribution-based selectors for pruning, safety editing, and interpretability.

Abstract: Attribution scores increasingly identify which neuron rows of a language model matter for applications such as pruning, interpretability, and editing for safety, yet whether they identify causally important rows is rarely tested directly. We address this with two paired audits built on one-shot neuron-row zeroing. We first audit selectors at the language-modeling level: attribution methods substantially outperform activation and magnitude-based baselines at identifying dispensable rows across five LLMs. We then adapt the same intervention into a behavior test by driving it with a contrastive harmful-versus-benign signal; the attributed rows are sufficient to install refusal on hate and crime while keeping benign over-refusal low and preserving language model fluency, and specific in that layer-matched random controls at the same depths fail. Highly rank-stable selectors can be among the least causally valid. Refusal moreover lives in a redundant subspace, where different attribution methods install it through largely disjoint row sets, so the recovered edit is one realization of a sufficient set rather than a unique mechanism. Together, these findings show that rank-stability proxies miss the kinds of selector failures a direct causal audit can surface.%
