### [107] [REDDIT: Correcting Model-Generated Timestamp Drift in ASR without Forgetting via Replay-Based Distribution Editing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05364)
*Cheng-Kang Chou,Ming-To Chuang,Ke-Han Lu,Chan-Jan Hsu,Hung-yi Lee*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper identifies and fixes a timestamp drift problem in autoregressive ASR models over long non-speech segments, introducing REDDIT, a lightweight post-training method that corrects timestamps without harming general ASR performance or needing human-labeled data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autoregressive ASR models that output timestamps as tokens can avoid separate alignment models, but their timestamps drift during long silences or non-speech gaps, causing the decoded time axis to become misaligned with the audio while transcripts still appear correct. Naive fine-tuning to fix timestamps improves alignment but badly damages the model’s other behaviors (catastrophic forgetting). The authors are motivated to develop a way to correct timestamp behavior that (1) works specifically on long-gap non-speech drift, (2) avoids catastrophic forgetting, and (3) does not require costly human transcripts or timestamp labels.

Method: The authors systematically study non-speech-induced timestamp drift using custom gap and long-gap benchmarks over 15 ASR and audio-language models that produce timestamp tokens. They then propose REDDIT (REplay-based Distribution eDITing), a two-stage, lightweight post-training scheme. Stage 1: edit timestamp targets while replaying the model’s own decoder context, constraining training so that the predicted distribution on non-timestamp tokens remains close to the frozen base model’s distribution, updating only a small subset of parameters. Stage 2: a short edited-prefix refinement that further adjusts timestamp behavior at the beginning of edited regions. For supervision, they generate synthetic training data by concatenating VAD-trimmed speech spans with controlled non-speech gaps and known offsets, avoiding any use of human transcripts or timestamp annotations.

Result: On Whisper-tiny, with 34.9 hours of targeted correction audio and updating only 1.6% of parameters, REDDIT dramatically improves timestamp alignment: long-gap mean Intersection-over-Union (mIoU) increases from 38.7% to 95.0%, and mixed-gap out-of-domain Average Absolute Shift (AAS) drops from 2752 ms to 223 ms. Crucially, this is achieved while maintaining Common Voice English MER at 41.3%, whereas ordinary supervised fine-tuning of the decoder leads to severe degradation (MER 524.2%), indicating strong resistance to catastrophic forgetting.

Conclusion: Non-speech-induced timestamp drift is a widespread problem in timestamp-producing ASR and audio-language models, especially over long gaps. Directly fine-tuning models to fix timestamps causes catastrophic forgetting and harms general ASR performance. The proposed REDDIT framework effectively corrects timestamp behavior using synthetic supervision derived from VAD-trimmed speech and inserted gaps, while modifying only a small fraction of parameters and preserving overall recognition quality. This shows that replay-based distribution editing can be a practical and scalable solution for timestamp correction without human-labeled data.

Abstract: Modern autoregressive ASR systems can emit timestamps as decoded tokens, enabling timestamped transcription without frame-level aligners or inference-time post-processing. We show that these generated timestamps can drift across long non-speech spans: the transcript may remain plausible, but the decoded time axis drifts away from the audio. We study this non-speech-induced timestamp drift with self-built gap and long-gap benchmarks across 15 evaluated timestamp-producing ASR and audio-language systems. Naive timestamp-corrected fine-tuning improves alignment but can severely degrade non-target ASR behavior, exposing a forgetting problem. We propose REDDIT(REplay-based Distribution eDITing), a lightweight two-stage post-training framework that corrects timestamps while avoiding this catastrophic forgetting: it first edits timestamp targets under the model's own replayed decoder context while matching the frozen base distribution on non-timestamp tokens, then applies a short edited-prefix refinement stage. In this framework, we construct correction supervision without human transcripts or human timestamp annotations by combining VAD-trimmed speech spans with inserted non-speech gaps and known concatenation offsets. On Whisper-tiny, 34.9 hours of targeted correction audio used and only 1.6% of model parameters updated, raising long-gap mIoU from 38.7% to 95.0% and reducing mixed-gap out-of-domain AAS from 2752 ms to 223 ms while preserving CV-en MER at 41.3% (versus 524.2% for ordinary SFT decoder tuning).

</details>

However, standard speech and text benchmarks do not capture whether these systems behave naturally in conversations, where timing, turn-taking, prosody, interpersonal stance, language and dialect consistency, and relationship-aware appropriateness jointly shape perceived quality. We introduce SPEARBench, a benchmark for evaluating naturalness in speech-to-speech language models from question-answer interactions. SPEARBench constructs controlled dialogue prompts from the Seamless Interaction corpus, runs inference across multiple models, and evaluates generated answers using a multidimensional protocol that covers response latency, interruptions, speech quality, ASR robustness, language and dialect consistency, emotional naturalness, interpersonal stance, and explainable distributional baselines. ### [109] [iFLYTEK-Embodied-Omni Technical Report](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02542)

Specific quantitative metrics are not given in the abstract.

Abstract: General-purpose embodied agents must understand multimodal instructions, anticipate how their environment will evolve, and produce precise control actions over extended horizons. Existing approaches typically specialize in visual-language reasoning, video-based world modeling, or action generation, while cascaded pipelines that first synthesize future observations and then infer actions can introduce interface bottlenecks and compound prediction errors. We present iFLYTEK-Embodied-Omni, a unified multimodal foundation model that jointly models vision(videos and images), language, and action within a single Omni framework. Its modality-specific visual-language, video-generation, and action-generation components communicate through shared multimodal self-attention. This design establishes brain-cerebellum collaboration: the vision-language modeland video generation model form a high-level brain for instruction understanding, task planning, progress tracking, and future visual-state prediction, whereas the action generation modelserves as a low-level cerebellum that directly converts planned subgoals and shared multimodal context into executable action chunks. To develop these capabilities, we combine action-annotated and action-free embodied videos from human demonstrations and robot interactions with embodied reasoning, embodied perception, and general-purpose image-text data to construct a comprehensive dataset. We further adopt a four-stage strategy that progressively trains the VLM, VGM, and AGM before jointly fine-tuning the complete model.

### [110] [Internal Pluralism and the Limits of Pairwise Comparisons](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02672)
*Bailey Flanigan,Michelle Si*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that standard local pairwise comparison methods can misrepresent people’s true preferences over automated decision rules when individuals have multiple, sometimes conflicting, high‑level principles, and it proposes a pluralistic preference model plus indecision-allowing elicitation that is more efficient and faithful.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Many systems learn what people want by asking for pairwise comparisons between local outcomes or decisions. This assumes (1) that local, case-by-case judgments are enough to reconstruct how a person wants a decision rule to behave globally, and (2) that people can always give a decisive answer. These assumptions are questionable when people care about several abstract principles simultaneously (e.g., proportionality, egalitarianism, equal treatment). The authors want to understand formally how such internal pluralism affects preference learning and how current elicitation practices may fail or distort what individuals truly value.

Method: The authors construct a formal model of ‘pluralistic preferences’ over decision rules, where a single individual’s evaluation is governed by multiple distinct, authoritative priorities. Using this model, they analyze when and how these priorities can or cannot be represented via local pairwise comparisons. They identify conditions under which global principles make local data insufficient and model the behavioral impact of forcing decisions in the presence of internal conflicts. They then analyze an alternative elicitation protocol that allows people to express indecision, studying (theoretically and likely via simulations) how this affects the query complexity of learning true preferences. Finally, they outline how their framework can inform new, more direct elicitation methods for high-level priorities.

Result: The analysis reveals two main failure modes of standard forced local pairwise comparisons. First, some key priorities are inherently global: what is preferred in one instance depends on what happens in other instances, so local comparisons cannot faithfully capture these values. Second, when multiple strong priorities conflict, forcing decisive comparisons induces internal conflict and can lead to systematically distorted responses. The authors show that allowing indecision in the elicitation process can substantially reduce the number of queries required to recover the underlying pluralistic preferences accurately. Their model also clarifies how particular global principles (like proportionality and egalitarianism) manifest in observable judgments.

Conclusion: Local, forced pairwise comparisons are an unreliable basis for learning people’s preferences over automated decision rules in the presence of internal pluralism. Some important normative priorities are global and thus cannot be inferred purely from local judgments, and forced decisiveness can create distortions when priorities conflict. Allowing indecision during elicitation and moving toward methods that directly elicit abstract priorities can reduce query complexity and produce more faithful, interpretable representations of what people value. The authors advocate designing preference-learning and participatory alignment methods around explicit, pluralistic models of human values rather than relying solely on local comparisons.

Abstract: Local pairwise comparisons are a standard tool for learning how people want decision rules to work, e.g., in participatory design or alignment. However, their use builds in two strong assumptions: that local comparisons are sufficient evidence about how a person wants an automated decision rule to behave, and that people can always answer those comparisons decisively. We investigate how these assumptions may be compromised under internal pluralism: the idea that an individual evaluates decision rules according to multiple authoritative priorities about how the rule should behave. We provide a formal model of such pluralistic preferences over decision rules, which then lets us identify two distinct failures of forced local pairwise comparison data. First, priorities such as proportionality, egalitarianism, and equal treatment are inherently global: what they imply in one case can depend on what happens elsewhere, so local comparisons may fail to capture them. Second, even when priorities are representable locally, tension between strongly-held priorities can generate internal conflict, producing potentially costly behavioral distortions when comparisons are forced. We then use our model to investigate the alternative -- allowing people to report indecision -- and our findings suggest that doing so can considerably reduce the number of queries needed to learn preferences accurately. We conclude by describing how our model points toward preference-learning methods that elicit these priorities directly, yielding more faithful and interpretable accounts of what people value.

</details>


### [111] [ASK in the Dark: Uncertainty-Gated LLM Assistance under Partial Observability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02686)
*Juarez Monteiro,Nathan Gavenski,Guilherme Lima,Francisco Galuppo,Odinaldo Rodrigues,Adriano Veloso*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that small language models can meaningfully guide reinforcement learning agents in partially observable environments if given trajectory-aware context and structured reasoning, and if their advice is selectively gated by action uncertainty.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In partially observable environments, RL agents must act with incomplete state information, which makes external reasoning priors from language models attractive. However, standard ways of letting language models override or assist policies almost never result in the model actually changing actions, especially when using small language models. The authors aim to understand why SLM guidance fails in this setting and how to make it effective and practical without large models.

Method: The authors analyze uncertainty-gated SLM guidance and diagnose the failure of vanilla, egocentric prompts that provide too little context for real reasoning. They then introduce ASK+, a method that (1) augments the SLM prompt with trajectory-aware context, including a partially revealed map, visited locations, and action history; (2) enforces structured chain-of-thought reasoning; and (3) uses predictive entropy of the RL policy over actions as an uncertainty-based gate for when to query the SLM. They evaluate ASK+ with Qwen3.5-2B and Qwen3.5-4B on several POMDP benchmarks such as DoorKey, FourRooms, and HigherLower.

Result: They find that prior ASK-style baselines have near-zero overwrite rates, indicating that SLMs almost never meaningfully influence behavior. With ASK+, the overwrite rate increases and the SLM provides useful corrections, leading to substantial performance gains: on DoorKey, success improves from 89% (PPO and vanilla ASK) to 93%; on FourRooms, from 53% to 70%; and on HigherLower, accuracy reaches 73.7%, essentially matching the upper bound of using the SLM alone. The analysis also shows that predictive entropy reflects action uncertainty rather than full state uncertainty but remains a reliable signal even under partial observability.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the main obstacle to effective SLM guidance in partially observable RL is not model size but prompt design and context provision. By giving SLMs trajectory-aware state summaries and structuring their reasoning, they can act as useful consultants that selectively override the policy when uncertainty is high. Uncertainty-gated assistance is therefore viable beyond fully observable settings, and small models like Qwen3.5-2B can match or outperform larger ones when properly prompted and gated, reducing the need for large LMs in RL guidance.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning agents operating under partial observability must act on incomplete information, making them natural candidates for guidance from small language models (SLMs) that carry broad reasoning priors. Yet integrating SLM guidance into this setting has proven difficult: across all test environments, vanilla uncertainty-gated approaches achieve an overwrite rate at or near zero, meaning the SLM almost never contributes an independent action. We trace this failure to the bare egocentric prompt, which provides insufficient context for genuine reasoning, and identify it as a context problem rather than a capacity problem. We propose ASK+, which supplies the SLM with trajectory-aware context (a partially revealed map, visited positions, and action history) and structured chain-of-thought reasoning, converting it from a passive redundancy check into a more informative consultant that occasionally corrects the policy. We further establish that the predictive entropy signal used for selective querying measures action uncertainty rather than state uncertainty and remains informative in POMDPs, making uncertainty-gated assistance viable beyond fully observable settings. The stateful prompt drives substantial gains: on DoorKey, where vanilla ASK matches PPO (both 89%), ASK+ reaches 93% success; on FourRooms, success climbs from 53% to 70%; on HigherLower, accuracy reaches 73.7%, matching the SLM-only upper bound. Across all environments, Qwen3.5-2B matches or exceeds Qwen3.5-4B, confirming that prompt design and selective gating dominate the impact of model scale, enabling guidance without large models.

Abstract: Leadership computing facilities steward large-scale scientific datasets that routinely require substantial transformation before serving as AI training data. However, no existing framework fully unifies automated transformation, readiness assessment, provenance tracking, and agent-native deployment. We present REDI, an open-source framework that addresses this gap through a unified five-stage pipeline (ingest, preprocess, transform, structure, and output) with per-stage instrumentation for reproducibility and deployment as an agent-callable skill; companion tool SetGo automates FAIR compliance and catalog publication. Evaluated across climate, proteomics, materials science, and nuclear fusion, REDI transforms all datasets from raw to AI-ready, with outputs validated against domain-expert references, and preliminary results show near-ideal parallel scaling to 100 nodes on Frontier for the climate case. Provenance-instrumented profiling reveals file I/O as the dominant pipeline cost, with format selection a first-order optimization lever. These results establish REDI as a cross-domain platform providing automated data readiness for scientific AI, transforming data preparation bottlenecks into reproducible, reusable community assets.

### [113] [SwarmResearch: Orchestrating Coding Agents for Open-Ended Discovery](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02807)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing long-running coding agents for open-ended optimization often converge prematurely on one high-level strategy and then only make incremental, low-level edits. This limits exploration of alternative, potentially better approaches. The authors suspect this is due to two design choices: (1) using a single agent with ever-growing context and (2) only allowing edits to a single program state, which reduces diversity of exploration.

Method: They design SwarmResearch, a harness where a top-level Shepherd Agent maintains global context and coordinates a population of Search Agents. Each Search Agent works with local context on its own git branch, exploring different solution directions. The orchestrator adaptively manages search depth and parallelism, steering high-level exploration and dynamically scaling how many agents operate at different stages of the search process.

Result: On a suite of open-ended optimization tasks, SwarmResearch outperforms or matches state-of-the-art LLM-driven evolution and multi-agent baselines on 13 out of 15 tasks. The improvement is attributed to better high-level exploration and orchestrator-guided adaptive scaling, which finds higher-quality solutions than fixed configurations of serial or parallel agents.

Conclusion: Decoupling global coordination from local search and maintaining multiple editable program states leads to more effective exploration in long-running coding agents. SwarmResearch demonstrates that an orchestrator-subagent architecture with adaptive parallelism can systematically uncover better solutions in open-ended optimization than traditional single-agent or fixed-scaling multi-agent setups.

Abstract: Long-running coding agents such as autoresearch can persistently discover optimizations for open-ended problems. However, they tend to converge onto a single high-level approach, then proceed with low-level edits while missing other superior approaches to the problem. We hypothesize two harness-level design choices contribute to this behavior: accumulating context in a single long-running agent and only exposing a single program state to edit. We introduce SwarmResearch, an orchestrator-subagent harness in which a Shepherd Agent uses global context to steer a population of Search Agents, each operating with local context in their respective git branch. On open-ended optimization tasks, SwarmResearch discovers better or comparable solutions to state-of-the-art LLM-guided evolution and multi-agent techniques on 13/15 tasks, driven by higher-level exploration. Compared with fixed scaling of serial and parallel agents, SwarmResearch's orchestrator-guided scaling discovers better-performing solutions by adapting parallelism at different search depths.

### [114] [Object-Centric Environment Modeling for Agentic Tasks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02846)
*Yiyang Li,Tianyi Ma,Zehong Wang,Yijun Ma,Yanfang Ye*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Object-Centric Environment Modeling (OCM), a framework that turns an LLM agent’s interaction history into an executable, object-oriented environment model plus reusable procedures, yielding better performance and fewer invalid actions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM agents either store raw text memories that become unwieldy and hard to validate, or use symbolic/programmatic approaches that learn narrow skills or oversimplified world models. There is a need for a scalable, verifiable, and reusable way to structure an agent’s experience so it can act more reliably in complex environments.

Method: The authors design OCM, which represents experience as two interconnected Python codebases: (1) object knowledge, where environment entities and mechanisms are implemented as Python classes; and (2) procedure knowledge, where reusable interaction patterns are implemented as code that imports and uses the object model. After each interaction episode, OCM reflects on the trajectory, updates both codebases, and then automatically verifies that all procedures still execute correctly against the revised object model. At inference time, the agent uses progressive knowledge disclosure, first consulting compact code signatures (interfaces) and reading full source only when needed.

Result: Across multiple benchmarks, agents using OCM attain the best average rank compared to baselines and commit fewer invalid actions. This suggests that structuring experience as an executable object-centric environment model improves both effectiveness and reliability of LLM agents.

Conclusion: Organizing an LLM agent’s experience into an executable, object-centric environment model with verified reusable procedures yields better performance and action validity than prior memory or symbolic approaches. OCM demonstrates that object-centric, code-based environment models are a promising direction for building more capable and dependable LLM agents.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) agents can improve through accumulated experience, but free-form textual memories become difficult to maintain, validate, and reuse as interactions grow. Recent symbolic approaches learn executable skills or programmatic world models, yet often store local procedures or assume simplified dynamics. We propose Object-Centric Environment Modeling (OCM), which organizes experience into an executable object-centric environment model. OCM maintains two connected code bases: object knowledge, which defines environment entities and mechanisms as Python classes, and procedure knowledge, which records reusable interaction patterns that must import and use the object model. OCM works in an online setting: after each episode, OCM reflects on the trajectory, updates both knowledge bases, and verifies that all procedures execute against the updated object model. During future interaction, the agent uses progressive knowledge disclosure to inspect compact code signatures first and read source code only when needed. Experiments show that OCM achieves the best average rank across benchmarks and reduces invalid actions, demonstrating that agents can benefit from building object-centric environment models.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors design VERITAS, a replication pipeline centered on command-line coding agents. Given a paper, its code repository, or both, VERITAS: (1) extracts key claims from the paper, (2) runs the described methodology by executing and debugging the code, interactively resolving issues as they occur, and (3) evaluates each claim by comparing it with the empirical evidence from the executed experiments. The system outputs an importance-weighted Replication Score, a severity-labeled log of all code fixes applied, and the patched codebase. They evaluate VERITAS on two replication benchmarks (CORE-Bench and ReplicationBench) covering 65 papers across multiple domains.

Result: On CORE-Bench and ReplicationBench, comprising 65 papers from computer science, social science, medicine, and astrophysics, VERITAS outperforms two strong Claude Code baselines that use the same base model and computing environment. It achieves state-of-the-art performance and leads on all reported metrics on both benchmarks.

Conclusion: VERITAS demonstrates that a domain-agnostic, CLI-based coding agent framework can effectively automate significant parts of scientific replication and outperform prior benchmark-bound approaches. This suggests such general-purpose tools can help scale and standardize the verification of published research across diverse scientific fields.

Abstract: AI tools are accelerating scientific publication while the systems that review it struggle to keep up, and independent verification of published research has become both harder and more important. As manual replication is slow and expensive, a growing line of work uses coding agents to automate parts of the process. Existing efforts are largely packaged as benchmarks with companion agents that only run inside the benchmark's own pipeline, and no general-purpose replication tool exists. We present VERITAS, a domain-agnostic replication framework built around CLI coding agents. Given a paper, a code repository, or both, VERITAS extracts the paper's claims, runs the methodology while resolving issues as they arise, and judges each claim against the evidence from experiment runs. The pipeline returns an importance-weighted Replication Score, a severity-rated log of every fix applied, and the patched codebase. We evaluate VERITAS on CORE-Bench and ReplicationBench, 65 papers spanning computer science, social science, medicine, and astrophysics. Against two strong Claude Code baselines on the same model and host environment, VERITAS achieves state-of-the-art performance and leads on every metric on both benchmarks.

The problem is formulated as a heterogeneous graph-based Markov decision process that captures the dual-layer kitting structure and the tail-product bottleneck dynamics that produce a sparse reward landscape. <details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing grounded benchmarks focus on executable actions and verifiable outcomes, while social simulations emphasize rich interactions but lack strong grounding. There is a need for an environment that combines both: realistic social interaction and grounded, measurable task performance, particularly when task-relevant knowledge is distributed across different agents or roles.

Method: The authors define socially distributed task environments, where task knowledge is partitioned among role-isolated participants and actions can only be executed through them. They implement Incognita, a framework built on Concordia, that separates social interaction from grounded execution. Stronger models also request more hidden knowledge, interact with more entities, and attempt more grounded write operations, but overall reliability remains low.

Conclusion: Socially distributed task environments like Incognita reveal important aspects of agent behavior before agents achieve high reliability. They expose how agents elicit knowledge, choose information sources, attempt grounded actions, and form (often incorrect) beliefs about task completion. Even though success rates are currently low, the framework surfaces meaningful behavioral differences across models and highlights the challenge of robust performance in socially distributed, grounded tasks.

Abstract: Effective agency in social environments depends on when an agent seeks knowledge, when it acts, and whether its actions are justified by acquired information. Existing grounded benchmarks provide executable actions, persistent state, and verifiable outcomes, while social simulation environments provide rich interaction among language agents. We study an evaluation setting that combines these requirements. We define socially distributed task environments as interactive environments where task-relevant knowledge is partitioned across role-isolated participants and consequential actions are accessible only through them. Communication serves as exploration over role-partitioned knowledge, while grounded action serves as exploitation over environment state. We introduce Incognita, a Concordia-based framework that separates social interaction from grounded execution. The evaluated agent routes messages to a user or specialist entities; specialists mediate admissible operations; a deterministic sub-environment executes accepted operations over a canonical state; and an offline evaluator scores outcomes with inherited rewards. Incognita-Retail transforms tau-bench retail into a multi-entity environment while preserving final-state reward semantics. We evaluate three generative agent models on 18 tasks stratified by social breadth, with 540 trials. Progress appears in reward and behavior: success rises from 0 percent to 8.9 percent and 17.2 percent, while premature finalization falls from 100 percent to 87 percent and 58 percent. Stronger models elicit more hidden knowledge, contact more entities, and attempt more grounded writes, yet reliability remains low. These findings show that socially distributed task environments expose behavior before reliable success, including knowledge elicitation, source selection, grounded action attempts, and premature completion belief.

### [120] [Reinforcement Learning for Evidence-Seeking Diagnostic Reasoning with Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.02983)

Method: 1) Formalize diagnosis as an Iterative Evidence-Seeking Task where the model repeatedly decides which question or test to request and updates its belief. 2) Use Reinforcement Learning with Verifiable Rewards (RLVR) in a closed-loop setting, designing reward functions to encourage accurate diagnoses and consistent, rational exam choices. 3) Build RAGES, a Retrieval-Augmented Generation-based Examination Simulator that, given the current dialog and requested examination, retrieves clinical knowledge and generates realistic follow-up evidence for the model to use. 4) Train and evaluate LLMs within this loop across multiple clinical datasets.

In contrast, real-world clinical intelligence is inherently an iterative investigative process requiring strategic evidence acquisition. To bridge this gap, we formalize medical diagnosis as an Iterative Evidence-Seeking Task. We leverage Reinforcement Learning with Verifiable Rewards (RLVR) to elicit intrinsic reasoning within a closed-loop environment, guided by a novel suite of rewards that enforce diagnostic precision and examination consistency. To facilitate this, we introduce the Retrieval-Augmented Generation-based Examination Simulator (RAGES), a high-fidelity clinical oracle that provides realistic, knowledge-grounded follow-up evidence. Empirical results across diverse datasets demonstrate that our framework enables LLMs to transition from passive responders to autonomous assistants. Notably, our model demonstrates comparable performance to larger and reasoning-enhanced baselines, while RAGES proves superior to vanilla LLMs in generating biologically plausible clinical feedback.

Method: The authors model human-AI collaboration as a stochastic game between a human and an AI agent, then design the Human-Centric Reflective Architecture (HCRA). Abstract: The use of Large Language Models (LLMs) across diverse areas of human activity-ranging from everyday tasks to safety-critical applications-aims to enhance decision-making effectiveness with minimal human feedback. Concurrently, it seeks to align decisions with human expectations, preferences, and needs while mitigating risks associated with AI non-determinism. However, humans frequently over- or under-rely on AI recommendations, and current AI systems remain poorly calibrated to human expectations. To address these challenges, we introduce a human-AI collaborative decision-making framework designed to augment human capabilities and align AI agents with human preferences and expectations. Specifically, this paper (a) formulates the collaborative decision-making task as a stochastic game between an AI agent and a human player, and (b) proposes the Human-Centric Reflective Architecture (HCRA), which integrates human-calibrated models with reinforcement learning agents that leverage linguistic feedback in an iterative, reflective process. Evaluation results demonstrate that HCRA enhances decision-making effectiveness and delivers high-quality recommendations.

### [123] [Silicon Sampling via Cross-Survey Transfer](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03091)
*Chan-Tung Ku,Chan Hsu,Pei-Cing Huang,Frank Cheng-shan Liu,I-Ling Cheng,Yihuang Kang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how well large language models can predict individual survey responses across different question sets, introducing a cross-survey transfer framework and benchmarking LLMs against supervised baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing work on "silicon sampling" mainly compares aggregate distributions of LLM-generated answers to human survey data, which can hide failures at the individual level and overstate how well models capture coherent respondent profiles. A more stringent, respondent-level evaluation is needed to understand whether LLMs can meaningfully augment or substitute for traditional surveys.

Method: The authors propose a cross-survey transfer evaluation: for each real human respondent, an LLM is given their answers to one subset of survey questions and must predict their answers to a distinct subset of questions from the same survey. They apply this to the 2024 Taiwan Election and Democratization Study using three open-weight LLMs (27B–120B parameters) in zero-shot mode and compare against supervised machine learning models, such as random forests trained on the same-population data. They analyze performance by construct type and examine phenomena like variance collapse and safety-alignment constraints.

Result: Zero-shot LLMs reach about 52% accuracy on predicting answers to unseen survey items, coming within roughly 6 percentage points of a supervised random forest trained on the same population. Predictability varies systematically across constructs, from about 67% accuracy for partisan attitudes down to 23% for sovereignty-related items. The study also finds that variance collapse is not unique to LLMs, as supervised models exhibit it too, and that safety alignment effects differ substantially across LLM families.

Conclusion: Cross-survey transfer provides a stricter and more informative test of LLMs as synthetic survey respondents than simple distributional matching. LLMs show substantial but uneven ability to recover individual-level response patterns, performing relatively well on some constructs and poorly on others. Common concerns about variance collapse and safety alignment require more nuanced treatment, as these issues depend on model type and implementation. Overall, silicon sampling is promising but has clear boundaries and should be used with awareness of construct-specific predictability and model-specific limitations.

Abstract: Silicon sampling-using large language models (LLMs) to simulate human survey respondents-has emerged as a promising approach for augmenting traditional survey research. However, most evaluations rely on distributional comparisons rather than individual-level prediction, which risks conflating pattern matching with coherent respondent-level prediction. We propose cross-survey transfer, a more rigorous evaluation framework in which an LLM is given a respondent's answers to one set of questions and must predict their answers to entirely different questions from the same survey. Using data from the Taiwan Election and Democratization Study (TEDS) 2024, three open-weight LLMs (27B-120B parameters), and supervised machine learning baselines, we find that: (1) zero-shot LLMs achieve 52% accuracy on genuinely unseen items, closing to within 6 percentage points (pp) of a supervised random forest trained on same-population data; (2) a stable construct predictability hierarchy emerges, from 67% for partisan attitudes to 23% for sovereignty; and (3) variance collapse and safety alignment effects-two commonly cited LLM limitations-turn out to be more nuanced than previously reported, with variance collapse affecting supervised models as well and alignment effects varying dramatically across model families. These findings clarify both the promise and boundaries of silicon sampling.

### [124] [APeB: Benchmarking Personalization Ability of Large Language Model Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03162)
*Garry Yang,Zizhe Chen,Xinru Chen,Yongqiang Chen,Jianxiang Wang,Deyu Zou,Linyi Ding,Jialiang Wu,Yunzhong He,Yu Gong,James Cheng,Huaixiao Tou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a benchmark and method for testing how LLM-based agents personalize product search from raw, underspecified queries using noisy user histories, and shows that better use of history via a simple query-refinement pipeline significantly improves performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-powered agents are increasingly used for tasks like product search, but users often provide short, vague queries. To serve them well, agents must infer latent intent and preferences from messy, real interaction logs, not from clean, refined prompts. Existing benchmarks typically assume clear queries or simplified histories, so they do not stress-test the agent’s ability to personalize in realistic early-stage search scenarios. The authors want a realistic setting and metrics to evaluate and improve such agentic personalization.

Method: They define a personalized product search (PPS) setting focused on raw, underspecified user queries plus diverse, noisy histories. From real action logs, they construct the Agent Personalized Benchmark (APeB), which pairs: (1) early, underspecified intents, (2) rich user interaction histories, and (3) sets of candidate products the user viewed. They then evaluate state-of-the-art LLMs running as multi-step agents over this benchmark, comparing performance on explicit vs early-stage queries and analyzing failures with rubric-based evaluation. Finally, they propose a simple history-aware query-refinement pipeline, VQRA, which incorporates user history to rewrite or enrich the user’s initial query before retrieval and recommendation.

Result: Experiments show that LLM agents perform well when queries are explicit but perform poorly for early-stage, vague queries that require inferring intent and preferences. Rubric-driven error analysis indicates that this performance gap is largely due to ineffective use of user history, rather than general model weaknesses. When they introduce the VQRA history-aware query-refinement pipeline, performance consistently improves across conditions, demonstrating that even simple mechanisms for leveraging history can close part of the gap.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that current LLM-based agents are not yet good at agentic personalization from raw queries, mainly because they fail to effectively exploit past interaction histories. Personalized Product Search and the APeB benchmark provide a more realistic and challenging testbed for this capability. The gains from the simple VQRA pipeline suggest that dedicated, explicit modules for utilizing user history and refining queries are crucial for future personalized agents, and that better designs in this area could substantially improve user experiences in early-stage search and recommendation tasks.

Abstract: LLM-powered agents struggle with personalization when users issue raw, underspecified queries. In this setting, agents must infer latent intent, extract preferences from noisy interaction histories, and select among competing alternatives. Existing benchmarks rarely test this capability, as they often rely on user-refined queries or simplified histories. We introduce personalized product search (PPS), a testbed for agentic personalization under raw queries and diverse histories. We construct Agent Personalized Benchmark (APeB) from action logs, pairing underspecified intents with rich histories and user-viewed candidate items. Evaluating state-of-the-art LLMs with multi-step agent workflows, we find that models handle explicit queries well but struggle with early-stage queries requiring intent and preference discovery. Rubric analysis attributes this gap mainly to ineffective history use. A simple history-aware query-refinement pipeline, VQRA, yields consistent gains, highlighting the need for dedicated history-utilization modules in personalized agents.

*Lukas Kirchdorfer,Adrian Rebmann,Christian Warmuth,Timotheus Kampik,Theiss Heilker,Gregor Berg*

  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for a shared, governable way for agents to consume this knowledge during process execution.

Method: The authors conceptually define the notion of an organizational memory for agentic business processes, derive design requirements for such a memory, and propose a reference architecture for how this memory is curated (created, updated, governed) and consumed (queried, used at runtime) by LLM-based agents. They then instantiate this architecture in a proof-of-concept implementation focused on a procurement process scenario to evaluate feasibility and effectiveness.

Result: The proof-of-concept shows that LLM-based agents connected to the proposed organizational memory can more reliably execute procurement-related process steps according to organizational rules and procedures, and that changes to rules can be centrally updated in the memory and immediately reflected across agents, reducing silos and duplication.

Conclusion: A dedicated organizational memory layer for agentic business process execution is both necessary and feasible to overcome the limitations of ad-hoc prompt engineering and per-agent retrieval setups. Centralizing enterprise procedural knowledge in a shared, governed, agent-consumable form improves scalability, maintainability, and consistency of LLM-based business process automation.

Abstract: LLM-based agents offer new opportunities for automating business process execution beyond the limits of rule-based systems. However, general-purpose LLMs lack the organization-specific knowledge required for reliable execution, which is typically fragmented across human-oriented artifacts such as policies, process models, and standard operating procedures. While such knowledge can technically be encoded in individual prompts or agent-specific retrieval setups, this approach does not scale in enterprises, as it gives rise to knowledge silos and rule duplicates, and makes consistent updates and learning across agents difficult. We argue that this calls for an organizational memory for agentic business process execution: a shared, governed, and agent-consumable reference layer of evolving organization-specific procedural knowledge about how work should be executed. We derive requirements for such a memory, propose an architecture for its curation and consumption, and demonstrate its effectiveness in a proof-of-concept based on a procurement scenario.

Conclusion: Treating subsystems as 'embodied operators'—modular, composable, and holistically evaluated components—provides a principled foundation for building reusable, scalable, and verifiable embodied intelligence systems. This work defines these modules as embodied operators and studies them as independent yet composable units in embodied intelligence pipelines. We clarify their definition boundary, emphasizing task semantics, standardized input-output contracts, deployability, reusability, and multi-layer optimizability. We further construct a taxonomy covering five categories: detection and segmentation, spatial localization and 3D understanding, hand motion recovery, embodied foundation models and task-decision operators, and planning, control, and system support operators. For each category, we summarize representative functions, technical paradigms, application roles, and practical limitations. Beyond taxonomy, we propose a multi-dimensional benchmark framework that evaluates embodied operators in terms of correctness, end-to-end efficiency, resource usage, temporal stability, portability, interface compatibility, deployment reliability, and downstream task utility. We also discuss workflow-level operator acceleration and open challenges in operator composition, data standardization, world models, VLA safety, edge deployment, and real-world application value. Overall, this work argues that embodied operators should be optimized and evaluated as holistic deployable components, providing a foundation for reusable, scalable, and verifiable embodied intelligence systems.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are increasingly used in education, but their impact on learning depends heavily on how students interact with them. There is concern that naive or efficiency-focused use may undermine deep learning. The paper seeks to understand how different designs of LLM tutors shape students’ prompting behaviors, immediate learning, and their subsequent independent use of LLMs.

Method: A two-phase study in a graduate mobile robotics course with 66 students initially using one of two LLM tutors for 6 weeks: (1) a Socratic-Guidance tutor that scaffolds learning through dialogic questioning, and (2) a Prompt-Refinement tutor that helps students craft more effective prompts. In a second phase, 52 students used an unconstrained LLM during a 3-week course project. Researchers compared task performance, prompting patterns, learning gains, and later LLM-use patterns between conditions.

Result: During the guided phase, both tutors yielded similar task performance and prompting patterns. However, differences emerged in later sessions and the unguided phase: students who used the Socratic-Guidance tutor achieved higher learning gains and were more likely to use understanding-driven prompting strategies with the unconstrained LLM. These strategies were associated with higher conceptual understanding. Students perceived the Socratic tutor as less efficient despite its superior long-term learning benefits.

Conclusion: Socratic-style LLM tutors, which emphasize dialogic questioning and conceptual engagement, better cultivate students’ capacity to learn with LLMs over time than tutors focused on optimizing prompt efficiency. Even if perceived as less efficient, Socratic guidance fosters understanding-driven prompting and higher learning gains, implying that LLM tutor design should prioritize instructional scaffolding and metacognitive development over short-term efficiency.

Abstract: While Large Language Models (LLMs) can provide personalized support in learning, several studies have raised concerns regarding their use in education. Importantly, learning depends on how students engage with LLMs. This study examined how two types of LLM-based tutors shape students' prompting practices, learning, and subsequent LLM-use: a Socratic-Guidance (SG) tutor, which structures interaction through dialogic questioning, and a Prompt-Refinement (PR) tutor that guides the formulation of effective prompts. We conducted a two-phase study in a graduate-level mobile robotics course: 66 students used either the SG or PR tutor during a 6-week intervention, followed by 52 students using an unconstrained LLM during a 3-week course project. Results show that while the SG- and PR tutors led to similar task performance and prompting patterns during guided use, they differ in learning outcomes and later LLM-use. SG-students, relative to PR-student, achieved higher learning gains in later sessions, and were more likely to adopt understanding-driven prompting strategies, which are predictive of higher understanding, when using an unconstrained LLM. Although learners perceived the SG tutor as less efficient, the findings suggest that Socratic guidance supports the development of students' capacity to learn with LLMs over time, highlighting its importance for LLM tutor design.

### [128] [When Aggregate Alignment Misleads: Auditing Policy Repair Without Per-State Expert Actions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03386)
*Peiying Zhu,Sidi Chang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to evaluate and repair decision policies with an LLM-based agentic editor using only coarse diagnostic feedback, in a hotel-pricing simulator, and shows that such an editor can nearly match a benchmark policy and meaningfully reduce behavioral distance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In many realistic settings, we want AI systems to edit or repair existing decision policies, but fine-grained expert labels or ground-truth rewards are unavailable. Instead, we often have only coarse, aggregate diagnostics about how a policy behaves relative to a benchmark. The authors are motivated to understand whether an agentic LLM-based editor can use such weak, region-level diagnostics to meaningfully repair a policy, and how to properly evaluate such repairs beyond simple revenue or distance metrics.

Method: They build a hotel-pricing simulator where a policy editor (implemented as a multi-restart LLM) can only see region-level diagnostic summaries comparing its price distribution to a benchmark policy across time, inventory, and market segments. The editor cannot access benchmark actions, code, rewards, or held-out outcomes; it can only propose constrained edits to a target-action table. They compare this LLM editor to various baselines: cheap diagnostic projections, non-semantic search-based proposers with many evaluations, shuffled-diagnostic controls that break the correspondence between diagnostics and regions, and a tree-based editor. Performance is measured on held-out episodes using revenue per available room (RevPAR), episode composition distance, pooled alignment, and reference-state D1 metrics.

Result: On 5,000 held-out episodes, the multi-restart LLM editor achieves RevPAR 108.47 (95% CI 107.61–109.34), close to the benchmark policy's 108.75 (107.81–109.68), with only a small paired gap of -0.276 RevPAR (95% CI [-0.692, 0.146]). A simple diagnostic projection already gets RevPAR 107.90, so the LLM's unique advantage is that it also significantly reduces episode composition distance from 1.153 to 0.609, yielding the strongest non-benchmark repair overall. Non-semantic proposers with up to 2,500 evaluations underperform by 8.77–14.57 RevPAR, and the shuffled-diagnostic control drops to RevPAR 94.30, indicating that the LLM is genuinely using structured diagnostic information. A tree-based editor attains even better pooled alignment and reference-state D1 but substantially worse revenue (98.91).

Conclusion: LLM-based agentic policy editors can effectively use coarse, region-level diagnostic feedback to repair decision policies, nearly matching benchmark revenue while improving behavioral alignment. However, simple behavioral-distance metrics are insufficient to judge repair quality: the crucial question is whether diagnostic feedback is converted into reliable, closed-loop outcome improvements. Strong alignment according to one distance metric can coexist with poor revenue, so evaluation of agentic policy repair must integrate both outcome and multi-faceted behavioral metrics.

Abstract: Agentic AI systems are increasingly used to edit, refine, and repair decision policies, but evaluating these edits is difficult when per-state expert action labels are unavailable. We study this problem in a hotel-pricing simulator where an agentic policy editor receives only region-level diagnostic feedback: summaries of how its price distribution differs from a benchmark policy across time, inventory, and market regions. The editor cannot observe benchmark actions, benchmark source code, reward numbers, or held-out outcomes, and may only propose constrained edits to a target-action table. On 5,000 held-out episodes, a multi-restart LLM editor reaches RevPAR 108.47 (95% CI 107.61 - 109.34), close to the benchmark policy's 108.75 (107.81 - 109.68), with paired gap (LLM minus benchmark) -0.276 and 95% CI [-0.692, 0.146]. A cheap diagnostic projection already recovers much of the revenue (107.90), so the LLM editor's distinctive gain is not raw revenue lift alone: it also reduces episode composition distance from 1.153 to 0.609. This is the strongest non-benchmark repair result. This profile is not explained by restart search alone: non-semantic proposers with up to 2,500 evaluations fall 8.77 - 14.57 RevPAR points short. Nor is it explained by plausible prompt format: a shuffled-diagnostic control breaks region-error correspondence and falls to RevPAR 94.30. The match is genuine but partial. A tree editor achieves stronger pooled alignment, 0.214 versus 0.266, and stronger reference-state D1, 0.328 versus 1.197, yet revenue falls to 98.91. These results show that agentic policy repair should be evaluated by whether diagnostic feedback becomes reliable closed-loop outcome, not by a single behavioral distance.

### [130] [Efficient bias mitigation in T2I diffusion models using Concept Graphs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03397)
*Mansi,Avinash Kori,Francesco Leofante*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces CO-ALIGN, a concept-ontology alignment method that mitigates bias in text-to-image diffusion models by aligning internal concept graphs in both the text encoder and denoiser, substantially reducing bias and incoherent generations while improving image quality and aiding concept unlearning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Text-to-image diffusion models inherit and propagate harmful biases from their training data. Existing mitigation methods either act only on the text encoder or rely on inference-time guidance, which often causes the generated images to become semantically incoherent or to lose fidelity. There is a need for a training-time, model-internal approach that reduces bias while preserving generative quality and semantic consistency.

Method: The authors propose CO-ALIGN (Concept Ontology Alignment), which explicitly models and aligns the internal concept ontology of diffusion models. They build and align concept graphs that capture relationships between concepts within both the text encoder and the denoiser. CO-ALIGN is applied in three variants: (1) alignment within text encoders, (2) alignment within denoisers, and (3) joint alignment across both components. The alignment process encourages the model’s internal concept representations and their relations to be debiased and consistent across components, without relying on ad-hoc inference-time guidance.

Result: Across multiple evaluation setups, CO-ALIGN substantially reduces bias in text-to-image diffusion models while improving or preserving image quality. The method improves fairness metrics by about 30%, achieves an FID improvement of 11.4 points, and boosts image fidelity by 2.8%. It also reduces the frequency of semantically incoherent generations by 88% compared to baselines. Additionally, CO-ALIGN improves robustness in concept unlearning tasks, making several unlearning techniques more effective and stable.

Conclusion: Aligning the internal concept ontologies of text-to-image diffusion models is an effective and general strategy for mitigating bias without sacrificing generative quality. CO-ALIGN shows that concept-graph alignment across both text encoders and denoisers can outperform existing methods on fairness, image quality, and semantic coherence, while also strengthening downstream tasks like concept unlearning. This suggests that ontology-level interventions in generative models are a powerful direction for building fairer and more controllable diffusion systems.

Abstract: Text-to-Image diffusion models often propagate harmful bias inherited from the training data. Existing bias mitigation techniques typically intervene only at the text encoder or provide inference-time guidance, often leading to generations that collapse into semantically incoherent outputs. To address these limitations, we introduce CO-ALIGN (Concept Ontology Alignment), a novel bias mitigation approach based on concept-graph alignment that operates on the model's internal concept ontology. By aligning concepts within the text encoder and denoiser, CO-ALIGN achieves substantial bias reduction while preserving generative integrity. We demonstrate the effectiveness of concept-graph alignment across three paradigms: text-encoders, denoisers and joint text-denoiser ontology alignment. CO-ALIGN outperforms the state of the art, improving fairness by $30\%$, $ΔFID=11.4$ in image quality, $2.8\%$ in image fidelity, all while reducing semantically incoherent outputs by $88\%$. Beyond bias mitigation, we show that CO-ALIGN benefits other downstream tasks as well. In particular, our experiments demonstrate that better-aligned internal ontologies enhance concept unlearning robustness across multiple unlearning techniques.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors design a human-in-the-loop framework that iteratively queries the user to obtain feedback about potential interventions and outcomes. Once a sufficiently accurate approximation is obtained, the framework computes recourse actions that are tailored to the user’s inferred causal dependencies and costs. The method is evaluated in simulation, where synthetic users with known linear and nonlinear causal models respond to queries according to their ground-truth SCMs.

Result: Simulation experiments on both linear and nonlinear structural causal models indicate that the framework can leverage user feedback to improve identification of relevant causal effects and generate more personalized, plausible, and cost-effective recourse compared to approaches that do not model user-specific causality. Conclusion: Interactive, human-in-the-loop causal model learning using Bayesian inference is a promising direction for algorithmic recourse, enabling recommendations that better align with individuals’ true causal structures and constraints. Nonetheless, the success of this approach hinges on accurately approximating potentially complex, nonlinear SCMs and robustly modeling noise, suggesting future work on richer causal representations, better elicitation strategies, and more resilient inference procedures.

Abstract: Algorithmic recourse addresses the challenge of providing tailored recommendations to users affected by unfavorable machine learning decisions, in potentially high-stakes scenarios. Traditional approaches to recourse often rely on the closest counterfactual explanations or assume a priori knowledge of a user's causal structure, resulting in interventions that overlook individual contexts and specific feature interactions. To overcome these limitations, we study a human-in-the-loop framework that iteratively approximates the user's structural causal model through interactive queries via Bayesian inference before producing recourse recommendations. This framework exploits humans' feedback to improve the identification of causal effects, allowing personalized recourse that is plausible, cost-effective, and aligned with the actual causal dependencies of each user. As a proof of concept, we evaluate this framework through simulated human responses. Our simulations across linear and non-linear causal models show promising results, though challenges remain in capturing complex, non-linear structures, emphasizing the importance of accurate approximations and robust noise distribution modeling.

### [132] [Demonstrating Generalization Failures via Mixtures of Conditional Policies](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03478)
*Jou Barzdukas,Jack Peck,Julian Schulz,Paulius Rauba,Steven Basart,Lennie Wells*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper builds simple "model organisms" of language models that show controlled, striking generalization failures after RL fine-tuning, to better study and stress-test alignment and generalization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Frontier language models are post-trained on curated task suites, creating a distribution shift between training and deployment that can lead to poorly understood generalization failures. The field lacks clean, controlled examples of such failures that can be used to systematically study when and how training success diverges from true generalization. The authors aim to create a simple, controllable setup that exposes and explains structured generalization failures arising from RL-style post-training.

Method: They construct language models via supervised fine-tuning on a dataset composed of transcripts from multiple "conditional policies"—each policy can exhibit different behaviors on different task distributions and can be independently configured. This yields a model that can be approximately interpreted as a mixture over these conditional policies. They then apply RL training on a particular distribution of tasks and study which conditional policies are selected by RL and how performance changes across different but related distributions (e.g., same questions with different prefix trigger strings). They also design specific experimental setups to probe failures under two kinds of distribution shift: shifts in task coverage and shifts in temporal context.

Result: They find that RL training tends to select the conditional policies that obtain the highest reward on the specific training distribution, even when this selection harms performance elsewhere. In an illustrative controlled experiment with two distributions that differ only by trigger strings prepended to otherwise identical questions, RL training on one distribution drives performance on the other distribution down to essentially zero, despite the underlying task being the same. Using similar constructions, they demonstrate two new failure modes of generalization under distribution shift: one related to which tasks are covered in training, and another related to temporal context changes.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that their simple construction provides useful "model organisms" for studying generalization failures in language models, showing that RL post-training can cause sharp, structured divergences between training performance and generalization. Even though their setups are stylized and may not directly mirror natural failures in the wild, they act as clear existence proofs and tools for alignment stress-testing and generalization research, helping clarify how and why models can systematically fail under distribution shift.

Abstract: Post-training of frontier language models is conducted on curated task suites, and inevitably leaves a distribution shift between training and deployment environments. This exposes developers to generalization failures, which are relatively poorly understood. To better understand such generalization failures, we believe the community should construct clean demonstrations under simplified conditions. To facilitate this, we propose a simple and flexible way to construct language models which fail to generalize in controllable ways when subsequently trained with Reinforcement Learning (RL) on a given distribution of training tasks. Our construction uses Supervised Fine-Tuning on a dataset of a mixture of transcripts corresponding to a collection of 'conditional policies', which can each independently be assigned certain behaviors on each different task distribution, to obtain a model that is then well approximated as a 'mixture of conditional policies.' We observe that RL training then selects for policies that obtain the highest reward on the training distribution. This can produce striking behaviors: in a controlled setting with two distributions containing identical questions prepended with two different 'trigger strings', RL training on either distribution actively degrades performance on the other to zero, even though the underlying task is identical. We also use our construction to illustrate two novel ways in which generalization may fail in future language models, corresponding to distribution shifts of task coverage and temporal context respectively. While our construction is deliberately simple and may not closely resemble 'natural' generalization failures, the resulting 'model organisms' are of interest for alignment stress-testing and generalization science, and can be used as existence proofs that training success and generalization can come apart in structured ways.

Abstract: We introduce MentalThink, a visual-symbolic reasoning paradigm that equips Multimodal LLMs (MLLMs) with an executable mechanism for "mental" visualization. The core of MentalThink is a think-with-SVG pipeline, where the model learns to generate, render, and interpret scalable vector graphics (SVG) code as an intermediate visual representation for multi-turn reasoning. By creating structured vector sketches, the model can externalize spatial hypotheses, inspect them through deterministic rendering, and reason within a constrained geometric space, effectively mimicking the human process of mental imagery. We instantiate this paradigm through a two-stage training framework, combining Supervised Fine-Tuning (SFT) for SVG syntactic alignment with multi-turn Reinforcement Learning (RL) to encourage iterative inspection, revision, and refinement of intermediate visual hypotheses. Extensive evaluations demonstrate that MentalThink achieves superior performance on spatial understanding and reasoning benchmarks (e.g., 55.1% on VSIBench, 76.0% on MindCube), showing that executable vector graphics provide a verifiable visual workspace for dynamic perspective taking, visual reflection, and compositional scene construction.

Focusing on a representative use case, we illustrate how the framework integrates numerically grounded inputs (such as outputs of machine learning models) with symbolic reasoning over qualitative labels. Key features, including learning-based membership functions and semantically enriched predicates, enable the combination of expert knowledge, contextual factors, and subjective interpretations within a unified declarative setting.

### [135] [How to Avoid Debate: Scalable AI Safety via Doubly-Efficient Interactive Proofs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03561)
*Liyan Chen,Yael Tauman Kalai,Zoe Xi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes methods for verifying AI model outputs using single-prover interactive proofs instead of debate between two models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI safety work often relies on debate, where two equally capable AI models argue to convince a weaker human verifier of the truth. This setup assumes equal capabilities and that one model is honest, which may not be realistic in practice. There is a need for verification methods that do not depend on these assumptions, especially when computations involve external oracles like human feedback or web data.

Method: The authors formalize and study single-prover interactive proofs in the AI safety context, specifically for computations that can query an oracle (e.g., human judgment or web search). They design doubly-efficient single-prover interactive proofs and arguments for such oracle-aided computations, under two structural conditions: (1) robustness of the target computation to a small fraction of incorrect oracle answers, and (2) the oracle itself being representable as a low-degree polynomial. These are relativizing (oracle-respecting) proof systems tailored to AI verification.

Result: They obtain concrete single-prover interactive proof/argument protocols that remain sound and efficient even when the verified computation queries an oracle, provided either robustness to limited oracle noise or low-degree structure of the oracle. The protocols are doubly-efficient (both prover and verifier are efficient relative to the computation and oracle) and work in settings previously handled mainly by debate-based approaches.

Conclusion: Interactive verification of AI systems can be achieved without relying on debate between two competing models. Under realistic structural assumptions—robustness to noisy oracle answers or low-degree structure of the oracle—single-prover, doubly-efficient interactive proofs for oracle-aided computations are possible, offering a new direction for AI safety verification mechanisms.

Abstract: As AI models continue to develop powerful capabilities, it becomes critical that we are able to verify that their output is aligned with our intentions. A recent line of work focuses on verification via debate, a model of interactive proofs where two competing powerful provers, or AI models, debate each other to convince a weak verifier, or a human, of the correctness of their claim. However, debate assumes that the two AI models possess equal abilities and that one of them is truthful, which may not be realistic.
  In this work, we show \emph{how to avoid debate}: we initiate the study of \emph{single-prover} interactive proofs for AI safety. Prior results in single-prover interactive proofs do not immediately carry over to the AI safety setting: for example, they do not work when the computation has access to an oracle, such as to human judgment or an external database such as the web. We present doubly-efficient single-prover interactive proofs and arguments for oracle-aided computations (also known as relativizing proofs), in the settings where (1) the computation is robust, in the sense that the output does not change if at most a small fraction of the answers to oracle queries are incorrect, or (2) the oracle is a low-degree polynomial. These results suggest that interactive verification is possible even without debate, under structured or noise-tolerant oracle access.

### [136] [The Role of Rigor in Artificial Intelligence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03634)
*Timothy Nguyen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes why AI has advanced rapidly despite weak theoretical foundations, proposing a framework of conceptual, epistemic, and operational rigor to explain AI’s current strengths and uncertainties.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI has achieved remarkable practical success through empirical deep learning and benchmark-driven progress, yet lacks the kind of conceptual clarity, scientific theory, and engineering reliability typical of mature disciplines. This gap causes confusion about what current systems can and cannot do, how to interpret their performance, and how to develop more principled methods. The authors are motivated to understand this tension and to provide a structure for thinking about rigor in AI, so as to diagnose current problems and guide AI toward becoming a more mature science and technology.

Method: The authors develop a three-part analytical framework: conceptual rigor (clarity about notions like intelligence and understanding), epistemic rigor (scientific methods and theories that explain system behavior), and operational rigor (practices that ensure robust, reliable performance in real-world deployment). They then apply this framework qualitatively to different aspects of modern AI: definitions of intelligence and understanding, empirical deep-learning practices, the design and use of benchmarks, and attempts at theoretical understanding of large models. This is a conceptual and philosophical analysis, not an empirical experiment.

Result: The application of the framework shows that modern deep learning is dominated by operational rigor—optimizing performance on tasks and benchmarks—while conceptual and epistemic rigor lag behind. The authors highlight how vague or conflicting conceptions of intelligence and understanding coexist with strong performance metrics, how benchmark-driven progress can obscure what models genuinely learn, and how the complexity and scale of modern systems impede the development of general theory. They identify specific strengths and weaknesses in each dimension of rigor as currently practiced in AI.

Conclusion: The distinctive trajectory of AI—rapid practical progress with lingering uncertainty about foundations, generalization, and safety—stems from an imbalance among conceptual, epistemic, and operational rigor, with operational rigor taking precedence in modern deep learning. To transform AI into a mature science and dependable technology, the field must deliberately cultivate conceptual clarity and scientific understanding alongside performance optimization. Their framework is offered as a tool to diagnose current practices and to guide more balanced, rigorous development of future AI systems.

Abstract: Artificial intelligence (AI) has achieved extraordinary capabilities despite lacking many of the conceptual and scientific foundations associated with mature disciplines. Unlike traditional sciences, where reliable technology typically emerges from theoretical understanding, modern AI has progressed largely through performance-driven iteration and "alchemical" experimentation. This tension motivates a systematic analysis of AI through the lens of rigor. We introduce a three-part framework consisting of conceptual rigor (clarifying foundational concepts), epistemic rigor (establishing scientific understanding), and operational rigor (ensuring reliable performance and deployment). Using this framework, we analyze competing conceptions of intelligence and understanding, the strengths and limitations of the empirical approach to deep learning, the power and pitfalls of benchmarks, and the obstacles to theory development posed by modern AI systems. We argue that the distinctive trajectory of AI arises from how forms of rigor interact across paradigms, resulting in the primacy of operational rigor in modern deep learning. This perspective helps explain both AI's rapid advances and its persistent uncertainties, while clarifying the challenges involved in transforming AI into a mature science and reliable technology.

*Oguzhan Karaahmetoglu,Hyong Kim*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a collaborative framework (CoRC) for solving very large Capacitated Vehicle Routing Problems by allowing subproblems to exchange customers and vehicles during optimization, improving feasibility and scalability over fixed-partition and end-to-end methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional approaches to large-scale CVRPs partition customers into independent subproblems to reduce complexity, but this can leave demand unserved even when overall fleet capacity is sufficient, and fixing this via global re-optimization is costly and not always successful. There is a need for scalable methods that preserve the computational benefits of partitioning while still using fleet resources globally and robustly constructing feasible solutions on very large instances.

Method: They introduce Collaborative Routing Constructors (CoRC), a framework in which routing subproblems—formed via any partitioning strategy—are optimized independently but can dynamically exchange customers and vehicles with one another during the optimization process. Result: Across all tested partitioning schemes, CoRC consistently finds feasible routing solutions in cases where standard partition-based approaches (with or without post hoc global re-optimization) fail to construct feasible solutions. Additionally, on very large instances for which contemporary end-to-end routing frameworks cannot produce any solution within the same computational budget, CoRC succeeds in generating feasible routes.

Conclusion: Allowing subproblems in a partitioned CVRP to collaborate by exchanging customers and vehicles during optimization yields a robust, scalable mechanism for feasible large-scale route construction. This collaborative paradigm preserves the tractability benefits of partitioning while overcoming its main drawback—resource fragmentation—and outperforms both fixed-partition and current end-to-end approaches in terms of feasibility on large instances.

Abstract: Large-scale Capacitated Vehicle Routing Problems (CVRPs) are commonly solved by partitioning customers into smaller routing problems that can be optimized independently. While this substantially reduces computational complexity, independently constructed routing solutions may leave some customer demand unserved even when sufficient resources exist elsewhere in the fleet. We present Collaborative Routing Constructors (CoRC), a routing framework that enables independently solved subproblems to exchange customers and vehicles during optimization rather than relying solely on a fixed partition or a subsequent global re-optimization stage. Computational experiments on AGS benchmark instances and synthetic instances containing up to 200,000 customers compare CoRC against independent routing, post-routing global re-optimization, and state-of-the-art, end-to-end routing frameworks. Across all evaluated partitioning strategies, CoRC consistently constructs feasible routing solutions where competing partition-based methods do not. Furthermore, it remains effective on problem instances for which the evaluated end-to-end routing frameworks did not produce solutions under the same computational budget. These results demonstrate that collaboration between routing subproblems provides a robust and scalable approach for feasible large-scale route construction.
