### [138] [Agent Reinforcement Learning via Pivotal-Aware Self-Feedback Retry](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03702)
*Weiyang Guo,Zesheng Shi,Longhui Zhang,Zeen Zhu,Min Zhang,Jing Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: PivoARL is a self-feedback retry framework for LLM agents that pinpoints the pivotal error step and retries only from there, improving sample efficiency and success rates versus full retries and prior RL-like methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM agents perform well on complex, long-horizon tasks but handle failures inefficiently. Full trajectory retries are costly, and naive experience replay or retrieval spreads attention over entire trajectories, diluting the most informative error signals. There is a need for a principled way to extract and exploit the most critical parts of failed experiences to both reduce interaction cost and improve learning and decision quality.

Method: PivoARL introduces a structured reflection process to locate the pivotal erroneous turn within a trajectory. Instead of restarting from scratch, the agent performs a local retry starting at the pivotal state, reusing the correct prefix. The authors interpret this as concentrating information gain near the error boundary and formalize a pivotal-aware credit assignment scheme: correct prefixes are rewarded while erroneous suffixes are isolated or penalized. They also refine reflection quality by using implicit reflection returns as a learning signal. The system is benchmarked on multiple agent tasks and search-based question-answering benchmarks.

Result: Across 4 agent tasks and 7 search-based QA benchmarks, PivoARL significantly boosts Pass@2/3, with an average improvement of about 11.5% over MetaRL. It also improves Pass@1 on more than 80% of evaluated tasks, indicating better single-shot performance. In the Minesweeper environment, PivoARL exceeds GiGPO by over 45% and cuts interaction turns by roughly 42% compared with full-retry strategies, demonstrating both higher effectiveness and efficiency.

Conclusion: Focusing retries and credit assignment around pivotal error turns allows LLM agents to extract more useful learning signals from failures while reducing interaction cost. PivoARL’s pivotal retry and credit assignment mechanisms alleviate experience-signal dilution and lead to consistent performance gains over strong baselines, suggesting that structured, state-aware exploitation of failed trajectories is a powerful direction for improving LLM-based agents.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) agents have shown strong decision-making capabilities in long-horizon interactive tasks, yet they still struggle to effectively leverage failed trajectories: full retries incur high interaction costs, while experience retrieval tends to dilute critical experience signals. To address this, we propose PivoARL, a self-feedback retry framework for experience exploitation in LLM agents. PivoARL identifies the pivotal erroneous turn through structured reflection and performs local retry only from the corresponding pivotal state, thereby reusing the correct prefix and reducing redundant interactions. From an information-gain perspective, we further show that pivotal retry concentrates useful experience signals near the error boundary, mitigating the signal dilution caused by state-agnostic experience utilization. Based on this insight, we design a pivotal-aware credit assignment mechanism that rewards correct prefixes while isolating erroneous suffixes, and optimize reflection quality through implicit reflection returns. We conduct a systematic evaluation on 4 agent tasks and 7 search-based QA benchmarks. Results show that PivoARL achieves significant improvements on Pass@2/3 across all tasks, with an average gain of about 11.5\% over MetaRL. Moreover, benefiting from contrastive preference signals induced by pivotal turns, PivoARL also consistently improves Pass@1 on over 80\% of the tasks. On Minesweeper environment, PivoARL improves over GiGPO by more than 45\% and reduces interaction turns by about 42\% on average compared with full-retry methods. Code is available at https://github.com/yuki-younai/PivoARL.

TL;DR: The paper proposes BRAID, a unified RL framework to jointly optimize text and image generation in multi-turn multimodal reasoning, overcoming previous limitations that treated image generation separately and only applied RL to text.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Unified multi-modal models are good at interleaved text-image reasoning, but current reinforcement learning methods only optimize text-based steps, using supervised training for images. This prevents true end-to-end optimization across both modalities and wastes the potential of RL for improving multi-modal reasoning.

Method: They formulate multi-turn text-image-text reasoning as a single Markov decision process. BRAID computes a shared trajectory-level advantage for the entire interleaved interaction and backpropagates it into both text tokens and image denoising steps, each with its own modality-appropriate policy gradient. Additionally, they introduce a VLM-based judge that scores intermediate images on their reasoning usefulness, providing dense, turn-level rewards to help with long-horizon credit assignment.

Result: On spatial reasoning and visual perception benchmarks, BRAID consistently outperforms baseline methods that either separate modalities or lack vision-guided feedback, demonstrating better multi-modal reasoning performance.

Conclusion: Casting interleaved multimodal reasoning as one unified MDP and jointly applying RL to both text and image generation, augmented with a VLM judge for intermediate visual feedback, is an effective approach for improving reasoning in unified multi-modal models.

Abstract: Unified multi-modal models (UMMs) have shown promising interleaved text-image reasoning capabilities, yet effectively optimizing such multi-turn generation via reinforcement learning (RL) remains an open challenge. Existing approaches apply RL exclusively to text steps, relegating image generation to supervised surrogates, preventing policy gradients from propagating through the full interleaved trajectory across heterogeneous modalities. This leaves the potential of RL for UMMs largely untapped. In the paper, we introduce \textbf{BRAID} (\textbf{B}ridging inte\textbf{R}le\textbf{A}ved mult\textbf{I}-modal reasoning as a unified \textbf{D}ecision process), a simple framework that casts multi-turn text-image-text reasoning as a unified Markov decision process (MDP), enabling joint optimization of textual and visual generation via a single, principled RL objective. BRAID computes a shared trajectory-level advantage and propagates it coherently into both text tokens and image denoising paths, each optimized through its modality-native policy gradient mechanism. To further address long-horizon credit assignment, BRAID employs a vision-language model (VLM) judge that scores each intermediate image on its reasoning utility, supplying dense turn-level feedback to sharpen learning at critical visual branches. Experiments on spatial reasoning and visual perception benchmarks show that BRAID consistently outperforms various baselines, confirming that a unified MDP formulation with vision-thinking guidance is essential for effective multi-modal reasoning.

</details>

We also identify two scaling-law directions for co-folding models, revealing practical routes for continued improvement through data, model, inference, and training scaling. By releasing training code, inference pipelines, checkpoints, and benchmarks, OpenDDE aims to democratize access to frontier biomolecular intelligence, accelerate global collaboration, and lay an open foundation for next-generation drug discovery systems that can move from predicting molecular structures toward designing, scoring, and optimizing therapeutic candidates for human health.

### [143] [Evaluating LLM Uncertainty in Long-Form Generation Using Deterministic Ground Truth](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03870)
*Ido Amit,Ido Galil,Ran El-Yaniv*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces SALT, a benchmark for evaluating fine-grained uncertainty estimation in long-form LLM outputs, enabling deterministic, unit-level assessment of correctness and confidence calibration, and uses it to analyze over 50 LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing uncertainty estimation for LLMs needs to detect errors at a fine-grained level in long outputs, but current benchmarks rely on noisy human or heuristic labels and lack deterministic ground truth. This makes it difficult to reliably evaluate token- or span-level confidence, calibration, and error detection. The authors are motivated to create a zero-noise, long-form benchmark with precise ground truth to rigorously study uncertainty methods and model behavior.

Method: The authors design SALT (Single-answer Atomic Long-form Target), a benchmark of six procedurally generated tasks where each example has a single deterministic long-text ground truth. They define atomic units (tokens or minimal spans) and higher-level units (e.g., lines) so that each unit can be judged correct or incorrect automatically. Using SALT, they systematically evaluate over 50 LLMs with different confidence/uncertainty functions, assessing correctness, calibration, and ranking performance at multiple granularities. They also perform controlled interventions at the atom level during generation to study how earlier errors propagate and how answer length affects future errors. Finally, they compare standard vs reasoning-augmented models (e.g., Chain-of-Thought or reasoning-trained) to assess the trade-offs between accuracy and confidence ranking quality.

Result: SALT enables fully automatic, noise-free evaluation of unit-level correctness and uncertainty estimation in long-form generations. The experiments on 50+ LLMs show: (1) Different confidence functions are better suited to different uncertainty aspects (e.g., calibration vs ranking). (2) Confidence ranking that works relatively well at coarser (line-level) granularity often breaks down at atomic resolution. (3) Atom-level interventions reveal two distinct drivers of future errors: one is error propagation from incorrect prefixes, strongly influenced by the correctness of the global context; the other is a bounded degradation linked to growing answer-context length. (4) Reasoning-style models (via Chain-of-Thought prompting or reasoning training) tend to be more accurate but exhibit worse confidence ranking, indicating a trade-off between correctness and reliable uncertainty estimation.

Conclusion: SALT provides a rigorous, deterministic benchmark for fine-grained evaluation of uncertainty in long-form LLM outputs and reveals important limitations of current confidence measures, especially at atomic resolution. The authors conclude that error propagation and context length are separable sources of future mistakes and that adding reasoning improves accuracy but harms confidence ranking. These insights are directly relevant for risk-sensitive applications where models must not only be accurate but also reliably identify and flag their own errors at a fine-grained level.

Abstract: As LLMs generate increasingly long outputs, effective uncertainty estimation must identify errors at fine-grained levels rather than discard entire responses. While such methods exist, evaluating uncertainty at any resolution (token to an entire generation) is challenging and highly sensitive to label imperfections, making zero-noise benchmarks essential; yet, long-form generation benchmarks tend to rely on fallible labels rather than deterministic ground truth. We introduce Single-answer Atomic Long-form Target (SALT), a benchmark of six procedurally generated tasks with single deterministic long textual ground truths, enabling unit-level evaluation of correctness, calibration, and ranking without external judges. Equipped with SALT, our analysis of 50+ LLMs reveals key insights: We identify which confidence functions dominate each uncertainty aspect and show that confidence ranking largely breaks at atomic resolution, even when clearer separability emerges at coarser line-level units. SALT further enables controlled atom-level interventions throughout generation, revealing two separable drivers of future errors: propagation from corrupted prefixes, dominated by global context correctness, and bounded degradation from increasing answer-context length. Finally, we demonstrate that reasoning, via Chain-of-Thought prompting or internalized through training, introduces a trade-off, improving accuracy while degrading confidence ranking. These findings directly impact risk-critical applications requiring reliable error identification and mitigation.

Abstract: Self-evolving frameworks usually optimize task solutions while treating the surrounding harness as fixed. HASE enables a single Qwen3-8B model to match the text-classification performance of a GPT-OSS-120B model that uses Claude Code as the harness proposer. In alpha factor mining, HASE outperforms the reported GPT-OSS-120B baseline. HASE also repairs imperfect evaluation components and converges to state-of-the-art performance in circle-packing algorithm discovery. These results show that HASE improves the harness and the solution through one unified agentic process.

### [145] [Online Linear Programming for Multi-Objective Routing in LLM Serving](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.03948)
*Zixi Chen,Yinyu Ye,Zijie Zhou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a principled online routing algorithm for LLM serving that optimizes multiple latency and throughput SLOs under batch size and KV-cache constraints, outperforming heuristic-based methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM request routing strategies are largely heuristic, lack explicit connection to service-level objectives, and provide poor control over latency–throughput trade-offs, especially under tight batching and KV-cache limitations in multi-worker environments. There is a need for a science-based, optimization-driven routing framework that can systematically manage these trade-offs and provide predictable performance across different SLO regimes.

Method: Formulate the LLM request routing problem as an online multi-objective linear program with interpretable decision rewards tied to SLOs. Use a bid-price control policy derived from the LP dual: accept a request if its SLO-weighted benefit exceeds its shadow price. Track and update these dual shadow prices online via warm-started, projected first-order optimization that meets millisecond-scale decision latency. Integrate this routing policy into the Vidur simulator to evaluate under realistic serving conditions.

Result: The proposed optimization-based router, when integrated into the Vidur simulator, achieves substantial performance gains over standard heuristic baselines across various SLO settings. Improvements are observed in end-to-end latency, time-to-first-token, overall throughput, and tail-latency behavior, demonstrating better control of latency–throughput trade-offs under batch-size and KV-cache constraints.

Conclusion: A principled, optimization-driven routing framework based on online linear programming and bid-price control can significantly outperform commonly used heuristic approaches for LLM serving. By explicitly encoding multiple SLOs and updating dual prices online with efficient first-order methods, the approach delivers robust improvements across diverse performance metrics, highlighting the value of a science-based design for request routing in large-scale LLM systems.

Abstract: We study the online routing problem in large language model serving, where requests arrive sequentially and must be dispatched to parallel decode workers under tight batch-size and KV-cache constraints. Unlike widely used routing heuristics that are not tied to explicit service-level objectives (SLOs) and offer limited control over latency-throughput trade-offs, we introduce a multi-objective optimization framework that formulates routing as an online linear programming with interpretable decision rewards. We apply an efficient bid-price control policy based on the online linear programming that admits requests when their SLO-weighted benefit exceeds their shadow prices. To meet millisecond decision requirements, we develop a warm-started, projected first-order updates that track the evolving dual shadow prices online with predictable runtime. We integrate our router into the Vidur simulator and demonstrate substantial improvements over standard baselines across multiple SLO regimes, including end-to-end latency, time-to-first-token, throughput, and tail performance. A big picture from our result: a science-based approach outperforms others based on heuristics.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Bangladesh has an extreme shortage of child mental-health professionals and lacks any culturally and linguistically adapted tool to screen for abuse-related psychological trauma in children. The work aims to design a feasible, ethically sensitive, and low-resource-appropriate AI-supported screening framework rather than a diagnostic system.

Method: The authors design ShishuRaksha AI, a decision-support framework that fuses four screening modalities: (1) validated questionnaires (SDQ, CPSS), (2) Bengali narrative text, (3) House-Tree-Person (HTP) drawing features, and (4) facial affect. The fusion is training-free, uses clinically defined weights, cross-modal attention, and a single-modality override rule to avoid missing strong signals in any one channel. Because real clinical data cannot be collected, they construct a 500-case synthetic benchmark with 23.2% positives, inject four layers of deliberate noise, encode literature-based HTP priors, and then train tree-ensemble surrogate models (excluding facial data) to approximate and evaluate the fusion design using 5-fold stratified cross-validation. They perform ablation, operating-point, subgroup, and calibration analyses.

Result: On the synthetic benchmark, the fused multi-modal surrogate model achieves an AUC of 0.874 (95% CI: 0.834–0.908), outperforming an SDQ-only baseline with AUC 0.756 (95% CI: 0.705–0.803). Analyses show benefits of fusion versus single-modality screening and reveal an urban-rural subgroup performance gap. The framework produces explainable, bilingual risk reports with referral mapping to national services, and sensitivity analyses around operating points and calibration are reported.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates the feasibility and potential value of a culturally adapted, multi-modal, explainable AI decision-support system for early screening of abuse-related psychological trauma in children in Bangladesh, substantially improving performance over SDQ-only screening on synthetic data. However, the authors stress that the work is not a clinical diagnostic tool and that its current evaluation is limited to synthetic data without an external hold-out set, with concerns including text-feature circularity and subgroup disparities. They present the system primarily as a design and methodological contribution and an ethically cautious step toward deployable child-protection screening in low-resource settings, emphasizing the need for future real-world validation and refinement.

Abstract: Bangladesh has an estimated 1.17 mental-health professionals per 100,000 population and only six child psychiatrists nationwide. No Bengali-language, culturally adapted tool exists for early screening of abuse-related psychological trauma in children. We present ShishuRaksha AI, a decision-support (not diagnostic) framework that fuses four screening modalities: validated questionnaires (SDQ, CPSS), Bengali narrative text, House-Tree-Person (HTP) drawing features, and facial affect. The fusion is training-free and clinically weighted, uses cross-modal attention, and includes a single-modality override rule. Every risk score is explained through clinically weighted, perturbation-based additive attribution and rendered as a bilingual (Bangla/English) report with referral routing to national child-protection services (OCC, DSS, NMHH) under the Children Act 2013. No clinical dataset of abused children can be collected ethically at this stage, so we introduce a noise-aware synthetic benchmark (500 cases, 116 positive [23.2%], four deliberate noise layers, literature-grounded HTP priors) and evaluate tree-ensemble surrogates of the fusion design (facial channel excluded) under 5-fold stratified cross-validation. The fused model reaches an AUC of 0.874 [0.834-0.908], against 0.756 [0.705-0.803] for an SDQ-only baseline, with ablation, operating-point, subgroup, and calibration analyses. We state all limitations openly, including synthetic-only data, no held-out set, text-feature circularity, and an urban-rural subgroup gap. This work is a feasibility study and a design contribution toward ethically deployable child-protection screening in low-resource settings.

### [147] [What is Left for Us? Second Scholarship Against the Degradation of Research by AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04049)
*Claudio Novelli,Luciano Floridi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that generative AI risks degrading research by undermining the human practices that form scholarly judgement and academic trust, and calls for a renewed, non-automatable practice of scholarship focused on tacit knowledge, commitment, socialisation, and deep reading.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To address growing concern that generative AI, while improving the appearance and efficiency of research outputs, may erode the underlying human practices through which researchers develop judgement, expertise, and trust, thereby threatening research as an intellectual and formative practice rather than merely a production of outputs.

Method: Conceptual and normative analysis: the authors examine how research practices function as constitutive conditions for knowledge production and validation, identify which elements cannot be automated, and develop the notion of “second scholarship” as a critical, reflective reappropriation of scholarly craft in the age of generative AI.

Result: They identify four central, non-automatable sources and warrants of research—tacit knowledge, personal commitment, socialisation into a scholarly community, and deep reading—and argue that delegating core research tasks to generative AI prevents researchers from properly engaging in these practices, leading to underdeveloped scholarly judgement even if the immediate outputs look improved.

Conclusion: Keeping humans merely as prompters or checkers of AI systems is insufficient to safeguard research as a formative intellectual practice. Instead, research communities must consciously reaffirm and protect the aspects of scholarship that cannot be delegated to AI, embracing what the authors call “second scholarship”: a deliberate, critically informed recommitment to human scholarly craft and responsibility in light of AI’s limits.

Abstract: We argue that generative AI can degrade research by eroding the very practices through which scholarly judgement is formed and academic trust is built. As constitutive conditions for the production and validation of knowledge, these practices cannot be reduced to the final outputs of research, which is what AI so effectively simulate. Accordingly, when researchers delegate central tasks of inquiry to systems like Large Language Models, they may stop enacting these practices and, with them, lose access to the formation they provide. An individual research output generated by AI may even appear improved but the researcher behind it fails to develop. Against this risk, merely keeping humans in the loop as prompters or quality checkers of AI outputs is insufficient to preserve research as a site of intellectual formation. What is needed instead is a renewed commitment to research as a lived practice in which judgement is formed gradually, often through frictions, and participation in a scholarly community. We defend it because it rests on four sources and warrants of research that cannot be automated: tacit knowledge, personal commitment, socialisation, and deep reading. This practice enacts what we call second scholarship, by which we understand the reappropriation of scholarly craft, chosen out of a critical experience of what generative AI can and cannot do. What cannot and should not be delegated becomes what research communities must value and answer for. This is what is left for us.

### [148] [PLACEMEM: Toward a Compute-Aware Memory Plane for Lifelong Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04089)
*Sukanta Ganguly*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: PLACEMEM proposes a correction-aware memory control plane for lifelong AI agents, using versioned capsules that unify data, provenance, validity, and reusable runtime state to enable persistent, evolvable memory without recomputing full history each turn.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing lifelong/agentic systems rely on large context windows or retrieval over logs, often recomputing history every turn or reusing opaque, stale runtime state. These approaches make it hard to correct past errors, track provenance, or manage state consistently across backends, especially under live updates. The authors are motivated to define a systems-level memory abstraction that supports persistence, evolution, correction, and reuse in a principled, implementation-ready way.

Method: They introduce PLACEMEM, a systems position and control-plane prototype where agent memory is represented as versioned capsules. Each capsule encapsulates semantics (content), provenance (where it came from), validity (whether it’s still correct), and reusable runtime state under a single identity that can be corrected and invalidated. The prototype integrates with vLLM and includes: persistent capsule storage, concurrency-safe invalidation mechanisms, routing via an OpenAI-compatible sidecar that is aware of KV/state reuse, a typed metadata contract for capsules, and a benchmark harness to evaluate latency, reuse, and post-correction behavior. It uses capsules to drive prompt-level text retrieval, KV-aware routing, and cascading invalidation across streaming backends; deeper replay integration is deferred as future work.

Result: The authors obtain an executable control-plane artifact that shows how correction-aware, capsule-based memory can operate in a real serving stack. It supports persistent capsule state, safe invalidation under concurrency, and routing that can reuse runtime state while remaining aware of corrections. The benchmark harness can quantify first-token latency improvements and reuse effects, and can observe how the system behaves after memory corrections; although specific quantitative numbers are not given in the abstract, the prototype validates feasibility and provides empirical hooks for evaluation.

Conclusion: PLACEMEM demonstrates that representing agent memory as versioned, correction-aware capsules is a viable systems design for lifelong agents. The prototype shows practical control-plane mechanisms for integrating such memory with modern LLM serving (vLLM, OpenAI-style APIs) and establishes a roadmap toward deeper replay-aware serving and lifelong-agent infrastructure. The work is positioned as both a running artifact and a design blueprint for more robust, evolvable, and debuggable agent memory in future systems.

Abstract: Lifelong agents need more than larger context windows and better retrieval. They need memories that can persist, evolve, and be corrected without forcing the serving stack to recompute the same history on every turn or silently reuse stale runtime state. We present PLACEMEM as a systems position on lifelong-agent memory, instantiated by an executable control-plane prototype. The central claim is that agent memory should be represented as versioned capsules that unify semantics, provenance, validity, and reusable runtime state under one correction-aware identity. In the current prototype, capsules drive prompt-level text retrieval, KV-aware routing, and cascading invalidation over live streamed backends; prospective layer-frontier replay is intentionally framed as a deeper integration agenda rather than a claimed engine feature. We describe a vLLM-first prototype with persistent capsule state, concurrency-safe invalidation, an OpenAI-compatible routing sidecar, a typed metadata contract, and a benchmark harness that measures live first-token latency, reuse, and post-correction behavior. The result is both an executable artifact that demonstrates correction-aware control-plane behavior today and a concrete roadmap for replay-aware serving integration in future lifelong-agent systems.

</details>


### [149] [Forethought: Verifiable Reasoning from Neurosymbolic Primitive Programming](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04096)
*Vishvesh Bhat,Jay Vaghasiya,Emmanuel Anaya Gonzalez*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Forethought is a neurosymbolic reasoning system that turns model reasoning into explicit, verifiable programs built from symbolic and neural primitives, yielding large accuracy gains and competitive performance with far less post-training cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing agentic workflows rely on long chains of thought embedded in model weights and context, which are expensive at inference, hard to verify step-by-step, and opaque to inspection or modification. Test-time scaling improves reasoning but keeps it entangled in the model and non-auditable. There is a need for a reasoning approach that is explicit, verifiable, cheaper, and model-agnostic.

Method: They design Forethought, a neurosymbolic reasoning system where reasoning is expressed as explicit programs written in a domain-specific language. These programs are composed from a library of symbolic and neural primitives, and executed via a tool-calling kernel. The reasoning programs are concrete artifacts that can be inspected, modified, and reused, separating reasoning structure from model weights. They evaluate Forethought on five benchmarks against vanilla prompting, RL-based scaffolds, and prompt-evolution methods.

Result: Forethought yields about a 30% relative accuracy improvement over base models across five benchmarks. It enables smaller models to match or exceed the capabilities of frontier models. A non-reasoning model augmented with Forethought competes with a dedicated reasoning model while requiring roughly three orders of magnitude less post-training investment. It also maintains model-agnosticism and auditability.

Conclusion: Explicit neurosymbolic reasoning programs, as implemented in Forethought, provide a more verifiable, auditable, and cost-efficient alternative to test-time scaling of chain-of-thought. By separating reasoning structure from model weights and using a DSL over symbolic and neural primitives, Forethought significantly boosts performance, lets small models rival frontier models, and reduces post-training costs while remaining compatible with different base models.

Abstract: Current agentic workflows usually involve decomposing user requests into sequences of tool calls with correctly resolved parameters, the results of which are processed through reasoning traces in the language model's context window. The prevailing route to improve such reasoning is test-time scaling, which trains models to search over long chains of thought; but the resulting capability is entangled in model weights, is not verifiable step-by-step, and is costly at inference. We present Forethought, a neurosymbolic reasoning system that instead treats reasoning as an explicit, verifiable program, that builds from a library of symbolic and neural primitives which are composed through a domain-specific language. The result are reasoning programs, which are concrete representations of the model's work, and as such can be inspected and modified before deployment. Instantiated as a tool-calling execution kernel and evaluated across five benchmarks, Forethought improves base-model accuracy by about 30% relative and outperforms vanilla prompting, reinforcement learning scaffolds, and prompt-evolution methods, enabling small models to match or exceed frontier models capabilities. In a direct comparison, a non-reasoning model augmented with Forethought competes with a dedicated reasoning model while requiring roughly three orders of magnitude less post-training investment, and remains model-agnostic and auditable.

### [150] [Language models guide symbolic equation discovery by controlling search](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04156)
*Zikai Xie,Wenmei Li,Man Luo,Jun Jiang,Linjiang Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces LLM-PySR, where a language model controls symbolic regression search instead of directly proposing equations, achieving better accuracy–complexity–stability–cost trade-offs and discovering meaningful scientific relations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scientific equation discovery needs both domain knowledge and rigorous numerical validation. Existing symbolic regression methods are numerically grounded but struggle with combinatorial search spaces, while LLM-based methods often ask the model to output or choose final formulas, which can be unreliable or opaque. The authors want a framework that leverages language models’ prior knowledge to guide exploration without entrusting them with final equation selection.

Method: They systematically compare different roles for language models in equation discovery: (1) equation author, (2) candidate decider, and (3) search controller, as well as end-to-end LLM and purely numerical baselines. PySR then enumerates and fits candidate expressions, while deterministic numerical metrics decide which expressions to keep. They evaluate performance across 74 AI-Feynman benchmark equations, seven complex formula-recovery tasks, and an independent real-world battery dataset.

Result: Search-control with LLM-PySR yields the best observed trade-off among accuracy, expression complexity, stability across runs, and computational cost, outperforming both direct LLM equation proposals and purely numerical methods on the tested benchmarks. On the real-world battery dataset, LLM-PySR discovers a simple piecewise-linear equation relating early voltage-curve displacement to cycle life, demonstrating practical scientific insight.

Conclusion: Language models are most effective in scientific equation discovery when used to control and structure the search process, not to directly choose which equations survive. By constraining LLMs to guide hypothesis generation and delegating evaluation and selection to symbolic regression with deterministic metrics, one can obtain accurate, interpretable, and stable equations at reasonable computational cost.

Abstract: Scientific equation discovery must combine broad domain priors with strict numerical testing. Symbolic regression supplies numerical grounding but faces a combinatorial search space, whereas many language-model systems ask the model to propose or select formulas directly. We test a different division of labour. We compare role specifications in which the language model acts as equation author, candidate decider or search controller, alongside end-to-end language-model and purely numerical baselines. In the controller setting we propose here, implemented as LLM-PySR, language models specify variables, operators, transformations and search depth; symbolic regression enumerates and fits expressions; and deterministic metrics govern retention. Across 74 AI-Feynman equations and seven complex formula-recovery tasks, search control achieved the strongest observed balance of accuracy, complexity, stability and cost. On an independent battery dataset, LLM-PySR identified a compact piecewise-linear relation between early voltage-curve displacement and cycle life. The results suggest that language models should shape hypothesis exploration rather than decide which equations survive.

*Asif Zaman,Romona Magdalene Sarkar,Sabiha Khair Ohi,Iftekharul Mobin*

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Market manipulation undermines trust in trading platforms and is hard to spot at scale, especially without labeled ground truth data. Existing systems often rely on supervised methods or manual rules that struggle with evolving fraud strategies, so there is a need for an automated, data-driven, and label-free way to surface suspicious trades for further investigation.

Method: The paper constructs a clustering-based pipeline for fraud detection on a large financial transaction dataset (2012–2024). It applies K-Means++ to group trades in an unsupervised manner and then maps resulting clusters to market-manipulation categories via heuristic thresholds based on domain practices. The quality of clustering is assessed using the Silhouette Score as an internal validation metric. Suspicious clusters are those that significantly deviate from normal trading patterns, and their constituent trades are labeled into types such as spoofing, pump-and-dump, insider trading, fake breakouts, or left unclassified if they do not match heuristics well.

Result: From roughly one million transactions, the system flags 2.02% as suspicious. Among these, 51.10% are aligned with spoofing patterns, 0.10% with pump-and-dump, 0.55% with insider trading, 1.43% with fake breakout behavior, and 46.83% remain unclassified. The clustering achieves a Silhouette Score of 0.561, indicating moderate separation between inferred behavior groups despite the absence of labeled ground truth.

Conclusion: The study shows that an unsupervised, K-Means++-based clustering pipeline can feasibly detect and categorize potentially fraudulent market activities from large-scale trade data without labeled examples. The moderate Silhouette Score supports the structural soundness of the clusters, and the distribution of suspicious categories suggests practical utility as an investigative tool, though the substantial unclassified portion and lack of external validation indicate that further refinement and expert review are necessary.

Abstract: Market manipulation is the dubious practice of manipulating stock prices in order to make a quick profit, which truly degrades confidence on trading platforms. We implemented an unsupervised fraud-detection toolkit that begins with K-Means++ clustering to address this issue. A dataset of roughly one million financial transactions from 2012 to 2024 is used. In order to identify fraudulent trades and categorize them using market practice heuristic thresholds, the study suggests a clustering-based pipeline. The method highlights 2.02% of trades as suspicious where 51.10% clearly indicate spoofing, 0.10% indicate pump and dump, 0.55% indicate insider trading, 1.43% indicate a fake breakout, and 46.83% are unclassified. Despite the lack of ground truth, the model's performance is confirmed by a Silhouette Score of 0.561.

### [153] [Unsupervised Features Mining via Activation Geometry](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04222)
*Amit LeVi,Elad David,Max Fomin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes MAG, an unsupervised method that uses changes in LLM activations caused by a natural-language instruction to mine internal reasoning features, show their approximate linear structure, steer model behavior, and select better datasets for classifier training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing interpretability methods usually rely on human-labeled concepts, which can import human biases and do not directly expose how LLMs internally represent reasoning features. The authors want a more direct, unsupervised way to uncover reasoning-related features from model activations and to understand how these features relate to the model's own judgments and world knowledge, as well as to use them for practical tasks like steering and dataset selection.

Method: They prepend a fixed natural-language instruction Q (describing a reasoning feature, e.g., malignancy, desert-ness) to every prompt p, and compare model activations with and without Q at a chosen readout layer, computing m(Q|p) - m(p). These activation differences define Mining via Activation Geometry (MAG) features. The authors explore eight variants of MAG, study whether these features can be summarized as single directions in activation space, test linearity and vector steering (injecting directions to change decisions), and apply a related representation (RFD-based similarity) to measure similarity between datasets when selecting training data for prompt-injection classifiers.

Result: The mined activation features align well with the model’s own world knowledge and judgments. Many reasoning features can be approximated as single activation directions, with varying degrees of linearity across features. Injecting these directions into the model’s activations can causally steer its decisions in line with the targeted reasoning feature. For dataset selection, ordinary activation similarity is nearly uninformative about downstream classifier performance, while RFD-based similarity (from their method) yields very strong selection performance: 94.7% Top-1 and 100% Top-2 accuracy in picking the best datasets for prompt-injection classifier probes.

Conclusion: Unsupervised Mining via Activation Geometry provides a simple and effective way to extract reasoning features directly from LLM activations using natural-language instructions. These features have approximately linear structure, can be used for activation steering to change model behavior, and serve as powerful signals for tasks like dataset selection, outperforming naive activation-based similarity measures.

Abstract: Interpretability methods aim to reveal the features represented inside large language models (LLMs). Many existing methods begin with labeled examples of a human-defined concept that may reflect human biases, and then identify how that concept is represented within the model, for example in its activation space or through other decomposition methods. We introduce \emph{Mining via Activation Geometry} (MAG), a simple unsupervised framework for extracting reasoning features from model activations by prepending the same natural-language instruction $Q$ to every input $p$, where $Q$ defines the reasoning feature of interest, such as ``Can this object be found in the desert?'' or ``Is this prompt malicious?'' We measure how the instruction changes the model's internal representation using $m(Q \mid p) - m(p)$ at a single readout point. We explore eight different MAGs. The extracted reasoning features predict the models' own world understanding and judgment, can be approximated into a single activation direction, we found that some features are more linearly represented and some less, this linear representation, which is vector steering, can change the LLMs' decisions through activation steering by injecting reasoning features. Finally, we use the same method to select the best training datasets for prompt-injection classifier probes: while similarity between ordinary activations is almost unrelated to downstream performance, RFD-based similarity achieves $94.7\%$ Top-1 and $100\%$ Top-2 accuracy.

*Bogdan Banu*

Method: Establish a typed interface correspondence between gene regulatory networks and agentic software via polynomial functors and wiring diagrams. Add an epistemic topology layer that uses the observation structure of wiring diagrams to induce Kripke-style knowledge operators, from which four theorems about multi-agent scaling are proved. Implement a reference software library with extensive tests and examples to validate feasibility.

Result: Five biological motifs are successfully mapped to concrete software design patterns addressing noise suppression, layered security, resource governance, neuro-symbolic integration, and spatial coordination in agent systems. The Agentic Operad provides formal composition rules and yields provable error suppression bounds for feed-forward topologies. The epistemic topology generates Kripke-style knowledge operators and supports four theorems—covering error amplification, sequential penalty, parallel acceleration, and tool density scaling—whose qualitative predictions match behaviors reported in multi-agent benchmarks. A full reference implementation with 1,813 tests and 116 examples demonstrates that the theory can be realized in practical code.

Conclusion: Biological control motifs, when abstracted through typed interfaces and categorical wiring diagrams, offer a principled design language for LLM-based agent systems. The Agentic Operad and accompanying epistemic topology give formal tools to reason about reliability, knowledge flow, and scaling behavior in multi-agent architectures, with theoretical predictions aligning with empirical benchmarks. This suggests that agentic LLM systems can be engineered more systematically, with compositional guarantees on error suppression and coordination, rather than relying on ad-hoc prompt engineering alone.

Abstract: The transition of Large Language Models (LLMs) from passive generators to autonomous agents has introduced significant challenges in reliability, security, and state management. Current agentic architectures are often constructed ad-hoc, prone to hallucination cascades, infinite loops, and prompt injection attacks. This paper argues that many of these failure modes can be analyzed using control motifs long studied in systems biology, provided the comparison is made at the level of typed interfaces and coordination structure rather than literal biological mechanism.
  We develop a typed interface correspondence between Gene Regulatory Networks and agentic software systems using polynomial functors and wiring diagrams. Five biological motifs are mapped to composable software design patterns: Coherent Feed-Forward Loops for noise suppression, Adaptive Immunity for layered security, Mitochondrial Signaling for resource governance, Endosymbiosis for neuro-symbolic integration, and Morphogen Diffusion for spatially varying coordination. An epistemic topology layer derives Kripke-style knowledge operators from the wiring diagram's observation structure and proves four predictive theorems for multi-agent scaling.
  The core contributions are: (1) the Agentic Operad, a typed syntax for agent composition with provable error suppression bounds for feed-forward topologies; (2) an epistemic topology with four theorems (error amplification, sequential penalty, parallel acceleration, and tool density scaling) whose qualitative predictions are consistent with published multi-agent benchmarks; and (3) a six-layer progression from structure through development, grounded in autonomous learning frameworks and convergence proxies from the empirical literature. A reference implementation with 1,813 tests and 116 examples illustrates practical feasibility.

### [155] [Progress- and Reliability-Oriented Group Policy Optimization for Agentic Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04242)
*Mingxuan Fan,Peiyang Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: ProGPO is a learned-critic-free, step-level group-based RL method that ensures context-consistent action comparisons and augments sparse peer signals with reliable potential-based credit, improving LLM agents on long-horizon tasks like ALFWorld and WebShop.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing group-based RL for LLM agents has shifted from trajectory-level to step-level learning to get finer-grained policy updates, but step-level advantage estimation is highly sensitive to how action groups are formed. Grouping by broad state keys yields good coverage but compares actions drawn from incompatible histories, while enforcing strict historical consistency gives fairer comparisons but creates small, fragmented groups with weak or missing peer signals. There is a need for a method that preserves fair, context-consistent comparisons without losing learning signal, and that does so without relying on a learned value critic, which can be unstable or expensive.

Method: The authors propose ProGPO (Progress- and Reliability-Oriented Group Policy Optimization), a group-based RL algorithm that operates at the step level and does not use a learned critic. ProGPO (1) enforces exact-prefix (history-consistent) action comparisons so that each step is only compared with peers that share the same interaction history, and (2) augments these often-sparse peer comparisons with transition credit based on rollout-derived state potentials. To obtain robust potentials, ProGPO uses semantic expansion to relate similar states and applies inverse-variance fusion across different history depths, thereby combining multiple noisy estimates into a more reliable signal. The resulting advantages drive policy updates for the LLM agent.

Result: On two long-horizon, interactive benchmarks—ALFWorld and WebShop—using Qwen2.5-1.5B-Instruct as the base agent, ProGPO outperforms comparable agentic RL baselines under roughly matched computational budgets. Additional experiments using the larger Qwen2.5-3B-Instruct model indicate that ProGPO’s benefits persist at increased model scales, suggesting that the approach scales favorably with model size.

Conclusion: ProGPO provides a practical and effective step-level group-based RL method for LLM agents that preserves context-consistent action comparisons and supplies reliable credit assignment without a learned critic. By combining rollout-based state potentials, semantic expansion, and inverse-variance fusion, it addresses the trade-off between fair comparisons and sufficient learning signal. Empirical gains on ALFWorld and WebShop, along with evidence of scalability to larger models, suggest that ProGPO is a promising direction for training LLM agents on complex, long-horizon tasks.

Abstract: Group-based reinforcement learning (RL) has become an effective paradigm for improving large language model agents on long-horizon interactive tasks. To obtain finer-grained policy updates than trajectory-level optimization, recent work has moved toward step-level group-based RL, where intermediate steps are grouped and compared within a rollout batch. However, step-level advantage estimation is sensitive to how groups are formed: grouping by broad state keys improves coverage but may compare actions taken under different histories, while enforcing historical consistency yields fairer comparisons at the cost of fragmented groups and missing peer-comparison signal. In this paper, we propose ProGPO (Progress- and Reliability-Oriented Group Policy Optimization), a learned-critic-free method for context-consistent step-level learning. ProGPO keeps exact-prefix action comparison, and complements sparse peer comparisons with transition credit derived from rollout-based state potentials. To estimate these potentials reliably, ProGPO combines semantic expansion with inverse-variance fusion across history depths. We evaluate ProGPO on two challenging agentic tasks, ALFWorld and WebShop, with Qwen2.5-1.5B-Instruct. Results show that ProGPO improves over matched agentic RL baselines under comparable computational overhead, and additional Qwen2.5-3B-Instruct experiments further test the scalability of the proposed method.

### [156] [Shortcut Learning in Legal Judgment Prediction: Empirical Evidence from the UK Employment Tribunal](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04261)
*Joe Watson,Joana Ribeiro de Faria,Marcus Tomalin,Måns Magnusson,Huiyuan Xie,Hao Tian Yeung,Felix Steffek*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that many legal judgment prediction models look good only because they exploit outcome-revealing clues hidden in already-written judicial texts, but if you remove these clues, models can still perform well using more genuine predictive signals.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Legal judgment prediction (LJP) has gained popularity, but most systems are trained on post-hoc judicial documents (e.g., full decisions) rather than information available before judgment. This raises the concern that models merely pick up on explicit or subtle cues in the text that reveal the outcome, inflating performance and undermining the claim that they are truly predictive. The authors want to empirically test how much shortcut learning via such leakage drives LJP performance, using a real-world tribunal dataset, and to see whether the field can remain viable if such leakage is addressed.

Method: The authors construct a dataset of 33,158 individual claim-level records from UK Employment Tribunal (UKET) decisions. They attempt to predict claim outcomes from two main text sources: (1) the claim texts themselves and (2) case summaries extracted by large language models. They develop and compare multiple models, from simple interpretable classifiers based on TF–IDF features to more complex black-box large language models. To study shortcut learning, they (a) obtain human judgments about which parts of the text contain outcome-revealing cues (leakage), (b) stratify test samples according to leakage levels to observe performance differences, (c) train a model exclusively on the small subset of features (about 4%) identified as leakage to assess its predictive power, and (d) retrain models after masking or removing these leakage features to measure the effect on performance (Macro-F1).

Result: They find that headline performance scores for LJP are misleading when models are trained on post-hoc judicial text: performance is significantly higher in cases where the test texts contain outcome-revealing cues. A model trained only on the 4% of features marked as leakage achieves strong predictive performance and even surpasses human experts, indicating that the model relies heavily on these shortcuts. However, when they actively audit and mask the leakage features and retrain, the performance drop is minimal in terms of Macro-F1, showing that the models can still learn genuine predictive patterns from the remaining text. Overall, the empirical results demonstrate both the extent of shortcut learning in LJP and the resilience of models once leakage is controlled.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that much of the apparent success of legal judgment prediction models using judicial texts is driven by shortcut learning from outcome-revealing artifacts in post-hoc decisions. This validates concerns that current evaluations overstate true forecasting capabilities. Nonetheless, the findings are cautiously optimistic: if researchers treat judicial texts as potentially contaminated and incorporate systematic auditing and leakage masking into their pipelines, LJP remains a viable research direction. Models can still capture meaningful predictive signals after leakage removal, but future work must explicitly control for and report on such shortcut phenomena to avoid inflated performance claims.

Abstract: Current Legal Judgment Prediction (LJP) is constrained by its reliance on post-hoc judicial materials, increasing the likelihood that models perform retrospective classification rather than true forecasting. This paper empirically investigates shortcut learning in this context by studying claim-level outcome prediction in UK Employment Tribunal (UKET) decisions. Using a corpus of 33,158 individual claims, we predict outcomes from claim texts and LLM-extracted case summaries, evaluating models ranging from interpretable TF-IDF-based classifiers to black-box LLMs. While headline predictive performance figures appear strong, we demonstrate that such performance in LJP systems trained on post-hoc judicial text can be driven by the retrospective nature of the source material. Stratifying the test data by human judgments of leakage reveals that performance increases where outcome-revealing cues are embedded in the narrative. Moreover, a model trained on just the 4% of features identified as leakage achieves high performance, outperforming human experts. These findings substantiate concerns that LJP performance may be exaggerated by linguistic artefacts. Yet this vulnerability is not fatal to the research agenda. Instead, post-hoc judgments might be treated as potentially contaminated texts, requiring active auditing. Retraining models after masking leakage features results in only a negligible reduction in Macro-F1. Hence, while models will opportunistically exploit shortcuts when available, they remain capable of extracting useful predictive signals when these artefacts are removed.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional ransomware detectors relying on static signatures or single monolithic models fail under concept drift, evasion tactics, and behavioural polymorphism of modern ransomware. There is a need for adaptive systems that can handle evolving threats, quantify uncertainty, and involve human analysts when the model is unsure, all while remaining interpretable and auditable.

Method: The authors design Agentic SABRE, a neuro-symbolic, multi-agent framework that fuses semantic (representation-based) features with behavioural telemetry over time windows. Each agent uses Monte Carlo Dropout to estimate epistemic uncertainty. A decision-layer orchestrator performs triage based on a risk score and an uncertainty budget: automatically containing high-risk, high-confidence cases and escalating uncertain or borderline ones to human analysts. Post-hoc explainability tools—gradient saliency, permutation importance, and counterfactual analysis—are integrated to provide local and global interpretability of decisions.

Result: On the RDset and RanSMAP datasets, SABRE maintains an AUC of 1.0 on saturated semantic datasets and improves robustness when behavioural signals are weak. It reduces false escalations by up to 4.9% at the same recall level while keeping predictive uncertainty well calibrated. Counterfactual experiments show that both semantic and behavioural model decisions can be flipped with bounded input changes, suggesting stable, well-structured decision boundaries.

Conclusion: Agentic SABRE offers an effective, adaptive ransomware detection approach that unifies semantic and behavioural evidence, incorporates uncertainty into automated response decisions, and meaningfully involves human analysts when needed. Its strong empirical performance, calibrated uncertainty, and explainability features support its suitability for real-world ransomware defence under evolving threat conditions.

Abstract: Ransomware has evolved into a complex, adaptive, and fast-moving adversary category in which static signatures and monolithic classifiers fail to generalise under concept drift, evasion, and behavioural polymorphism. In this paper, we present Agentic SABRE (Semantic-Behavioural Arbitration for Ransomware Evaluation), an uncertainty-aware, neuro-symbolic, multi-agent framework for adaptive ransomware detection. SABRE fuses semantic, representation-based evidence with behavioural, time-window forensic telemetry and employs Monte Carlo Dropout inference to quantify epistemic uncertainty for each agent. We introduce a decision-layer orchestrator that performs risk- and uncertainty-aware triage using two interpretable thresholds: a risk score and an uncertainty budget. High-confidence, high-risk samples are automatically contained, while uncertain or borderline cases are escalated to human analysts, establishing a flexible computational contract between autonomous response and analyst oversight. To support auditability and trust, SABRE integrates post-hoc explainability mechanisms, including gradient saliency, permutation importance, and counterfactual analysis, enabling both local and global interpretation of agent decisions. Extensive evaluation on RDset and RanSMAP demonstrates that Agentic SABRE preserves perfect discrimination on saturated semantic datasets, with AUC equal to 1.0, while improving robustness under weak behavioural signals. It achieves up to a 4.9 percent relative reduction in false escalations at equal recall while maintaining calibrated predictive uncertainty. Counterfactual analysis further shows that semantic and behavioural decisions can be reversed with bounded perturbation cost, indicating stable and interpretable decision boundaries.

Experiments across six domains show that human participation can substantially improve task completion and failure recovery, but the gains depend on when, how, and by whom human input is exercised.

### [159] [Do GUI Agents Believe Their Eyes? Diagnosing State-Belief Reliance on Pixels versus Structure](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04334)
*Guijia Zhang,Harry Yang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces and measures 'visual state reliance' in multimodal GUI agents, quantifying how much their interface understanding truly depends on pixels versus serialized structure (DOM/accessibility), revealing that current agents over-rely on structured text despite high image-only accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multimodal GUI agents are evaluated mainly on task success or grounding but not on whether their beliefs about UI state are genuinely visually grounded. Because agents can access both pixels and structured representations, they may exploit shortcuts via the structure, undermining robustness, security, and the intended visual grounding. There was no principled way to measure or attribute an agent’s state beliefs to pixels vs structure vs priors.

Method: The authors formalize 'visual state reliance' as an attribution problem: given beliefs about interface state, how much comes from pixels vs structure vs priors? They construct 310 real-world probes across web, mobile, and desktop interfaces, and perform paired interventions where either the pixel view or the structured channel is perturbed while the other is kept correct. Models answer forced-choice questions under these single-channel conflicts, with fully deterministic scoring and no model-generated items/judging. They run this across five commercial multimodal models, including agents that act via indexed actions vs coordinate actions, and also conduct white-box ablations to trace how structural values propagate through the network.

Result: Across all tested models, when pixels and structure disagree about textual state, models’ beliefs systematically defer to the structured representation, even though their image-only accuracy for the same items is near ceiling. This yields a consistently positive 'Perception-Fusion Gap'—the fraction of cases where the model can perceive correctly from pixels yet resolves conflicts in favor of structure. Non-textual identity information (icons, colors, etc.) remains mostly governed by pixels. The over-reliance effect is specific to serialized text plus indexed-action interfaces; coordinate-action agents, which act via screen coordinates instead of element indices, show much less vulnerability. White-box analysis reveals that a single copied structural field can drive the misattribution. In live end-to-end environments, such conflicts lead to wrong actions and real task failures.

Conclusion: Visual state reliance is a distinct, measurable property of multimodal GUI agents that existing benchmarks miss. Current state-of-the-art agents often exhibit poor visual grounding of textual state, preferring structured channels over pixels even when this contradicts what they can visually recognize. This structural shortcutting is localized to particular channels and architectural choices and can directly cause harmful action errors in real applications. The proposed framework and metric (Perception-Fusion Gap) provide a diagnostic tool for designing, evaluating, and hardening GUI agents that are genuinely visually grounded.

Abstract: Multimodal GUI agents read an interface through two redundant channels: the rendered pixels of a screenshot and a serialized structure such as a DOM or accessibility tree. Before acting, an agent forms a belief about the current interface state, but existing benchmarks score task success, element grounding, or attack resistance and do not ask whether that belief is drawn from the pixels. We formalize visual state reliance, the attribution of a state belief to pixels, structure, or priors, and measure it with paired single-channel interventions over 310 real web, mobile, and desktop probes. Every probe is scored by deterministic forced choice, with no model-generated item and no model judge. Our central metric is the Perception-Fusion Gap, the fraction of probes a model perceives correctly yet resolves toward structure under conflict. Across five models from three vendors, textual state beliefs defer to structure while image-only accuracy stays near ceiling, and Perception-Fusion Gap is positive for every model; non-text identity, by contrast, stays largely pixel-bound. The substitution is specific to the serialized-text and indexed-action channel, and coordinate-action agents are largely immune. For textual conflicts, a white-box ablation traces the effect to a single copied structural value, and in two live environments the conflict drives wrong actions and real task failure. Visual state reliance therefore gives a measurable diagnostic of whether agent state beliefs are visually grounded, and the errors it exposes propagate to actions.

### [160] [Server-side Anti-cheat in FPS games for Aimbot detection using Deep learning and Machine learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04336)
*Siddhesh A. Dhinge,Shubham G. Sukum,Harsh S. Ranjane,Ruturajsingh R. Rajput,Jyoti H. Jadhav*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents YAACS, a server-side system that uses deep-learning-based sequence modeling to detect aimbot cheaters in FPS games with low false positives.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Cheating in online multiplayer FPS games, especially via tools like aimbots, undermines fairness and player experience. Existing anti-cheat mechanisms struggle to reliably distinguish cheaters from skilled players while keeping false accusations low. There is a need for robust, server-side methods that can detect aimbot use using in-game behavioral data and temporal patterns.

Method: The authors focus on aimbot detection and define a set of discriminative features, including time-series data such as aim velocity, number of shots, and distance to target, as well as behavioral indicators like utility usage and movement patterns. They build a server-side classifier called YAACS consisting of a data parser, a deep learning model, and connection utilities for integration with game servers. YAACS uses a Stacked LSTM with Dense layers trained on sequences of 128 ticks (with specified negative and positive tick deltas) to model temporal context, and it is compared against a Decision Tree baseline.

Result: YAACS with a Stacked LSTM and Dense layers achieves 88.6% classification accuracy with a false positive rate of 0.97%. The Decision Tree baseline attains higher raw accuracy at 96.2% but suffers a higher false positive rate of 2.68%, which is 2.76 times worse than the best LSTM configuration. This shows that although the LSTM has slightly lower overall accuracy, it significantly reduces false positives, which is crucial in this application.

Conclusion: Incorporating temporal context through sequence modeling is essential for effective aimbot detection with minimal false accusations. The proposed YAACS system demonstrates that deep learning models, particularly Stacked LSTMs using tick-based sequences of gameplay data, can outperform simpler models like Decision Trees in terms of the key metric of false positive rate, making them more suitable for practical deployment in FPS anti-cheat systems.

Abstract: Modern video games are becoming more complex day by day. Most of these modern games are multiplayer first-person shooter (FPS) games. The rising popularity of FPS games emphasizes the need to combat cheating for fair and enjoyable gaming. As the number of players using cheating techniques like aimbots, wallhacks, and speed hacks is also increasing, we need a way to detect players who are using cheating tools to gain an unfair advantage over regular players. In this system, we focus exclusively on detecting aimbot cheats. Players who use aimbot cheats generally do not prioritize other aspects of the game. To distinguish between regular and cheating players, we identify specific features encompassing time-series data such as aim velocity, number of shots, distance to target, and more, along with behavioral data such as utility usage, player movement, and other gameplay patterns. Utilizing these features, we construct a server-side aimbot detection classifier named 'YAACS'. YAACS comprises a parser, a deep learning model, and intermediary connection utilities designed for integration with the game server. The proposed system achieves a classification accuracy of 88.6% with a false positive rate of 0.97% using a Stacked LSTM with Dense layers trained on sequences of 128 ticks (Tick Delta Negative=56, Tick Delta Positive=24), outperforming the Decision Tree baseline which achieves a higher accuracy of 96.2% but at a false positive rate of 2.68%, 2.76x worse than the best LSTM configuration. These results demonstrate that incorporating temporal context through sequence modelling is critical for minimising false accusations in FPS cheat detection.

</details>

  <summary>Details</summary>

Abstract: We present Nemotron-Labs-3-Puzzle-75B-A9B, a compressed variant of Nemotron-3-Super optimized for interactive deployment. We designed the model to maximize server throughput under high user throughput constraints. In interactive serving workloads on a single 8xB200 node, Puzzle-75B-A9B achieves approximately 2x higher server throughput than Nemotron-3-Super at matched user throughput constraints. In ultra-long-context deployment on a single H100 GPU, the compressed model increases 1M-token concurrency from 1 request to 8 requests. Puzzle-75B-A9B is constructed using a multi-stage pipeline that combines the Iterative Puzzle compression framework with knowledge distillation, reinforcement learning, quantization, and a Multi-Token Prediction head. The compression process jointly optimizes heterogeneous MoE pruning, active parameter budget, and Mamba pruning to improve inference efficiency while preserving model quality. We evaluate Puzzle-75B-A9B on a broad suite of reasoning, coding, multilingual, long-context, and agentic benchmarks. Despite substantial compression, the model retains strong downstream accuracy relative to the parent model across a wide range of tasks. These results demonstrate that large hybrid MoE models can be substantially optimized for deployment efficiency while maintaining strong downstream capability.

### [162] [Decentralized Aggregation of LLM Predictions via Wagering Mechanisms](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04389)
*Yuhong Luo,David M. Pennock,Xintong Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces WALLA, a wagering-based scheme to aggregate predictions from multiple LLMs in a decentralized, incentive-compatible, and robust way, achieving performance comparable to centralized aggregation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As multiple specialized LLMs are used together, there is a need to aggregate their predictions effectively, especially when each has private data, tools, or domain expertise. Existing aggregation often assumes centralized access to models' internals or trust in truthful reporting, which is unrealistic in decentralized or strategic settings. The authors aim to design an aggregation mechanism that (i) does not require access to private information, (ii) is robust to strategic behavior, and (iii) can learn how much to trust each model based on its actual predictive advantage.

Method: The authors design a family of wagering mechanisms called WALLA (Wagering mechanisms for LLM Aggregation). Each model outputs both a prediction and a learned wager, with the aggregated prediction being a wager-weighted combination. They modify traditional wagering mechanisms by adding a leave-one-out baseline into the net payout function. This structure ensures that (1) truthfully reporting beliefs is a dominant strategy, (2) models are incentivized to wager in proportion to their expected scoring-rule advantage, and (3) wager policies can be optimized without requiring perfectly optimal predictions. They further propose two specific variants of WALLA that trade off statistical normality and no-arbitrage properties, while keeping the mechanism’s worst-case budget deficit bounded. Empirical evaluation is done on QA and forecasting tasks with heterogeneous models and private information.

Result: Empirically, WALLA achieves predictive performance comparable to strong centralized aggregation methods, even though it operates in a decentralized, information-limited setting. The mechanism successfully learns advantage-aligned aggregation weights, so better-performing models contribute more heavily. It also captures uncertainty information and preserves incentive-compatibility for truthful prediction and appropriate wagering in experiments across QA and forecasting benchmarks with heterogeneous models and private-information setups.

Conclusion: WALLA provides a practical and theoretically grounded approach for aggregating predictions from multiple LLMs when models are strategic and hold private information. By aligning wagers with expected predictive advantage and keeping truthful prediction as a dominant strategy, it enables decentralized learning of both predictions and aggregation weights. The proposed variants demonstrate that one can balance desirable economic properties (like no-arbitrage and bounded deficits) while matching centralized methods in predictive performance, making WALLA a promising tool for real-world multi-LLM systems.

Abstract: It is increasingly common to aggregate predictions from multiple LLMs, each with domain expertise or access to private tools and data, to improve collective prediction performance. In decentralized settings, aggregation weights need to be determined without access to models' private information and should remain robust to strategic reporting. We propose a family of advantage-aligned wagering mechanisms for LLM aggregation (WALLA), in which each model reports a prediction and a learned wager, and predictions are aggregated using wagers as weights. WALLA introduces a leave-one-out baseline into the net payout function, yielding three desirable properties: (1) dominant-strategy incentive compatibility of prediction under arbitrary belief structure, (2) advantage--wager alignment, where the optimal wager is proportional to the model's expected score advantage, and (3) prediction-agnostic wager optimization, enabling decentralized learning of wager policies without requiring optimal predictions. We further instantiate two mechanism variants that trade off normality and no-arbitrage while maintaining a bounded worst-case deficit for the mechanism. Experiments on question-answering and forecasting benchmarks across heterogeneous models and private-information settings show that WALLA matches centralized aggregation methods in predictive performance, while simultaneously achieving decentralized learning, advantage-aligned aggregation weights, uncertainty awareness, and incentive-compatible prediction.

</details>

Across a two-month deployment, 11 problems have been solved, demonstrating its capacity to act as a co-pilot throughout the entire research cycle.
