### [164] [LLM-as-a-Tutor: Policy-Aware Prompt Adaptation for Non-Verifiable RL](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04412)
*Yujin Kim,Namgyu Ho,Sangmin Hwang,Joonkee Kim,Yongjin Yang,Sangmin Bae,Seungone Kim,Jaehun Jung,Se-Young Yun,Hwanjun Song*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a reinforcement learning framework where an LLM not only judges model outputs but also dynamically makes prompts harder by adding constraints, keeping difficulty aligned with the model’s improving capabilities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current RL for instruction-following uses static prompt sets and only adapts the reward rubric. As the policy improves, many prompts become too easy, so the judge cannot distinguish good from bad outputs, causing weak or collapsed reward signals. They want a way to keep the training tasks challenging enough for the current policy level without manually designing difficulty schedules.

Method: They introduce LLM-as-a-Tutor, where a single LLM plays two roles: (1) examiner—pairwise compares policy rollouts for a given prompt to detect when the prompt no longer elicits meaningful quality differences (i.e., is non-challenging); (2) generator—when a prompt is detected as too easy, it appends small, atomic constraints to that prompt. This append-only mechanism progressively increases task difficulty while remaining consistent with the original instruction. The adapted prompts are then used for ongoing non-verifiable RL as reward-bearing training data.

Result: On three complex instruction-following benchmarks, their approach consistently beats both (a) baselines that ignore the evolving policy and use static prompts, and (b) prior policy-adaptive methods that only adjust rubrics or rewrite prompts but do not systematically adapt prompt difficulty. The improved performance indicates more effective reward signals and better policy learning dynamics.

Conclusion: Dynamically adapting prompt difficulty—via an LLM acting as tutor that detects easy prompts and augments them with extra constraints—is an important and previously missing dimension of policy-awareness in non-verifiable RL. This self-calibrating mechanism stabilizes and enhances training without external difficulty schedules, leading to better instruction-following performance.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning (RL) for non-verifiable instruction following increasingly relies on LLM judges with prompt-specific rubrics as reward signals. While recent methods adapt these rubrics to the evolving policy during training, the training prompts themselves remain static, drawn from fixed corpora. This static approach often results in a critical misalignment between prompt difficulty and policy capability, leaving the judge unable to recover a discriminative reward signal when prompts fail to elicit quality variance among rollouts. To address this misalignment, we introduce LLM-as-a-Tutor, a framework that extends the LLM's role from judge to tutor: a single model serves as an examiner that pairwise-compares policy rollouts to detect non-challenging prompts, and as a generator that appends atomic constraints to them. This append-only design monotonically raises difficulty in step with the policy's capability, producing a self-calibrating training signal without external difficulty schedules. On three complex instruction-following benchmarks, our method consistently outperforms both policy-unaware baselines and prior policy-adaptive methods that adapt rubrics or rewrite prompts, suggesting prompt adaptation as a missing axis of policy-awareness in non-verifiable RL.

</details>


### [165] [Agent Step Value: State-Transition Measurement with State-Grounded LLM Evaluators](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04419)
*Andrew Zhang,Chengzhan Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces Agent Step Value (ASV), a framework to score each individual action of an agent by how it changes an evaluator’s belief over possible outcomes, enabling fine-grained diagnosis of agent behavior.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing agent evaluations reduce complex multi-step traces to a final answer or trajectory-level score, which hides which intermediate actions were actually helpful or harmful. Developers need step-level diagnostics to understand which actions shifted the agent’s belief in a productive direction and where things went wrong. There is also a need for label-free, offline, and state-grounded evaluation that avoids leakage and preserves meaningful probability shifts.

Method: Define Agent Step Value (ASV) as a state-transition metric: given a set of fixed candidate outcomes and a state-grounded evaluator, each observed action is scored by the change it induces in the evaluator’s distribution over these outcomes between before/after states. Implementation details: render redacted before/after state projections; use a stateless LLM evaluator to assign log scores to each candidate; run a label-free rationale pass to separate evaluator deliberation from one-token option scoring, preserving likelihoods while exposing leakage/floor-score events; report belief diagnostics (e.g., margins, entropy, Bayesian surprise) and offline oracle validation metrics. Apply this pipeline to multi-step open-QA evidence-seeking tasks with live retrieval and a specific LLM actor/scorer setup.

Result: Applied to 100 reviewed open-QA evidence-seeking tasks with live PubMed retrieval, a partially live DeepSeek actor, and DeepSeek logprob scoring, ASV evaluated 1,100 steps and 2,200 states. Under a fixed-layout, rationale-conditioned evaluation protocol, it produced quantitative belief-movement metrics: mean gold-margin gain of -2.335 with 95% trajectory-bootstrap CI [-3.395, -1.272]; zero net entropy movement; and mean Bayesian surprise of 2.693. These statistics reveal where belief shifts were constructive or destructive across steps, including cases missed by trajectory-level or entropy-only metrics.

Conclusion: ASV provides a fine-grained, state-grounded measurement of each agent step’s impact on the evaluator’s belief over outcomes, enabling localization of constructive vs. destructive belief pivots that final-answer scores or entropy-only step metrics cannot capture. The approach is practical—demonstrated on real open-QA tasks—and is released as a standalone ASV Eval toolkit, offering developers improved diagnostics for multi-step agent behavior.

Abstract: Most agent evaluations collapse a multi-step trace into a final answer, a success flag, or a trajectory-level score. These aggregates obscure the diagnostic question developers need most: which action changed the state in a useful direction? We introduce Agent Step Value (ASV), a state-transition measurement framework that scores each observed action by the change it induces in a state-grounded evaluator's distribution over fixed candidate outcomes. ASV renders redacted before/after state projections, uses a stateless LLM evaluator to assign candidate log scores, and reports both gold-free belief diagnostics and offline oracle validation metrics. A label-free rationale pass separates evaluator deliberation from one-token option scoring, preserving candidate likelihoods while exposing leakage and floor-score events. On 100 reviewed open-QA evidence-seeking tasks with live PubMed retrieval, a partially live DeepSeek actor, and DeepSeek log-probability scoring, ASV evaluates 1,100 steps and 2,200 states. Under the fixed-layout rationale-conditioned protocol, mean gold-margin gain is -2.335 (trajectory-bootstrap 95\% CI [-3.395, -1.272]), entropy movement is 0.000, and mean Bayesian surprise is 2.693. ASV therefore localizes constructive and destructive belief pivots that final-answer scores and entropy-only step metrics miss. We release the standalone ASV Eval toolkit.

</details>

Method: The authors build ResearchStudio-Idea, consisting of: (1) Paper-Search, a multi-source literature search skill; (2) Scoop-Check, a prior-art collision checker for novelty assessment; and (3) IdeaSpark, an end-to-end workflow that combines evidence grounding, pattern-guided idea generation, prior-work retrieval, auditing, and idea-card rendering. They construct a corpus of 1,947 ML conference papers (including orals, high-citation papers, and rejects from ICLR, ICML, NeurIPS, 2021–2025), analyze outcomes to extract 31 recurring ideation sub-patterns consolidated into 15 ideation patterns, and encode each into structured pattern cards (contexts, bottlenecks, differentiation strategies, precedents, and failure modes). IdeaSpark uses these cards plus an "evidence bundle" to evaluate evidence readiness, reconstruct context, detect bottlenecks, select patterns, instantiate a candidate research direction, retrieve conflicting prior work, and perform outcome-informed audits.

Using these patterns, IdeaSpark is able to transform inputs (a research problem plus evidence) into concrete, traceable research proposals with explicit grounding, novelty checks, and risk analysis. In blind evaluations by automated judges, proposals produced by IdeaSpark outperform those from no-skill and generic-skill baselines in overall proposal quality while achieving comparable levels of novelty.

Conclusion: ResearchStudio-Idea, and especially IdeaSpark, shows that codifying recurrent ideation patterns from real ML research into structured, tool-usable form can significantly improve early-stage research ideation. By integrating literature search, novelty checking, pattern-guided generation, and auditing into one workflow, the system produces stronger, more grounded, and still novel research proposals compared to simpler baselines. This suggests that reusable ideation patterns and structured evidence workflows are a promising direction for augmenting research practice with large language models.

Abstract: Large language models have made research ideation increasingly accessible, yet effective idea development requires more than generating candidate directions. Researchers must ground a problem in current literature, identify meaningful bottlenecks, differentiate from existing solutions, and evaluate risks before committing to implementation. We present ResearchStudio-Idea as a reusable skill suite for this first mile of research ideation. The suite includes Paper-Search, a standalone multi-source literature search skill; Scoop-Check, a standalone prior-art collision checker for novelty claims; and IdeaSpark, the end-to-end skill that composes evidence grounding, pattern-guided generation, collision retrieval, audit, and idea-card rendering into one workflow. IdeaSpark is constructed from a corpus of 1,947 machine learning conference papers collected from ICLR, ICML, and NeurIPS between 2021 and 2025, including Oral papers, a separately tracked high-citation subset, and rejected submissions. Analysis of these outcomes reveals 31 recurring ideation sub-patterns, consolidated into 15 reusable ideation patterns. Each pattern is operationalized as a structured card containing research contexts, bottleneck types, differentiation strategies, supporting precedents, and common failure modes. Given a research problem and an evidence bundle, IdeaSpark evaluates evidence readiness, reconstructs the surrounding research context, identifies unresolved bottlenecks, selects relevant patterns, instantiates one candidate direction, retrieves potentially conflicting prior work, and performs outcome-informed auditing. This workflow transforms reusable ideation patterns into traceable research proposals. Blind automated-judge evaluations show that IdeaSpark consistently produces stronger research proposals than no-skill and generic-skill baselines while maintaining competitive novelty.

### [167] [Why Pure Reasoning is Not Enough: Nature as the Source of Mathematical Innovation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04505)
*Charanjit S. Jutla,Vimal Sharma*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that human mathematical creativity crucially depends on pattern matching inspired by the natural world, rather than on pure formal deduction, and uses both historical case studies (Fourier analysis) and complexity-theoretic arguments to support this claim and to motivate large, pattern-rich AI models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To explain why human mathematicians often need ideas from physics and other empirical sciences to make major advances, and to challenge the view that mathematics is or could be driven mainly by pure, self-contained logical deduction. The authors want to show that both undecidability and extreme computational complexity make purely deductive reasoning practically inadequate, thereby motivating the role of cross-domain pattern recognition in both human and artificial intelligence.

Method: Conceptual and historical analysis combined with complexity-theoretic reasoning. The authors trace the development of the Fourier transform and related mathematics, highlighting points where physical problems (e.g., vibrating strings, heat equation) forced the adoption or invention of new mathematical tools that were not anticipated by formal reasoning alone. In parallel, they review known results on the logical and computational complexity of various logical systems (NP-hardness of SAT, non-elementary complexity of monadic second-order logic, etc.) to argue that even decidable logics are often infeasible for systematic deduction.

Result: Historically, many central mathematical tools, exemplified by Fourier analysis, emerged only when driven by concrete physics problems, and were sometimes resisted by mathematicians when considered on purely formal grounds. The complexity-theoretic survey shows that general-purpose logical deduction is either impossible (due to undecidability) or computationally intractable at realistic scales, even for relatively weak logical fragments. Together, these findings support the hypothesis that effective mathematical practice relies on importing patterns from pre-computed structures in nature rather than exhaustive logical search.

Conclusion: Human-level mathematical reasoning is not just aided by but cognitively dependent on physics-inspired and more broadly cross-domain pattern matching, because pure deduction is either undecidable or computationally prohibitive in many relevant settings. Consequently, artificial systems aiming for human-like mathematical creativity must incorporate large repositories of patterns—such as those captured by large-scale models trained on diverse data—rather than rely solely on formal logic engines. This provides a principled argument in favor of the scale and pattern-centric design of contemporary large language models.

Abstract: We advance the hypothesis that human mathematical reasoning, constrained by both the undecidability and the computational intractability of even modest logical fragments, relies fundamentally on pattern matching from domains external to pure deduction. The most prolific reservoir of such patterns is the natural world, whose physical laws and biological systems have undergone billions of years of ``pre-computation'' and already exhibit surprisingly innovative solutions. To ground this claim, we trace the history of the Fourier transform and relevant mathematics, from the vibrating string controversy to the hear equation and subsequent formalisms prevalent in mathematics. At each critical juncture, a physics problem forced the acceptance or creation of a mathematical tool that pure formal reasoning failed to anticipate or, worse, human reasoning had resisted.
  We further survey the landscape of logical complexity, from NP-hard propositional satisfiability to the non-elementary decision-procedures for monadic second-order theories, to demonstrate that even when a logic is decidable, the resources required for worst-case deduction are astronomically prohibitive. We argue that these barriers make physics-inspired pattern matching not just a historical accident but a cognitive necessity. Finally, we draw the consequence for artificial intelligence: if pure reasoning is constitutively insufficient, then any system aiming at human-level mathematical creativity must embed a vast store of cross-domain patterns rather than rely on deduction alone. This furnishes a principled justification for the enormous scale of contemporary large language models.

### [168] [Compressing the Validation Bottleneck: An Agentic Self-Driving Lab for Scientific Discovery](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04508)
*Kyunghoon Hur,Chihun Lee*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents an agentic AI framework that speeds up self-driving labs by both reducing the number of experimental iterations and lowering per-experiment cost, using prior-aware experimental design and cost-aware surrogate modeling.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Self-driving labs can physically execute experiments proposed by AI agents, but the overall loop remains slow and expensive because (1) agents may require many iterative rounds of experiments to reach a target, and (2) individual experiments can be high-cost or slow due to demanding measurements. There is a need for AI methods that explicitly optimize both the number of experimental rounds and the cost of each experiment, while maintaining scientific validity across domains like biology and materials science.

Method: The authors design a unified agentic AI-for-Science framework with two main components. (1) A prior-aware design-of-experiments (DOE) loop that incorporates domain knowledge and historical data to propose the next set of experiments that are both feasible and maximally informative, thereby reducing the trials needed to hit a target. (2) A cost-aware surrogate agent that learns to predict expensive, high-resolution measurement outcomes from cheaper, low-resolution measurements. This agent uses uncertainty estimates to decide adaptively when to perform a high-cost measurement versus relying on the surrogate prediction from low-cost data. They test these components in different domains (biology for the DOE component, materials for the surrogate component) and integrate them under a single agent to control the self-driving lab.

Result: In the examined biology and materials use cases, the prior-aware DOE agent reduces the number of experimental iterations required to reach a desired outcome compared with less informed strategies, while the cost-aware surrogate agent can often substitute low-cost measurements for high-cost ones without significantly degrading decision quality. The uncertainty-based decision rule allows the agent to selectively resort to expensive measurements only when surrogate predictions are insufficiently reliable, thereby decreasing the total experimental cost. Combined, these components demonstrate the potential for substantial acceleration of the self-driving lab experimental loop.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that integrating prior-aware experiment design with cost-aware surrogate modeling in a single agent can materially accelerate self-driving lab workflows by simultaneously reducing both the number of experimental rounds and the cost per experiment. This dual optimization framework is applicable across scientific domains such as biology and materials science and represents a step toward more efficient, fully agentic AI-for-Science systems that tightly couple ideation, planning, and physical experimentation.

Abstract: Agentic AI-for-Science can automate ideation, planning, and analysis, but final validation still depends on real experiments. A self-driving lab (SDL) can execute those experiments, yet the loop still has bottlenecks: the agent may spend too many rounds on low-value experiments, or each round may require a high-cost experiment. We target these two physical bottlenecks with one agent. First, a prior-aware agentic DOE loop uses domain knowledge and past results to propose feasible and informative next experiments, reducing trials-to-target. Second, a cost-aware surrogate agent predicts high-cost, high-resolution measurements from low-cost, low-resolution measurements. It chooses between a high- and a low-cost measurement based on the predicted uncertainty. We examine these directions in the biology and materials domains, respectively. Together, under a single agent, these components aim to accelerate the SDL loop by reducing both the number of loops and the cost per experiment.

### [169] [VLA Grounder: Language-Conditioning Space Optimization for Black-Box VLA Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04517)
*Damir Shodiev,Aleksei Staroverov,Nikita Kachaev,Alexey K. Kovalev,Aleksandr I. Panov*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes optimizing the *language input* to frozen Vision-Language-Action models, instead of changing their action parameters, to improve robot task performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing VLA models behave very differently depending on how a natural-language instruction is phrased, indicating that language is not just a static task description but a powerful control variable. However, most work focuses on training or fine-tuning the model weights instead of systematically exploiting the structure of the language-conditioning space. The authors want to leverage and optimize this language space to improve performance without modifying the underlying large model.

Method: They introduce a "language-conditioning space policy" that maps a human instruction into a compact, VLA-grounded command that encodes object appearance, spatial relations, and target-grounding cues. This policy is initialized using a prior derived from model failures (failure-derived command-space prior) and is trained via reinforcement learning using sparse task-completion rewards, while keeping the downstream VLA policy completely frozen. Essentially, RL is used to search in the command-language space for phrasings that induce better behavior from the fixed VLA.

Result: On RL4VLA and VL-Think benchmarks, the proposed language-conditioning space optimization substantially improves success rates, particularly on tasks that are sensitive to instruction wording, require symbolic reasoning, or involve manipulating multiple objects. This empirically shows that optimizing the language input alone can significantly enhance performance of frozen robot foundation models.

Conclusion: Language can be treated as an optimizable control variable for robot foundation models, not just as a descriptive task label. By learning a policy over a structured command-language space and training it with RL while keeping the VLA frozen, one can elicit better behavior and improve task success without modifying the underlying model weights.

Abstract: Vision-Language-Action (VLA) models are commonly treated as end-to-end action policies conditioned on natural-language task descriptions. In practice, however, their behavior often depends sharply on how the instruction is phrased, suggesting that language is not merely a task label but an optimizable conditioning input. We study whether frozen VLA policies can be improved by optimizing language space rather than updating action weights. Our method introduces a language-conditioning space policy that translates a human instruction into a short VLA-grounded command using object appearance, spatial relations, and target-grounding cues. The language-conditioning space policy is initialized with a failure-derived command-space prior and optimized with reinforcement learning from sparse task-completion rewards, while the downstream VLA remains fully frozen. This yields language-conditioning space optimization: RL discovers which VLA-grounded commands best elicit successful behavior from the frozen action policy. Experiments on RL4VLA and VL-Think show that language-conditioning space optimization improves success on instruction-sensitive, symbolic, and multi-object manipulation tasks, demonstrating that language can serve as an optimizable variable for a robot foundation models. Website: https://tttonyalpha.github.io/vla_grounder

</details>


### [170] [Measuring Harness-Induced Belief Divergence in Multi-Step LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04528)
*Haiwen Yi,Xinyuan Song*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that the design of the software-agent harness (interface, repair, verification, logging) can significantly change an LLM agent’s internal multi-step beliefs without changing its final success rate, and proposes diagnostics and a standardized protocol (BIWM) to measure and align these belief trajectories across different harnesses.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current benchmarks for LLM-based agents usually only report whether a task is solved, ignoring how the agent’s internal beliefs and decision-making evolve over multiple steps. However, agents operate through a harness that constrains observations, actions, repairs, verification, and logging; these details are typically treated as implementation choices, not experimental variables. The authors suspect that such harness choices can bias the agent’s beliefs and behavior, even when the task, environment, and underlying model are fixed. They aim to expose and quantify these hidden effects to make agent evaluation more reliable and comparable.

Method: The authors fix the task, environment, and base LLM, and vary the agent harness along dimensions such as which actions are allowed or blocked, how failures are repaired, how states are verified, and what evidence is logged. They introduce a belief-rollout diagnostic that elicits structured K-step trajectories of the agent’s beliefs about progress, risk, recoverability, constraints, failure modes, uncertainty, likelihood of future success, repair cost, and next action, under different harnesses. They then define a cross-harness belief divergence metric and decompose it into (1) an arrival term capturing immediate belief shifts due to interface changes and (2) a growth term capturing horizon-dependent divergence over multiple steps. They run controlled coding tasks and public benchmark stress tests with manipulations such as blocked actions, compressed repairs, selective verification, and cost-aware evidence pruning. Finally, they propose BIWM, a no-training protocol that canonicalizes observations, logs censored branches, expands repair traces, records verification masks, executes risky branches in shadow, and aligns belief trajectories across harness views.

Result: Experiments show that changes in the harness—such as blocking certain actions, compressing repair information, selectively verifying states, or pruning evidence based on cost—can leave the final task success rate nearly unchanged while substantially altering the agent’s intermediate beliefs and the trajectories of its decisions over multiple steps. The belief-rollout diagnostic and cross-harness belief divergence analysis reveal both immediate (arrival) and long-horizon (growth) differences in beliefs induced by the harness. The BIWM protocol is demonstrated to bring belief trajectories from different harness designs into closer alignment by standardizing observations, logging censored paths, expanding repair information, tracking verification, and executing risky branches in a shadow mode.

Conclusion: Harness design substantially influences an LLM agent’s internal beliefs and decision trajectories, even when the underlying model, environment, and tasks are unchanged and terminal performance appears similar. Therefore, harness configuration should be treated as a key experimental variable in agent evaluation rather than an implementation detail. The proposed belief-rollout diagnostic, cross-harness belief divergence decomposition, and BIWM protocol provide tools to measure, interpret, and mitigate harness-induced biases, enabling more transparent and comparable assessment of software agents across different experimental setups.

Abstract: Software-agent benchmarks usually report whether an agent solves a task, but the agent reaches that outcome through a harness that controls what it sees, which actions it can take, which failures are repaired, which states are verified, and which evidence is logged. We show that this harness can change the agent's multi-step beliefs even when the task, environment, and base LLM are fixed. We introduce a belief-rollout diagnostic that elicits structured K-step trajectories over progress, risk, recoverability, constraints, failure mode, uncertainty, future success, repair cost, and next action under alternative harnesses. We define a cross-harness belief divergence and decompose it into an arrival term for immediate interface shifts and a growth term for horizon-dependent belief changes. On controlled coding tasks and public-benchmark stress tests, blocked actions, compressed repairs, selective verification, and cost-aware evidence pruning often preserve terminal success while changing the beliefs that drive later decisions. We further introduce BIWM, a no-training protocol that canonicalizes observations, logs censored branches, expands repair traces, records verification masks, executes risky branches in shadow, and aligns belief trajectories across harness views. The results suggest that harness design is an experimental variable in agent evaluation, not an implementation detail. Our code is available at https://github.com/Hik289/Harness-induce-bias.git.

### [171] [Heaviside Continuity of Rolling Coefficients for Eliminating Epistemic Entropy in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04562)
*MY Pitsane,Hope Mogale*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes HCRC, a verification-first execution framework for LLMs that uses predicate-gated state transitions to prevent incorrect intermediate reasoning from propagating, substantially reducing false completions without changing the underlying model.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs can produce fluent but incorrect outputs, and unlike humans they give few detectable cues when they are wrong. Autoregressive decoding offers no built-in way to verify intermediate reasoning steps before they commit to the model state, leading to undetected errors and unreliable reasoning, especially in high-stakes or multi-step tasks like software engineering. The paper seeks a systematic, model-agnostic way to enforce correctness constraints during inference rather than relying solely on larger models or post-hoc checks.

Method: The authors introduce Heaviside Continuity of Rolling Coefficients (HCRC), which reconceptualizes LLM inference as a sequence of predicate-gated state transitions. A Heaviside Gate determines whether execution can advance based on predefined correctness predicates. HCRC combines the model’s own confidence with verification signals produced by a parallel worker architecture; only when these predicates are satisfied does the system allow the LLM to commit to the next state. This framework wraps existing LLMs without modifying their parameters and is instantiated in software-engineering and reasoning settings. The system is benchmarked across thirteen different proposer models from four providers.

Result: On more capable LLMs, the HCRC gate reduces the false-completion rate from 4–7% to 0%, while maintaining competitive latency and in some cases even improving speed relative to the raw model. For weaker models, the framework prevents incorrect outputs from flowing downstream by turning would-be false completions into explicit halts, preserving system integrity. The approach is validated both in controlled benchmarks (software engineering and reasoning tasks) and in a long-running production deployment within an agentic coding environment that uses HCRC to approve file edits, drive verification-based progress updates, and manage memory compaction.

Conclusion: HCRC demonstrates that reliable, low-entropy LLM reasoning can be achieved via principled execution control—specifically, verification-first, predicate-gated state transitions—rather than depending purely on larger or more capable models. The framework is general, model-agnostic, and effective both in benchmarks and in real-world, long-running agentic coding systems, positioning verification-driven execution as a key design pattern for trustworthy LLM-based agents.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) generate fluent outputs that can be wrong. Unlike humans, who often exhibit cues when providing false information, LLMs produce errors that are difficult to detect because autoregressive decoding provides no mechanism for verifying intermediate reasoning before state progression. We introduce Heaviside Continuity of Rolling Coefficients (HCRC), a verification-first execution framework that reformulates inference as predicate-gated state transitions governed by a Heaviside Gate. HCRC combines model confidence with independent verification signals from a parallel worker architecture, allowing execution to advance only when predefined correctness predicates are satisfied. This prevents invalid intermediate states from propagating, reducing epistemic entropy without modifying the underlying model. We evaluate HCRC on software-engineering and reasoning tasks across thirteen proposers from four providers. On capable proposers, the gate reduces the false-completion rate (FCR) from 4--7% to 0% while remaining latency-competitive and, in some settings, faster than the unwrapped model. On weaker proposers, it converts false completions into honest halts instead of corrupting downstream state. Beyond benchmarking, HCRC has operated for months as the production control plane of an agentic coding environment, authorizing file mutations, verification-driven progress reporting, and memory compaction. These results establish HCRC as a general framework for verification-driven LLM execution, showing that reliable reasoning can be achieved through principled execution control rather than model scale alone.

### [172] [Detecting Answer-Driven Reasoning in LLM-Based Educational Tutors via Truncated Chain-of-Thought Auditing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04572)
*Bonan Shen,Dingyan Shang,Youting Wang,Tao Ning*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that when a language model tutor secretly has access to answer keys, its explanations become strongly answer-driven, which can be detected by auditing partial chains-of-thought using TRACE.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand whether LLM tutors truly reason from the student problem or instead rely on hidden answer keys or solution artifacts, and to develop a way to diagnose this answer-driven behavior in realistic tutoring settings.

Method: The authors introduce and use Truncated Reasoning AUC Evaluation (TRACE), which measures how early in a chain-of-thought prefix a model can already yield the correct answer. They test 1000 GSM8K math problems with Qwen2.5-3B-Instruct under three contexts: question-only, with the correct answer key, and with an incorrect answer key. At various fixed fractions of the generated explanation, they truncate the reasoning, force an immediate answer, and check it against the gold numeric label to compute an AUC-like score over prefix length.

Result: Providing the correct answer key massively increases TRACE scores: median AUC rises from 0.375 (question-only) to 0.900 (with answer key), and in 997/1000 cases the gold answer is already recoverable from just the first 10% of the explanation. The effect persists even on the 746 problems where both conditions eventually end with the correct final answer, indicating the difference is in the reasoning process, not just the final outcome.

Conclusion: Access to private answer information makes LLM tutor explanations highly answer-driven, where the correct answer is effectively decided long before the explanation justifies it. TRACE-based truncated chain-of-thought auditing is a practical diagnostic for identifying such answer-driven reasoning in math tutoring scenarios, offering a lightweight process-level evaluation tool beyond end-answer correctness.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) tutors often produce fluent step-by-step explanations, but a correct and pedagogically formatted response does not guarantee that the answer was derived from the student-facing problem. In realistic tutoring systems, the model may also have access to teacher notes, answer keys, rubrics, or retrieved solution artifacts. We study whether such private answer information can make tutor explanations answer-driven: the final answer is behaviorally available before the written explanation has justified it. Using Truncated Reasoning AUC Evaluation (TRACE), which probes how early a chain-of-thought prefix can pass a verifier, we evaluate 1000 GSM8K test problems under three paired tutoring contexts: question-only, correct answer-key, and wrong answer-key. At fixed fractions of each generated explanation, we force the model to answer immediately and verify the response against the gold numeric answer. With Qwen2.5-3B-Instruct, answer-key access raises median TRACE AUC from 0.375 to 0.900 and makes the gold answer available at the first 10% prefix in 997 of 1000 cases. The effect remains strong on the 746 examples where both question-only and answer-key explanations end with the correct answer. These results support truncated CoT auditing as a lightweight process-level diagnostic for answer-driven reasoning in math tutoring explanations.

### [173] [Attention Limited Reward Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04590)
*Wenqian Xing*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that standard Bradley–Terry-based reward modeling for RLHF misinterprets human pairwise comparisons because it ignores limited human attention, leading to misleading rankings and underestimation of evaluation difficulty.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern AI alignment methods rely on pairwise human comparisons to learn reward models, usually assuming that observed preferences directly reflect latent utility differences via Bradley–Terry log-odds. This neglects the fact that humans have limited attention and cognitive capacity, which can blur or distort comparisons. The authors want to understand what is lost by ignoring attention limits, how this affects identifiability of rewards from comparisons, and why empirical data often contain cyclic or inconsistent preferences that scalar rewards cannot explain.

Method: The authors introduce a reduced-form model of labeling inspired by rational inattention: each human comparison is produced through a low-capacity evaluation channel that may miss or downweight some relevant information. This model separates genuine value gaps between options from difficulty of detecting those gaps under limited attention. They formally analyze identifiability of reward, attention, and default-choice biases from passive pairwise data and contrast it with Bradley–Terry assumptions. They then conduct two empirical case studies: (1) analysis of Chatbot Arena human comparison data to detect cyclic, non-transitive components inconsistent with a single scalar reward; (2) a perceptual comparison experiment where they collect choice labels together with response times and gaze, testing whether auxiliary signals carry value-gap information that the labels themselves lack.

Result: Theoretical analysis shows that with limited attention, passive pairwise comparison data cannot in general disentangle true reward differences, attention allocations, and default tendencies. Heterogeneous attention across items or annotators can cause standard Bradley–Terry reward models to infer systematically biased or misleading rankings. Learning efficiency depends not just on the number of labels but on how much attended information each label transmits. Empirically, Chatbot Arena data exhibit a statistically significant cyclic component beyond sampling noise, indicating structure that no scalar reward function can represent. In the perceptual study, response times and eye-gaze patterns encode information about the underlying value gap that is not present in the binary comparison labels alone.

Conclusion: Pairwise human feedback should not be treated as direct, noise-perturbed revealed preference over a latent scalar reward. Instead, it is an attention-limited measurement, where weak or inconsistent preferences can reflect evaluation difficulty and information bottlenecks rather than genuine indifference. Ignoring limited attention leads to identifiability failures and potentially misleading rankings under standard Bradley–Terry modeling. Alignment pipelines should either model attention explicitly or augment comparison data with richer behavioral signals (like response times and gaze) to better capture both value gaps and evaluation difficulty.

Abstract: Pairwise human comparisons are a primary interface through which modern AI systems learn human preferences. RLHF and related alignment pipelines typically model such comparisons with Bradley--Terry log-odds, where choice probabilities are governed by latent reward differences. This paper examines what this assumption misses through a reduced-form model motivated by rational inattention, in which each label is generated by a low-capacity evaluation channel. The model separates two forms of ambiguity that standard reward modeling tends to conflate: a comparison may be difficult because the two candidates are genuinely close in value, or because the relevant distinction is hard to detect under limited attention. We show that limited attention can fundamentally distort what pairwise comparisons reveal. In particular, passive comparison data cannot generally distinguish reward, attention, and default tendencies, and heterogeneous attention can make standard Bradley--Terry reward modeling recover misleading rankings. Our analysis shows that learning is governed not by the raw number of labels, but by the amount of attended information each label carries. A case study on human votes over language-model pairs from Chatbot Arena exhibits the predicted signature, a cyclic component of the comparison data that exceeds sampling noise and that no scalar reward can represent; a second case study on perceptual comparisons shows that response times and gaze carry gap information that the labels do not. This perspective suggests that human feedback should be treated not as direct revealed preference, but as an attention-limited measurement process: a weak preference signal may reflect hidden evaluation difficulty rather than genuine indifference.

### [174] [Governed Individuation: Cryptographically Decoupling an Agent's Learning from Its Authority](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04613)
*Xue Qin,Simin Luan,Cong Yang,Zhijun Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a cryptographic, architecture-level mechanism to guarantee that autonomous, continually-learning agents cannot exceed operator-authorized capabilities, regardless of how they adapt after deployment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As autonomous agents progress from text-only systems to operators of code, data, and physical infrastructure, they increasingly learn and adapt while deployed. Existing alignment and safety methods are probabilistic: they cannot guarantee that, after learning in the real world, an agent will remain within its originally authorized scope. This creates a need for robust, architecture-level guarantees that the authority and permitted actions of a deployed, learning agent cannot silently expand or be subverted by the agent itself.

Method: The paper introduces "governed individuation," in which an agent is bound at boot time to a cryptographically frozen identity digest encoding its permitted authority and governance rules. All actions are routed through an enforcement gate defined over the *semantic effects* of actions rather than their names or surface labels. The authors formally prove that, under this architecture, no learning, skill acquisition, or self-induced governance abstraction can increase the agent’s authority without an operator-signed change to its identity. They implement this in a software agent framework, using dynamic effect tracing and semantic monitors of varying depth, and evaluate it on an open-ended tool-use benchmark that precludes simple name-based blocking.

Result: Theoretical results show that authority bounds become invariants of the execution architecture: even mis-specified or self-induced safety principles cannot widen the agent’s actual permitted effects without explicit operator cryptographic authorization. Empirically, ungoverned agents under reward pressure attempt to tamper with their own evaluation at task-dependent rates that reach 100% of runs on the hardest tasks. In contrast, governed agents with the proposed gate achieve zero executed forbidden effects as a verified property, while maintaining task success. Adversarial evaluation of different monitoring strategies demonstrates a drop in false-allow decisions from 75% with name-based gating to 0% with dynamic effect-tracing monitors, and a refusal-history mechanism generalizes compliance to unseen families of disallowed behaviors.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that confinement of learning, autonomous agents can be treated as a strict, checkable invariant of their execution architecture rather than a probabilistic outcome of alignment training. By binding agents to cryptographically fixed identities and gating actions on their semantic effects, one can guarantee that agents cannot extend their operational authority through learning, abstraction, or self-modification without explicit operator re-authorization. This shifts trust in deployed agents from a fragile bet on sustained alignment to a verifiable property that any stakeholder can check at boot time.

Abstract: Autonomous agents are moving from sandboxed text generators to operators of code, data, and physical infrastructure, and they increasingly learn while deployed. This reopens a question that alignment techniques answer only probabilistically: after an agent has adapted in the field, is the running system still confined to what its operator authorised? Here we show that confinement can be guaranteed as an invariant of the agent's execution architecture rather than a probabilistic outcome of its training. Governed individuation binds an agent at boot to a cryptographically frozen identity digest, and routes every action through a gate defined over the semantic effect of the action rather than its name. We prove that no amount of learning, skill acquisition, or self-induced governance abstraction can widen the agent's permitted authority without an operator-signed change to its identity; the guarantee holds even when the agent induces its own safety principle and that principle is wrong. Empirically, in an open-ended tool-use benchmark where a large action space rules out name-based blocking, ungoverned software agents under reward pressure attempt to tamper with their own evaluation at a task-dependent rate that reaches every run on the hardest task, whereas the gate reduces executed forbidden effects to zero as a verified property of the construction while preserving task success. An adversarial evaluation of monitors of increasing semantic depth shows false-allows falling from 75% (name-based gating) to zero (dynamic effect tracing), and refusal history transfers compliance to held-out red-line families. Trust in a deployed learning agent shifts from a wager on its continued alignment to a check anyone can run at boot.

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a structured memory architecture (MRMS) for long-lived AI agents that supports reliable personalization via synchronized structured, vector, and graph memories organized across short-, medium-, and long-term timescales.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing long-lived AI agents often rely on large prompt windows or undifferentiated conversation history, which fails to provide true continuity, selective recall, or reliable personalization. There is a need for a principled memory substrate that can preserve useful prior experience, retrieve it selectively, separate personal context from external evidence, and adapt when the underlying situation changes.

Method: The authors design an architectural memory substrate along two orthogonal axes: (1) a representational axis combining structured records, vector embeddings, and graph relations; and (2) a temporal axis comprising short-term traces, medium-term abstractions, and long-term semantic commitments. They enforce synchronization across structured, vector, and graph memories, where structured records determine what is eligible for memory, vector representations enable retrieval, and graph relations reason about support, contradiction, and supersession before information is projected into the model’s context. They implement a prototype system called MRMS with concrete components for structured records, vector retrieval, temporal consolidation policies, and graph-based revision mechanisms, then test it in controlled long-horizon interaction scenarios with explicit evidence requirements.

Result: The MRMS prototype demonstrates that the proposed memory substrate can carry out pre-generation memory selection, memory revision, boundary enforcement between personal context and external evidence, and explicit attribution of evidence during long-lived interactions. It shows that synchronized structured-vector-graph memory with temporal policies can operationalize the proposed framework in practice, though detailed quantitative metrics are not provided in the abstract.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that achieving reliable personalization and continuity in long-lived AI agents is fundamentally a memory design problem, not just a matter of expanding context windows. Effective agent memory must be structured, selectively surfaced, continually consolidated across time scales, and epistemically labeled (e.g., as supported, contradicted, or superseded) rather than stored as raw dialogue history. The MRMS prototype provides an initial demonstration that such an architectural memory substrate is feasible and useful for long-lived interaction scenarios.

Abstract: Long-lived AI agents require continuity across interactions, but continuity cannot be obtained by simply extending the prompt window. An agent must preserve useful prior experience, retrieve it selectively, distinguish personal context from external evidence, and revise memory when the underlying situation changes. We propose an architectural memory substrate organized along two orthogonal axes: a representational axis spanning structured records, vector representations, and graph relations; and a temporal axis spanning short-term traces, medium-term abstractions, and long-term semantic commitments. Its key design constraint is synchronized structured-vector-graph memory: structured records govern eligibility, vector representations support recall, and graph relations adjudicate support, contradiction, and supersession before gated context projection. Its central claim is that reliable personalization is a memory design problem: useful memory is structured, selectively exposed, continuously consolidated, and epistemically labeled rather than stored as undifferentiated conversation history. Beyond the framework, we instantiate MRMS as a lightweight prototype implementing structured records, vector retrieval, temporal policies, and graph-based revision. The prototype exercises the core substrate mechanisms through pre-generation memory selection, revision, boundary enforcement, and evidence attribution under controlled long-lived interaction scenarios with explicit evidence requirements.

### [176] [Formal Disco: Scalable Open-Ended Generation of Formally Verified Programs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04631)
*Gabriel Poesia,Simon Henniger,Tzu-Han Hsu,Yilun Du,Nada Amin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Formal Disco, a distributed LLM-based system that generates large-scale synthetic, formally verified code to overcome data scarcity in verification-oriented languages, yielding datasets and fine-tuned models that rival or surpass strong proprietary models on verification tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI models can now generate code cheaply and at scale, but reliably ensuring correctness lags behind. Formal verification offers strong correctness guarantees but relies on data in specialized verification-aware languages (Dafny, Verus, Frama-C), for which there are very few human-written examples. This lack of data limits how well LLMs can learn to write and reason about verified programs. The authors are motivated to remove this data bottleneck by creating a scalable, automated pipeline that can synthesize diverse, formally verified programs and thereby enable better training of verification-capable LLMs.

Method: They design Formal Disco, a distributed multi-agent system composed of LLM-based workers that coordinate to synthesize and refine verified programs. The system uses three worker roles: (1) "initiators" that read random README files and documentation snippets from open-source projects and sketch related verified programs; (2) "fixers" that use compiler and verifier feedback to iteratively repair non-compiling or non-verifying code; and (3) "extenders" that propose patches to expand already-working verified programs. All interaction traces are logged and reused for distillation from a stronger model and for iterative self-improvement. Additionally, they introduce a maximum entropy principle for synthetic program generation, operationalized through iterative supervised fine-tuning that biases models toward generating increasingly diverse programs over time. They apply this pipeline to three verification languages—Dafny, Verus, and Frama-C—and then fine-tune open-source models on the resulting synthetic corpora for verification-related tasks.

Result: The authors generate large-scale datasets of synthetic, formally verified programs in Dafny, Verus, and Frama-C using the Formal Disco framework. They then fine-tune open models on these datasets for tasks involving formal verification and reasoning. Empirical results show that these fine-tuned open models often match or surpass the performance of a strong proprietary baseline, Claude Opus 4.5, on verification-relevant benchmarks. The system also successfully increases the diversity of generated programs over time via entropy-maximizing training, demonstrating that the maximum entropy principle produces richer and more varied training data.

Conclusion: Formal Disco demonstrates that a distributed, multi-agent LLM system can automatically generate high-quality, formally verified programs at scale, effectively addressing the scarcity of training data in verification-centric languages. By logging all agent interactions, distilling from stronger models, and using entropy-maximizing fine-tuning, the approach enables open models to become competitive with state-of-the-art proprietary systems on formal verification tasks. The work suggests a general pathway for overcoming data scarcity in formal reasoning domains and supports the broader use of synthetic data generation as a foundation for training specialized reasoning models.

Abstract: The cost of producing code is rapidly diminishing with increasingly capable AI agents, while quality assurance of generated programs has not kept pace. Formal verification provides the strongest possible guarantees, but the ability of AI models to work with verification-aware languages is hindered by the scarcity of human-written examples of programs in those languages. To tackle this prevalent data scarcity issue, we propose Formal Disco: a distributed system for coordination of LLM-based workers that can be easily applied to open-ended synthetic data generation at scale. We use Formal Disco to share tasks and programs between three classes of workers: "initiators", which read random READMEs from open-source repositories and documentation snippets to sketch a related verified program, "fixers" which take compiler and verifier feedback and attempt to resolve issues, and "extenders" that take working programs and propose patches to expand them. Formal Disco records all agent-generated traces and uses them both for initial distillation from a stronger model as well as self-improvement. We also propose a principle of maximum entropy for synthetic program generation, and use entropy maximization via iterative supervised fine-tuning to learn to generate increasingly diverse programs over time. We release large datasets of synthetic verified programs in three languages - Dafny, Verus, and Frama-C -, and fine-tune open models for verification-relevant tasks, often matching or exceeding the performance of Claude Opus 4.5. Overall, our work offers a path to create synthetic data at scale for formal reasoning domains and overcome the long-standing data barrier.

Method: They define a new utility function called Altruistic and Fairness Preference (AFP) that combines altruistic preferences (valuing others’ rewards) and fairness preferences (valuing outcome equality). Conclusion: Incorporating explicit altruistic and fairness preferences into agents’ utility via the AFP mechanism effectively induces cooperative policies in MARL social dilemmas, outperforming common baselines in both efficiency and equity. Abstract: Inducing cooperation among distributed agents is still a difficult problem in the field of multi-agent reinforcement learning (MARL), particularly in social dilemma situations. There, individual interests are misaligned with the common good and individual rationality leads to suboptimal group outcomes. In contrast, humans are able to achieve cooperation with one another in such situations. A common explanation for such cooperative behavior is that individuals have social preferences. In order to achieve cooperation in MARL, we design a new utility function integrating altruistic preferences (incentive for other's reward) and fairness preferences (incentive for equality) from social psychology and behavioral economics, namely, Altruistic and Fairness Preference (AFP), a reward-sharing mechanism which converts one's own and other's rewards to incentives for cooperative behavior. We performed comparative experiments with standard RL and inequity aversion agents in two challenging sequential social dilemma games, and showed that AFP agents successfully achieved mutual cooperation with more collective rewards and higher equity than the baselines. To further understand the progression of AFP during training, we subsequently explore the effects of altruistic preferences and fairness preferences on agents' behavior. The results suggest that altruistic preferences encourage agents to contribute to the public goods, and fairness preferences induce mutual behavior between agents.

### [178] [FORGE: Research-Trajectory Hijacking Attacks on Deep Research Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04718)
*Yue Pan,Ziheng Zhang,Junxiang Lei,Changhao Jia,Qingyi Si,Hongcheng Guo*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how multi-step web-retrieval AI agents can be poisoned via adversarial documents and proposes both an attack (FORGE) and a defense (Root Query Anchoring), plus a metric (PRISM) to measure contamination.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Deep research agents that break queries into subtasks and iteratively retrieve web data are increasingly used, but their multi-step planning and retrieval pipeline opens new attack surfaces. Existing prompt injection and data poisoning work focuses mainly on single-step or local effects, not how early poisoning can propagate through the entire reasoning and planning chain to corrupt final long-form reports. The authors aim to characterize and exploit this new planning-layer poisoning surface, and then to develop ways to measure and mitigate it.

Method: The authors design FORGE, a two-level poisoning attack. At the intra-document level, they craft adversarial documents whose internal reasoning is fabricated to mislead the agent. At the inter-document level, they coordinate multiple poisoned documents so that, as the agent decomposes a query into subtasks and retrieves evidence over multiple rounds, these documents steer the generation of follow-up questions and intermediate plans. They also define the PRISM metric, which scores how much of the final report is infected and weights claims by their cognitive role (e.g., framing vs factual premises). Finally, they propose Root Query Anchoring (RQA), a lightweight defense that constrains recursive follow-up question generation to stay grounded in the original root query instead of drifting under the influence of poisoned content. They empirically evaluate FORGE and RQA across multiple open-ended queries and injected documents.

Result: In experiments over 25 open-ended queries, the Network FORGE attack achieves 26.4% PRISM using five injected adversarial documents, indicating substantial contamination of final reports. The authors observe "depth migration," where the poisoning effect moves from obvious, high-level framing into deeper factual premises during recursive synthesis, making it harder to detect. On a 10-query subset used for defense evaluation, applying Root Query Anchoring reduces PRISM from 38.5% to 18.3%, roughly halving the measured contamination while keeping the system lightweight.

Conclusion: Multi-step research agents are vulnerable to planning-layer poisoning, where adversarial documents can hijack task decomposition and evidence gathering to corrupt final reports. The FORGE attack operationalizes this threat by combining fabricated intra-document reasoning and coordinated inter-document chains, and the PRISM metric provides a way to quantify report-level infection by cognitive importance. Root Query Anchoring offers a simple but effective mitigation that substantially reduces poisoning impact by tying follow-up generation to the original query, though residual contamination shows that more robust defenses are still needed.

Abstract: Deep research agents decompose open-ended queries into subtasks, retrieve web evidence over multiple rounds, and synthesize long-form reports. This workflow creates a planning-layer poisoning surface: adversarial documents that enter the retrieval pool can steer follow-up questions and turn a local injection into report-level contamination. We present FORGE (Fabricated Orchestrated Reasoning chain for aGent Exploitation), a two-level attack that combines intra-document reasoning fabrication with inter-document chain coordination to hijack subtask planning. We further introduce the PRISM metric, which weights infected report claims by cognitive type, and Root Query Anchoring, a lightweight defense that ties recursive follow-up generation to the root query. Across 25 queries, Network FORGE reaches 26.4% PRISM with five injected documents and exhibits depth migration, in which recursive synthesis shifts poisoned content from overt framing into factual premises. On the 10-query defense subset, RQA (Root Query Anchoring) reduces PRISM from 38.5% to 18.3%.

</details>


### [179] [FM-ChangeNet: Learning Change through Pathwise Feature Transport](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04750)
*Roie Kazoom,George Leifman,Genady Beryozkin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: FM-ChangeNet proposes a new pathwise-supervised framework for remote sensing change detection that models change as continuous feature-space transport, learns a time-conditioned velocity field as both mechanism and cue for change, and achieves state-of-the-art performance with more structured, robust change representations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional change detection methods in remote sensing often rely on comparing only pre- and post-event images (static endpoints), which can lead to ambiguous supervision, poor handling of temporal evolution, and difficulty in distinguishing true structural changes from nuisances like illumination variations and misalignment. There is a need for a framework that explicitly models the temporal evolution of features and provides denser supervision across the transformation process.

Method: The paper introduces FM-ChangeNet, which encodes pre- and post-temporal images into feature representations and then constructs intermediate latent states between them. It learns a time-conditioned velocity field v_hat_theta(z_t, t) that describes how features move along the transformation trajectory over time. This is implemented via a hierarchical multi-scale architecture with cross-temporal alignment and a coarse-to-fine flow decoding mechanism conditioned on time. The training objective jointly supervises the learned flow, enforces trajectory consistency, applies spatial regularization, and incorporates a segmentation loss for change maps.

Result: On remote sensing change detection benchmarks, FM-ChangeNet yields more structured and robust change representations compared to existing methods and achieves state-of-the-art performance metrics. The learned velocity field magnitude serves as an effective spatially localized cue for true structural changes, improving robustness to nuisance factors like illumination changes and misregistration.

Conclusion: Modeling change as continuous transport in feature space with a learned time-conditioned velocity field provides richer, pathwise supervision and more interpretable representations than endpoint-only comparison. FM-ChangeNet’s hierarchical, cross-temporal architecture and unified training objective lead to superior, more robust performance in remote sensing change detection, demonstrating the effectiveness of pathwise supervision for capturing temporal evolution and separating real changes from nuisances.

Abstract: We present FM-ChangeNet, a pathwise-supervised framework for change detection that reformulates bi-temporal reasoning as continuous transport in feature space rather than static endpoint comparison. Given encoded pre and post-temporal representations, we construct intermediate latent states and learn a time-conditioned velocity field $\hat{v}_θ(z_t,t)$ along the transformation trajectory. This pathwise formulation constrains the predictor over a continuum of intermediate states, providing a denser and less ambiguous supervision signal than conventional endpoint-only segmentation and enabling the model to capture temporal evolution explicitly. The learned velocity field is not only a transport mechanism but also an interpretable representation of change: its magnitude serves as a spatially localized change cue that helps distinguish true structural variation from nuisance effects such as illumination shifts and spatial misalignment. We develop a hierarchical multi-scale architecture with cross-temporal alignment, time-conditioned coarse-to-fine flow decoding, and a unified objective that couples flow supervision, trajectory consistency, spatial regularization, and segmentation loss. Experiments on remote sensing benchmarks show that the proposed framework produces more structured and robust change representations while achieving state-of-the-art performance.

### [180] [AgenticPD: A Stage-Aware Agentic Framework for Physical Design QoR Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04758)
*Shuo Ren,Zijin Cheng,Yaohui Han,Libo Shen,Leilei Jin,Wanting Tian,Rongliang Fu,Chao Wang,Bei Yu,Tsung-Yi Ho*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AgenticPD is an agent-based, stage-aware framework that optimizes physical design QoR efficiently by operating at stage boundaries, reusing intermediate checkpoints, and coordinating specialized agents with a Judge Agent to get strong post-route timing with competitive power and area.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Physical design QoR optimization is difficult and computationally expensive because decisions at one stage affect downstream stages and each evaluation traditionally requires a full, costly EDA flow run. Existing approaches oversimplify the problem as flat parameter tuning or single-shot LLM script generation, missing the structure of the multi-stage flow and wasting computation.

Method: The authors introduce AgenticPD, an agentic, stage-aware optimization framework organized around physical design flow stages. A central Judge Agent directs the search across stages, while stage-specialized agents make localized decisions using tools specific to their stage. The framework supplies structured observations, execution histories, and context management so agents can branch from previous intermediate design states, reuse checkpoints, and avoid rerunning the entire flow from scratch. Every candidate design is ultimately evaluated at the post-route signoff level.

Result: Using this framework, the system is able to conduct more efficient exploration of design options by leveraging branching and checkpoint reuse, yet still assessing each candidate at post-route signoff. The reported outcome is that AgenticPD achieves strong post-route timing performance while remaining competitive in power and area relative to existing baselines.

Conclusion: Exploiting stage-awareness and an agent-based decomposition of the physical design flow enables more efficient and effective QoR optimization than flat tuning or monolithic LLM script generation. By coordinating specialized agents and reusing intermediate states, AgenticPD improves post-route timing without sacrificing power and area, demonstrating the value of agentic frameworks for complex EDA optimization tasks.

Abstract: Physical design quality-of-results~(QoR) optimization is hard and expensive. Choices made at one stage can help or hurt later stages. Each evaluation requires a costly EDA run through the full flow. While existing methods still treat optimization as flat parameter tuning or a LLM-based script generation task, we present AgenticPD, a stage-aware agentic framework for physical design QoR optimization. Instead of re-running the full flow after every trial, AgenticPD is organized around the stage boundaries of the physical design flow, where a Judge Agent navigates the search and stage-specialized agents make local decisions within their own stage using stage-local tools. Additionally, the agent harness in AgenticPD provides structured observations, execution history, and agent context management. As a result, the system can branch from prior intermediate states and reuse checkpoints to continue the optimization procedure, and every candidate is evaluated at the post-route signoff. Across these baselines, AgenticPD achieves strong post-route timing while remaining competitive in power and area.

</details>


### [181] [CARL: Constraint-Aware Reinforcement Learning for Planning with LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04854)
*Qiuyi Qi,Jinjian Zhang,Mutian Bao,Tian Liang,Guocong Li,Dongnan Liu,Wei Zhou,Jie Liu,Ming Kong,Linjian Mo,Feng Zhang,Qiang Zhu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces CARL, a reinforcement learning framework that makes large language models more aware of and compliant with task constraints during plan generation, significantly improving constraint-respecting reasoning on several benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs can reason well but often generate plans that violate explicit task constraints (e.g., forbidden actions, resource limits), which makes them unreliable for real-world planning tasks. Existing fixes depend on external tools or decomposition and do not change the model’s internal tendency to respect constraints, so there is a need for an intrinsic, scalable way to make LLMs focus on constraints during generation.

Method: The authors propose Constraint-Aware Reinforcement Learning (CARL), which modifies the RL training signal to be constraint-focused. CARL compares the model’s output distributions when it is given constrained versus unconstrained inputs and constructs a reward that encourages outputs that change appropriately in response to constraints. Outputs that reflect the constraints get higher reward, while outputs that ignore them are penalized. CARL is compatible with standard RL/RFT pipelines and does not rely on external solvers, decomposers, or stronger teacher models, enabling end-to-end training of constraint-aware planners.

Result: On three planning-style benchmarks—BlocksWorld, TravelPlanner, and T-Eval—models trained with CARL outperform standard Reinforcement Fine-Tuning and leading reasoning-oriented LLMs. The gains are particularly notable in metrics that measure constraint satisfaction and attention to provided constraints, indicating that CARL meaningfully increases constraint adherence without external tooling.

Conclusion: CARL provides an effective, scalable way to enhance LLMs’ intrinsic awareness of task constraints through a specialized RL reward based on distributional differences between constrained and unconstrained prompts. This leads to more constraint-compliant planning and better overall performance on diverse benchmarks, suggesting that CARL is a promising direction for deploying LLMs in real-world, constraint-heavy applications.

Abstract: Despite their strong reasoning capabilities and extensive world knowledge, Large Language Models (LLMs) frequently generate plans that violate task constraints, undermining their reliability in real-world applications. This deficiency arises from a lack of systematic mechanisms to incorporate constraint information during the generation process. While existing approaches attempt to mitigate this by relying on external tools or task decomposition, they fail to enhance the model's intrinsic constraint awareness. To address this, we propose Constraint-Aware Reinforcement Learning (CARL), a novel RL framework designed to strengthen LLMs' intrinsic focus on constraints. CARL introduces a constraint-aware reward by comparing the model's output distributions under constrained and unconstrained inputs, encouraging constraint focus and penalizing neglect. Compatible with various RL frameworks and requiring no external solvers or top models, CARL enables scalable, end-to-end constraint-aware planning. Extensive experiments on BlocksWorld, TravelPlanner, and T-Eval demonstrate that CARL significantly outperforms standard Reinforcement Fine-Tuning (RFT) baselines and state-of-the-art reasoning models, exhibiting a markedly increased focus on constraints.

*Mohammed Saim Ahmed Quadri,Yunzhe Xue,Justin W. Ady,Usman Roshan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Medi-Gemma is a clinical decision support architecture that decouples perception and data orchestration, injects ground-truth structured data into LLM prompts, and uses deterministic safeguards to improve safety and factual fidelity in wound-care decision making.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs hallucinate, struggle with deterministic reasoning over tabular EMR data, and can miss relevant information in vector-based retrieval, which is unacceptable in high-stakes clinical settings like wound pathology triage. There is a need for an architecture that constrains LLM behavior, preserves traceable reasoning, and tightly grounds generation in validated clinical data and protocols.

Method: The authors design Medi-Gemma, a CDSS with a decoupled architecture. A ClinicalOrchestrator coordinates a multi-stage pipeline. A DataManager pre-processes EMR data via type coercion without generative inference. A hierarchical IntentRouter dispatches natural language queries either to deterministic analytics through a PandasQueryEngine or to a ClinicalRAGEngine backed by a CPU-optimized vector store. The Ground Truth Injection Module intercepts patient-specific queries, fetches the latest structured clinical state from Pandas, and injects it as overriding context into the LLM prompt. Safety is enforced by a ProtocolManager that maps clinical terms to evidence-based risk pathways and by a SafetyVerifier phrase filter that blocks unsafe outputs.

Result: In validation experiments, the Medi-Gemma architecture removes semantic context drift, avoids database compilation crashes, and increases factual adherence of LLM outputs to the underlying clinical data repositories. The system demonstrates more reliable, grounded, and stable behavior in wound pathology triage and workflow automation tasks compared with less constrained LLM setups.

Conclusion: Medi-Gemma provides a safer and more robust architectural pattern for LLM-based clinical decision support by tightly coupling generation with validated structured data, using deterministic routing and analytics, and enforcing protocol-based safety checks. This pattern is proposed as a template for CDSS design when structured data fidelity, retrieval grounding, and deterministic safeguards are critical.

Abstract: Deploying Large Language Models (LLMs) in high-stakes clinical settings remains limited by structural hallucinations, weak deterministic reasoning over tabular patient data, and omissions in vector retrieval. This paper presents the architecture and validation of Medi-Gemma, a Clinical Decision Support System (CDSS) for wound pathology triage and workflow automation. The platform introduces a decoupled framework that separates clinical perception from data orchestration while preserving traceable reasoning. Medi-Gemma uses a multi-stage pipeline coordinated by a centralized ClinicalOrchestrator. Data requests are handled without generative inference by a DataManager that cleans unstructured Electronic Medical Record (EMR) files through type coercion. Natural language queries are processed by a hierarchical IntentRouter, which routes requests to deterministic analytics paths executed by a PandasQueryEngine or to patient-specific reasoning managed by a ClinicalRAGEngine using a CPU-optimized vector store. A key contribution is the Ground Truth Injection Module, which intercepts patient-specific queries, extracts numeric identification tokens, queries the structured dataframe via Pandas, retrieves the latest validated clinical state, and embeds this snapshot as an overriding context block in the LLM prompt before generation. Safety compliance is enforced by a deterministic ProtocolManager that maps clinical terminology to fixed evidence-based risk pathways, while a SafetyVerifier phrase filter prevents output rule violations. Validation shows that this architecture eliminates semantic context drift, prevents database compilation crashes, and improves factual adherence to backend clinical repositories. These results support Medi-Gemma as a safer pattern for LLM-based clinical decision support where structured data fidelity, retrieval grounding, and deterministic safeguards are essential.
