### [183] [STAPO: Selective Trajectory-Aware Policy Optimization for LLM Agent Training](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.04963)
*Qiuyi Qi,Tian Liang,Mutian Bao,Jinjian Zhang,Dongnan Liu,Wei Zhou,Linjian Mo,Ming Kong,Jie Liu,Feng Zhang,Qiang Zhu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new RL method, STAPO, that uses a normalized entropy signal to identify and correct low-quality, off-trajectory decisions of LLM agents in long-horizon tasks, achieving state-of-the-art performance and reducing trajectory neglect.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RL training for LLM agents on long-horizon tasks suffers from sparse, delayed rewards, causing agents to lose track of goals and prior context (“trajectory neglect”). Prior step-level supervision methods rely on Shannon entropy of action distributions, which mixes up environment/state difficulty with the agent’s own confidence and thus fails to reliably flag poor decisions. There is a need for a better reliability signal that more precisely identifies steps where the agent drifts from the intended trajectory, so that learning can explicitly target those steps.

Method: The authors introduce normalized entropy as a confidence measure that compares the entropy of the current action distribution to the agent’s typical entropy for the same state, isolating deviations that signal trajectory neglect rather than inherent state complexity. Using this signal, they design STAPO (Selective Trajectory-Aware Policy Optimization), a hierarchical group-based RL framework that (1) detects outlier steps likely associated with trajectory neglect; and (2) applies a combined optimization scheme that includes trajectory-aware rewards (taking into account the role of each step in the full trajectory) and trajectory-independent penalties to correct those steps while maintaining training stability.

Result: In experiments on ALFWorld, WebShop, and Search-Augmented QA benchmarks, STAPO outperforms prior state-of-the-art RL methods on long-horizon LLM agent tasks. The results show improved task success and clear reductions in trajectory neglect behaviors, indicating that normalized entropy is a more effective supervision signal for identifying and repairing poor intermediate decisions.

Conclusion: Normalized entropy provides a more reliable indicator of decision quality than raw Shannon entropy by decoupling confidence from state complexity, enabling targeted correction of trajectory neglect. The proposed STAPO framework effectively exploits this signal to selectively optimize problematic steps, improving trajectory awareness and stability in RL training of LLM agents. Overall, STAPO delivers state-of-the-art performance and robustly mitigates trajectory neglect in complex, long-horizon agentic tasks.

Abstract: Reinforcement Learning (RL) is the dominant paradigm for training Large Language Model (LLM) agents on long-horizon tasks. However, sparse and delayed rewards often lead to trajectory neglect, in which agents lose focus on the task goal and interaction history at intermediate steps. Prior work has explored step-level supervision using Shannon-entropy-based uncertainty signals, which conflate inherent state complexity with agent confidence and therefore provide unreliable estimates of decision reliability. To address this issue, we propose normalized entropy, which measures confidence deviations relative to an agent's average behavior under a given state, thereby strengthening the association between low-quality actions and trajectory neglect. Building on this insight, we introduce Selective Trajectory-Aware Policy Optimization (STAPO), a hierarchical group-based RL framework. STAPO leverages normalized entropy to locate outlier steps associated with trajectory neglect and optimizes them via a joint mechanism of trajectory-aware reward and trajectory-independent penalty, enhancing trajectory awareness while preserving training stability. Extensive experiments on ALFWorld, WebShop, and Search-Augmented QA demonstrate that STAPO achieves state-of-the-art performance while substantially alleviating trajectory neglect, validating its effectiveness and robustness for agentic tasks.

</details>

Evaluate the framework on pieces such as "Autumn Leaves" and "It’s a Long Way to Tipperary" via quantitative metrics and expert judgments.

Result: The optimization stage is found to decrease chord density, increase harmonic stability, and improve functional organization of the generated harmonizations. Expert evaluations show that higher harmonic complexity does not necessarily correspond to more natural-sounding harmonizations, underscoring the dependence on stylistic context. Overall, the system successfully frames harmonic generation as structured decision-making in a constrained search space.

Conclusion: Quantum-inspired methods can serve as a promising framework for modeling complex, creative decision processes in music harmonization, effectively integrating domain-specific musical knowledge with interference-based search. Although still preliminary, the approach supports broader research into quantum-inspired models of cognition and decision-making in biological and creative systems.

Abstract: This paper introduces a quantum-inspired computational framework for harmonic decision-making in music. The proposed approach formulates harmonization as an optimization problem within a structured combinatorial space, where multiple candidate chord sequences are evaluated under interacting musical constraints. The model combines an interference-based harmonization stage with a classical optimization procedure grounded in tonal harmony. The quantum-inspired component enables the parallel consideration of multiple harmonic alternatives, while the classical stage refines the resulting sequences to ensure structural coherence and stylistic plausibility.
  The framework is evaluated on selected musical examples, including Autumn Leaves and It's a Long Way to Tipperary. Quantitative analysis shows that the optimization stage significantly reduces chord density, increases harmonic stability, and improves functional organization. At the same time, expert evaluation highlights the importance of stylistic context, demonstrating that increased harmonic complexity is not always perceived as more natural. The results suggest that harmonic generation can be interpreted as a structured decision-making process in a constrained search space. The proposed approach provides a computational model that integrates domain-specific knowledge with an interference-based search mechanism.
  Although preliminary, this work indicates that quantum-inspired methods may offer a useful framework for modeling complex decision processes in creative domains such as music. The proposed framework contributes to ongoing research on quantum-inspired models of cognition and decision-making in complex biological and creative systems.

These findings show that carefully scoped and rigorously safeguarded LLM-based agents can reliably automate complex healthcare operational workflows while maintaining safety guarantees and achieving substantial cost efficiency. The source code and system implementation are publicly available at https://github.com/Hadi-Hsn/CareConnect.

### [186] [Diffusion-Guided Uncertainty-Aware Delayed Policy Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05064)
*Junqi Tu,Zejiao Liu,Fangfei Li,Yang Tang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies reinforcement learning with delayed observations and proposes DUPO, a diffusion-based method that models the discrepancy between delayed and true states to improve policy performance under stochastic delays.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In real-world reinforcement learning applications, feedback and observations are often delayed, which significantly degrades performance. Existing methods attempt to handle delays with augmented states or state prediction but usually ignore the stochastic mismatch between delayed and true states in Markov decision processes. The authors want to formally characterize this discrepancy and design an algorithm that remains robust and performant under random, potentially long delays.

Method: The authors first provide a theoretical analysis showing that in stochastic MDPs, there is an intrinsic discrepancy between delayed observations and the current true state, and that this discrepancy harms the optimal policy. They then introduce Diffusion Guided Uncertainty Aware Delayed Policy Optimization (DUPO), which uses a diffusion model to learn the mapping from delayed state messages to current states. DUPO estimates the uncertainty/discrepancy between delayed and current states and uses these estimates to adaptively weight delayed policies during policy optimization, thereby accounting for delay-induced uncertainty.

Result: On continuous-control robotic benchmarks with multiple stochastic delays, DUPO achieves higher returns than previous delay-handling methods. The experiments show that DUPO maintains strong performance even when delays are long and randomly varying, highlighting its robustness to challenging delayed-feedback conditions.

Conclusion: Delayed observations in stochastic MDPs inherently create a discrepancy that can degrade optimal policies if ignored. By explicitly modeling the relationship between delayed and current states with a diffusion model and making the policy delay- and uncertainty-aware, DUPO improves reinforcement learning performance under stochastic, long, and random delays, outperforming existing baselines on robotic control tasks.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning in real world environments often suffers from severe performance degradation due to delayed feedback. Existing approaches typically mitigate performance degradation caused by observation delays by constructing augmented states or predicting the true states. However, these methods often overlook the inherent discrepancy between delayed state and true states induced by stochastic MDP. We theoretically prove the existence of such a discrepancy and show that it leads to the degradation of the optimal policy. To address this challenge, we propose Diffusion Guided Uncertainty Aware Delayed Policy Optimization (DUPO). Our method explicitly models the relationship between delayed state message and the current state using a diffusion model, and leverages the resulting discrepancy estimates to weight delayed policies. Extensive experiments on continuous robotic control tasks with multiple stochastic delays demonstrate that DUPO consistently outperforms existing methods and remains effective even under long and random delay scenarios.

### [187] [ASSEMCAD: Production-Ready CAD Assembly Generation from Natural Language](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05123)
*Yurui Dong,Shu Zou,Siqi Li,Nianchen Deng,Hongbin Zhou,Xuemeng Yang,Pinlong Cai,Licheng Wen,Xinyu Cai,Botian Shi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AssemCAD is a framework that turns natural-language descriptions into production-ready mechanical CAD assemblies by introducing an interpretable, axiom-grounded assembly specification and a deterministic geometry execution library.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing Text-to-CAD methods work reasonably well for single parts but fail for practical mechanical assemblies because assemblies require coordinated reasoning about multiple interacting components, functional interfaces, assembly constraints, engineering principles, and physical validity. Directly generating monolithic CAD code cannot guarantee mechanically valid, reusable assemblies or provide interpretability and verification. The authors aim to fill this gap and make text-driven design suitable for real engineering workflows.

Method: The authors design AssemCAD, which first converts a natural-language description into an explicit Assembly Specification. This specification includes typed parts, geometry-backed ports (interfaces), executable mates (constraints between parts), and engineering axioms that ground each assembly relation in underlying engineering principles. They implement a port- and mate-based CAD assembly library that executes the symbolic assembly via deterministic mate transformations and validates interfaces using B-Rep geometry as evidence. On top of this representation, AssemCAD can synthesize reusable parametric component factories for both standard and open-world geometries. They evaluate the framework using the AssemBench benchmark and run it with different large foundation-model backbones.

Result: On the AssemBench benchmark, AssemCAD significantly outperforms code-centric CAD generation baselines in preserving the intended assembly structure and achieving physically valid assemblies. It also demonstrates that the approach generalizes across different large model backbones, indicating that the gains are due to the axiom-grounded representation and geometric execution rather than a single particular model.

Conclusion: Axiom-grounded assembly reasoning combined with deterministic geometric execution enables robust, interpretable, and reusable Text-to-CAD for mechanical assemblies. AssemCAD moves beyond single-part generation and monolithic CAD code toward production-ready mechanical assembly design, providing a structured assembly specification and execution environment that better aligns with real engineering practice.

Abstract: Recent advances in large language models and programmatic CAD have significantly improved Text-to-CAD generation for individual parts. However, production-ready mechanical assembly generation remains largely unsolved. Unlike single-part modeling, assemblies require coordinated reasoning over multiple components, functional interfaces, assembly relations, engineering principles, and physical consistency. Consequently, directly generating executable CAD code is insufficient for constructing mechanically valid and reusable assemblies. We present AssemCAD, an axiom-grounded framework for production-ready CAD assembly generation from natural language. Instead of representing an assembly as monolithic CAD code, AssemCAD first constructs an axiomatic Assembly Specification consisting of typed parts, geometry-backed ports, executable mates, and engineering axioms. Each assembly relation is explicitly grounded in one or more engineering principles, making the resulting specification interpretable, reusable, and verifiable. To realize this specification, AssemCAD introduces a port- and mate-based CAD assembly library that executes symbolic assembly relations through deterministic mate transformations and validates declared interfaces using concrete B-Rep geometric evidence. Built on this representation and library, AssemCAD further supports on-demand synthesis of reusable parametric component factories for both standard and open-world geometries. Experiments on AssemBench show that AssemCAD substantially improves assembly preservation and physical validity over code-centric CAD generation baselines, while generalizing across different foundation-model backbones. ### [189] [DSpark: Confidence-Scheduled Speculative Decoding with Semi-Autoregressive Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05147)

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes DSpark, a speculative decoding framework that speeds up LLM inference by combining semi-autoregressive drafting with adaptive, confidence-based verification to reduce rejection and system waste.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing speculative decoding methods use parallel drafters that generate long token blocks in one pass, but these lack inter-token dependencies, causing rapid acceptance decay (especially toward the suffix). Moreover, naive verification of all drafted tokens wastes verification capacity on low-confidence tokens, harming throughput in multi-user serving systems. The authors aim to maintain or improve draft acceptance while better utilizing verification resources in realistic, high-concurrency deployments.

Method: DSpark introduces: (1) a semi-autoregressive drafter architecture that combines a parallel backbone with a lightweight sequential module, adding intra-block dependency modeling to improve draft quality and reduce suffix acceptance decay; and (2) confidence-scheduled verification, which dynamically decides how many drafted tokens to verify per request using estimated prefix survival probabilities and the measured throughput characteristics of the serving engine. This makes verification both quality-aware and load-aware.

Result: In offline evaluations on multiple domains, DSpark achieves longer accepted token lengths than strong autoregressive and parallel drafters. In an online deployment within the DeepSeek-V4 serving system, DSpark cuts verification waste and, relative to the production baseline (MTP-1), improves per-user generation speed by about 60–85% at the same overall system throughput.

Conclusion: DSpark effectively unifies high-throughput parallel drafting with adaptive verification, improving both model-side token acceptance and system-side efficiency. It not only speeds up per-user generation without sacrificing throughput, but also avoids throughput collapse under strict latency constraints, extending the attainable performance–interactivity tradeoff (the Pareto frontier) for practical LLM serving systems.

Abstract: Speculative decoding accelerates Large Language Model (LLM) inference by decoupling draft generation from target verification. While recent parallel drafters efficiently propose long token sequences in a single forward pass, they suffer from rapid acceptance decay due to a lack of inter-token dependencies. Furthermore, indiscriminately verifying these extended blocks wastes critical batch capacity on tokens with high rejection risks, severely degrading throughput in high-concurrency serving systems. We introduce DSpark, a speculative decoding framework that unifies high-throughput parallel generation with adaptive, load-aware verification. To maintain draft quality, DSpark utilizes a semi-autoregressive architecture, coupling a parallel backbone with a lightweight sequential module, to introduce intra-block dependency modeling and mitigate suffix decay. To optimize system efficiency, DSpark employs confidence-scheduled verification, dynamically tailoring the verification length for each request based on estimated prefix survival probabilities and engine-specific throughput profiles. On offline benchmarks across diverse domains, DSpark substantially improves the accepted length over state-of-the-art autoregressive and parallel drafters. When deployed within the DeepSeek-V4 serving system under live user traffic, DSpark successfully mitigates verification waste. Compared to the established production baseline (MTP-1), DSpark accelerates per-user generation speeds by 60 to 85 percent at matched throughput levels. More importantly, by preventing severe throughput degradation under strict interactivity constraints, it enables performance tiers that were previously unattainable, shifting the Pareto frontier of our serving system.

### [190] [The Changing Role of Symbolic Methods in Artificial Intelligence](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05168)
*Jun Sun*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that explicit symbolic reasoning is not an inherent hallmark of intelligence but a byproduct of using simplified models, and that in the era of powerful foundation models, symbols will matter more as human–AI interfaces than as core reasoning engines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Historically, AI has treated symbolic reasoning as central to intelligence, but modern foundation models work impressively well with little explicit symbolic logic. This raises a conceptual and practical puzzle: if such models can solve many tasks, what is the true role and future value of symbolic methods in AI? The authors want a unifying principle that explains both the past importance of symbolic AI and the present success of large neural models, and that clarifies where symbolic methods still matter.

Method: The paper is primarily conceptual/theoretical. It introduces the Compression Principle, which views any computational model as a compressed, simplified representation of reality, and then analyzes how explicit symbolic reasoning emerges as a compensatory mechanism for information lost in this compression. From this, it derives the Modeling–Reasoning Trade-off as a qualitative relationship between model richness and the need for separate symbolic reasoning. The authors then apply this framework to interpret historical AI paradigms and today’s foundation models, and to reason about human–AI interaction and governance.

Result: The authors obtain two linked theoretical claims: (1) explicit symbolic reasoning is best understood as a computational workaround for information omitted during model construction rather than as an essential ingredient of intelligence; and (2) as models become richer and less compressed—like modern foundation models—the need for such explicit symbolic reasoning within the system diminishes. They also characterize a shift: symbolic structures become increasingly important as external artifacts (interfaces for specification, verification, regulation, and trust) instead of internal mechanisms of AI systems.

Conclusion: Symbolic reasoning should not be treated as the defining feature of intelligence. Instead, it arises from the necessity to reason over simplified, compressed models of the world. As AI systems grow more powerful and internally non-symbolic, symbolic methods will be most vital as human-facing interfaces—ways for people to express constraints, inspect and verify behavior, regulate autonomous systems, and cultivate justified trust—rather than as the primary computational substrate of the AI itself.

Abstract: Why do intelligent systems need to perform explicit symbolic reasoning? Computer science has traditionally regarded symbolic reasoning as a defining component of intelligence. Yet the remarkable success of modern foundation models raises a fundamental question: if increasingly capable AI systems can operate with little explicit symbolic reasoning, what role do symbolic methods actually play?
  This article argues that explicit symbolic reasoning is not a fundamental property of intelligence, but a computational consequence of operating on simplified models of reality. We propose the Compression Principle: every computational model is a simplified representation of reality, and explicit symbolic reasoning compensates for information omitted during model construction. From this principle, we derive the Modeling--Reasoning Trade-off: as computational models preserve richer representations of the world, the need for explicit symbolic reasoning correspondingly decreases. This perspective provides a unified explanation for both the historical success of symbolic methods and the remarkable effectiveness of modern foundation models.
  Paradoxically, the same development makes symbolic methods increasingly important for humans. As intelligent systems become more capable and more opaque, symbolic representations increasingly serve as interfaces through which humans specify requirements, verify behavior, regulate autonomous systems, and establish trust. We therefore argue that the future of symbolic methods lies not primarily as the computational engine of intelligent systems, but as the symbolic interface between increasingly capable AI systems and the humans who build, govern, and depend upon them.

However, most existing benchmarks evaluate agents in simplified, idealized settings. They typically rely on pre-packaged tool interfaces, overlook critical steps, and assume inputs are clean and fully specified. Consequently, they understate the difficulty of real deployments, where uncertainty and noise are ubiquitous and agents must proactively explore the environment to uncover new tools. To bridge this gap, we present AgentGym2, a new evaluation framework with task instances grounded in real-world end-to-end working demands. Beyond reasoning and planning, it measures agents' ability to execute end-to-end procedures, discover tools via exploration, compose tools for unseen tasks, and remain robust to noisy and underspecified information. Experiments on 15 proprietary and open-source models show that even SOTA systems like Gemini and GPT-5 struggle on AgentGym2, revealing a substantial gap between the capability of current agents and the demands of real-world applications.

### [192] [CP-WSP: A Declarative CP-SAT Framework for Configurable Multi-Constraint Workforce Scheduling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05177)
*Vipul Patel,Anirudh Deodhar,Dagnachew Birru*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a flexible CP-SAT framework (CP-WSP) for workforce scheduling that strictly enforces many regulatory constraints while optimizing multiple soft objectives, all configurable via JSON, and demonstrate its effectiveness on benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Workforce scheduling is NP-hard and real-world instances involve complex regulatory, fairness, and operational requirements. Existing constraint programming formulations are simplified: they usually support only a small set of constraints, lack explicit handling of mandatory breaks with control over when they occur, do not model acuity-weighted workload equity, have limited temporal granularity, struggle with inter-week schedule stability, and handle cross-midnight shifts poorly. There is a need for a more expressive, configurable, and practical framework that can represent these real-world constraints and objectives without requiring users to modify solver code.

Method: They design CP-WSP, a declarative CP-SAT model. It encodes 14 hard constraints as strict requirements, and 15 soft objectives combined into a single weighted penalty function. The model is driven by a JSON specification so that different problem variants can be configured without code changes. Methodological innovations include: a shift-window variable decomposition that allows explicit scheduling of mandatory breaks with control over their centrality within shifts; modeling of acuity-weighted workload equity; support for multiple temporal resolutions (30–120 minutes) for demand-responsive staffing; a grid-offset preprocessing technique to correctly represent cross-midnight shifts; and mechanisms for inter-week schedule stability. They build a 36-configuration benchmark suite and evaluate the framework on both standard INRC-II benchmarks (hourly and shift-level) and synthetic instances.

Result: CP-WSP successfully models complex workforce scheduling requirements that existing CP formulations do not natively support, while maintaining zero violations of hard (regulatory) constraints by construction. The unified weighted penalty function enables simultaneous optimization of 15 soft objectives. The framework runs across a wide range of configurations, including varying time granularities, cross-midnight shifts, and stability constraints, and is validated on INRC-II benchmark sets and 36 synthetic benchmark configurations. The experimental results indicate that CP-WSP is effective and generally applicable, though precise numerical performance metrics are not given in the abstract.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that CP-WSP is a practical, expressive, and configurable CP-SAT framework for workforce scheduling that can capture a broader set of real-world constraints than prior models. By treating regulatory constraints as inviolable and optimizing multiple soft objectives through a unified penalty function, it offers both compliance and flexibility. The JSON-driven specification, support for complex features like mandatory break placement, acuity-based equity, multi-granularity time modeling, inter-week stability, and cross-midnight shifts, along with a public benchmark suite, positions CP-WSP as a useful platform for both practitioners and researchers for modeling, solving, and fairly comparing workforce scheduling problems.

Abstract: Workforce scheduling is an NP-hard combinatorial optimization problem requiring simultaneous satisfaction of labor regulations, coverage requirements, employee preferences and operational objectives. Existing CP formulations typically model simplified instances with 6-12 constraints at shift-level granularity and critically lack explicit support for: mandatory break scheduling with midpoint placement control; acuity weighted workload equity; sub-shift temporal granularity enabling demand-driven staffing; inter-week schedule stability; and cross-midnight shift patterns common in 24-hour operations. This paper presents CP-WSP: a declarative CP-SAT framework enforcing 14 hard constraints as mathematically inviolable requirements (zero regulatory violations by construction) while optimizing 15 soft objectives through a unified weighted penalty function -- all configurable via a JSON specification with no code changes required. Key contributions include: a shift-window variable decomposition enabling mandatory break scheduling with centrality control; acuity-weighted workload equity; multi-granularity temporal resolution from 30 minutes to 2 hours; inter-week schedule stability; a grid-offset preprocessing technique for cross-midnight shifts; and a reproducible 36-configuration benchmark suite for community comparison. Evaluated on INRC-II benchmarks at both hourly and shift-level granularity and on 36 synthetic configurations.

### [193] [Rethinking On-Policy Self-Distillation for Thinking Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05184)
*Simran Kaur,Narutatsu Ri,Yinghui He,Liam Fowl,Sanjeev Arora*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper finds that giving language models privileged information during self-distillation unexpectedly harms strong "thinking" models on long reasoning tasks, especially at high reasoning budgets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Self-distillation is widely seen as a way for language models to improve themselves by learning from their own outputs, especially when given extra information such as worked solutions. For reasoning-intensive "thinking" models with long test-time rollouts, it is natural to expect that providing such privileged context would help them learn better reasoning strategies. The authors want to test this assumption and understand when and why privileged self-distillation might fail, particularly for advanced reasoning models and long-chain mathematical benchmarks.

Method: The authors conduct empirical studies on five thinking-oriented language models from the Qwen3 and OLMo families. They evaluate performance on math competition benchmarks such as AIME24, AIME25, and HMMT25 under different distillation regimes: standard self-distillation, privileged-context self-distillation (where the teacher sees extra solution information that the student does not), standard on-policy distillation (OPD), and privileged OPD. They analyze performance as a function of rollout length (avg@16 and other budgets) and examine token-level behavior at high-entropy forking positions—points in the reasoning where multiple plausible continuations exist. They track how privileged information affects fork rates, self-correction branches, and usage of verification, backtracking, and hedging tokens, comparing thinking models to instruction-tuned models.

Result: Privileged self-distillation consistently degrades the performance of strong thinking models on long reasoning traces, leading to relative drops of up to 17% in avg@16 accuracy on the examined math benchmarks. The performance degradation increases with the amount of privileged context the teacher has that is withheld from the student and is most severe at longer rollout budgets, which are otherwise where thinking models show the greatest gains. While standard OPD helps thinking models, introducing privileged context into OPD reverses these benefits. The privileged teacher context alters learning at high-entropy forking positions: it reduces fork rates and discourages sampled reconsideration tokens in thinking models but not in instruction-tuned models. Students distilled from privileged teachers exhibit fewer verification, backtracking, and hedging markers in their reasoning, even after accounting for sequence length.

Conclusion: Privileged-context self-distillation is not universally beneficial and can be harmful for strong thinking models, especially on long reasoning chains. When the teacher sees information that the student never sees at inference time, the distillation process can suppress valuable exploration, self-correction, and branching behavior at key decision points, leading to worse performance despite seemingly richer supervision. In contrast, instruction-tuned models can benefit from privileged context. For advanced thinking models, effective self-distillation must carefully preserve and shape token-level signals related to correction, verification, and reasoning steps rather than simply exposing the teacher to extra solution information. Designing distillation schemes that respect the dynamics at high-entropy forks is crucial for improving, rather than degrading, long-form reasoning ability.

Abstract: Self-distillation is a promising recipe for self-improvement in language models. In this setting, a model can serve as its own teacher when given privileged information, such as a solution to a math problem. This seems especially appealing for thinking models, which can use test-time reasoning to absorb the privileged information. Surprisingly, we show that privileged self-distillation degrades thinking models on long reasoning traces: across five Qwen3 and OLMo thinking models evaluated on AIME24, AIME25, and HMMT25, privileged-context distillation causes a relative drop of up to 17% in avg@16 accuracy. The degradation scales with the amount of privileged context withheld from the student and is most pronounced at long rollout budgets, where thinking models otherwise obtain their largest gains. This failure mode is not specific to self-distillation: on-policy distillation (OPD) improves thinking models, but privileged OPD reverses these gains. Our diagnostics link this failure mode to how privileged teacher context reshapes learning at high-entropy forking positions, where multiple continuations remain plausible and may lead to different reasoning paths. Privileged context lowers fork rates in thinking-model rollouts but not in instruction-model rollouts. This leads to an interesting dichotomy, where privileged context can help instruction-tuned models but hurts stronger thinking models. The effect is visible when the student begins a self-correction branch, where privileged OPD penalizes sampled reconsideration tokens that vanilla OPD supports. Thinking models trained with a privileged teacher produce fewer verification, backtracking, and hedging markers, even after length normalization. These findings indicate that self-distillation for strong thinking models requires attention to token-level signal, especially around correction and reasoning steps.

### [195] [Reason, Reward, Refine: Step-Level Errors Corrections with Structured Feedback for Physics Reasoning in Small Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05199)
*Raj Jaiswal,Dhruv Jain,Rishabh Dhawan,Sree Krishna Uppalapati,Shin'ichi Satoh,Tanuja Ganu,Rajiv Ratn Shah*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper identifies a structural weakness in small language models’ physics reasoning—early-step errors propagate and corrupt later inferences—and introduces a training framework that rewards correct intermediate steps without using full solution supervision, significantly improving reasoning accuracy on physics benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Small language models struggle with multi-step physics reasoning: if they make an early mistake, subsequent steps tend to build on that error, leading to fully incorrect solutions. This is worsened by limited domain knowledge, hallucinations in extended derivations, and sensitivity to distributional shifts. Existing step-level improvement methods often require costly human annotations or preference data and may depend on exposing ground-truth solutions. The authors aim to develop a training framework that targets the first reasoning error, improves robustness of multi-step reasoning, and avoids dependence on expensive annotation or full-solution supervision.

Method: The authors propose a step-level reward framework. During training, an external verifier inspects the model’s chain-of-thought solution, detects the first incorrect reasoning step, and provides targeted, structured feedback about that error. The model is then trained to revise its solution using policy gradient optimization with KL regularization, which constrains the updated policy from drifting too far from the original. Crucially, the model is never given full ground-truth solutions as generation targets, and no explicit preference data or step-level human annotation is needed. The verifier is used only in training and not at inference time.

Result: Evaluated on five physics reasoning benchmarks, the framework yields substantial performance gains for small language models. It improves overall answer accuracy by 17–20 percentage points over standard Chain-of-Thought prompting and by 10–16 points over the strongest existing baseline. In detailed error analysis, calculation errors decrease from 56.9% to 23.5%, and miscomprehension errors decline from 22.3% to 12.0% in the best observed configurations. Conceptual errors are also reduced—from 89.7% to 68.7%—though they remain the dominant and most difficult failure mode.

Conclusion: The study shows that explicitly targeting and correcting the first error in a reasoning chain, via a verifier-driven step-level reward and revision framework, can markedly enhance the physics reasoning capabilities of small language models without requiring ground-truth solution supervision or preference datasets. While the method significantly cuts calculation and miscomprehension errors and improves benchmark accuracy, conceptual understanding remains a major challenge, suggesting future work should focus on deeper conceptual learning and more robust handling of abstract physics principles.

Abstract: Physics reasoning fails structurally in small language models: an error at any step propagates forward, corrupting every inference that follows. Limited domain knowledge, hallucination under multi-step derivation, and distributional sensitivity compound this failure. We propose a step-level reward framework that identifies the first reasoning error, generates targeted structured feedback, and trains the model to revise its solution via policy gradient with KL regularization, without exposing it to ground truth solutions as generation targets. Unlike annotation-dependent step-level methods, no preference data construction is required and the external verifier operates exclusively at training time. Across five physics benchmarks, our framework delivers accuracy gains of 17-20% over CoT prompting and 10-16% over the strongest baseline, reduces calculation errors from 56.9% to 23.5%, and reduces miscomprehension errors from 22.3% to 12.0% in the best observed cases. Conceptual errors reduce from 89.7% to 68.7%, yet persist as the hardest failure mode across all conditions.

### [196] [EvoAgentBench: Benchmarking Agent Self-Evolution via Ability Transfer](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05202)
*Xingze Gao,Chuanrui Hu,Hongda Chen,Pengfei Yao,Zhao Wang,Yi Bai,Zhengwei Wu,Yunyun Han,Xiaofeng Cong,Jie Gui,Yafeng Deng,Teng Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents EvoAgentBench, a benchmark to evaluate how LLM-based agents self-evolve by reusing learned procedural abilities across tasks and domains, rather than just recalling information or solving isolated episodes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing benchmarks for LLM agents either focus on single-episode task performance or on memory as static information retention. They do not isolate or measure the agent’s ability to distill past experience into reusable procedures (for search, debugging, verification, etc.) and transfer these across different but related tasks. The authors aim to fill this gap by creating a benchmark that targets procedural, ability-level transfer and self-evolution in long-horizon agent systems.

Method: The authors construct EvoAgentBench across four domains: web research, algorithmic reasoning, software engineering, and knowledge work. They run agent executions and extract trace-grounded “Abilities” (i.e., concrete procedures inferred from execution traces). These abilities are canonicalized into operational units and organized into domain-specific Ability Graphs that connect tasks sharing procedural overlap. The benchmark uses a train/test split (528/267 tasks) where each test task is guaranteed to have verified supporting abilities from the training side. They evaluate transfer performance using two scaffolding approaches and three LLM backbones, and they also study how curated Ability content can be reused across model families.

Result: Curated Ability content is shown to transfer reliably across different model families, demonstrating that the benchmark captures meaningful procedural knowledge that is model-agnostic. However, no fully automatic method for extracting, routing, and applying these abilities achieves consistent positive performance gains across all domains and settings. This indicates current systems are limited in robustly encoding and exploiting procedural experience for self-evolution.

Conclusion: EvoAgentBench provides a new, fine-grained evaluation framework for agent self-evolution that moves beyond overall task accuracy to examine how agents encode, route, and apply procedural experience. The benchmark reveals both the potential and current limitations of automatic ability transfer methods, and is intended as a public resource to drive progress on more robust, self-improving LLM agents.

Abstract: Agent self-evolution in long-horizon LLM systems is largely procedural: useful experience is not merely stored information, but reusable procedures for searching, debugging, and verification. Yet current evaluations do not isolate this form of transfer. Agent benchmarks test single-episode task solving; memory benchmarks target information retention rather than procedural reuse. We introduce EvoAgentBench, a benchmark for agent self-evolution via Ability-guided transfer across four agentic domains: web research, algorithmic reasoning, software engineering, and knowledge work. EvoAgentBench extracts trace-grounded Abilities from agent executions, canonicalizes them into operational units, and builds domain-specific Ability Graphs linking tasks that share procedural overlap. By design, every test task is backed by verified training-side Ability support. Across a 528/267 train/test split, two scaffolds, and three backbones, curated Ability content transfers reliably across model families, but no current automatic method sustains positive gain in all settings. EvoAgentBench shifts self-evolution evaluation from aggregate accuracy comparison to fine-grained diagnosis of experience encoding, routing, and uptake. The benchmark is publicly available at https://huggingface.co/datasets/EverMind-AI/EvoAgentBench.

</details>


### [197] [MoP-JEPA: Hard-Assigned Predictor Mixtures for Stochastic JEPA World Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05238)
*Zhi Song,Ximing Xing,Zhenchao Tang,hanbo Huang,Tianxu Lv,minghao Yang,Zhongzheng Niu,He Bing,Lusheng Wang,Jianhua Yao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that standard JEPA world models fail in stochastic environments and proposes a hard-assigned mixture-of-predictors variant that predicts discrete successor modes instead of conditional means, enabling effective planning and providing a verification protocol to avoid coverage freeloading.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Joint Embedding Predictive Architecture (JEPA) world models are attractive for model-based planning but are structurally mismatched to stochastic environments because a single deterministic latent regressor predicts conditional means at branching points, which are not valid successor states and thus undermine planning. There is a need for a method that represents multimodal future dynamics in a form that planners can consume and that can be reliably evaluated without allowing methods to game multi-prediction metrics via coverage freeloading.

Method: The authors analyze the failure mode of deterministic and gated mixture-of-experts JEPA predictors in stochastic environments, proving that they collapse to conditional means at branching transitions. They then introduce MoP-JEPA with hard-assigned predictors (mixture of predictors), where each head specializes to a mode of the transition distribution, effectively quantizing successor states. To ensure fair comparison and guard against coverage freeloading, they design a verification protocol comprising input-agnostic codebook controls, shuffled-context tests, router-gated readouts, transition-precision guards, and a verified-route criterion where the model proposes an entire transition graph without seeing ground truth during proposal, which is then only used for verification.

Result: Theoretical results show that single deterministic JEPA predictors and gated MoE variants structurally regress to non-realizable conditional mean states at stochastic branching points, while MoP-JEPA converges to a quantization of the transition distribution with distinct heads for distinct successor modes. Empirically, on OGBench offline data with leak-free evaluation, planners using single-predictor rollouts achieve low success rates (0.02–0.09), whereas planning over MoP-JEPA’s predicted modes yields success up to 0.85, outperforming deterministic, gated-MoE, and variational predictors on all tasks. Under the verified-route criterion, the proposed method beats the strongest soft alternative by 2–5× on all three maze tasks, and the verification protocol exposes that the remaining performance gap in baseline raw scores comes from exploiting transitions that do not exist. The same model deployed in the real environment ranks second of seven published OGBench baselines on the hardest maze.

Conclusion: JEPA world models with a single deterministic predictor are fundamentally incapable of representing multimodal stochastic dynamics for planning, because they collapse to unrealizable conditional mean states at branching points. A mixture of predictors with hard assignment is a minimal extension that yields discrete successor modes suitable for planners and can be rigorously evaluated via a verification protocol that prevents coverage freeloading. This approach substantially improves planning performance on OGBench mazes and is competitive in real-environment execution, indicating that properly modeling multimodal dynamics is crucial for making JEPA-based world models effective for planning.

Abstract: JEPA world models predict the next latent state with a single deterministic predictor trained by latent regression. We show that this fails structurally when the environment is stochastic: at a branching transition, the regression-optimal predictor outputs the conditional mean of the successor embeddings, a point between the true next states that corresponds to no state at all. We prove this collapse for deterministic and gated mixture-of-experts predictors, and prove that MoP-JEPA's hard-assigned predictors converge instead to a quantizer of the transition distribution: one head per successor mode, enumerable in a single forward pass, which is the interface a planner consumes. On official OGBench offline data with leak-free evaluation, planning over single-predictor rollouts performs poorly ($0.02$--$0.09$ success) while planning over our predicted modes reaches up to $0.85$, ahead of deterministic, gated-MoE, and variational predictors on every task. Because multi-prediction evaluation invites coverage freeloading, a verification protocol is part of the method: an input-agnostic codebook control, a shuffled-context test, router-gated readouts, transition-precision guards, and a verified-route criterion in which the model proposes its transition graph blind and ground truth is used only to check the result. Under this criterion our method outperforms the strongest soft alternative on all three mazes ($2$--$5\times$), and the protocol identifies the remaining gap in that baseline's raw scores as routes through predicted transitions that do not exist. The same model executes in the real environment, placing second of seven against the published OGBench baselines on the hardest maze. Multimodal dynamics decide whether a JEPA world model can plan at all; a mixture of predictors with hard assignment is a minimal and verifiable fix.

### [198] [MetaSkill-Evolve: Recursive Self-Improvement of LLM Agents via Two-Timescale Meta-Skill Evolution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05297)
*Zefeng Wang,Minxi Yan,Jinhe Bi,Sikuan Yan,Volker Tresp,Yunpu Ma*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes MetaSkill-Evolve, a recursive framework that jointly evolves both task-specific skills and the meta-skills that govern how skills are improved, leading to better performance on long-horizon agent benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM agents can use external, hand-authored skills to solve complex, long-horizon tasks, but these skills are static, rarely optimal, and cannot adapt to diverse tasks. Recent self-improving agents can rewrite their own skills from traces, but only at the task level: the improvement procedure itself is fixed and not learned. There is a need for a system where not only what the agent does (task skills) but also how it improves itself (meta-skill pipeline) can adapt over time without extra models or objectives.

Method: The authors introduce MetaSkill-Evolve, a two-timescale evolutionary framework. Each branch in the search space holds a task skill s and a meta-skill m = (ψ, σ, α, π, ε). These five components parameterize the Analyzer, Retriever, Allocator, Proposer, and Evolver sub-agents that together form the skill-improvement pipeline. Task skills are updated on a fast loop by applying this pipeline to execution traces, while the meta-skill itself is updated on a slower loop using the same pipeline applied recursively to the meta-skill components. All five agents share a single frozen LLM backbone; adaptation happens by evolving the skill and meta-skill files rather than fine-tuning the model.

Result: On three benchmarks—OfficeQA, SealQA, and ALFWorld—MetaSkill-Evolve significantly outperforms baselines including agents without skills, with static skills, and with only single-level (non-recursive) evolution. It increases held-out test accuracy relative to the raw backbone model by +23.54 points on OfficeQA, +16.09 on SealQA, and +1.92 on ALFWorld.

Conclusion: Recursive evolution of both task skills and meta-skills is feasible using only a frozen backbone LLM and a unified improvement pipeline. By allowing the improvement process itself to adapt, MetaSkill-Evolve yields consistent gains over static and single-level self-improvement approaches on long-horizon, agentic benchmarks, suggesting that meta-skill evolution is a powerful approach for scalable, self-improving LLM agents.

Abstract: Recent LLM agents tackle increasingly long-horizon, open-ended tasks, and external skills, reusable procedural knowledge supplied to the agent, further extend this capability. However, a fixed, hand-authored skill is rarely optimal, and cannot adapt to the diversity of tasks an agent encounters. Self-improving agents address this by rewriting their own skill files from execution traces, yielding meaningful gains on challenging benchmarks. Yet such self-evolution remains non-recursive: it improves only the task skill (what the agent does) while the improvement procedure (how it improves) is authored once and held fixed. We introduce MetaSkill-Evolve, a two-timescale framework that makes agentic skill improvement recursive: every branch carries both a task skill $s$ and a branch-local meta-skill $m=(ψ,σ,α,π,\varepsilon)$ whose five components parameterise the Analyzer, Retriever, Allocator, Proposer, and Evolver agents of the improvement pipeline. Task skills evolve on a fast loop while the meta-skill evolves on a slower one under the same pipeline applied to itself, with no additional model or objective. With all five pipeline agents sharing a single frozen backbone, MetaSkill-Evolve outperforms no-skill, static-skill, and single-level evolution baselines on three agentic benchmarks (OfficeQA, SealQA, ALFWorld), improving held-out test accuracy over the raw backbone by +23.54, +16.09, and +1.92 points respectively.

</details>


### [199] [Evaluating and Understanding Model Editing for Medical Vision Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05310)
*Guli Zhu,Chenwei Wu,Liyue Shen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes M3Bench, a clinically grounded benchmark to systematically evaluate and stress-test multimodal model editing methods for medical vision-language models, revealing key trade-offs and failure modes of current editors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multimodal model editing benchmarks are designed for general-purpose tasks and do not capture the practical requirements, variability, and safety constraints of clinical medical imaging scenarios. Medical VLMs deployed in practice can make mistakes that need fast, targeted correction without full retraining, but there is no domain-appropriate way to measure whether edits remain reliable, local, and safe under realistic medical distribution shifts and complex clinical reasoning needs.

Method: The authors design M3Bench, a large benchmark of 16,276 clinically grounded questions covering diverse anatomies, imaging modalities, specialties, and clinical scenarios. The benchmark explicitly tests several dimensions critical for safe editing: robustness to image and text variation, modality and protocol shifts, compositional clinical knowledge, temporal progression, and both single and sequential edits. They then systematically evaluate four representative model editing methods—covering gradient-based and memory-based approaches—on six medical and general vision-language models, analyzing their behavior across M3Bench criteria and relating performance patterns to the underlying latent space geometry and the way each editor perturbs it.

Result: Empirical evaluation shows that none of the tested editing methods performs well across all criteria. Gradient-based editors have strong transfer and generalization of edits but frequently violate locality, causing unintended changes and catastrophic side effects. Memory-based editors better preserve locality but have limited compositional generalization and are highly sensitive to hyperparameters that depend on the particular model backbone. Analysis links these shortcomings to how the different editing strategies reshape the VLM latent representation space and its decision boundaries.

Conclusion: M3Bench provides a rigorous, clinically relevant stress test for multimodal model editing in medical vision-language models, revealing key trade-offs between locality, generality, and robustness in current editing methods. The benchmark exposes latent-space-related failure modes, offers practical guidance for choosing and tuning editors for safer post-deployment adaptation, and establishes a standardized platform for developing improved, clinically reliable editing techniques. It is released publicly to support further research and benchmarking.

Abstract: Model editing promises a fast, targeted way to correct post-deployment mistakes in medical vision-language models (VLMs) without costly retraining. However, existing multimodal model editing benchmarks focus on general-purpose tasks and do not reflect realistic clinical domain requirements and variability. To address this, we introduce M3Bench, a clinically grounded benchmark for multimodal model editing that evaluates whether an edit remains reliable, precise, and generalizable under the challenges of image and text variation, modality and protocol shifts, clinical knowledge composition, and temporal progression. M3Bench contains 16,276 questions spanning diverse anatomy, modalities, and specialties, and supports both single and sequential edits.
  By evaluating 4 representative editors across 6 medical and general VLMs, we find that no method excels across all criteria. Gradient-based editors achieve strong transfer but suffer from catastrophic locality violations, whereas memory-based methods preserve locality but lack compositional generality and exhibit high backbone-dependent hyperparameter sensitivity. We further attribute these failures to the latent space geometry of VLMs and how different editing methods shift its landscape. Overall, M3Bench establishes a rigorous clinical stress test for multimodal model editing and offers actionable guidance for safer post-deployment adaptation. The benchmark is publicly available at https://github.com/BioMed-AI-Lab-U-Michgan/M3Bench .

### [200] [OptiAgent: End-to-End Optimization Modeling via Multi-Agent Iterative Refinement](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05346)
*Adriana Laurindo Monteiro,Nayse Fagundes,Gabriel Mattos Langeloh,Gustavo de Oliveira Kanno,Priscila Louise Aguirre,Thiago Costa Rizuti da Rocha,Victor Leme Beltran*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: OptiAgent is a multi-agent LLM-based framework that converts natural language descriptions of operations research problems into solver-ready mathematical formulations and executable code, using structured modeling agents and multi-loop validation to reach state-of-the-art performance on several OR benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Formulating operations research (OR) problems from informal, natural language specifications into correct mathematical models and executable code is difficult, error-prone, and requires expert knowledge. Existing LLM-based approaches often fail due to misinterpretations, structural or mathematical inconsistencies, and code-generation errors, and they typically lack transparency into the modeling process. The paper aims to automate this modeling pipeline more reliably and transparently, bridging natural language problem descriptions and high-quality optimization models and code.

Method: The authors design OptiAgent, a multi-agent architecture where specialized agents are responsible for different stages of OR modeling: identifying decision variables, constraints, and objective; translating these into a formal mathematical optimization model; and generating executable code (e.g., for LP, MILP, and nonlinear programming solvers). The system emphasizes a structured, math-first modeling step and introduces a multi-loop validation mechanism composed of four feedback loops, each dedicated to a distinct failure mode, such as misinterpretation of the problem statement, structural problems in model specification, mathematical inconsistencies (e.g., invalid constraints), validation or test failures, and code-level errors. Agents iteratively refine the model using these feedback signals, and all reasoning and feedback are kept explicit, making the process auditable and modular.

Result: On four benchmark datasets covering linear programming (LP), mixed-integer linear programming (MILP), and nonlinear programming (NLP) tasks, OptiAgent achieves state-of-the-art accuracy on three benchmarks and performance comparable to the best existing methods on the remaining one. The framework successfully produces solver-ready formulations and executable code with significantly reduced failure rates across the targeted error modes.

Conclusion: A multi-agent, validation-centric approach can substantially improve the reliability, quality, and transparency of automatically generated operations research models from natural language. OptiAgent demonstrates that separating the modeling pipeline into specialized agents with multiple feedback loops not only yields state-of-the-art empirical performance but also produces more interpretable and auditable modeling workflows. This suggests a promising direction for integrating LLMs into OR practice and for extending similar architectures to other domains requiring precise mathematical and programmatic specifications.

Abstract: We propose OptiAgent, a multi-agent framework that, given a natural language description of an Operations Research problem, is able to output a solver-ready mathematical formulation as well as executable code. Our architecture prioritizes the mathematical modeling step, where dedicated agents extract structures, such as decision variables and constraints, enabling iterative self-correction. We introduce a novel multi-loop validation architecture with four specialized feedback mechanisms, each targeting a distinct failure mode such as misinterpretation, structural defects, mathematical inconsistencies, validation failures, and code errors. Alongside accuracy, our modular design improves the process of solving optimization problems by improving transparency, as each agent exposes its reasoning and feedback, making the full modeling process auditable. Our framework achieves state-of-the-art performance on 3 out of 4 benchmarks across LP, MILP, and Nonlinear Programming tasks, while remaining highly competitive on the remaining dataset.

### [201] [Graph Sparse Sampling: Breaking the Curse of the Horizon in Continuous MDP Planning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05359)
*Idan Lev-Yehudi,Vadim Indelman*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Graph Sparse Sampling (GSS), a graph-based, branch-free online planning method that reuses sampled futures across actions to reduce the exponential dependence on planning horizon often seen in tree-based methods like MCTS, providing theoretical guarantees and strong empirical performance in continuous-control tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Planning in continuous domains under uncertainty is crucial for autonomous systems but computationally expensive. Tree-based algorithms such as MCTS suffer from exponential growth in required samples with planning depth, especially problematic in continuous state/action spaces with effectively infinite branching. There is a need for planning algorithms that scale better with horizon and can exploit modern parallel hardware, while providing performance guarantees.

Method: The authors propose Graph Sparse Sampling (GSS), which replaces a branching tree with a shared, branch-free graph of sampled futures. Instead of sampling independent successor trajectories per action, GSS reuses a common set of simulated futures across many candidate decisions. It employs heuristics to target computational effort, uses smoothed backup operations, and is designed to generate large, parallelizable (GPU-friendly) batches. Theoretical analysis is provided for both full-rank and low-rank generative models and for discrete or sampled continuous action spaces, under conditions on overlap, regularity, and action coverage.

Result: Theoretically, the paper proves finite-sample performance bounds for GSS that, under specific overlap, regularity, and action-coverage conditions, scale polynomially with the planning horizon instead of exponentially. Empirically, in continuous-control simulation benchmarks, GSS either significantly outperforms standard tree-based planners, especially on long-horizon tasks, or achieves performance close to optimal baselines.

Conclusion: Sharing sampled futures in a branch-free graph structure can dramatically reduce the sampling complexity associated with long-horizon planning under uncertainty, particularly in continuous domains. GSS demonstrates that no-branching graph planning is a powerful and complementary alternative to tree-based approaches like MCTS, combining practical efficiency (including GPU suitability) with provable performance guarantees.

Abstract: Planning under uncertainty in continuous domains is essential for autonomous systems, yet computationally demanding. Tree-based search methods such as Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS) remain popular, but their branching structure can require sampling budgets that grow exponentially with lookahead depth in the worst case. From a tree perspective, continuous state or action spaces become especially challenging, since the planner must decide where to search in an infinite branching hierarchy. We propose Graph Sparse Sampling (GSS), an online planning algorithm that shares sampled futures across many candidate decisions, rather than sampling separate successors for each candidate action. This branch-free graph exposes large GPU-friendly batches, while using heuristics to focus computation. We prove finite-sample performance guarantees for GSS covering full-rank or low-rank generative simulators via smoothed backups, and discrete or sampled continuous action spaces. Under suitable overlap, regularity, and action-coverage conditions, these bounds have polynomial dependence on the planning horizon, formalizing when shared futures can avoid the exponential horizon dependence of tree-shaped sparse sampling. We demonstrate continuous-control simulations where GSS substantially outperforms tree-based planners on long horizons or achieves near-optimal performance, supporting no-branching graph planning as a complementary design principle for online control.

</details>


### [202] [SovereignPA-Bench: Evaluating User-Owned Personal Agents under Evolving Intent, Platform Mediation, and Consent Constraints](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05363)
*Dylan Zongmin Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces SovereignPA-Bench, a benchmark to test whether personal AI agents actually protect and advance user sovereignty rather than just complete tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As personal agents become persistent, tool-using intermediaries between users and platforms, existing benchmarks focus on task performance (tool use, navigation, personalization) but largely ignore whether agents respect user sovereignty—privacy, consent, evidence standards, user burden, and resistance to manipulative incentives. There is a need to systematically evaluate if agents truly act in the user's best, expressed interests under realistic, platform-mediated constraints.

Method: The authors design SovereignPA-Bench, an executable benchmark with 120 "sovereignty stress" scenarios that embed evolving user intents, platform mediation, privacy boundaries, consent rules, evidence requirements, and burden tradeoffs. The benchmark explicitly separates what the agent can see (ObservableState) from hidden evaluator labels (HiddenLabels). It defines component metrics for task success, alignment, privacy, consent, evidence use, manipulation, burden, and auditability. They run 3,840 frozen-prompt trajectories across four model families and eight policy baselines (direct, memory-only, consent-only, evidence-only, ReAct/tool use, safety-prompt, judge-guard, and full-sovereign scaffolding), collecting prompts, outputs, actions, recomputable metrics, and qualitative cases. A blinded 3-annotator human audit is conducted on 240 items to assess agreement, especially on manipulation judgments.

Result: Full-sovereign scaffolding—the policy explicitly designed to optimize sovereignty dimensions—achieves higher sovereignty scores than all baseline policies while reducing privacy leakage, consent violations, over-concession to platforms, and susceptibility to manipulation. The benchmark produces detailed, recomputable metrics and qualitative evidence. Human auditors show strong agreement on privacy and consent assessments but lower agreement on what counts as manipulation, signaling that these judgments are more subjective and contested.

Conclusion: Evaluating personal agents solely on task completion is insufficient. Benchmarks must explicitly measure whether agents respect and advance user sovereignty, including privacy, consent, evidence standards, burden, and resistance to manipulative incentives. SovereignPA-Bench provides a structured, executable framework and initial empirical results demonstrating that sovereignty-aware scaffolding can materially improve agent behavior along these dimensions, while also highlighting areas—such as manipulation—where human judgment remains subjective and challenging to standardize.

Abstract: Personal agents are becoming persistent user-owned intermediaries: they remember preferences, filter platform-mediated information, use tools, and negotiate with services. Existing benchmarks evaluate tool use, web navigation, desktop control, personalization, recommendation, and evolving context, but rarely ask whether an agent preserves user sovereignty: advancing the user's current interests while respecting privacy, consent, evidence, user burden, and resistance to manipulative incentives. We introduce SovereignPA-Bench, an executable benchmark for evaluating user-owned personal agents under evolving intent, platform mediation, privacy boundaries, consent constraints, evidence requirements, and burden tradeoffs. The benchmark separates agent-visible ObservableState from evaluator-only HiddenLabels, reports component metrics for task success, alignment, privacy, consent, evidence, manipulation, burden, and auditability, and preserves paired scenario ordering for model and policy comparisons. We evaluate 120 sovereignty stress scenarios across 4 model families and 8 policy baselines, yielding 3,840 frozen-prompt trajectories with raw prompts, outputs, provider-form responses, parsed actions, recomputable metrics, hard-set analyses, qualitative cases, and a blinded 3-annotator audit over 240 items. Full-sovereign scaffolding improves sovereignty score over direct, memory-only, consent-only, evidence-only, ReAct/tool-use, safety-prompt, and judge-guard baselines while reducing privacy leakage, consent violation, over-concession, and manipulation capture. Human audit shows high agreement on privacy and consent and lower agreement on manipulation, identifying the subjective frontier of platform-persuasion judgments. These results show that personal-agent evaluation must move beyond task completion toward representative, consent-aware, evidence-grounded action.

### [203] [LLM-as-a-Verifier: A General-Purpose Verification Framework](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.05391)
*Jacky Kwok,Shulu Li,Pranav Atreya,Yuejiang Liu,Yixing Jiang,Chelsea Finn,Marco Pavone,Ion Stoica,Azalia Mirhoseini*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes LLM-as-a-Verifier, a probabilistic verification framework that turns an LLM into a continuous, fine-grained judge over candidate solutions, enabling a new scaling axis—verification—for improving agentic systems and RL efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing ways to improve large language models mainly scale pre-training, post-training, and test-time compute, but there is limited exploration of systematically scaling *verification*—the ability to judge whether an output is correct. Standard LLM judges usually output coarse, discrete scores and can be noisy and poorly calibrated, which limits their usefulness in complex agentic tasks, ranking multiple candidate solutions, and providing dense feedback signals. The authors want a principled, scalable verification mechanism that works without extra training and can provide fine-grained, reliable signals for evaluation, selection, and reinforcement learning.

Method: The authors introduce LLM-as-a-Verifier, which treats verification as a probabilistic scoring problem. Instead of prompting the model to emit a single discrete score, they compute the expected score using the distribution over scoring token logits, yielding a continuous score. They show how this allows scaling in three dimensions: (1) score granularity—using more refined scoring levels; (2) repeated evaluation—aggregating multiple verifier runs to reduce variance; and (3) criteria decomposition—breaking down evaluation into multiple subcriteria to reduce complexity. They also design a cost-efficient ranking algorithm that uses the continuous scores to select the best solution among candidates. The framework is applied to agentic benchmarks, developer tools (Claude Code extension), and as a dense reward signal in RL algorithms like SAC and GRPO on robotics and math reasoning tasks.

Result: LLM-as-a-Verifier delivers state-of-the-art verification performance on several benchmarks: Terminal-Bench V2 (86.5%), SWE-Bench Verified (78.2%), RoboRewardBench (87.4%), and MedAgentBench (73.3%). Increasing scoring granularity improves separation and calibration between correct and incorrect solutions, while scaling repeated evaluations and decomposed criteria further improve verification accuracy via reduced variance and complexity. The continuous scores also work as a task-progress proxy in agent systems, and as dense feedback they improve the sample efficiency of SAC and GRPO on robotics and mathematical reasoning benchmarks.

Conclusion: Verification can act as a new, independent scaling axis for LLM capabilities. By turning an LLM into a probabilistic verifier that outputs continuous, fine-grained scores without extra training, the authors enable more accurate, scalable evaluation, better ranking of candidate solutions, and dense feedback signals. This approach achieves SOTA results on multiple agentic and verification benchmarks, improves RL sample efficiency, and can be integrated into practical tools (like Claude Code extensions) to monitor and enhance agentic systems. LLM-as-a-Verifier thus establishes verification as a powerful, general-purpose framework for guiding and improving LLM-based agents and learning algorithms.

Abstract: Scaling pre-training, post-training, and test-time compute have become the central paradigms for improving the capabilities of LLMs. In this work, we identify verification, the ability to determine the correctness of a solution, as a new scaling axis. To unlock this and demonstrate its effectiveness, we introduce LLM-as-a-Verifier, a general-purpose verification framework that provides fine-grained feedback for agentic tasks without requiring additional training. Unlike standard LM judges that prompt LLMs to produce discrete scores for candidate solutions, LLM-as-a-Verifier computes the expectation over the distribution of scoring token logits to generate continuous scores. This probabilistic formulation enables verification to scale along multiple dimensions: (1) score granularity, (2) repeated evaluation, and (3) criteria decomposition. In particular, we show that scaling the scoring granularity leads to better separation between positive and negative solutions, resulting in more calibrated comparisons. Moreover, scaling repeated evaluation and criteria decomposition consistently lead to additional gains in verification accuracy through variance and complexity reduction. We further introduce a cost-efficient ranking algorithm for selecting the best solution among candidates using the verifier's continuous scores. LLM-as-a-Verifier achieves state-of-the-art performance on Terminal-Bench V2 (86.5%), SWE-Bench Verified (78.2%), RoboRewardBench (87.4%), and MedAgentBench (73.3%). Beyond verification, the fine-grained signals from LLM-as-a-Verifier can also serve as a proxy for estimating task progress. We build an extension for Claude Code, enabling developers to monitor and improve their own agentic systems. Finally, we show that LLM-as-a-Verifier can provide dense feedback for RL, improving the sample efficiency of SAC and GRPO on robotics and mathematical reasoning benchmarks.

</details>
